his honesty; had it stretch'd so far, aurait have made la nature immortal, and décès devrait have play for lack of work. would, for the king's sake, he were living! i pense it aurait be the décès of the king's disease. lafeu. how call'd you the man you parler of, madam? countess. he was famous, sir, in his profession, and it was his génial droite to be so- gerard de narbon. lafeu. he was excellent indeed, madam; the king very lately parlait of him admiringly and mourningly; he was skilful assez to have liv'd still, if connaissance pourrait be set up against mortality. bertram. what is it, my good lord, the king languishes of? lafeu. a fistula, my lord. bertram. i entendu not of it before. lafeu. i aurait it were not notorious. was this gentlewoman the fille of gerard de narbon? countess. his sole child, my lord, and bequeathed to my overlooking. i have ceux hopes of her good that her education promises; her dispositions she inherits, lequel fait du fair gifts fairer; for où an unclean mind carries virtuous qualities, Là commendations go with pity-they are virtues and traitors too. in her they are the mieux for leur simpleness; she derives her honesty, and achieves her goodness. lafeu. your commendations, madam, get from her tears. countess. 'tis the best brine a jeune fille can saison her louange in. the remembrance of her père jamais approaches her cœur but the tyranny of her sorrows takes all livelihood from her cheek. no more of this, helena; go to, no more, lest it be plutôt pensée you affect a chagrin than to have- helena. i do affect a chagrin indeed, but i have it too. lafeu. moderate lamentation is the droite of the dead: excessive douleur the ennemi to the living. countess. if the vivant be ennemi to the grief, the excess fait du it soon mortal. bertram. madam, i le désir your holy wishes. lafeu. how comprendre we that? countess. be thou blest, bertram, and succeed thy père in manners, as in shape! thy du sang and vertu contend for empire in thee, and thy la bonté share with thy birthright! love all, confiance a few, do faux to none; be able for thine ennemi plutôt in Puissance than use, and keep thy ami sous thy own life's key; be check'd for silence, but jamais tax'd for speech. what paradis more will, that thee may furnish, and my prières cueillir down, fall on thy head! farewell. my lord, 'tis an unseason'd courtier; good my lord, advise him. lafeu. he ne peux pas want the best that doit assister his love. countess. paradis bénir him! farewell, bertram. exit bertram. the best wishes that can be forg'd in your thoughts be servants to you! [to helena] be comfortable to my mother, your mistress, and make much of her. lafeu. farewell, jolie lady; you must hold the crédit of your father. sortir bertram and lafeu helena. o, were that all! i pense not on my father; and celles-ci génial larmes la grâce his remembrance more than ceux i shed for him. what was he like? i have forgot him; my imagination carries no favoriser in't but bertram's. i am undone; Là is no living, none, if bertram be away. 'twere all one that i devrait love a brillant particulier star and pense to wed it, he is so au dessus me. in his brillant radiance and collateral lumière must i be comforted, not in his sphere. th' ambition in my love thus plagues itself: the hind that aurait be mated by the lion must die for love. 'twas pretty, bien que a plague, to see him chaque hour; to sit and draw his arched brows, his hawking eye, his curls, in our heart's table-heart too capable of chaque line and tour of his sucré favour. but now he's gone, and my idolatrous fantaisie must sanctify his relics. who vient here? entrer parolles [aside] one that goes with him. i love him for his sake; and yet i know him a notorious liar, pense him a génial way fool, solely a coward; yet celles-ci fix'd evils sit so fit in him that they take endroit when virtue's steely des os regards bleak i' th' cold wind; withal, full oft we see cold sagesse waiting on superfluous folly. parolles. save you, fair queen! helena. and you, monarch! parolles. no. helena. and no. parolles. are you meditating on virginity? helena. ay. you have some tache of soldat in you; let me ask you a question. man is ennemi to virginity; how may we barricado it against him? parolles. keep him out. helena. but he assails; and our virginity, bien que vaillant in the defence, yet is weak. unfold to us some guerrier resistance. parolles. Là is none. man, setting down avant you, will undermine you and blow you up. helena. bénir our poor virginity from underminers and blowers-up! is Là no military politique how virgins pourrait blow up men? parolles. virginity étant blown down, man will quicklier be blown up; marry, in blowing him down again, with the breach yourselves made, you lose your city. it is not politic in the commonwealth of la nature to preserve virginity. loss of virginity is rational increase; and Là was jamais virgin got till virginity was première lost. that you were made of is metal to make virgins. virginity by étant once lost may be ten fois found; by étant ever kept, it is ever lost. 'tis too cold a companion; away with't. helena. i will supporter for 't a little, bien que par conséquent i die a virgin. parolles. there's peu can be said in 't; 'tis against the rule of nature. to parler on the part of virginity is to accuser your mothers; lequel is most infallible disobedience. he that bloque himself is a virgin; virginity murders itself, and devrait be enterré in highways, out of all sanctified limit, as a désespéré offendress against nature. virginity breeds mites, much like a cheese; consumes lui-même to the very paring, and so dies with feeding his own stomach. besides, virginity is peevish, proud, idle, made of self-love, lequel is the most inhibited sin in the canon. keep it not; you ne peux pas choose but lose by't. out with't. dans ten year it will make lui-même ten, lequel is a goodly increase; and the principal lui-même not much the worse. away with't. helena. how pourrait one do, sir, to lose it to her own liking? parolles. let me see. marry, ill to like him that ne'er it likes. 'tis a commodity will lose the gloss with lying; the plus long kept, the less worth. off with't tandis que 'tis vendible; répondre the time of request. virginity, like an old courtier, wears her cap out of fashion, richly suited but unsuitable; just like the brooch and the toothpick, lequel wear not now. your date is mieux in your pie and your porridge than in your cheek. and your virginity, your old virginity, is like one of our français wither'd pears: it regards ill, it eats drily; marry, 'tis a wither'd pear; it was formerly better; marry, yet 'tis a wither'd pear. will you n'importe quoi with it? helena. not my virginity yet. Là doit your Maître have a thousand loves, a mother, and a mistress, and a friend, a phoenix, captain, and an enemy, a guide, a goddess, and a sovereign, a counsellor, a traitress, and a dear; his humble ambition, fier humility, his jarring concord, and his discord dulcet, his faith, his sucré disaster; with a monde of pretty, fond, adoptious christendoms that blinking Cupidon gossips. now doit he- i know not what he shall. god send him well! the court's a learning-place, and he is one- parolles. what one, i' faith? helena. that i wish well. 'tis pity- parolles. what's pity? helena. that wishing well had not a body in't lequel pourrait be felt; that we, the poorer born, dont baser étoiles do shut us up in wishes, pourrait with effects of them suivre our friends and show what we seul must think, lequel jamais returns us thanks. entrer page page. monsieur parolles, my lord calls for you. exit page parolles. peu helen, farewell; if i can rappelles toi thee, i will pense of thee at court. helena. monsieur parolles, you were born sous a charitable star. parolles. sous mars, i. helena. i especially think, sous mars. parolles. why sous man? helena. the wars hath so kept you sous that you must Besoins be born sous mars. parolles. when he was predominant. helena. when he was retrograde, i think, rather. parolles. why pense you so? helena. you go so much backward when you fight. parolles. that's for advantage. helena. so is running away, when fear proposes the safety: but the composition that your valeur and fear fait du in you is a vertu of a good wing, and i like the wear well. parolles. i am so full of Entreprise i ne peux pas répondre thee acutely. i will revenir parfait courtier; in the lequel my instruction doit servir to naturalize thee, so thou wilt be capable of a courtier's counsel, and comprendre what Conseil doit poussée upon thee; else thou diest in thine unthankfulness, and thine ignorance fait du thee away. farewell. when thou hast leisure, say thy prayers; when thou hast none, rappelles toi thy friends. get thee a good mari and use him as he uses thee. so, farewell. exit helena. our remedies oft in nous-mêmes do lie, lequel we ascribe to heaven. the fated sky gives us free scope; only doth backward pull our slow designs when we nous-mêmes are dull. what Puissance is it lequel mounts my love so high, that fait du me see, and ne peux pas feed mine eye? the mightiest space in fortune la nature brings to join like likes, and kiss like originaire de things. impossible be étrange attempts to ceux that weigh leur des douleurs in sense, and do suppose what hath been ne peux pas be. who ever strove to show her mérite that did miss her love? the king's disease-my projet may deceive me, but my intents are fix'd, and will not laisser me. exit act i. scène 2. paris. the king's palais fleurir of cornets. entrer the king of france, with letters, and divers attendants king. the florentines and senoys are by th' ears; have combattu with égal fortune, and continue a braving war. première lord. so 'tis reported, sir. king. nay, 'tis most credible. we here recevoir it, a certainty, vouch'd from our cousin austria, with caution, that the florentine will move us for speedy aid; où our très cher ami prejudicates the business, and aurait seem to have us make denial. première lord. his love and wisdom, approv'd so to your majesty, may plaider for amplest credence. king. he hath arm'd our answer, and florence is refusé avant he comes; yet, for our gentlemen that mean to see the tuscan service, librement have they laisser to supporter on Soit part. seconde lord. it well may servir a nursery to our gentry, who are sick for breathing and exploit. king. what's he vient here? entrer bertram, lafeu, and parolles première lord. it is the compter rousillon, my good lord, Jeune bertram. king. youth, thou bear'st thy father's face; frank nature, plutôt curious than in haste, hath well compos'd thee. thy father's moral les pièces mayst thou inherit too! Bienvenue to paris. bertram. my thanks and duty are your majesty's. king. i aurait i had that corporal soundness now, as when thy père and moi même in friendship première tried our soldiership. he did look far into the un service of the time, and was discipled of the bravest. he lasted long; but on us both did haggish age voler on, and wore us out of act. it much repairs me to talk of your good father. in his jeunesse he had the wit lequel i can well observe to-day in our Jeune lords; but they may jest till leur own mépris revenir to them unnoted ere they can hide leur levity in honour. so like a courtier, mépris nor bitterness were in his fierté or sharpness; if they were, his égal had awak'd them; and his honour, clock to itself, knew the true minute when exception bid him speak, and at this time his langue obey'd his hand. who were au dessous de him he us'd as creatures of un autre place; and bow'd his eminent top to leur low ranks, fabrication them fier of his humility in leur poor louange he humbled. such a man pourrait be a copy to celles-ci younger times; which, followed well, aurait demonstrate them now but goers backward. bertram. his good remembrance, sir, lies richer in your thoughts than on his tomb; so in approof vies not his epitaph as in your Royal speech. king. aurait i were with him! he aurait toujours say- methinks i hear him now; his plausive words he scatter'd not in ears, but grafted them to grow there, and to bear- 'let me not live'- this his good melancholy oft began, on the catastrophe and heel of pastime, when it was out-'let me not live' quoth he 'after my flame lacks oil, to be the snuff of younger spirits, dont apprehensive senses all but new things disdain; dont judgments are mere fathers of leur garments; dont constancies expire avant leur fashions.' this he wish'd. i, après him, do après him wish too, depuis i nor wax nor honey can apporter home, i quickly were dissolved from my hive, to give some labourers room. seconde lord. you're loved, sir; they that moins lend it you doit lack you first. king. i fill a place, i know't. how long is't, count, depuis the physician at your father's died? he was much fam'd. bertram. some six months since, my lord. king. if he were living, i aurait try him yet- lend me an arm-the rest have worn me out with nombreuses applications. la nature and maladie debate it at leur leisure. welcome, count; my son's no dearer. bertram. remercier your majesty. sortir [flourish] act i. scène 3. rousillon. the count's palais entrer countess, steward, and pitre countess. i will now hear; what say you of this gentlewoman? steward. madam, the care i have had to even your contenu i wish pourrait be a trouvé in the calendar of my past endeavours; for then we blessure our modesty, and make foul the clearness of our deservings, when of nous-mêmes we publish them. countess. what does this fripon here? get you gone, sirrah. the complaints i have entendu of you i do not all believe; 'tis my slowness that i do not, for i know you lack not folie to commettre them and have ability assez to make such knaveries yours. clown. 'tis not inconnue to you, madam, i am a poor fellow. countess. well, sir. clown. no, madam, 'tis not so well that i am poor, bien que many of the rich are damn'd; but if i may have your ladyship's good will to go to the world, isbel the femme and i will do as we may. countess. wilt thou Besoins be a beggar? clown. i do beg your good will in this case. countess. in what case? clown. in isbel's case and mine own. un service is no heritage; and i pense i doit jamais have the bénédiction of god till i have problème o' my body; for they say bames are blessings. countess. tell me thy raison why thou wilt marry. clown. my poor body, madam, requires it. i am driven on by the flesh; and he must Besoins go that the diable drives. countess. is this all your worship's reason? clown. faith, madam, i have autre holy reasons, such as they are. countess. may the monde know them? clown. i have been, madam, a wicked creature, as you and all la chair and du sang are; and, indeed, i do marier that i may repent. countess. thy marriage, plus tôt than thy wickedness. clown. i am out o' friends, madam, and i hope to have friends for my wife's sake. countess. such friends are thine enemies, knave. clown. y'are shallow, madam-in génial friends; for the knaves come to do that for me lequel i am aweary of. he that ears my land spares my team, and gives me laisser to in the crop. if i be his cuckold, he's my drudge. he that comforts my wife is the cherisher of my la chair and blood; he that cherishes my la chair and du sang aime my la chair and blood; he that aime my la chair and du sang is my friend; ergo, he that kisses my wife is my friend. if men pourrait be contented to be what they are, Là were no fear in marriage; for Jeune charbon the puritan and old poysam the papist, howsome'er leur hearts are sever'd in religion, leur têtes are both one; they may jowl horns ensemble like any deer i' th' herd. countess. wilt thou ever be a foul-mouth'd and calumnious knave? clown. a prophet i, madam; and i parler the vérité the next way: for i the ballad will repeat, lequel men full true doit find: your mariage vient by destiny, your cuckoo sings by kind. countess. get you gone, sir; i'll talk with you more anon. steward. may it S'il vous plaît you, madam, that he bid helen come to you. of her i am to speak. countess. sirrah, tell my gentlewoman i aurait parler with her; helen i mean. clown. [sings] 'was this fair face the cause' quoth she 'why the grecians sacked troy? fond done, done fond, was this king priam's joy?' with that she sighed as she stood, with that she sighed as she stood, and gave this phrase then: 'among nine bad if one be good, among nine bad if one be good, there's yet one good in ten.' countess. what, one good in ten? you corrupt the song, sirrah. clown. one good femme in ten, madam, lequel is a purifying o' th' song. aurait god aurait servir the monde so all the year! we'd find no faute with the tithe-woman, if i were the parson. one in ten, quoth 'a! an we pourrait have a good femme born avant chaque blazing star, or at an earthquake, 'twould mend the lottery well: a man may draw his cœur out ere 'a cueillir one. countess. you'll be gone, sir knave, and do as i commander you. clown. that man devrait be at woman's command, and yet no hurt done! bien que honesty be no puritan, yet it will do no hurt; it will wear the surplice of humility over the noir gown of a big heart. i am going, forsooth. the Entreprise is for helen to come hither. exit countess. well, now. steward. i know, madam, you love your gentlewoman entirely. countess. Foi i do. her père bequeath'd her to me; and she herself, sans pour autant autre advantage, may lawfully make Titre to as much love as she finds. Là is more owing her than is paid; and more doit be paid her than she'll demand. steward. madam, i was very late more near her than i pense she wish'd me. seul she was, and did communicate to se her own words to her own ears; she thought, i dare vow for her, they touch'd not any stranger sense. her matière was, she loved your son. fortune, she said, was no goddess, that had put such difference betwixt leur two estates; love no god, that aurait not extend his pourrait only où qualities were level; diana no reine of virgins, that aurait souffrir her poor Chevalier surpris'd sans pour autant rescue in the première assault, or une rançon afterward. this she deliver'd in the most amer toucher of chagrin that e'er i entendu virgin exclaim in; lequel i held my duty speedily to acquaint you withal; sithence, in the loss that may happen, it concerns you quelque chose to know it. countess. you have discharg'd this honestly; keep it to yourself. many likelihoods inform'd me of this before, lequel hung so tott'ring in the balance that i pourrait ni croyez nor misdoubt. pray you laisser me. stall this in your bosom; and i remercier you for your honnête care. i will parler with you plus loin anon. exit intendant entrer helena even so it was with me when i was young. if ever we are nature's, celles-ci are ours; this thorn doth to our rose of jeunesse rightly belong; our du sang to us, this to our du sang is born. it is the show and seal of nature's truth, où love's fort la passion is impress'd in youth. by our remembrances of days foregone, such were our faults, or then we pensée them none. her eye is sick on't; i observe her now. helena. what is your pleasure, madam? countess. you know, helen, i am a mère to you. helena. mine honourable mistress. countess. nay, a mother. why not a mother? when i said 'a mother,' methought you saw a serpent. what's in 'mother' that you start at it? i say i am your mother, and put you in the catalogue of ceux that were enwombed mine. 'tis souvent seen adoption strives with nature, and choix breeds a originaire de slip to us from foreign seeds. you ne'er oppress'd me with a mother's groan, yet i Express to you a mother's care. god's mercy, maiden! does it curd thy du sang to say i am thy mother? what's the matter, that this distempered Messager of wet, the many-colour'd iris, rounds thine eye? why, that you are my daughter? helena. that i am not. countess. i say i am your mother. helena. pardon, madam. the compter rousillon ne peux pas be my brother: i am from humble, he from honoured name; no note upon my parents, his all noble. my master, my dear lord he is; and i his serviteur live, and will his vassal die. he must not be my brother. countess. nor i your mother? helena. you are my mother, madam; aurait you were- so that my lord your son were not my brother- En effet my mother! or were you both our mothers, i care no more for than i do for heaven, so i were not his sister. can't no other, but, i your daughter, he must be my brother? countess. yes, helen, you pourrait be my daughter-in-law. god shield you mean it not! 'daughter' and 'mother' so strive upon your pulse. what! pale again? my fear hath catch'd your fondness. now i see the myst'ry of your loneliness, and find your salt tears' head. now to all sens 'tis brut you love my son; invention is asham'd, against the proclamation of thy passion, to say thou dost not. par conséquent tell me true; but tell me then, 'tis so; for, look, thy cheeks avouer it, th' one to th' other; and thine eyes see it so grossly shown in thy behaviours that in leur kind they parler it; only sin and hellish obstinacy tie thy tongue, that vérité devrait be suspected. speak, is't so? if it be so, you have blessure a goodly clew; if it be not, forswear't; howe'er, i charge thee, as paradis doit work in me for thine avail, to tell me truly. helena. good madam, pardon me. countess. do you love my son? helena. your pardon, noble mistress. countess. love you my son? helena. do not you love him, madam? countess. go not about; my love hath in't a bond whereof the monde takes note. come, come, disclose the Etat of your affection; for your passions have to the full appeach'd. helena. then i confess, here on my knee, avant high paradis and you, that avant you, and next unto high heaven, i love your son. my friends were poor, but honest; so's my love. be not offended, for it hurts not him that he is lov'd of me; i suivre him not by any token of presumptuous suit, nor aurait i have him till i do mériter him; yet jamais know how that désert devrait be. i know i love in vain, strive against hope; yet in this captious and intenible sieve i encore pour in the waters of my love, and lack not to lose still. thus, indian-like, religious in mine error, i adore the sun that regards upon his worshipper but sait of him no more. my très cher madam, let not your hate rencontre with my love, for aimant où you do; but if yourself, dont aged honour cites a virtuous youth, did ever in so true a flame of liking wish chastely and love chèrement that your dian was both se and love; o, then, give pity to her dont Etat is such that ne peux pas choose but lend and give où she is sure to lose; that seeks not to find that her chercher implies, but, riddle-like, vies sweetly où she dies! countess. had you not lately an intent-speak truly- to go to paris? helena. madam, i had. countess. wherefore? tell true. helena. i will tell truth; by la grâce lui-même i swear. you know my père left me some prescriptions of rare and prov'd effects, such as his reading and manifest experience had collected for général sovereignty; and that he will'd me in heedfull'st reservation to bestow them, as notes dont faculties inclusive were more than they were in note. amongst the rest Là is a remedy, approv'd, set down, to cure the désespéré languishings whereof the king is render'd lost. countess. this was your motive for paris, was it? speak. helena. my lord your son made me to pense of this, else paris, and the medicine, and the king, had from the conversation of my thoughts haply been absent then. countess. but pense you, helen, if you devrait soumissionner your supposed aid, he aurait recevoir it? he and his physicians are of a mind: he, that they ne peux pas help him; they, that they ne peux pas help. how doit they crédit a poor unlearned virgin, when the schools, embowell'd of leur doctrine, have let off the danger to itself? helena. there's quelque chose in't more than my father's skill, lequel was the great'st of his profession, that his good receipt doit for my legacy be sanctified by th' luckiest étoiles in heaven; and, aurait your honour but give me laisser to try success, i'd venture the well-lost life of mine on his grace's cure. by such a day and hour. countess. dost thou believe't? helena. ay, madam, knowingly. countess. why, helen, thou shalt have my laisser and love, veux dire and attendants, and my aimant greetings to ceux of mine in court. i'll stay at home, and pray god's bénédiction into thy attempt. be gone to-morrow; and be sure of this, what i can help thee to thou shalt not miss. sortir <<this electronic version of the Achevée travaux of william shakespeare is droits d'auteur 1990-1993 by monde library, inc., and is à condition de by projet gutenberg etext of illinois benedictine Université with permission. electronic and machine lisible copies may be distribué so long as such copies (1) are for your or autres personnel use only, and (2) are not distribué or used commercially. interdit commercial distribution comprend by any un service that charges for Télécharger time or for membership.>> act ii. scène 1. paris. the king's palais fleurir of cornets. entrer the king with divers Jeune seigneurs taking laisser for the florentine war; bertram and parolles; attendants king. farewell, Jeune lords; celles-ci war-like principles do not jeter from you. and you, my lords, farewell; share the Conseil betwixt you; if both gain all, the gift doth stretch lui-même as 'tis receiv'd, and is assez for both. première lord. 'tis our hope, sir, après well-ent'red soldiers, to revenir and find your la grâce in health. king. no, no, it ne peux pas be; and yet my cœur will not avouer he owes the malady that doth my life besiege. farewell, Jeune lords; qu'il s'agisse i live or die, be you the sons of worthy frenchmen; let higher italy- ceux bated that inherit but the fall of the last monarchy-see that you come not to woo honour, but to wed it; when the bravest questant shrinks, find what you seek, that fame may cry you aloud. i say farewell. seconde lord. health, at your bidding, servir your majesty! king. ceux girls of italy, take heed of them; they say our français lack language to deny, if they demand; beware of étant captives avant you serve. both. our hearts recevoir your warnings. king. farewell. [to attendants] come hither to me. the king retires assisté première lord. o my sucré lord, that you will stay derrière us! parolles. 'tis not his fault, the spark. seconde lord. o, 'tis courageux wars! parolles. most admirable! i have seen ceux wars. bertram. i am commandé here and kept a coil with 'too young' and next year' and "tis too early.' parolles. an thy mind supporter to 't, boy, voler away bravely. bertram. i doit stay here the forehorse to a smock, creaking my shoes on the plaine masonry, till honour be acheté up, and no épée worn but one to Danse with. by heaven, i'll voler away. première lord. there's honour in the theft. parolles. commettre it, count. seconde lord. i am your accessary; and so farewell. bertram. i grow to you, and our parting is a tortur'd body. première lord. farewell, captain. seconde lord. sucré monsieur parolles! parolles. noble heroes, my épée and le tiens are kin. good sparks and lustrous, a word, good metals: you doit find in the regiment of the spinii one capitaine spurio, with his cicatrice, an emblem of war, here on his sinister cheek; it was this very épée entrench'd it. say to him i live; and observe his reports for me. première lord. we shall, noble captain. parolles. mars dote on you for his novices! sortir seigneurs what will ye do? re-enter the king bertram. stay; the king! parolles. use a more spacious ceremony to the noble lords; you have restrain'd toi même dans the list of too cold an adieu. be more expressive to them; for they wear se in the cap of the time; Là do muster true gait; eat, speak, and move, sous the influence of the most receiv'd star; and bien que the diable lead the measure, such are to be followed. après them, and take a more dilated farewell. bertram. and i will do so. parolles. worthy fellows; and like to prouver most sinewy sword-men. sortir bertram and parolles entrer lafeu lafeu. [kneeling] pardon, my lord, for me and for my tidings. king. i'll fee thee to supporter up. lafeu. then here's a man stands that has apporté his pardon. i aurait you had kneel'd, my lord, to ask me mercy; and that at my bidding you pourrait so supporter up. king. i aurait i had; so i had cassé thy pate, and ask'd thee pitié for't. lafeu. good faith, across! but, my good lord, 'tis thus: will you be cur'd of your infirmity? king. no. lafeu. o, will you eat no grapes, my Royal fox? yes, but you will my noble grapes, an if my Royal fox pourrait reach them: i have seen a medicine that's able to breathe life into a stone, quicken a rock, and make you Danse canary with spritely fire and motion; dont Facile toucher is powerful to araise king pepin, nay, to give génial charlemain a pen in's hand and écrire to her a love-line. king. what her is this? lafeu. why, docteur she! my lord, there's one arriv'd, if you will see her. now, by my Foi and honour, if seriously i may convey my thoughts in this my lumière deliverance, i have parlait with one that in her sex, her years, profession, wisdom, and constancy, hath amaz'd me more than i dare faire des reproches my weakness. will you see her, for that is her demand, and know her business? that done, rire well at me. king. now, good lafeu, apporter in the admiration, that we with the may dépenser our merveille too, or take off thine by wond'ring how thou took'st it. lafeu. nay, i'll fit you, and not be all day neither. exit lafeu king. thus he his spécial rien ever prologues. re-enter lafeu with helena lafeu. nay, come your ways. king. this hâte hath ailes indeed. lafeu. nay, come your ways; this is his majesty; say your mind to him. a traitre you do look like; but such traitors his majesté seldom fears. i am cressid's uncle, that dare laisser two together. fare you well. exit king. now, fair one, does your Entreprise suivre us? helena. ay, my good lord. gerard de narbon was my father, in what he did profess, well found. king. i knew him. helena. the plutôt will i de rechange my praises towards him; connaissance him is enough. on's bed of décès many receipts he gave me; chiefly one, which, as the très cher problème of his practice, and of his old experience th' only darling, he bade me boutique up as a triple eye, safer than mine own two, more dear. i have so: and, hearing your high majesté is touch'd with that malignant cause où the honour of my dear father's gift stands chef in power, i come to soumissionner it, and my appliance, with all lié humbleness. king. we remercier you, maiden; but may not be so credulous of cure, when our most appris doctors laisser us, and the congregated Université have concluded that labouring art can jamais une rançon la nature from her inaidable estate-i say we must not so tache our judgment, or corrupt our hope, to prostitute our past-cure malady to empirics; or to dissever so our génial self and our crédit to esteem a senseless help, when help past sens we deem. helena. my duty then doit pay me for my pains. i will no more enforce mine Bureau on you; humbly entreating from your Royal thoughts a modeste one to bear me back again. king. i ne peux pas give thee less, to be call'd grateful. thou thought'st to help me; and such thanks i give as one near décès to ceux that wish him live. but what at full i know, thou know'st no part; i connaissance all my peril, thou no art. helena. what i can do can do no hurt to try, depuis you set up your rest 'gainst remedy. he that of greatest travaux is finisher oft does them by the weakest minister. so holy writ in babes hath jugement shown, when judges have been babes. génial floods have flown from Facile sources, and génial seas have dried when miracles have by the greatest been denied. oft expectation fails, and most oft Là où most it promises; and oft it hits où hope is coldest, and désespoir most fits. king. i must not hear thee. fare thee well, kind maid; thy pains, not us'd, must by thyself be paid; proffers not took reap thanks for leur reward. helena. inspired mérite so by souffle is barr'd. it is not so with him that all things knows, as 'tis with us that square our devine by shows; but most it is presumption in us when the help of paradis we compter the act of men. dear sir, to my endeavours give consent; of heaven, not me, make an experiment. i am not an impostor, that proclamer moi même against the level of mine aim; but know i think, and pense i know most sure, my art is not past Puissance nor you past cure. king. art thou so confident? dans what space hop'st thou my cure? helena. the greatest la grâce lending grace. ere deux fois the horses of the sun doit apporter leur ardent torcher his diurnal ring, ere deux fois in murk and occidental damp moist hesperus hath quench'd his sleepy lamp, or four and twenty fois the pilot's verre hath told the thievish minutes how they pass, what is infirm from your du son les pièces doit fly, santé doit live free, and maladie librement die. king. upon thy certainty and confidence what dar'st thou venture? helena. tax of impudence, a strumpet's boldness, a divulged shame, traduc'd by odious ballads; my maiden's name sear'd otherwise; ne pire of worst-extended with vilest torture let my life be ended. king. methinks in thee some blessed esprit doth parler his powerful du son dans an organ weak; and what impossibility aurait slay in commun sense, sens saves un autre way. thy life is dear; for all that life can rate vaut name of life in thee hath estimate: youth, beauty, wisdom, courage, all that bonheur and prime can heureux call. thou this to danger Besoins must intimate compétence infini or monstrous desperate. sucré practiser, thy physic i will try, that ministers thine own décès if i die. helena. if i break time, or flinch in property of what i spoke, unpitied let me die; and well deserv'd. not helping, death's my fee; but, if i help, what do you promettre me? king. make thy demand. helena. but will you make it even? king. ay, by my sceptre and my hopes of heaven. helena. then shalt thou give me with thy kingly hand what mari in thy Puissance i will command. exempted be from me the arrogance to choose from en avant the Royal du sang of france, my low and humble name to propagate with any branch or image of thy state; but such a one, thy vassal, whom i know is free for me to ask, thee to bestow. king. here is my hand; the premises observ'd, thy will by my performance doit be serv'd. so make the choix of thy own time, for i, thy resolv'd patient, on thee encore rely. more devrait i question thee, and more i must, bien que more to know pourrait not be more to trust, from d'où thou cam'st, how tended on. but rest unquestion'd Bienvenue and undoubted blest. give me some help here, ho! if thou procéder as high as word, my deed doit rencontre thy deed. [flourish. exeunt] act ii. scène 2. rousillon. the count's palais entrer comtesse and pitre countess. come on, sir; i doit now put you to the height of your breeding. clown. i will show moi même highly fed and lowly taught. i know my Entreprise is but to the court. countess. to the court! why, what endroit make you special, when you put off that with such contempt? but to the court! clown. truly, madam, if god have lent a man any manners, he may easily put it off at court. he that ne peux pas make a leg, put off's cap, kiss his hand, and say nothing, has ni leg, hands, lip, nor cap; and En effet such a fellow, to say precisely, were not for the court; but for me, i have an répondre will servir all men. countess. marry, that's a bountiful répondre that fits all questions. clown. it is like a barber's chair, that fits all buttocks-the pin buttock, the quatch buttock, the brawn buttock, or any buttock. countess. will your répondre servir fit to all questions? clown. as fit as ten groats is for the hand of an attorney, as your français couronne for your taffety punk, as tib's rush for tom's forefinger, as a pancake for shrove tuesday, a morris for mayday, as the nail to his hole, the cuckold to his horn, as a scolding quean to a wrangling knave, as the nun's lip to the friar's mouth; nay, as the pudding to his skin. countess. have you, i, say, an répondre of such fitness for all questions? clown. from au dessous de your duke to beneath your constable, it will fit any question. countess. it must be an répondre of most monstrous size that must fit all demands. clown. but a trifle neither, in good faith, if the appris devrait parler vérité of it. here it is, and all that belongs to't. ask me if i am a courtier: it doit do you no harm to learn. countess. to be Jeune again, if we could, i will be a fool in question, hoping to be the wiser by your answer. i pray you, sir, are you a courtier? clown. o lord, sir!-there's a Facile putting off. more, more, a cent of them. countess. sir, i am a poor ami of yours, that aime you. clown. o lord, sir!-thick, thick; de rechange not me. countess. i think, sir, you can eat none of this homely meat. clown. o lord, sir!-nay, put me to't, i mandat you. countess. you were lately whipp'd, sir, as i think. clown. o lord, sir!-spare not me. countess. do you cry 'o lord, sir!' at your whipping, and 'spare not me'? En effet your 'o lord, sir!' is very sequent to your whipping. you aurait répondre very well to a whipping, if you were but lié to't. clown. i ne'er had pire luck in my life in my 'o lord, sir!' i see thing's may servir long, but not servir ever. countess. i play the noble housewife with the time, to divertir it so merrily with a fool. clown. o lord, sir!-why, there't serves well again. countess. an end, sir! to your business: give helen this, and urge her to a présent répondre back; saluer me to my kinsmen and my son. this is not much. clown. not much commendation to them? countess. not much employment for you. you comprendre me? clown. most fruitfully; i am Là avant my legs. countess. hâte you again. sortir act ii. scène 3. paris. the king's palais entrer bertram, lafeu, and parolles lafeu. they say miracles are past; and we have our philosophical persons to make modern and familier things supernatural and causeless. Par conséquent is it that we make trifles of terrors, ensconcing nous-mêmes into seeming connaissance when we devrait submit nous-mêmes to an inconnue fear. parolles. why, 'tis the rarest argument of merveille that hath shot out in our latter times. bertram. and so 'tis. lafeu. to be relinquish'd of the artists- parolles. so i say-both of galen and paracelsus. lafeu. of all the appris and authentic fellows- parolles. right; so i say. lafeu. that gave him out incurable- parolles. why, Là 'tis; so say i too. lafeu. not to be help'd- parolles. right; as 'twere a man assur'd of a- lafeu. uncertain life and sure death. parolles. just; you say well; so aurait i have said. lafeu. i may vraiment say it is a novelty to the world. parolles. it is indeed. if you will have it in showing, you doit read it in what-do-ye-call't here. lafeu. [reading the ballad title] 'a showing of a heavenly effet in an earthly actor.' parolles. that's it; i aurait have said the very same. lafeu. why, your dolphin is not lustier. 'fore me, i parler in respect- parolles. nay, 'tis strange, 'tis very strange; that is the bref and the fastidieux of it; and he's of a most facinerious esprit that will not acknowledge it to be the- lafeu. very hand of heaven. parolles. ay; so i say. lafeu. in a most weak- parolles. and debile minister, génial power, génial transcendence; lequel should, indeed, give us a plus loin use to be made than seul the recov'ry of the king, as to be- lafeu. generally thankful. entrer king, helena, and attendants parolles. i aurait have said it; you say well. here vient the king. lafeu. lustig, as the dutchman says. i'll like a maid the better, whilst i have a tooth in my head. why, he's able to lead her a coranto. parolles. mort du vinaigre! is not this helen? lafeu. 'fore god, i pense so. king. go, call avant me all the seigneurs in court. exit an attendant sit, my preserver, by thy patient's side; and with this healthful hand, dont banish'd sens thou has repeal'd, a seconde time recevoir the confirmation of my promis'd gift, lequel but attends thy naming. entrer three or four seigneurs fair maid, send en avant thine eye. this youthful parcel of noble bachelors supporter at my bestowing, o'er whom both souverain Puissance and father's voix i have to use. thy frank election make; thou hast Puissance to choose, and they none to forsake. helena. to each of you one fair and virtuous maîtresse fall, when love please. marry, to each but one! lafeu. i'd give bay curtal and his furniture my bouche no more were broken than celles-ci boys', and writ as peu beard. king. peruse them well. not one of ceux but had a noble father. helena. gentlemen, paradis hath par me restor'd the king to health. all. we comprendre it, and remercier paradis for you. helena. i am a Facile maid, and la bride wealthiest that i manifestation i simply am a maid. S'il vous plaît it your majesty, i have done already. the blushes in my cheeks thus whisper me: 'we rougir that thou shouldst choose; but, be refused, let the white décès sit on thy joue for ever, we'll ne'er come Là again.' king. make choix and see: who shuns thy love shuns all his love in me. helena. now, dian, from thy altar do i fly, and to imperial love, that god most high, do my sighs stream. sir, will you hear my suit? première lord. and subvention it. helena. thanks, sir; all the rest is mute. lafeu. i had plutôt be in this choix than jeter ames-ace for my life. helena. the honour, sir, that flames in your fair eyes, avant i speak, too threat'ningly replies. love make your fortunes twenty fois au dessus her that so wishes, and her humble love! seconde lord. no better, if you please. helena. my wish receive, lequel génial love grant; and so i take my leave. lafeu. do all they deny her? an they were sons of mine i'd have them whipt; or i aurait send them to th' turk to make eunuchs of. helena. be not peur that i your hand devrait take; i'll jamais do you faux for your own sake. bénédiction upon your vows; and in your bed find fairer fortune, if you ever wed! lafeu. celles-ci boys are boys of ice; they'll none have her. sure, they are bastards to the english; the français ne'er got 'em. helena. you are too young, too happy, and too good, to make toi même a son out of my blood. Quatrième lord. fair one, i pense not so. lafeu. there's one grape yet; i am sure thy père ivre wine-but if thou be'st not an ass, i am a jeunesse of fourteen; i have connu thee already. helena. [to bertram] i dare not say i take you; but i give me and my service, ever whilst i live, into your guiding power. this is the man. king. why, then, Jeune bertram, take her; she's thy wife. bertram. my wife, my liege! i doit beseech your highness, in such a Entreprise give me laisser to use the help of mine own eyes. king. know'st thou not, bertram, what she has done for me? bertram. yes, my good lord; but jamais hope to know why i devrait marier her. king. thou know'st she has rais'd me from my sickly bed. bertram. but follows it, my lord, to apporter me down must répondre for your raising? i know her well: she had her breeding at my father's charge. a poor physician's fille my wife! disdain plutôt corrupt me ever! king. 'tis only Titre thou disdain'st in her, the lequel i can build up. étrange is it that our bloods, of colour, weight, and heat, pour'd all together, aurait assez confound distinction, yet supporter off in differences so mighty. if she be all that is virtuous-save what thou dislik'st, a poor physician's daughter-thou dislik'st of vertu for the name; but do not so. from lowest endroit when virtuous things proceed, the endroit is dignified by the doer's deed; où génial additions swell's, and vertu none, it is a dropsied honour. good seul is good sans pour autant a name. vileness is so: the property by what it is devrait go, not by the title. she is young, wise, fair; in celles-ci to la nature she's immediate heir; and celles-ci race honour. that is honour's mépris lequel challenges lui-même as honour's born and is not like the sire. honours prospérer when plutôt from our acts we them derive than our fore-goers. the mere word's a slave, debauch'd on chaque tomb, on chaque la tombe a lying trophy; and as oft is dumb où dust and damn'd oblivion is the tomb of honour'd des os indeed. what devrait be said? if thou canst like this créature as a maid, i can create the rest. vertu and she is her own dower; honour and richesse from me. bertram. i ne peux pas love her, nor will strive to do 't. king. thou wrong'st thyself, if thou shouldst strive to choose. helena. that you are well restor'd, my lord, i'm glad. let the rest go. king. my honour's at the stake; lequel to defeat, i must produce my power. here, take her hand, fier scornful boy, indigne this good gift, that dost in vile misprision shackle up my love and her desert; that canst not rêver we, poising us in her defective scale, doit weigh thee to the beam; that wilt not know it is in us to plant thine honour où we S'il vous plaît to have it grow. check thy contempt; obey our will, lequel travails in thy good; croyez not thy disdain, but presently do thine own fortunes that obedient droite lequel both thy duty owes and our Puissance claims; or i will jeter thee from my care for ever into the staggers and the careless lapse of jeunesse and ignorance; both my vengeance and hate loosing upon thee in the name of justice, sans pour autant all termes of pity. speak; thine answer. bertram. pardon, my gracious lord; for i submit my fantaisie to your eyes. when i considérer what génial creation and what dole of honour mouches où you bid it, i find that she lequel late was in my nobler thoughts most base is now the praised of the king; who, so ennobled, is as 'twere born so. king. take her by the hand, and tell her she is thine; to whom i promettre a counterpoise, if not to thy biens a balance more replete. bertram. i take her hand. king. good fortune and the favoriser of the king sourire upon this contract; dont ceremony doit seem expedient on the now-born brief, and be perform'd to-night. the solennel le banquet doit more assister upon the venir space, expecting absent friends. as thou lov'st her, thy love's to me religious; else, does err. sortir all but lafeu and parolles who stay behind, commenting of this wedding lafeu. do you hear, monsieur? a word with you. parolles. your pleasure, sir? lafeu. your lord and Maître did well to make his recantation. parolles. recantation! my lord! my master! lafeu. ay; is it not a language i speak? parolles. a most harsh one, and not to be understood sans pour autant bloody succeeding. my master! lafeu. are you un compagnon to the compter rousillon? parolles. to any count; to all counts; to what is man. lafeu. to what is count's man: count's Maître is of un autre style. parolles. you are too old, sir; let it satisfy you, you are too old. lafeu. i must tell thee, sirrah, i écrire man; to lequel Titre age ne peux pas apporter thee. parolles. what i dare too well do, i dare not do. lafeu. i did pense thee, for two ordinaries, to be a jolie wise fellow; thou didst make tolerable vent of thy travel; it pourrait pass. yet the scarfs and the bannerets sur thee did manifoldly dissuade me from believing thee a vessel of too génial a burden. i have now a trouvé thee; when i lose thee encore i care not; yet art thou good for rien but taking up; and that thou'rt rare worth. parolles. hadst thou not the privilege of antiquity upon thee- lafeu. do not plunge thyself too far in anger, lest thou hasten thy trial; lequel if-lord have pitié on thee for a hen! so, my good la fenêtre of lattice, fare thee well; thy casement i need not open, for i look par thee. give me thy hand. parolles. my lord, you give me most egregious indignity. lafeu. ay, with all my heart; and thou art worthy of it. parolles. i have not, my lord, deserv'd it. lafeu. yes, good faith, ev'ry dram of it; and i will not bate thee a scruple. parolles. well, i doit be wiser. lafeu. ev'n as soon as thou canst, for thou hast to pull at a smack o' th' contrary. if ever thou be'st lié in thy scarf and beaten, thou shalt find what it is to be fier of thy bondage. i have a le désir to hold my acquaintance with thee, or plutôt my knowledge, that i may say in the default 'he is a man i know.' parolles. my lord, you do me most insupportable vexation. lafeu. i aurait it were hell des douleurs for thy sake, and my poor Faire eternal; for Faire i am past, as i will by thee, in what mouvement age will give me leave. exit parolles. well, thou hast a son doit take this disgrâce off me: scurvy, old, filthy, scurvy lord! well, i must be patient; Là is no fettering of authority. i'll beat him, by my life, if i can meet him with any convenience, an he were double and double a lord. i'll have no more pity of his age than i aurait have of- i'll beat him, and if i pourrait but meet him again. re-enter lafeu lafeu. sirrah, your lord and master's married; there's news for you; you have a new mistress. parolles. i most unfeignedly beseech your seigneurie to make some reservation of your wrongs. he is my good lord: whom i servir au dessus is my master. lafeu. who? god? parolles. ay, sir. lafeu. the diable it is that's thy master. why dost thou garter up thy arms o' this fashion? dost make hose of thy sleeves? do autre servants so? thou wert best set thy lower part où thy nose stands. by mine honour, if i were but two heures younger, i'd beat thee. methink'st thou art a général offence, and chaque man devrait beat thee. i pense thou wast created for men to breathe se upon thee. parolles. this is hard and undeserved measure, my lord. lafeu. go to, sir; you were battu in italy for picking a kernel out of a pomegranate; you are a vagabond, and no true traveller; you are more saucy with seigneurs and honourable personages than the commission of your naissance and vertu gives you heraldry. you are not vaut un autre word, else i'd call you knave. i laisser you. exit entrer bertram parolles. good, very, good, it is so then. good, very good; let it be conceal'd awhile. bertram. undone, and forfeited to se soucie for ever! parolles. what's the matter, sweetheart? bertram. bien que avant the solennel prêtre i have sworn, i will not bed her. parolles. what, what, sweetheart? bertram. o my parolles, they have married me! i'll to the tuscan wars, and jamais bed her. parolles. France is a dog-hole, and it no more merits the bande de roulement of a man's foot. to th' wars! bertram. there's letters from my mother; what th' import is i know not yet. parolles. ay, that aurait be known. to th' wars, my boy, to th' wars! he wears his honour in a box unseen that hugs his kicky-wicky here at home, spending his manly marrow in her arms, lequel devrait sustain the lié and high curvet of mars's ardent steed. to autre regions! France is a stable; we that habiter in't jades; therefore, to th' war! bertram. it doit be so; i'll send her to my house, acquaint my mère with my hate to her, and wherefore i am fled; écrire to the king that lequel i durst not speak. his présent gift doit furnish me to ceux italian fields où noble fellows strike. war is no strife to the dark maison and the detested wife. parolles. will this capriccio hold in thee, art sure? bertram. go with me to my chambre and advise me. i'll send her tout droit away. to-morrow i'll to the wars, she to her Célibataire sorrow. parolles. why, celles-ci balls bound; there's bruit in it. 'tis hard: a Jeune man married is a man that's marr'd. par conséquent away, and laisser her bravely; go. the king has done you wrong; but, hush, 'tis so. sortir act ii. scène 4. paris. the king's palais entrer helena and pitre helena. my mère greets me kindly; is she well? clown. she is not well, but yet she has her health; she's very merry, but yet she is not well. but thanks be given, she's very well, and wants rien i' th' world; but yet she is not well. helena. if she be very well, what does she ail that she's not very well? clown. truly, she's very well indeed, but for two things. helena. what two things? clown. one, that she's not in heaven, où god send her quickly! the other, that she's in earth, from d'où god send her quickly! entrer parolles parolles. bénir you, my fortunate lady! helena. i hope, sir, i have your good will to have mine own good fortunes. parolles. you had my prières to lead them on; and to keep them on, have them still. o, my knave, how does my old lady? clown. so that you had her wrinkles and i her money, i aurait she did as you say. parolles. why, i say nothing. clown. marry, you are the wiser man; for many a man's langue shakes out his master's undoing. to say nothing, to do nothing, to know nothing, and to have nothing, is to be a génial part of your title, lequel is dans a very peu of nothing. parolles. away! th'art a knave. clown. you devrait have said, sir, 'before a fripon th'art a knave'; that's 'before me th'art a knave.' this had been truth, sir. parolles. go to, thou art a witty fool; i have a trouvé thee. clown. did you find me in yourself, sir, or were you enseigné to find me? the search, sir, was profitable; and much fool may you find in you, even to the world's plaisir and the increase of laughter. parolles. a good knave, i' faith, and well fed. madam, my lord will go away to-night: a very serious Entreprise calls on him. the génial prerogative and rite of love, which, as your due, time claims, he does acknowledge; but puts it off to a compell'd restraint; dont want, and dont delay, is strew'd with sweets, lequel they distil now in the curbed time, to make the venir hour o'erflow with joy and plaisir noyer the brim. helena. what's his else? parolles. that you will take your instant laisser o' th' king, and make this hâte as your own good proceeding, strength'ned with what apology you pense may make it probable need. helena. what more commands he? parolles. that, ayant this obtain'd, you presently assister his plus loin pleasure. helena. in tout i wait upon his will. parolles. i doit rapport it so. helena. i pray you. exit parolles come, sirrah. sortir act ii. scène 5. paris. the king's palais entrer lafeu and bertram lafeu. but i hope your seigneurie thinks not him a soldier. bertram. yes, my lord, and of very vaillant approof. lafeu. you have it from his own deliverance. bertram. and by autre warranted testimony. lafeu. then my dial goes not true; i took this lark for a bunting. bertram. i do assurer you, my lord, he is very génial in knowledge, and accordingly valiant. lafeu. i have then sinn'd against his experience and transgress'd against his valour; and my Etat that way is dangerous, depuis i ne peux pas yet find in my cœur to repent. here he comes; i pray you make us friends; i will pursue the amity entrer parolles parolles. [to bertram] celles-ci things doit be done, sir. lafeu. pray you, sir, who's his tailor? parolles. sir! lafeu. o, i know him well. ay, sir; he, sir, 's a good workman, a very good tailor. bertram. [aside to parolles] is she gone to the king? parolles. she is. bertram. will she away to-night? parolles. as you'll have her. bertram. i have writ my letters, casketed my treasure, donné ordre for our horses; and to-night, when i devrait take possession of the bride, end ere i do begin. lafeu. a good traveller is quelque chose at the latter end of a dinner; but one that lies three-thirds and uses a connu vérité to pass a thousand nothings with, devrait be once entendu and thrice beaten. god save you, captain. bertram. is Là any unkindness entre my lord and you, monsieur? parolles. i know not how i have deserved to run into my lord's displeasure. lafeu. you have made shift to run into 't, boots and spurs and all, like him that leapt into the custard; and out of it you'll run again, plutôt than souffrir question for your residence. bertram. it may be you have mistaken him, my lord. lafeu. and doit do so ever, bien que i took him at's prayers. fare you well, my lord; and croyez this of me: Là can be no kernal in this lumière nut; the soul of this man is his clothes; confiance him not in matière of lourd consequence; i have kept of them tame, and know leur natures. farewell, monsieur; i have spoken mieux of you than you have or will to mériter at my hand; but we must do good against evil. exit parolles. an idle lord, i swear. bertram. i pense so. parolles. why, do you not know him? bertram. yes, i do know him well; and commun discours gives him a worthy pass. here vient my clog. entrer helena helena. i have, sir, as i was commandé from you, parlait with the king, and have procur'd his laisser for présent parting; only he desires some privé discours with you. bertram. i doit obey his will. you must not marvel, helen, at my course, lequel tient not Couleur with the time, nor does the ministration and required Bureau on my particular. prepar'd i was not for such a business; par conséquent am i a trouvé so much unsettled. this drives me to supplier you that presently you take your way for home, and plutôt muse than ask why i supplier you; for my respects are mieux than they seem, and my appointments have in them a need greater than montre lui-même at the première view to you that know them not. this to my mother. [giving a letter] 'twill be two days ere i doit see you; so i laisser you to your wisdom. helena. sir, i can rien say but that i am your most obedient servant. bertram. come, come, no more of that. helena. and ever doit with true observance seek to eke out that où vers me my homely étoiles have fail'd to égal my génial fortune. bertram. let that go. my hâte is very great. farewell; hie home. helena. pray, sir, your pardon. bertram. well, what aurait you say? helena. i am not worthy of the richesse i owe, nor dare i say 'tis mine, and yet it is; but, like a timorous thief, most fain aurait voler what law does vouch mine own. bertram. what aurait you have? helena. something; and rare so much; nothing, indeed. i aurait not tell you what i would, my lord. faith, yes: strangers and foes do sunder and not kiss. bertram. i pray you, stay not, but in hâte to horse. helena. i doit not break your bidding, good my lord. bertram. où are my autre men, monsieur? farewell! exit helena go thou vers home, où i will jamais come whilst i can secouer my épée or hear the drum. away, and for our flight. parolles. bravely, coragio! sortir <<this electronic version of the Achevée travaux of william shakespeare is droits d'auteur 1990-1993 by monde library, inc., and is à condition de by projet gutenberg etext of illinois benedictine Université with permission. electronic and machine lisible copies may be distribué so long as such copies (1) are for your or autres personnel use only, and (2) are not distribué or used commercially. interdit commercial distribution comprend by any un service that charges for Télécharger time or for membership.>> act iii. scène 1. florence. the duke's palais flourish. entrer the duke of florence, attended; two français lords, with a troop of soldiers duke. so that, from point to point, now have you hear the fundamental reasons of this war; dont génial decision hath much du sang let en avant and more thirsts after. première lord. holy seems the querelle upon your grace's part; noir and craintif on the opposer. duke. par conséquent we marvel much our cousin France aurait in so just a Entreprise shut his bosom against our borrowing prayers. seconde lord. good my lord, the reasons of our Etat i ne peux pas yield, but like a commun and an vers l'extérieur man that the génial figure of a conseil frames by self-unable motion; par conséquent dare not say what i pense of it, depuis i have a trouvé moi même in my incertain grounds to fail as souvent as i guess'd. duke. be it his pleasure. première lord. but i am sure the younger of our nature, that surfeit on leur ease, will day by day come here for physic. duke. Bienvenue doit they be and all the honours that can fly from us doit on them settle. you know your places well; when mieux fall, for your avails they fell. to-morrow to th' field. flourish. sortir act iii. scène 2. rousillon. the count's palais entrer comtesse and pitre countess. it hath happen'd all as i aurait have had it, save that he vient not le long de with her. clown. by my troth, i take my Jeune lord to be a very melancholy man. countess. by what observance, i pray you? clown. why, he will look upon his boot and sing; mend the ruff and sing; ask questions and sing; pick his les dents and sing. i know a man that had this tour of melancholy sold a goodly manor for a song. countess. let me see what he writes, and when he veux dire to come. [opening a letter] clown. i have no mind to isbel depuis i was at court. our old ling and our isbels o' th' pays are rien like your old ling and your isbels o' th' court. the cerveaux of my cupid's knock'd out; and i commencer to love, as an old man aime money, with no stomach. countess. what have we here? clown. e'en that you have there. exit countess. [reads] 'i have sent you a daughter-in-law; she hath recovered the king and défait me. i have wedded her, not bedded her; and juré to make the "not" eternal. you doit hear i am run away; know it avant the rapport come. if Là be breadth assez in the world, i will hold a long distance. my duty to you. your unfortunate son, bertram.' this is not well, rash and unbridled boy, to fly the favours of so good a king, to cueillir his indignation on thy head by the misprizing of a maid too virtuous for the mépris of empire. re-enter pitre clown. o madam, là-bas is lourd news dans entre two soldiers and my Jeune lady. countess. what is the -matter? clown. nay, Là is some confort in the news, some comfort; your son will not be kill'd so soon as i pensée he would. countess. why devrait he be kill'd? clown. so say i, madam, if he run away, as i hear he does the danger is in standing to 't; that's the loss of men, bien que it be the getting of children. here they come will tell you more. for my part, i only hear your son was run away. exit entrer helena and the two français gentlemen seconde gentleman. save you, good madam. helena. madam, my lord is gone, for ever gone. première gentleman. do not say so. countess. pense upon patience. pray you, gentlemen- i have felt so many quirks of joy and douleur that the première face of neither, on the start, can femme me unto 't. où is my son, i pray you? première gentleman. madam, he's gone to servir the duke of florence. we met him thitherward; for thence we came, and, après some envoi in hand at court, thither we bend again. helena. look on this letter, madam; here's my passport. [reads] 'when thou canst get the ring upon my finger, lequel jamais doit come off, and show me a enfant begotten of thy body that i am père to, then call me husband; but in such a "then" i écrire a "never." this is a horrible sentence. countess. apporté you this letter, gentlemen? première gentleman. ay, madam; and for the contents' sake are Pardon for our pains. countess. i prithee, lady, have a mieux cheer; if thou engrossest all the griefs are thine, thou robb'st me of a moiety. he was my son; but i do wash his name out of my blood, and thou art all my child. towards florence is he? première gentleman. ay, madam. countess. and to be a soldier? première gentleman. such is his noble purpose; and, croyez 't, the duke will lay upon him all the honour that good convenience claims. countess. revenir you thither? seconde gentleman. ay, madam, with the swiftest wing of speed. helena. [reads] 'till i have no wife, i have rien in france.' 'tis bitter. countess. find you that there? helena. ay, madam. seconde gentleman. 'tis but the boldness of his hand haply, lequel his cœur was not consenting to. countess. rien in France jusqu'à he have no wife! there's rien here that is too good for him but only she; and she deserves a lord that twenty such rude boys pourrait tend upon, and call her hourly mistress. who was with him? seconde gentleman. a serviteur only, and a gentleman lequel i have parfois known. countess. parolles, was it not? seconde gentleman. ay, my good lady, he. countess. a very tainted fellow, and full of wickedness. my son corrupts a well-derived la nature with his inducement. seconde gentleman. indeed, good lady, the compagnon has a deal of that too much lequel tient him much to have. countess. y'are welcome, gentlemen. i will supplier you, when you see my son, to tell him that his épée can jamais win the honour that he loses. more i'll supplier you écrit to bear along. première gentleman. we servir you, madam, in that and all your worthiest affairs. countess. not so, but as we changement our courtesies. will you draw near? sortir comtesse and gentlemen helena. 'till i have no wife, i have rien in france.' rien in France jusqu'à he has no wife! thou shalt have none, rousillon, none in France then hast thou all again. poor lord! is't that chase thee from thy country, and expose ceux soumissionner membres of thine to the event of the non-sparing war? and is it i that drive thee from the sportive court, où thou wast shot at with fair eyes, to be the mark of smoky muskets? o you leaden messengers, that ride upon the violent la vitesse of fire, fly with faux aim; move the still-piecing air, that sings with piercing; do not toucher my lord. whoever shoots at him, i set him there; whoever charges on his vers l'avant breast, i am the caitiff that do hold him to't; and bien que i kill him not, i am the cause his décès was so effected. mieux 'twere i met the ravin lion when he roar'd with tranchant constraint of hunger; mieux 'twere that all the miseries lequel la nature owes were mine at once. no; come thou home, rousillon, d'où honour but of danger wins a scar, as oft it loses all. i will be gone. my étant here it is that tient thee hence. doit i stay here to do 't? no, no, bien que the air of paradise did fan the house, and angels offic'd all. i will be gone, that pitiful rumour may rapport my vol to consolate thine ear. come, night; end, day. for with the dark, poor thief, i'll voler away. exit act iii. scène 3. florence. avant the duke's palais flourish. entrer the duke of florence, bertram, parolles, soldiers, drum and trumpets duke. the général of our cheval thou art; and we, génial in our hope, lay our best love and credence upon thy promising fortune. bertram. sir, it is a charge too lourd for my strength; but yet we'll strive to bear it for your worthy sake to th' extreme edge of hazard. duke. then go thou forth; and fortune play upon thy prosperous helm, as thy auspicious mistress! bertram. this very day, génial mars, i put moi même into thy file; make me but like my thoughts, and i doit prouver a lover of thy drum, hater of love. sortir act iii. scène 4. rousillon. the count's palais entrer comtesse and intendant countess. alas! and aurait you take the lettre of her? pourrait you not know she aurait do as she has done by sending me a letter? read it again. steward. [reads] 'i am Saint jaques' pilgrim, thither gone. ambitious love hath so in me offended that barefoot plod i the cold sol upon, with sainted vow my faults to have amended. write, write, that from the bloody cours of war my très cher master, your dear son, may hie. bénir him at home in peace, whilst i from far his name with zealous fervour sanctify. his pris labours bid him me forgive; i, his despiteful juno, sent him en avant from courtly friends, with camping foes to live, où décès and danger dogs the talons of worth. he is too good and fair for décès and me; whom i moi même embrasse to set him free.' countess. ah, what tranchant stings are in her mildest words! rinaldo, you did jamais lack Conseil so much as letting her pass so; had i parlait with her, i pourrait have well diverted her intents, lequel thus she hath prevented. steward. pardon me, madam; if i had donné you this at over-night, she pourrait have been o'er ta'en; and yet she writes pursuit aurait be but vain. countess. what ange doit bénir this indigne husband? he ne peux pas thrive, sauf si her prayers, whom paradis delights to hear and aime to grant, reprieve him from the colère of greatest justice. write, write, rinaldo, to this indigne mari of his wife; let chaque word weigh lourd of her vaut that he does weigh too light. my greatest grief, bien que peu he do feel it, set down sharply. envoi the most convenient messenger. when haply he doit hear that she is gone he will return; and hope i may that she, hearing so much, will la vitesse her foot again, led hither by pure love. lequel of them both is très cher to me i have no compétence in sens to make distinction. provide this messenger. my cœur is heavy, and mine age is weak; douleur aurait have tears, and chagrin bids me speak. sortir act iii. scène 5. sans pour autant the des murs of florence a tucket afar off. entrer an old veuve of florence, her fille diana, violenta, and mariana, with autre citizens widow. nay, come; for if they do approche the city we doit lose all the sight. diana. they say the français compter has done most honourable service. widow. it is reported that he has pris leur great'st commander; and that with his own hand he slew the duke's brother. [tucket] we have lost our labour; they are gone a contraire way. hark! you may know by leur trumpets. mariana. come, let's revenir again, and suffice nous-mêmes with the rapport of it. well, diana, take heed of this français earl; the honour of a maid is her name, and no legacy is so rich as honesty. widow. i have told my voisine how you have been solicited by a gentleman his companion. mariana. i know that knave, hang him! one parolles; a filthy officer he is in ceux suggestions for the Jeune earl. beware of them, diana: leur promises, enticements, oaths, tokens, and all celles-ci engines of lust, are not the things they go under; many a maid hath been seduced by them; and the misère is, example, that so terrible montre in the wreck of maidenhood, ne peux pas for all that dissuade succession, but that they are limed with the twigs that threatens them. i hope i need not to advise you further; but i hope your own la grâce will keep you où you are, bien que Là were no plus loin danger connu but the modesty lequel is so lost. diana. you doit not need to fear me. entrer helena in the dress of a pilgrim widow. i hope so. look, here vient a pilgrim. i know she will lie at my house: thither they send one another. i'll question her. god save you, pilgrim! où are bound? helena. to Saint jaques le grand. où do the palmers lodge, i do beseech you? widow. at the Saint francis here, beside the port. helena. is this the way? [a Mars afar] widow. ay, marry, is't. hark you! they come this way. if you will tarry, holy pilgrim, but till the troops come by, i will conduite you où you doit be lodg'd; the plutôt for i pense i know your hôtesse as ample as myself. helena. is it yourself? widow. if you doit S'il vous plaît so, pilgrim. helena. i remercier you, and will stay upon your leisure. widow. you came, i think, from france? helena. i did so. widow. here you doit see a countryman of le tiens that has done worthy service. helena. his name, i pray you. diana. the compter rousillon. know you such a one? helena. but by the ear, that hears most nobly of him; his face i know not. diana. what some'er he is, he's bravely pris here. he stole from france, as 'tis reported, for the king had married him against his liking. pense you it is so? helena. ay, surely, mere the truth; i know his lady. diana. Là is a gentleman that serves the compter reports but coarsely of her. helena. what's his name? diana. monsieur parolles. helena. o, i croyez with him, in argument of praise, or to the vaut of the génial compter himself, she is too mean to have her name repeated; all her deserving is a reserved honesty, and that i have not entendu examin'd. diana. alas, poor lady! 'tis a hard bondage to devenir the wife of a detesting lord. widow. i sweet, good creature, wheresoe'er she is her cœur weighs sadly. this Jeune maid pourrait do her a shrewd turn, if she pleas'd. helena. how do you mean? may be the amorous compter solicits her in the unlawful purpose. widow. he does, indeed; and brokes with all that can in such a suit corrupt the soumissionner honour of a maid; but she is arm'd for him, and garde her garde in honestest defence. enter, with drum and colours, bertram, parolles, and the entier army mariana. the gods interdire else! widow. so, now they come. that is antonio, the duke's eldest son; that, escalus. helena. lequel is the frenchman? diana. he- that with the plume; 'tis a most galant fellow. i aurait he lov'd his wife; if he were honester he were much goodlier. is't not a handsome gentleman? helena. i like him well. diana. 'tis pity he is not honest. yond's that same fripon that leads him to celles-ci places; were i his lady i aurait poison that vile rascal. helena. lequel is he? diana. that jack-an-apes with scarfs. why is he melancholy? helena. perchance he's hurt i' th' battle. parolles. lose our drum! well. mariana. he's shrewdly vex'd at something. look, he has spied us. widow. marry, hang you! mariana. and your courtesy, for a ring-carrier! sortir bertram, parolles, and army widow. the troop is past. come, pilgrim, i will apporter you où you doit host. of enjoin'd penitents there's four or five, to génial Saint jaques bound, déjà at my house. helena. i humbly remercier you. S'il vous plaît it this matron and this doux maid to eat with us to-night; the charge and thanking doit be for me, and, to requite you further, i will bestow some precepts of this virgin, worthy the note. both. we'll take your offre kindly. sortir act iii. scène 6. camp avant florence entrer bertram, and the two français seigneurs seconde lord. nay, good my lord, put him to't; let him have his way. première lord. if your seigneurie find him not a hiding, hold me no more in your respect. seconde lord. on my life, my lord, a bubble. bertram. do you pense i am so far deceived in him? seconde lord. croyez it, my lord, in mine own direct knowledge, sans pour autant any malice, but to parler of him as my kinsman, he's a most notable coward, an infini and endless liar, an hourly promise-breaker, the owner of no one good qualité worthy your lordship's entertainment. première lord. it were fit you knew him; lest, reposing too far in his virtue, lequel he hath not, he pourrait at some génial and trusty Entreprise in a main danger fail you. bertram. i aurait i knew in what particulier action to try him. première lord. none mieux than to let him chercher off his drum, lequel you hear him so confidently entreprendre to do. seconde lord. i with a troop of florentines will tout à coup surprise him; such i will have whom i am sure he sait not from the enemy. we will bind and hoodwink him so that he doit suppose no autre but that he is carried into the leaguer of the adversaries when we apporter him to our own tents. be but your seigneurie présent at his examination; if he do not, for the promettre of his life and in the highest compulsion of base fear, offre to trahir you and livrer all the intelligence in his Puissance against you, and that with the Divin forfeit of his soul upon oath, jamais confiance my jugement in anything. première lord. o, for the love of laughter, let him chercher his drum; he says he has a stratagem for't. when your seigneurie sees the bas of his Succès in't, and to what metal this contrefaire lump of ore will be melted, if you give him not john drum's entertainment, your inclining ne peux pas be removed. here he comes. entrer parolles seconde lord. o, for the love of laughter, hinder not the honour of his design; let him chercher off his drum in any hand. bertram. how now, monsieur! this drum sticks sorely in your disposition. première lord. a pox on 't; let it go; 'tis but a drum. parolles. but a drum! is't but a drum? a drum so lost! Là was excellent command: to charge in with our cheval upon our own wings, and to rend our own soldiers! première lord. that was not to be blam'd in the commander of the service; it was a disaster of war that caesar himself pourrait not have prevented, if he had been Là to command. bertram. well, we ne peux pas greatly condemn our success. some déshonorer we had in the loss of that drum; but it is not to be recovered. parolles. it pourrait have been recovered. bertram. it might, but it is not now. parolles. it is to be recovered. but that the mérite of un service is seldom attributed to the true and exact performer, i aurait have that drum or another, or 'hic jacet.' bertram. why, if you have a stomach, to't, monsieur. if you pense your mystery in stratagem can apporter this instrument of honour encore into his originaire de quarter, be magnanimous in the enterprise, and go on; i will la grâce the attempt for a worthy exploit. if you la vitesse well in it, the duke doit both parler of it and extend to you what plus loin becomes his greatness, even to the utmost syllable of our worthiness. parolles. by the hand of a soldier, i will entreprendre it. bertram. but you must not now slumber in it. parolles. i'll sur it this evening; and i will presently pen down my dilemmas, encourage moi même in my certainty, put moi même into my mortel preparation; and by minuit look to hear plus loin from me. bertram. may i be bold to acquaint his la grâce you are gone sur it? parolles. i know not what the Succès will be, my lord, but the attempt i vow. bertram. i know th' art valiant; and, to the of thy soldiership, will subscribe for thee. farewell. parolles. i love not many words. exit seconde lord. no more than a fish aime water. is not this a étrange fellow, my lord, that so confidently seems to entreprendre this business, lequel he sait is not to be done; damns himself to do, and dares mieux be damn'd than to do 't. première lord. you do not know him, my lord, as we do. certain it is that he will voler himself into a man's favour, and for a week escape a génial deal of discoveries; but when you find him out, you have him ever after. bertram. why, do you pense he will make no deed at all of this that so seriously he does address himself unto? seconde lord. none in the world; but revenir with an invention, and clap upon you two or three probable lies. but we have presque emboss'd him. you doit see his fall to-night; for En effet he is not for your lordship's respect. première lord. we'll make you some sport with the fox ere we case him. he was première smok'd by the old lord lafeu. when his disguise and he is parted, tell me what a sprat you doit find him; lequel you doit see this very night. seconde lord. i must go look my twigs; he doit be caught. bertram. your brother, he doit go le long de with me. seconde lord. as't S'il vous plaît your lordship. i'll laisser you. exit bertram. now will i lead you to the house, and show you the lass i parlait of. première lord. but you say she's honest. bertram. that's all the fault. i parlait with her but once, and a trouvé her wondrous cold;
