but i sent to her, by this same coxcomb that we have i' th' wind, tokens and letters lequel she did re-send; and this is all i have done. she's a fair creature; will you go see her? première lord. with all my heart, my lord. sortir act iii. scène 7. florence. the widow's maison entrer helena and veuve helena. if you misdoubt me that i am not she, i know not how i doit assurer you plus loin but i doit lose the grounds i work upon. widow. bien que my biens be fall'n, i was well born, rien connaissance with celles-ci businesses; and aurait not put my réputation now in any staining act. helena. nor aurait i wish you. première give me confiance the compter he is my husband, and what to your juré Conseil i have spoken is so from word to word; and then you cannot, by the good aid that i of you doit borrow, err in bestowing it. widow. i devrait croyez you; for you have show'd me that lequel well approves y'are génial in fortune. helena. take this bourse of gold, and let me buy your friendly help thus far, lequel i will over-pay and pay encore when i have a trouvé it. the compter he woos your fille lays down his wanton siege avant her beauty, resolv'd to porter her. let her in fine consent, as we'll direct her how 'tis best to bear it. now his important du sang will néant deny that she'll demand. a ring the county wears that downward hath succeeded in his maison from son to son some four or five descents depuis the première père wore it. this ring he tient in most rich choice; yet, in his idle fire, to buy his will, it aurait not seem too dear, howe'er repented after. widow. now i see the bas of your purpose. helena. you see it légitime then. it is no more but that your daughter, ere she seems as won, desires this ring; appoints him an encounter; in fine, delivers me to fill the time, se most chastely absent. après this, to marier her, i'll add three thousand crowns to what is pass'd already. widow. i have yielded. instruct my fille how she doit persever, that time and endroit with this deceit so légitime may prouver coherent. chaque nuit he vient with musics of all sorts, and songs compos'd to her unworthiness. it rien steads us to gronder him from our eaves, for he persists as if his life lay on 't. helena. why then to-night let us assay our plot; which, if it speed, is wicked sens in a légitime deed, and légitime sens in a légitime act; où both not sin, and yet a sinful fact. but let's sur it. sortir <<this electronic version of the Achevée travaux of william shakespeare is droits d'auteur 1990-1993 by monde library, inc., and is à condition de by projet gutenberg etext of illinois benedictine Université with permission. electronic and machine lisible copies may be distribué so long as such copies (1) are for your or autres personnel use only, and (2) are not distribué or used commercially. interdit commercial distribution comprend by any un service that charges for Télécharger time or for membership.>> act iv. scène 1. sans pour autant the florentine camp entrer seconde français lord with five or six autre soldiers in ambush seconde lord. he can come no autre way but by this hedge-corner. when you sally upon him, parler what terrible language you will; bien que you comprendre it not yourselves, no matter; for we must not seem to comprendre him, sauf si some one among us, whom we must produce for an interpreter. première soldier. good captain, let me be th' interpreter. seconde lord. art not connaissance with him? sait he not thy voice? première soldier. no, sir, i mandat you. seconde lord. but what linsey-woolsey has thou to parler to us again? première soldier. e'en such as you parler to me. seconde lord. he must pense us some band of strangers i' th' adversary's entertainment. now he hath a smack of all neighbouring languages, par conséquent we must chaque one be a man of his own fancy; not to know what we parler one to another, so we seem to know, is to know tout droit our purpose: choughs' language, gabble enough, and good enough. as for you, interpreter, you must seem very politic. but couch, ho! here he comes; to beguile two heures in a sleep, and then to revenir and jurer the lies he forges. entrer parolles parolles. ten o'clock. dans celles-ci three heures 'twill be time assez to go home. what doit i say i have done? it must be a very plausive invention that carries it. they commencer to smoke me; and disgraces have of late knock'd to souvent at my door. i find my langue is too foolhardy; but my cœur hath the fear of mars avant it, and of his creatures, not daring the reports of my tongue. seconde lord. this is the première vérité that e'er thine own langue was coupable of. parolles. what the diable devrait move me to entreprendre the recovery of this drum, étant not ignorant of the impossibility, and connaissance i had no such purpose? i must give moi même some hurts, and say i got them in exploit. yet slight ones will not porter it. they will say 'came you off with so little?' and génial ones i dare not give. wherefore, what's the instance? tongue, i must put you into a butterwoman's mouth, and buy moi même un autre of bajazet's mule, if you prattle me into celles-ci perils. seconde lord. is it possible he devrait know what he is, and be that he is? parolles. i aurait the cutting of my garments aurait servir the turn, or the breaking of my spanish sword. seconde lord. we ne peux pas afford you so. parolles. or the baring of my beard; and to say it was in stratagem. seconde lord. 'twould not do. parolles. or to noyer my clothes, and say i was stripp'd. seconde lord. hardly serve. parolles. bien que i juré i leap'd from the la fenêtre of the citadel- seconde lord. how deep? parolles. thirty fathom. seconde lord. three génial serments aurait rare make that be believed. parolles. i aurait i had any drum of the enemy's; i aurait jurer i recover'd it. seconde lord. you doit hear one anon. [alarum within] parolles. a drum now of the enemy's! seconde lord. throca movousus, cargo, cargo, cargo. all. cargo, cargo, cargo, villianda par corbo, cargo. parolles. o, ransom, ransom! do not hide mine eyes. [they blindfold him] première soldier. boskos thromuldo boskos. parolles. i know you are the muskos' regiment, and i doit lose my life for want of language. if Là be here german, or dane, low dutch, italian, or french, let him parler to me; i'll découvrir that lequel doit undo the florentine. première soldier. boskos vauvado. i comprendre thee, and can parler thy tongue. kerely-bonto, sir, betake thee to thy faith, for seventeen poniards are at thy bosom. parolles. o! première soldier. o, pray, pray, pray! manka revania dulche. seconde lord. oscorbidulchos volivorco. première soldier. the général is contenu to de rechange thee yet; and, hoodwink'd as thou art, will lead thee on to gather from thee. haply thou mayst inform quelque chose to save thy life. parolles. o, let me live, and all the secrets of our camp i'll show, leur force, leur purposes. nay, i'll parler that lequel you will merveille at. première soldier. but wilt thou faithfully? parolles. if i do not, damn me. première soldier. acordo linta. come on; thou art granted space. exit, parolles guarded. a court alarum dans seconde lord. go, tell the compter rousillon and my frère we have caught the woodcock, and will keep him muffled till we do hear from them. seconde soldier. captain, i will. seconde lord. 'a will trahir us all unto ourselves- inform on that. seconde soldier. so i will, sir. seconde lord. till then i'll keep him dark and safely lock'd. sortir act iv. scène 2. florence. the widow's maison entrer bertram and diana bertram. they told me that your name was fontibell. diana. no, my good lord, diana. bertram. titled goddess; and vaut it, with addition! but, fair soul, in your fine Cadre hath love no quality? if the rapide fire of jeunesse lumière not your mind, you are no maiden, but a monument; when you are dead, you devrait be such a one as you are now, for you are cold and stern; and now you devrait be as your mère was when your sucré self was got. diana. she then was honest. bertram. so devrait you be. diana. no. my mère did but duty; such, my lord, as you owe to your wife. bertram. no more o'that! i prithee do not strive against my vows. i was compell'd to her; but i love the by love's own sucré constraint, and will for ever do thee all rights of service. diana. ay, so you servir us till we servir you; but when you have our roses you barely laisser our thorns to prick ourselves, and mock us with our bareness. bertram. how have i sworn! diana. 'tis not the many serments that fait du the truth, but the plaine Célibataire vow that is vow'd true. what is not holy, that we jurer not by, but take the high'st to witness. then, pray you, tell me: if i devrait jurer by jove's génial attributes i lov'd you dearly, aurait you croyez my serments when i did love you ill? this has no holding, to jurer by him whom i manifestation to love that i will work against him. par conséquent your serments are words and poor conditions, but unseal'd- at moins in my opinion. bertram. changement it, changement it; be not so holy-cruel. love is holy; and my integrity ne'er knew the crafts that you do charge men with. supporter no more off, but give thyself unto my sick desires, who then recovers. say thou art mine, and ever my love as it begins doit so persever. diana. i see that men make ropes in such a scarre that we'll forsake ourselves. give me that ring. bertram. i'll lend it thee, my dear, but have no Puissance to give it from me. diana. will you not, my lord? bertram. it is an honour 'longing to our house, bequeathed down from many ancestors; lequel were the greatest obloquy i' th' monde in me to lose. diana. mine honour's such a ring: my chastity's the bijou of our house, bequeathed down from many ancestors; lequel were the greatest obloquy i' th' monde in me to lose. thus your own correct sagesse brings in the champion honour on my part against your vain assault. bertram. here, take my ring; my house, mine honour, yea, my life, be thine, and i'll be bid by thee. diana. when minuit comes, frappe at my chambre window; i'll ordre take my mère doit not hear. now will i charge you in the band of truth, when you have conquer'd my yet jeune fille bed, rester Là but an hour, nor parler to me: my reasons are most strong; and you doit know them when back encore this ring doit be deliver'd. and on your doigt in the nuit i'll put un autre ring, that what in time proceeds may token to the future our past deeds. adieu till then; then fail not. you have won a wife of me, bien que Là my hope be done. bertram. a paradis on Terre i have won by wooing thee. exit diana. for lequel live long to remercier both paradis and me! you may so in the end. my mère told me just how he aurait woo, as if she sat in's heart; she says all men have the like oaths. he had juré to marier me when his wife's dead; par conséquent i'll lie with him when i am buried. depuis frenchmen are so braid, marier that will, i live and die a maid. only, in this disguise, i think't no sin to cozen him that aurait unjustly win. exit act iv. scène 3. the florentine camp entrer the two français lords, and two or three soldiers seconde lord. you have not donné him his mother's letter? première lord. i have deliv'red it an hour since. Là is quelque chose in't that stings his nature; for on the reading it he chang'd presque into un autre man. seconde lord. he has much worthy faire des reproches laid upon him for shaking off so good a wife and so sucré a lady. première lord. especially he hath incurred the everlasting mécontentement of the king, who had even tun'd his prime to sing bonheur to him. i will tell you a thing, but you doit let it habiter darkly with you. seconde lord. when you have spoken it, 'tis dead, and i am the la tombe of it. première lord. he hath perverted a Jeune gentlewoman here in florence, of a most chaste renown; and this nuit he fleshes his will in the spoil of her honour. he hath donné her his monumental ring, and thinks himself made in the unchaste composition. seconde lord. now, god delay our rebellion! as we are ourselves, what things are we! première lord. merely our own traitors. and as in the commun cours of all treasons we encore see them reveal se till they attain to leur abhorr'd ends; so he that in this action contrives against his own nobility, in his correct stream, o'erflows himself. seconde lord. is it not signifiait damnable in us to be trumpeters of our unlawful intents? we doit not then have his entreprise to-night? première lord. not till après midnight; for he is dieted to his hour. seconde lord. that approaches apace. i aurait gladly have him see his entreprise anatomiz'd, that he pourrait take a mesure of his own judgments, où so curiously he had set this counterfeit. première lord. we will not meddle with him till he come; for his présence must be the whip of the other. seconde lord. in the meantime, what hear you of celles-ci wars? première lord. i hear Là is an overture of peace. seconde lord. nay, i assurer you, a paix concluded. première lord. what will compter rousillon do then? will he travel higher, or revenir encore into france? seconde lord. i perceive, by this demand, you are not altogether of his counsel. première lord. let it be forbid, sir! so devrait i be a génial deal of his act. seconde lord. sir, his wife, some two months since, fled from his house. her pretence is a pilgrimage to Saint jaques le grand; lequel holy undertaking with most austere sanctimony she accomplish'd; and, Là residing, the tenderness of her la nature became as a prey to her grief; in fine, made a groan of her last breath, and now she sings in heaven. première lord. how is this justified? seconde lord. the stronger part of it by her own letters, lequel fait du her récit true even to the point of her death. her décès itself, lequel pourrait not be her Bureau to say is come, was faithfully confirm'd by the rector of the place. première lord. hath the compter all this intelligence? seconde lord. ay, and the particulier confirmations, point from point, to the full arming of the verity. première lord. i am heartily Pardon that he'll be glad of this. seconde lord. how mightily sometimes we make us comforts of our losses! première lord. and how mightily some autre fois we noyer our gain in tears! the génial dignity that his valeur hath here acquir'd for him doit at home be encount'red with a la honte as ample. seconde lord. the web of our life is of a mingled yarn, good and ill together. our virtues aurait be fier if our faults whipt them not; and our crimes aurait désespoir if they were not cherish'd by our virtues. entrer a Messager how now? where's your master? servant. he met the duke in the street, sir; of whom he hath pris a solennel leave. his seigneurie will next Matin for france. the duke hath offered him letters of commendations to the king. seconde lord. they doit be no more than needful there, if they were more than they can commend. première lord. they ne peux pas be too sucré for the king's tartness. here's his seigneurie now. entrer bertram how now, my lord, is't not après midnight? bertram. i have to-night dispatch'd sixteen businesses, a month's length apiece; by an abstract of success: i have congied with the duke, done my adieu with his nearest; enterré a wife, mourn'd for her; writ to my lady mère i am returning; entertain'd my convoy; and entre celles-ci main parcels of envoi effected many nicer needs. the last was the greatest, but that i have not ended yet. seconde lord. if the Entreprise be of any difficulty and this Matin your departure hence, it requires hâte of your lordship. bertram. i mean the Entreprise is not ended, as fearing to hear of it hereafter. but doit we have this dialogue entre the fool and the soldier? come, apporter en avant this contrefaire module has deceiv'd me like a double-meaning prophesier. seconde lord. apporter him forth. [exeunt soldiers] has sat i' th' stocks all night, poor galant knave. bertram. no matter; his talons have deserv'd it, in usurping his spurs so long. how does he porter himself? seconde lord. i have told your seigneurie déjà the stocks porter him. but to répondre you as you aurait be understood: he weeps like a jeune fille that had shed her milk; he hath confess'd himself to morgan, whom he supposes to be a friar, from the time of his remembrance to this very instant disaster of his setting i' th' stocks. and what pense you he hath confess'd? bertram. rien of me, has 'a? seconde lord. his confession is taken, and it doit be read to his face; if your seigneurie be in't, as i croyez you are, you must have the la patience to hear it. entrer parolles guarded, and première soldat as interpreter bertram. a peste upon him! muffled! he can say rien of me. seconde lord. hush, hush! hoodman comes. portotartarossa. première soldier. he calls for the tortures. what will you say sans pour autant 'em? parolles. i will avouer what i know sans pour autant constraint; if ye pinch me like a pasty, i can say no more. première soldier. bosko chimurcho. seconde lord. boblibindo chicurmurco. première soldier. you are a merciful general. our général bids you répondre to what i doit ask you out of a note. parolles. and truly, as i hope to live. première soldier. 'first demande of him how many cheval the duke is strong.' what say you to that? parolles. five or six thousand; but very weak and unserviceable. the troops are all scattered, and the commanders very poor rogues, upon my réputation and credit, and as i hope to live. première soldier. doit i set down your répondre so? parolles. do; i'll take the sacrament on 't, how and lequel way you will. bertram. all's one to him. what a past-saving esclave is this! seconde lord. y'are deceiv'd, my lord; this is monsieur parolles, the galant militarist-that was his own phrase-that had the entier theoric of war in the knot of his scarf, and the entraine toi in the chape of his dagger. première lord. i will jamais confiance a man encore for keeping his épée clean; nor croyez he can have tout in him by wearing his vêtements neatly. première soldier. well, that's set down. parolles. 'five or six thousand horse' i said-i will say true- 'or thereabouts' set down, for i'll parler truth. seconde lord. he's very near the vérité in this. bertram. but i con him no thanks for't in the la nature he delivers it. parolles. 'poor rogues' i pray you say. première soldier. well, that's set down. parolles. i humbly remercier you, sir. a truth's a truth-the rogues are marvellous poor. première soldier. 'demand of him of what force they are a-foot.' what say you to that? parolles. by my troth, sir, if i were to live this présent hour, i will tell true. let me see: spurio, a cent and fifty; sebastian, so many; corambus, so many; jaques, so many; guiltian, cosmo, lodowick, and gratii, two cent fifty each; mine own company, chitopher, vaumond, bentii, two cent fifty each; so that the muster-file, pourri and sound, upon my life, amounts not to fifteen thousand poll; half of the lequel dare not secouer the snow from off leur cassocks lest they secouer se to pieces. bertram. what doit be done to him? seconde lord. nothing, but let him have thanks. demande of him my condition, and what crédit i have with the duke. première soldier. well, that's set down. 'you doit demande of him qu'il s'agisse one capitaine dumain be i' th' camp, a frenchman; what his réputation is with the duke, what his valour, honesty, expertness in wars; or qu'il s'agisse he thinks it were not possible, with well-weighing sums of gold, to corrupt him to a revolt.' what say you to this? what do you know of it? parolles. i beseech you, let me répondre to the particulier of the inter'gatories. demande them singly. première soldier. do you know this capitaine dumain? parolles. i know him: 'a was a botcher's prentice in paris, from d'où he was whipt for getting the shrieve's fool with child-a dumb innocent that pourrait not say him nay. bertram. nay, by your leave, hold your hands; bien que i know his cerveaux are forfeit to the next tile that falls. première soldier. well, is this capitaine in the duke of florence's camp? parolles. upon my knowledge, he is, and lousy. seconde lord. nay, look not so upon me; we doit hear of your seigneurie anon. première soldier. what is his réputation with the duke? parolles. the duke sait him for no autre but a poor officer of mine; and writ to me this autre day to turn him out o' th' band. i pense i have his lettre in my pocket. première soldier. marry, we'll search. parolles. in good sadness, i do not know; Soit it is Là or it is upon a file with the duke's autre letters in my tent. première soldier. here 'tis; here's a paper. doit i read it to you? parolles. i do not know if it be it or no. bertram. our interpreter does it well. seconde lord. excellently. première soldier. [reads] 'dian, the count's a fool, and full of gold.' parolles. that is not the duke's letter, sir; that is an advertisement to a correct maid in florence, one diana, to take heed of the allurement of one compter rousillon, a insensé idle boy, but for all that very ruttish. i pray you, sir, put it up again. première soldier. nay, i'll read it première by your favour. parolles. my sens in't, i protest, was very honnête in the nom of the maid; for i knew the Jeune compter to be a dangerous and lascivious boy, who is a whale to virginity, and devours up all the fry it finds. bertram. damnable both-sides rogue! première soldier. [reads] 'when he swears oaths, bid him drop gold, and take it; après he scores, he jamais pays the score. half won is rencontre well made; match, and well make it; he ne'er pays after-debts, take it before. and say a soldier, dian, told thee this: men are to mell with, boys are not to kiss; for compter of this, the count's a fool, i know it, who pays before, but not when he does owe it. thine, as he vow'd to thee in thine ear, parolles.' bertram. he doit be whipt par the army with this rhyme in's forehead. première lord. this is your devoted friend, sir, the manifold linguist, and the amnipotent soldier. bertram. i pourrait supporter n'importe quoi avant but a cat, and now he's a cat to me. première soldier. i perceive, sir, by our general's regards we doit be fain to hang you. parolles. my life, sir, in any case! not that i am peur to die, but that, my offences étant many, i aurait se repentir out the remainder of nature. let me live, sir, in a dungeon, i' th' stocks, or anywhere, so i may live. première soldier. we'll see what may be done, so you avouer freely; therefore, once more to this capitaine dumain: you have answer'd to his réputation with the duke, and to his valour; what is his honesty? parolles. he will steal, sir, an egg out of a cloister; for rapes and ravishments he parallels nessus. he professes not keeping of oaths; in breaking 'em he is stronger than hercules. he will lie, sir, with such volubility that you aurait pense vérité were a fool. drunkenness is his best virtue, for he will be swine-drunk; and in his sommeil he does peu harm, save to his bedclothes sur him; but they know his conditions and lay him in straw. i have but peu more to say, sir, of his honesty. he has tout that an honnête man devrait not have; what an honnête man devrait have he has nothing. seconde lord. i commencer to love him for this. bertram. for this description of thine honesty? a pox upon him! for me, he's more and more a cat. première soldier. what say you to his expertness in war? parolles. faith, sir, has led the drum avant the english tragedians-to belie him i will not-and more of his soldier-ship i know not, sauf in that pays he had the honour to be the officer at a endroit Là called mile-end to instruct for the doubling of files-i aurait do the man what honour i can-but of this i am not certain. seconde lord. he hath out-villain'd villainy so far that the rarity redeems him. bertram. a pox on him! he's a cat still. première soldier. his qualities étant at this poor price, i need not to ask you if gold will corrupt him to revolt. parolles. sir, for a cardecue he will sell the fee-simple of his salvation, the inheritance of it; and cut th' entail from all remainders and a perpetual succession for it perpetually. première soldier. what's his brother, the autre capitaine dumain? première lord. why does he ask him of me? première soldier. what's he? parolles. e'en a crow o' th' same nest; not altogether so génial as the première in goodness, but greater a génial deal in evil. he excels his frère for a coward; yet his frère is reputed one of the best that is. in a retreat he outruns any lackey: marry, in venir on he has the cramp. première soldier. if your life be saved, will you entreprendre to trahir the florentine? parolles. ay, and the capitaine of his horse, compter rousillon. première soldier. i'll whisper with the general, and know his pleasure. parolles. [aside] i'll no more drumming. a peste of all drums! only to seem to mériter well, and to beguile the supposition of that lascivious Jeune boy the count, have i run into this danger. yet who aurait have suspected an ambush où i was taken? première soldier. Là is no remedy, sir, but you must die. the général says you that have so traitorously discover'd the secrets of your army, and made such pestiferous reports of men very nobly held, can servir the monde for no honnête use; par conséquent you must die. come, headsman, of with his head. parolles. o lord, sir, let me live, or let me see my death! première soldier. that doit you, and take your laisser of all your friends. [unmuffling him] so look sur you; know you any here? bertram. good morrow, noble captain. première lord. god bénir you, capitaine parolles. seconde lord. god save you, noble captain. première lord. captain, what greeting will you to my lord lafeu? i am for france. seconde lord. good captain, will you give me a copy of the sonnet you writ to diana in nom of the compter rousillon? an i were not a very lâche i'd compel it of you; but fare you well. sortir bertram and seigneurs première soldier. you are undone, captain, all but your scarf; that has a knot on 't yet. parolles. who ne peux pas be crush'd with a plot? première soldier. if you pourrait find out a pays où but women were that had received so much shame, you pourrait commencer an impudent nation. fare ye well, sir; i am for France too; we doit parler of you there. exit with soldiers parolles. yet am i thankful. if my cœur were great, 'twould burst at this. capitaine i'll be no more; but i will eat, and drink, and sommeil as soft as capitaine shall. simply the chose i am doit make me live. who sait himself a braggart, let him fear this; for it will come to pass that chaque braggart doit be a trouvé an ass. rust, sword; cool, blushes; and, parolles, live safest in shame. étant fool'd, by fool'ry thrive. there's endroit and veux dire for chaque man alive. i'll après them. exit act iv scène 4. the widow's maison entrer helena, widow, and diana helena. that you may well apercevoir i have not wrong'd you! one of the greatest in the christian monde doit be my surety; fore dont trône 'tis needful, ere i can parfait mine intents, to kneel. time was i did him a desired office, dear presque as his life; lequel gratitude par flinty tartar's bosom aurait peep forth, and répondre 'thanks.' i duly am inform'd his la grâce is at marseilles, to lequel endroit we have convenient convoy. you must know i am supposed dead. the army breaking, my mari hies him home; where, paradis aiding, and by the laisser of my good lord the king, we'll be avant our welcome. widow. doux madam, you jamais had a serviteur to dont confiance your Entreprise was more welcome. helena. nor you, mistress, ever a ami dont thoughts more vraiment la main d'oeuvre to recompense your love. doute not but paradis hath apporté me up to be your daughter's dower, as it hath fated her to be my motive and helper to a husband. but, o étrange men! that can such sucré use make of what they hate, when saucy trusting of the cozen'd thoughts defiles the pitchy night. so lust doth play with what it loathes, for that lequel is away. but more of this hereafter. you, diana, sous my poor instructions yet must souffrir quelque chose in my behalf. diana. let décès and honesty go with your impositions, i am le tiens upon your will to suffer. helena. yet, i pray you: but with the word the time will apporter on summer, when briers doit have leaves as well as thorns and be as sucré as sharp. we must away; our waggon is prepar'd, and time revives us. all's well that ends well. encore the fine's the crown. whate'er the course, the end is the renown. sortir act iv scène 5. rousillon. the count's palais entrer countess, lafeu, and pitre lafeu. no, no, no, son was misled with a snipt-taffeta compagnon there, dont villainous saffron aurait have made all the unbak'd and doughy jeunesse of a nation in his colour. your daughter-in-law had been vivant at this hour, and your son here at home, more advanc'd by the king than by that red-tail'd humble-bee i parler of. countess. i aurait i had not connu him. it was the décès of the most virtuous gentlewoman that ever la nature had louange for creating. if she had partaken of my flesh, and cost me the très cher groans of a mother. i pourrait not have owed her a more rooted love. lafeu. 'twas a good lady, 'twas a good lady. we may pick a thousand sallets ere we lumière on such un autre herb. clown. indeed, sir, she was the sweet-marjoram of the sallet, or, rather, the herb of grace. lafeu. they are not sallet-herbs, you knave; they are nose-herbs. clown. i am no génial nebuchadnezzar, sir; i have not much compétence in grass. lafeu. qu'il s'agisse dost thou profess thyself-a fripon or a fool? clown. a fool, sir, at a woman's service, and a fripon at a man's. lafeu. your distinction? clown. i aurait cozen the man of his wife, and do his service. lafeu. so you were a fripon at his service, indeed. clown. and i aurait give his wife my bauble, sir, to do her service. lafeu. i will subscribe for thee; thou art both fripon and fool. clown. at your service. lafeu. no, no, no. clown. why, sir, if i ne peux pas servir you, i can servir as génial a prince as you are. lafeu. who's that? a frenchman? clown. faith, sir, 'a has an english name; but his fisnomy is more hotter in France than there. lafeu. what prince is that? clown. the noir prince, sir; alias, the prince of darkness; alias, the devil. lafeu. hold thee, there's my purse. i give thee not this to suggest thee from thy Maître thou talk'st of; servir him still. clown. i am a woodland fellow, sir, that toujours loved a génial fire; and the Maître i parler of ever garde a good fire. but, sure, he is the prince of the world; let his nobility rester in's court. i am for the maison with the narrow gate, lequel i take to be too peu for pomp to enter. some that humble se may; but the many will be too chill and tender: and they'll be for the flow'ry way that leads to the broad gate and the génial fire. lafeu. go thy ways, i commencer to be aweary of thee; and i tell thee so before, car i aurait not fall out with thee. go thy ways; let my horses be well look'd to, sans pour autant any tricks. clown. if i put any tricks upon 'em, sir, they doit be jades' tricks, lequel are leur own droite by the law of nature. exit lafeu. a shrewd knave, and an unhappy. countess. so 'a is. my lord that's gone made himself much sport out of him. by his autorité he remains here, lequel he thinks is a patent for his sauciness; and En effet he has no pace, but runs où he will. lafeu. i like him well; 'tis not amiss. and i was sur to tell you, depuis i entendu of the good lady's death, and that my lord your son was upon his revenir home, i moved the king my Maître to parler in the nom of my daughter; which, in the minority of them both, his majesté out of a self-gracious remembrance did première propose. his altesse hath promis'd me to do it; and, to stop up the mécontentement he hath conceived against your son, Là is no fitter matter. how does your Madame like it? countess. with very much content, my lord; and i wish it happily effected. lafeu. his altesse vient post from marseilles, of as able body as when he number'd thirty; 'a will be here to-morrow, or i am deceiv'd by him that in such intelligence hath seldom fail'd. countess. it rejoices me that i hope i doit see him ere i die. i have letters that my son will be here to-night. i doit beseech your seigneurie to rester with me tal they meet together. lafeu. madam, i was thinking with what manners i pourrait safely be admitted. countess. you need but plaider your honourable privilege. lafeu. lady, of that i have made a bold charter; but, i remercier my god, it tient yet. re-enter pitre clown. o madam, yonder's my lord your son with a patch of velvet on's face; qu'il s'agisse Là be a scar sous 't or no, the velvet knows; but 'tis a goodly patch of velvet. his left joue is a joue of two pile and a half, but his droite joue is worn bare. lafeu. a scar nobly got, or a noble scar, is a good liv'ry of honour; so être comme is that. clown. but it is your carbonado'd face. lafeu. let us go see your son, i pray you; i long to talk with the Jeune noble soldier. clown. faith, there's a dozen of 'em, with delicate fine hats, and most courteous feathers, lequel bow the head and nod at chaque man. sortir <<this electronic version of the Achevée travaux of william shakespeare is droits d'auteur 1990-1993 by monde library, inc., and is à condition de by projet gutenberg etext of illinois benedictine Université with permission. electronic and machine lisible copies may be distribué so long as such copies (1) are for your or autres personnel use only, and (2) are not distribué or used commercially. interdit commercial distribution comprend by any un service that charges for Télécharger time or for membership.>> act v. scène 1. marseilles. a rue entrer helena, widow, and diana, with two attendants helena. but this exceeding posting day and nuit must wear your spirits low; we ne peux pas help it. but depuis you have made the days and nights as one, to wear your doux membres in my affairs, be bold you do so grow in my requital as rien can unroot you. entrer a gentleman in heureux time! this man may help me to his majesty's ear, if he aurait dépenser his power. god save you, sir. gentleman. and you. helena. sir, i have seen you in the tribunal of france. gentleman. i have been sometimes there. helena. i do presume, sir, that you are not fall'n from the rapport that goes upon your goodness; and therefore, goaded with most tranchant occasions, lequel lay nice manners by, i put you to the use of your own virtues, for the lequel i doit continue thankful. gentleman. what's your will? helena. that it will S'il vous plaît you to give this poor petition to the king; and aid me with that boutique of Puissance you have to come into his presence. gentleman. the king's not here. helena. not here, sir? gentleman. not indeed. he Par conséquent remov'd last night, and with more hâte than is his use. widow. lord, how we lose our pains! helena. all's well that ends well yet, bien que time seem so adverse and veux dire unfit. i do beseech you, où is he gone? gentleman. marry, as i take it, to rousillon; où i am going. helena. i do beseech you, sir, depuis you are like to see the king avant me, saluer the papier to his gracious hand; lequel i presume doit rendre you no blame, but plutôt make you remercier your des douleurs for it. i will come après you with what good la vitesse our veux dire will make us means. gentleman. this i'll do for you. helena. and you doit find toi même to be well thank'd, whate'er des chutes more. we must to cheval again; go, go, provide. sortir act v scène 2. rousillon. the inner tribunal of the count's palais entrer pitre and parolles parolles. good monsieur lavache, give my lord lafeu this letter. i have ere now, sir, been mieux connu to you, when i have held familiarity with fresher clothes; but i am now, sir, muddied in fortune's mood, and odeur somewhat fort of her fort displeasure. clown. truly, fortune's mécontentement is but sluttish, if it odeur so strongly as thou speak'st of. i will désormais eat no fish of fortune's butt'ring. prithee, allow the wind. parolles. nay, you need not to stop your nose, sir; i spake but by a metaphor. clown. indeed, sir, if your metaphor stink, i will stop my nose; or against any man's metaphor. prithee, get thee further. parolles. pray you, sir, livrer me this paper. clown. foh! prithee supporter away. a papier from fortune's close-stool to give to a nobleman! look here he vient himself. entrer lafeu here is a pur of fortune's, sir, or of fortune's cat, but not a musk-cat, that has fall'n into the unclean fishpond of her displeasure, and, as he says, is muddied withal. pray you, sir, use the carp as you may; for he regards like a poor, decayed, ingenious, foolish, rascally knave. i do pity his distress in my similes of comfort, and laisser him to your lordship. exit parolles. my lord, i am a man whom fortune hath cruelly scratch'd. lafeu. and what aurait you have me to do? 'tis too late to pare her nails now. où have you played the fripon with fortune, that she devrait scratch you, who of se is a good lady and aurait not have knaves prospérer long sous her? there's a cardecue for you. let the justices make you and fortune friends; i am for autre business. parolles. i beseech your honour to hear me one Célibataire word. lafeu. you beg a Célibataire penny more; come, you doit ha't; save your word. parolles. my name, my good lord, is parolles. lafeu. you beg more than word then. cox my passion! give me your hand. how does your drum? parolles. o my good lord, you were the première that a trouvé me. lafeu. was i, in sooth? and i was the première that lost thee. parolles. it lies in you, my lord, to apporter me in some grace, for you did apporter me out. lafeu. out upon thee, knave! dost thou put upon me at once both the Bureau of god and the devil? one brings the in grace, and the autre brings thee out. [trumpets sound] the king's coming; i know by his trumpets. sirrah, inquire plus loin après me; i had talk of you last night. bien que you are a fool and a knave, you doit eat. go to; follow. parolles. i louange god for you. sortir act v scène 3. rousillon. the count's palais flourish. entrer king, countess, lafeu, the two français lords, with attendants king. we lost a bijou of her, and our esteem was made much poorer by it; but your son, as mad in folly, lack'd the sens to know her estimation home. countess. 'tis past, my liege; and i beseech your majesté to make it Naturel rebellion, done i' th' blaze of youth, when oil and fire, too fort for reason's force, o'erbears it and burns on. king. my honour'd lady, i have forgiven and forgotten all; bien que my revenges were high bent upon him and watch'd the time to shoot. lafeu. this i must say- but first, i beg my pardon: the Jeune lord did to his majesty, his mother, and his lady, infraction of puissant note; but to himself the greatest faux of all. he lost a wife dont beauté did astonish the survey of richest eyes; dont words all ears took captive; dont dear perfection hearts that scorn'd to servir humbly call'd mistress. king. praising what is lost fait du the remembrance dear. well, call him hither; we are reconcil'd, and the première view doit kill all repetition. let him not ask our pardon; the la nature of his génial infraction is dead, and deeper than oblivion do we bury th' incensing relics of it; let him approach, a stranger, no offender; and inform him so 'tis our will he should. gentleman. i shall, my liege. exit gentleman king. what says he to your daughter? have you spoke? lafeu. all that he is hath reference to your highness. king. then doit we have a match. i have letters sent me that sets him high in fame. entrer bertram lafeu. he regards well on 't. king. i am not a day of season, for thou mayst see a sunshine and a hail in me at once. but to the brightest beams distracted des nuages give way; so supporter thou forth; the time is fair again. bertram. my high-repented blames, dear sovereign, pardon to me. king. all is whole; not one word more of the consumed time. let's take the instant by the vers l'avant top; for we are old, and on our quick'st decrees th' inaudible and noiseless foot of time steals ere we can effet them. you rappelles toi the fille of this lord? bertram. admiringly, my liege. at première i stuck my choix upon her, ere my cœur durst make too bold herald of my tongue; où the impression of mine eye infixing, mépris his scornful perspective did lend me, lequel warp'd the line of chaque autre favour, scorn'd a fair Couleur or express'd it stol'n, extended or contracted all proportions to a most hideous object. thence it came that she whom all men prais'd, and whom myself, depuis i have lost, have lov'd, was in mine eye the dust that did offenser it. king. well excus'd. that thou didst love her, strikes some scores away from the génial compt; but love that vient too late, like a remorseful pardon slowly carried, to the génial sender se tourne a sour offence, crying 'that's good that's gone.' our rash faults make trivial price of serious things we have, not connaissance them jusqu'à we know leur grave. oft our displeasures, to nous-mêmes unjust, destroy our friends, and après weep leur dust; our own love waking cries to see what's done, tandis que shameful hate sleeps out the afternoon. be this sucré helen's knell. and now oublier her. send en avant your amorous token for fair maudlin. the main consents are had; and here we'll stay to see our widower's seconde marriage-day. countess. lequel mieux than the first, o dear heaven, bless! or, ere they meet, in me, o nature, cesse! lafeu. come on, my son, in whom my house's name must be digested; give a favoriser from you, to sparkle in the spirits of my daughter, that she may quickly come. [bertram gives a ring] by my old beard, and ev'ry hair that's on 't, helen, that's dead, was a sucré creature; such a ring as this, the last that e'er i took her laisser at court, i saw upon her finger. bertram. hers it was not. king. now, pray you, let me see it; for mine eye, tandis que i was speaking, oft was fasten'd to't. this ring was mine; and when i gave it helen i bade her, if her fortunes ever se tenait necessitied to help, that by this token i aurait relieve her. had you that craft to reave her of what devrait stead her most? bertram. my gracious sovereign, howe'er it pleases you to take it so, the ring was jamais hers. countess. son, on my life, i have seen her wear it; and she reckon'd it at her life's rate. lafeu. i am sure i saw her wear it. bertram. you are deceiv'd, my lord; she jamais saw it. in florence was it from a casement thrown me, wrapp'd in a paper, lequel contain'd the name of her that threw it. noble she was, and pensée i se tenait engag'd; but when i had subscrib'd to mine own fortune, and inform'd her fully i pourrait not répondre in that cours of honour as she had made the overture, she ceas'd, in lourd satisfaction, and aurait jamais recevoir the ring again. king. plutus himself, that sait the tinct and multiplying med'cine, hath not in nature's mystery more science than i have in this ring. 'twas mine, 'twas helen's, whoever gave it you. then, if you know that you are well connaissance with yourself, avouer 'twas hers, and by what rugueux enforcement you got it from her. she call'd the saints to surety that she aurait jamais put it from her doigt sauf si she gave it to toi même in bed- où you have jamais come- or sent it us upon her génial disaster. bertram. she jamais saw it. king. thou speak'st it falsely, as i love mine honour; and mak'st conjectural peurs to come into me lequel i aurait fain shut out. if it devrait prouver that thou art so inhuman- 'twill not prouver so. and yet i know not- thou didst hate her deadly, and she is dead; lequel nothing, but to proche her eyes myself, pourrait win me to croyez more than to see this ring. take him away. [guards seize bertram] my fore-past proofs, howe'er the matière fall, doit tax my peurs of peu vanity, ayant vainly fear'd too little. away with him. we'll sift this matière further. bertram. if you doit prouver this ring was ever hers, you doit as easy prouver that i husbanded her bed in florence, où she yet jamais was. exit, guarded king. i am wrapp'd in dismal thinkings. entrer a gentleman gentleman. gracious sovereign, qu'il s'agisse i have been to faire des reproches or no, i know not: here's a petition from a florentine, who hath, for four or five removes, come court to soumissionner it herself. i undertook it, vanquish'd thereto by the fair la grâce and discours of the poor suppliant, who by this, i know, is here attending; her Entreprise regards in her with an importing visage; and she told me in a sucré verbal bref it did concern your altesse with herself. king. [reads the letter] 'upon his many protestations to marier me when his wife was dead, i rougir to say it, he won me. now is the compter rousillon a widower; his vows are forfeited to me, and my honour's paid to him. he stole from florence, taking no leave, and i suivre him to his pays for justice. subvention it me, o king! in you it best lies; otherwise a seducer flourishes, and a poor maid is undone. diana capilet.' lafeu. i will buy me a son-in-law in a fair, and toll for this. i'll none of him. king. the heavens have pensée well on thee, lafeu, to apporter en avant this discov'ry. seek celles-ci suitors. go speedily, and apporter encore the count. sortir attendants i am afeard the life of helen, lady, was foully snatch'd. countess. now, Justice on the doers! entrer bertram, guarded king. i wonder, sir, sith épouses are monsters to you. and that you fly them as you jurer them lordship, yet you le désir to marry. entrer veuve and diana what woman's that? diana. i am, my lord, a wretched florentine, derived from the ancien capilet. my suit, as i do understand, you know, and par conséquent know how far i may be pitied. widow. i am her mother, sir, dont age and honour both souffrir sous this complaint we bring, and both doit cease, sans pour autant your remedy. king. come hither, count; do you know celles-ci women? bertram. my lord, i ni can nor will deny but that i know them. do they charge me further? diana. why do you look so étrange upon your wife? bertram. she's none of mine, my lord. diana. if you doit marry, you give away this hand, and that is mine; you give away heaven's vows, and ceux are mine; you give away myself, lequel is connu mine; for i by vow am so embodied le tiens that she lequel marries you must marier me, Soit both or none. lafeu. [to bertram] your réputation vient too court for my daughter; you are no mari for her. bertram. my lord, this is a fond and desp'rate créature whom parfois i have laugh'd with. let your altesse lay a more noble pensée upon mine honour than for to pense that i aurait sink it here. king. sir, for my thoughts, you have them ill to ami till your actes gain them. fairer prouver your honour than in my pensée it lies! diana. good my lord, ask him upon his oath if he does pense he had not my virginity. king. what say'st thou to her? bertram. she's impudent, my lord, and was a commun gamester to the camp. diana. he does me wrong, my lord; if i were so he pourrait have acheté me at a commun price. do not croyez him. o, voir this ring, dont high le respect and rich validity did lack a parallel; yet, for all that, he gave it to a commoner o' th' camp, if i be one. countess. he blushes, and 'tis it. of six preceding ancestors, that gem conferr'd by testament to th' sequent issue, hath it been ow'd and worn. this is his wife: that ring's a thousand proofs. king. methought you said you saw one here in tribunal pourrait témoin it. diana. i did, my lord, but loath am to produce so bad an instrument; his name's parolles. lafeu. i saw the man to-day, if man he be. king. find him, and apporter him hither. exit an attendant bertram. what of him? he's quoted for a most perfidious slave, with all the spots o' th' monde tax'd and debauch'd, dont la nature sickens but to parler a truth. am i or that or this for what he'll prononcer that will parler anything? king. she hath that ring of yours. bertram. i pense she has. certain it is i lik'd her, and boarded her i' th' wanton way of youth. she knew her distance, and did angle for me, madding my eagerness with her restraint, as all impediments in fancy's cours are motives of more fancy; and, in fine, her infini ruse with her modern la grâce subdu'd me to her rate. she got the ring; and i had that lequel any inferior pourrait at market-price have bought. diana. i must be patient. you that have turn'd off a première so noble wife may justly diet me. i pray you yet- depuis you lack virtue, i will lose a husband- send for your ring, i will revenir it home, and give me mine again. bertram. i have it not. king. what ring was yours, i pray you? diana. sir, much like the same upon your finger. king. know you this ring? this ring was his of late. diana. and this was it i gave him, étant abed. king. the story, then, goes faux you threw it him out of a casement. diana. i have parlait the truth. entrer parolles bertram. my lord, i do avouer the ring was hers. king. you boggle shrewdly; chaque feather starts you. is this the man you parler of? parolles. so S'il vous plaît your majesty, my Maître hath been an honourable gentleman; tricks he hath had in him, lequel gentlemen have. king. come, come, to th' purpose. did he love this woman? parolles. faith, sir, he did love her; but how? king. how, i pray you? parolles. he did love her, sir, as a gentleman aime a woman. king. how is that? parolles. he lov'd her, sir, and lov'd her not. king. as thou art a fripon and no knave. what an equivocal un compagnon is this! parolles. i am a poor man, and at your majesty's command. lafeu. he's a good drum, my lord, but a naughty orator. diana. do you know he promis'd me marriage? Là is your ring, and, look you, here's your letter. this it says: 'when from my doigt you can get this ring, and are by me with child,' etc. this is done. will you be mine now you are doubly won? bertram. if she, my liege, can make me know this clearly, i'll love her dearly, ever, ever dearly. helena. if it apparaître not plain, and prouver untrue, mortel divorce step entre me and you! o my dear mother, do i see you living? lafeu. mine eyes odeur onions; i doit weep anon. cuckold! good isis, hear me this prayer, bien que thou deny me a matière of more weight; good isis, i beseech thee! iras. amen. dear goddess, hear that prayer of the people! for, as it is a heartbreaking to see a handsome man loose-wiv'd, so it is a mortel chagrin to voir a foul fripon uncuckolded. therefore, dear isis, keep decorum, and fortune him accordingly! charmian. amen. alexas.
