my part, he garde me rustically at home, or, to parler more properly, stays me here at home unkept; for call you that keeping for a gentleman of my naissance that differs not from the stalling of an ox? his horses are bred better; for, outre that they are fair with leur feeding, they are enseigné leur manage, and to that end riders chèrement hir'd; but i, his brother, gain rien sous him but growth; for the lequel his animals on his dunghills are as much lié to him as i. outre this rien that he so plentifully gives me, the quelque chose that la nature gave me his countenance seems to take from me. he lets me feed with his hinds, bars me the endroit of a brother, and as much as in him lies, mines my gentility with my education. this is it, adam, that grieves me; and the esprit of my father, lequel i pense is dans me, begins to mutiny against this servitude. i will no plus long supporter it, bien que yet i know no wise remède how to éviter it. entrer oliver adam. là-bas vient my master, your brother. orlando. go apart, adam, and thou shalt hear how he will secouer me up. [adam retires] oliver. now, sir! what make you here? orlando. nothing; i am not enseigné to make any thing. oliver. what mar you then, sir? orlando. marry, sir, i am helping you to mar that lequel god made, a poor indigne frère of yours, with idleness. oliver. marry, sir, be mieux employed, and be néant awhile. orlando. doit i keep your hogs, and eat husks with them? what prodigal portion have i spent that i devrait come to such penury? oliver. know you où you are, sir? orlando. o, sir, very well; here in your orchard. oliver. know you avant whom, sir? orlando. ay, mieux than him i am avant sait me. i know you are my eldest brother; and in the doux état of blood, you devrait so know me. the courtoisie of nations allows you my mieux in that you are the first-born; but the same tradition takes not away my blood, were Là twenty brothers betwixt us. i have as much of my père in me as you, albeit i avouer your venir avant me is nearer to his reverence. oliver. what, boy! [strikes him] orlando. come, come, aîné brother, you are too Jeune in this. oliver. wilt thou lay mains on me, villain? orlando. i am no villain; i am the youngest son of sir rowland de boys. he was my father; and he is thrice a scélérat that says such a père begot villains. wert thou not my brother, i aurait not take this hand from thy gorge till this autre had pull'd out thy langue for en disant so. thou has rail'd on thyself. adam. [coming forward] sucré masters, be patient; for your father's remembrance, be at accord. oliver. let me go, i say. orlando. i will not, till i please; you doit hear me. my père charg'd you in his will to give me good education: you have train'd me like a peasant, obscuring and hiding from me all gentleman-like qualities. the esprit of my père grows fort in me, and i will no plus long supporter it; par conséquent allow me such exercises as may devenir a gentleman, or give me the poor allottery my père left me by testament; with that i will go buy my fortunes. oliver. and what wilt thou do? beg, when that is spent? well, sir, get you in. i will not long be troubled with you; you doit have some part of your will. i pray you laisser me. orlando. i no plus loin offenser you than becomes me for my good. oliver. get you with him, you old dog. adam. is 'old dog' my reward? most true, i have lost my les dents in your service. god be with my old master! he aurait not have parlait such a word. sortir orlando and adam oliver. is it even so? commencer you to grow upon me? i will physic your rankness, and yet give no thousand crowns neither. holla, dennis! entrer dennis dennis. calls your worship? oliver. not charles, the duke's wrestler, here to parler with me? dennis. so S'il vous plaît you, he is here at the door and importunes access to you. oliver. call him in. [exit dennis] 'twill be a good way; and to-morrow the wrestling is. entrer charles charles. good demain to your worship. oliver. good monsieur charles! what's the new news at the new court? charles. there's no news at the court, sir, but the old news; that is, the old duke is banished by his younger frère the new duke; and three or four aimant seigneurs have put se into voluntary exile with him, dont terres and revenues enrich the new duke; par conséquent he gives them good laisser to wander. oliver. can you tell if rosalind, the duke's daughter, be banished with her father? charles. o, no; for the duke's daughter, her cousin, so aime her, étant ever from leur cradles bred together, that she aurait have followed her exile, or have died to stay derrière her. she is at the court, and no less beloved of her oncle than his own daughter; and jamais two Dames loved as they do. oliver. où will the old duke live? charles. they say he is déjà in the forêt of arden, and a many joyeux men with him; and Là they live like the old robin hood of england. they say many Jeune gentlemen flock to him chaque day, and fleet the time carelessly, as they did in the d'or world. oliver. what, you wrestle to-morrow avant the new duke? charles. marry, do i, sir; and i came to acquaint you with a matter. i am given, sir, secretly to comprendre that your younger brother, orlando, hath a disposition to come in disguis'd against me to try a fall. to-morrow, sir, i wrestle for my credit; and he that escapes me sans pour autant some broken limb doit acquit him well. your frère is but Jeune and tender; and, for your love, i aurait be loath to foil him, as i must, for my own honour, if he come in; therefore, out of my love to you, i came hither to acquaint you withal, that Soit you pourrait stay him from his intendment, or ruisseau such disgrâce well as he doit run into, in that it is chose of his own chercher and altogether against my will. oliver. charles, i remercier thee for thy love to me, lequel thou shalt find i will most kindly requite. i had moi même notice of my brother's objectif herein, and have by underhand veux dire laboured to dissuade him from it; but he is resolute. i'll tell thee, charles, it is the stubbornest Jeune compagnon of france; full of ambition, an envious emulator of chaque man's good parts, a secret and villainous contriver against me his Naturel brother. par conséquent use thy discretion: i had as lief thou didst break his neck as his finger. and thou wert best look to't; for if thou dost him any slight disgrace, or if he do not mightily la grâce himself on thee, he will practise against thee by poison, entrap thee by some treacherous device, and jamais laisser thee till he hath ta'en thy life by some indirect veux dire or other; for, i assurer thee, and presque with larmes i parler it, Là is not one so Jeune and so villainous this day living. i parler but brotherly of him; but devrait i anatomize him to thee as he is, i must rougir and weep, and thou must look pale and wonder. charles. i am heartily glad i came hither to you. if he come to-morrow i'll give him his payment. if ever he go seul again, i'll jamais wrestle for prix more. and so, god keep your worship! exit oliver. farewell, good charles. now will i stir this gamester. i hope i doit see an end of him; for my soul, yet i know not why, hates rien more than he. yet he's gentle; jamais school'd and yet learned; full of noble device; of all sorts enchantingly beloved; and, indeed, so much in the cœur of the world, and especially of my own people, who best know him, that i am altogether misprised. but it doit not be so long; this wrestler doit clair all. rien remains but that i kindle the boy thither, lequel now i'll go about. exit <<this electronic version of the Achevée travaux of william shakespeare is droits d'auteur 1990-1993 by monde library, inc., and is à condition de by projet gutenberg etext of illinois benedictine Université with permission. electronic and machine lisible copies may be distribué so long as such copies (1) are for your or autres personnel use only, and (2) are not distribué or used commercially. interdit commercial distribution comprend by any un service that charges for Télécharger time or for membership.>> scène ii. a lawn avant the duke's palais entrer rosalind and celia celia. i pray thee, rosalind, sucré my coz, be merry. rosalind. dear celia, i show more gaieté than i am maîtresse of; and aurait you yet i were merrier? sauf si you pourrait enseigner me to oublier a banished father, you must not apprendre me how to rappelles toi any extraordinary pleasure. celia. herein i see thou lov'st me not with the full poids that i love thee. if my uncle, thy banished father, had banished thy uncle, the duke my father, so thou hadst been encore with me, i pourrait have enseigné my love to take thy père for mine; so wouldst thou, if the vérité of thy love to me were so righteously temper'd as mine is to thee. rosalind. well, i will oublier the état of my estate, to rejoice in yours. celia. you know my père hath no enfant but i, nor none is like to have; and, truly, when he dies thou shalt be his heir; for what he hath pris away from thy père perforce, i will rendre thee encore in affection. by mine honour, i will; and when i break that oath, let me turn monster; therefore, my sucré rose, my dear rose, be merry. rosalind. from désormais i will, coz, and concevoir sports. let me see; what pense you of falling in love? celia. marry, i prithee, do, to make sport withal; but love no man in good earnest, nor no plus loin in sport ni than with sécurité of a pure rougir thou mayst in honour come off again. rosalind. what doit be our sport, then? celia. let us sit and mock the good housewife fortune from her wheel, that her gifts may désormais be bestowed equally. rosalind. i aurait we pourrait do so; for her benefits are mightily misplaced; and the bountiful aveugle femme doth most erreur in her gifts to women. celia. 'tis true; for ceux that she fait du fair she rare fait du honest; and ceux that she fait du honnête she fait du very ill-favouredly. rosalind. nay; now thou goest from fortune's Bureau to nature's: fortune reigns in gifts of the world, not in the lineaments of nature. entrer touchstone celia. no; when la nature hath made a fair creature, may she not by fortune fall into the fire? bien que la nature hath donné us wit to flout at fortune, hath not fortune sent in this fool to cut off the argument? rosalind. indeed, Là is fortune too hard for nature, when fortune fait du nature's Naturel the cutter-off of nature's wit. celia. peradventure this is not fortune's work neither, but nature's, who perceiveth our Naturel wits too dull to raison of such goddesses, and hath sent this Naturel for our whetstone; for toujours the dullness of the fool is the whetstone of the wits. how now, wit! où wander you? touchstone. mistress, you must come away to your father. celia. were you made the messenger? touchstone. no, by mine honour; but i was bid to come for you. rosalind. où appris you that oath, fool? touchstone. of a certain Chevalier that juré by his honour they were good pancakes, and juré by his honour the mustard was naught. now i'll supporter to it, the pancakes were naught and the mustard was good, and yet was not the Chevalier forsworn. celia. how prouver you that, in the génial heap of your knowledge? rosalind. ay, marry, now unmuzzle your wisdom. touchstone. supporter you both en avant now: accident vasculaire cérébral your chins, and jurer by your beards that i am a knave. celia. by our beards, if we had them, thou art. touchstone. by my knavery, if i had it, then i were. but if you jurer by that that not, you are not forsworn; no more was this knight, swearing by his honour, for he jamais had any; or if he had, he had juré it away avant ever he saw ceux pancackes or that mustard. celia. prithee, who is't that thou mean'st? touchstone. one that old frederick, your father, loves. celia. my father's love is assez to honour him. enough, parler no more of him; you'll be whipt for taxation one of celles-ci days. touchstone. the more pity that imbéciles may not parler wisely what wise men do foolishly. celia. by my troth, thou sayest true; for depuis the peu wit that imbéciles have was silenced, the peu foolery that wise men have fait du a génial show. here vient monsieur le beau. entrer le beau rosalind. with his bouche full of news. celia. lequel he will put on us as pigeons feed leur young. rosalind. then doit we be news-cramm'd. celia. all the better; we doit be the more marketable. bon jour, monsieur le beau. what's the news? le beau. fair princess, you have lost much good sport. celia. sport! of what colour? le beau. what colour, madam? how doit i répondre you? rosalind. as wit and fortune will. touchstone. or as the destinies decrees. celia. well said; that was laid on with a trowel. touchstone. nay, if i keep not my rank- rosalind. thou losest thy old smell. le beau. you amaze me, ladies. i aurait have told you of good wrestling, lequel you have lost the vue of. rosalind. yet tell us the manière of the wrestling. le beau. i will tell you the beginning, and, if it S'il vous plaît your ladyships, you may see the end; for the best is yet to do; and here, où you are, they are venir to perform it. celia. well, the beginning, that is dead and buried. le beau. Là vient an old man and his three sons- celia. i pourrait rencontre this beginning with an old tale. le beau. three correct Jeune men, of excellent growth and presence. rosalind. with bills on leur necks: 'be it connu unto all men by celles-ci presents'- le beau. the eldest of the three wrestled with charles, the duke's wrestler; lequel charles in a moment threw him, and cassé three of his ribs, that Là is peu hope of life in him. so he serv'd the second, and so the third. là-bas they lie; the poor old man, leur father, fabrication such pitiful dole over them that all the beholders take his part with weeping. rosalind. alas! touchstone. but what is the sport, monsieur, that the Dames have lost? le beau. why, this that i parler of. touchstone. thus men may grow wiser chaque day. it is the première time that ever i entendu breaking of ribs was sport for ladies. celia. or i, i promettre thee. rosalind. but is Là any else longs to see this broken la musique in his sides? is Là yet un autre dotes upon rib-breaking? doit we see this wrestling, cousin? le beau. you must, if you stay here; for here is the endroit appointed for the wrestling, and they are prêt to perform it. celia. yonder, sure, they are coming. let us now stay and see it. flourish. entrer duke frederick, lords, orlando, charles, and attendants frederick. come on; depuis the jeunesse will not be entreated, his own péril on his forwardness. rosalind. is là-bas the man? le beau. even he, madam. celia. alas, he is too young; yet he regards successfully. frederick. how now, fille and cousin! are you crept hither to see the wrestling? rosalind. ay, my liege; so S'il vous plaît you give us leave. frederick. you will take peu délice in it, i can tell you, Là is such odds in the man. in pity of the challenger's jeunesse i aurait fain dissuade him, but he will not be entreated. parler to him, ladies; see if you can move him. celia. call him hither, good monsieur le beau. frederick. do so; i'll not be by. [duke frederick goes apart] le beau. monsieur the challenger, the princess calls for you. orlando. i assister them with all le respect and duty. rosalind. Jeune man, have you challeng'd charles the wrestler? orlando. no, fair princess; he is the général challenger. i come but in, as autres do, to try with him the force of my youth. celia. Jeune gentleman, your spirits are too bold for your years. you have seen cruel preuve of this man's strength; if you saw toi même with your eyes, or knew toi même with your judgment, the fear of your adventure aurait Conseil you to a more égal enterprise. we pray you, for your own sake, to embrasse your own sécurité and give over this attempt. rosalind. do, Jeune sir; your réputation doit not par conséquent be misprised: we will make it our suit to the duke that the wrestling pourrait not go forward. orlando. i beseech you, punish me not with your hard thoughts, où i avouer me much coupable to deny so fair and excellent Dames any thing. but let your fair eyes and doux wishes go with me to my trial; où if i be foil'd Là is but one sham'd that was jamais gracious; if kill'd, but one dead that is prêt to be so. i doit do my friends no wrong, for i have none to lament me; the monde no injury, for in it i have nothing; only in the monde i fill up a place, lequel may be mieux supplied when i have made it empty. rosalind. the peu force that i have, i aurait it were with you. celia. and mine to eke out hers. rosalind. fare you well. pray paradis i be deceiv'd in you! celia. your heart's desires be with you! charles. come, où is this Jeune galant that is so desirous to lie with his mère earth? orlando. ready, sir; but his will hath in it a more modeste working. frederick. you doit try but one fall. charles. no, i mandat your grace, you doit not supplier him to a second, that have so mightily persuaded him from a first. orlando. you mean to mock me after; you devrait not have mock'd me before; but come your ways. rosalind. now, hercules be thy speed, Jeune man! celia. i aurait i were invisible, to capture the fort compagnon by the leg. [they wrestle] rosalind. o excellent Jeune man! celia. if i had a thunderbolt in mine eye, i can tell who devrait down. [charles is thrown. shout] frederick. no more, no more. orlando. yes, i beseech your grace; i am not yet well breath'd. frederick. how dost thou, charles? le beau. he ne peux pas speak, my lord. frederick. bear him away. what is thy name, Jeune man? orlando. orlando, my liege; the youngest son of sir rowland de boys. frederick. i aurait thou hadst been son to some man else. the monde esteem'd thy père honourable, but i did find him encore mine enemy. thou shouldst have mieux pleas'd me with this deed, hadst thou descended from un autre house. but fare thee well; thou art a galant youth; i aurait thou hadst told me of un autre father. sortir duke, train, and le beau celia. were i my father, coz, aurait i do this? orlando. i am more fier to be sir rowland's son, his youngest son- and aurait not changement that calling to be adopted heir to frederick. rosalind. my père lov'd sir rowland as his soul, and all the monde was of my father's mind; had i avant connu this Jeune man his son, i devrait have donné him larmes unto entreaties ere he devrait thus have ventur'd. celia. doux cousin, let us go remercier him, and encourage him; my father's rugueux and envious disposition sticks me at heart. sir, you have well deserv'd; if you do keep your promises in love but justly as you have exceeded all promise, your maîtresse doit be happy. rosalind. gentleman, [giving him a chaîne from her neck] wear this for me; one out of suits with fortune, that pourrait give more, but that her hand lacks means. doit we go, coz? celia. ay. fare you well, fair gentleman. orlando. can i not say 'i remercier you'? my mieux les pièces are all thrown down; and that lequel here stands up is but a quintain, a mere lifeless block. rosalind. he calls us back. my fierté fell with my fortunes; i'll ask him what he would. did you call, sir? sir, you have wrestled well, and overthrown more than your enemies. celia. will you go, coz? rosalind. have with you. fare you well. sortir rosalind and celia orlando. what la passion bloque celles-ci weights upon my tongue? i ne peux pas parler to her, yet she urg'd conference. o poor orlando, thou art overthrown! or charles or quelque chose weaker masters thee. re-enter le beau le beau. good sir, i do in friendship Conseil you to laisser this place. albeit you have deserv'd high commendation, true applause, and love, yet such is now the duke's état that he misconstrues all that you have done. the duke is humorous; what he is, indeed, more suits you to conceive than i to parler of. orlando. i remercier you, sir; and pray you tell me this: lequel of the two was fille of the duke that here was at the wrestling? le beau. ni his daughter, if we juge by manners; but yet, indeed, the smaller is his daughter; the autre is fille to the banish'd duke, and here detain'd by her usurping uncle, to keep his fille company; dont aime are dearer than the Naturel bond of sisters. but i can tell you that of late this duke hath ta'en mécontentement 'gainst his doux niece, grounded upon no autre argument but that the gens louange her for her virtues and pity her for her good father's sake; and, on my life, his malice 'gainst the lady will tout à coup break forth. sir, fare you well. hereafter, in a mieux monde than this, i doit le désir more love and connaissance of you. orlando. i rest much bounden to you; fare you well. exit le beau thus must i from the smoke into the smother; from tyran duke unto a tyran brother. but heavenly rosalind! exit scène iii. the duke's palais entrer celia and rosalind celia. why, cousin! why, rosalind! Cupidon have mercy! not a word? rosalind. not one to jeter at a dog. celia. no, thy words are too précieux to be cast away upon curs; jeter some of them at me; come, lame me with reasons. rosalind. then Là were two cousins laid up, when the one devrait be lam'd with reasons and the autre mad sans pour autant any. celia. but is all this for your father? rosalind. no, some of it is for my child's father. o, how full of briers is this working-day world! celia. they are but burs, cousin, thrown upon thee in holiday foolery; if we walk not in the trodden paths, our very petticoats will capture them. rosalind. i pourrait secouer them off my coat: celles-ci burs are in my heart. celia. hem them away. rosalind. i aurait try, if i pourrait cry 'hem' and have him. celia. come, come, wrestle with thy affections. rosalind. o, they take the part of a mieux wrestler than myself. celia. o, a good wish upon you! you will try in time, in malgré of a fall. but, turning celles-ci jests out of service, let us talk in good earnest. is it possible, on such a sudden, you devrait fall into so fort a liking with old sir rowland's youngest son? rosalind. the duke my père lov'd his père dearly. celia. doth it par conséquent ensue that you devrait love his son dearly? by this kind of chase i devrait hate him, for my père hated his père dearly; yet i hate not orlando. rosalind. no, faith, hate him not, for my sake. celia. why devrait i not? doth he not mériter well? entrer duke frederick, with seigneurs rosalind. let me love him for that; and do you love him car i do. look, here vient the duke. celia. with his eyes full of anger. frederick. mistress, envoi you with your safest haste, and get you from our court. rosalind. me, uncle? frederick. you, cousin. dans celles-ci ten days if that thou beest a trouvé so near our Publique tribunal as twenty miles, thou diest for it. rosalind. i do beseech your grace, let me the connaissance of my faute bear with me. if with moi même i hold intelligence, or have acquaintance with mine own desires; if that i do not dream, or be not frantic- as i do confiance i am not- then, dear uncle, jamais so much as in a pensée unborn did i offenser your highness. frederick. thus do all traitors; if leur purgation did consist in words, they are as innocent as la grâce itself. let it suffice thee that i confiance thee not. rosalind. yet your mistrust ne peux pas make me a traitor. tell me whereon the likelihood depends. frederick. thou art thy father's daughter; there's enough. rosalind. so was i when your altesse took his dukedom; so was i when your altesse banish'd him. trahison is not inherited, my lord; or, if we did derive it from our friends, what's that to me? my père was no traitor. then, good my liege, erreur me not so much to pense my poverty is treacherous. celia. dear sovereign, hear me speak. frederick. ay, celia; we stay'd her for your sake, else had she with her père rang'd along. celia. i did not then supplier to have her stay; it was your pleasure, and your own remorse; i was too Jeune that time to value her, but now i know her. if she be a traitor, why so am i: we encore have slept together, rose at an instant, learn'd, play'd, eat together; and wheresoe'er we went, like juno's swans, encore we went coupled and inseparable. frederick. she is too subtle for thee; and her smoothness, her very silence and her patience, parler to the people, and they pity her. thou art a fool. she robs thee of thy name; and thou wilt show more brillant and seem more virtuous when she is gone. then open not thy lips. firm and irrevocable is my doom lequel i have pass'd upon her; she is banish'd. celia. pronounce that sentence, then, on me, my liege; i ne peux pas live out of her company. frederick. you are a fool. you, niece, provide yourself. if you outstay the time, upon mine honour, and in the greatness of my word, you die. sortir duke and seigneurs celia. o my poor rosalind! où wilt thou go? wilt thou changement fathers? i will give thee mine. i charge thee be not thou more griev'd than i am. rosalind. i have more cause. celia. thou hast not, cousin. prithee be cheerful. know'st thou not the duke hath banish'd me, his daughter? rosalind. that he hath not. celia. no, hath not? rosalind lacks, then, the love lequel teacheth thee that thou and i am one. doit we be sund'red? doit we part, sucré girl? no; let my père seek un autre heir. par conséquent concevoir with me how we may fly, où to go, and what to bear with us; and do not seek to take your charge upon you, to bear your griefs yourself, and laisser me out; for, by this heaven, now at our sorrows pale, say what thou canst, i'll go le long de with thee. rosalind. why, où doit we go? celia. to seek my oncle in the forêt of arden. rosalind. alas, what danger will it be to us, servantes as we are, to travel en avant so far! beauté provoketh thieves plus tôt than gold. celia. i'll put moi même in poor and mean attire, and with a kind of umber smirch my face; the like do you; so doit we pass along, and jamais stir assailants. rosalind. were it not better, car that i am more than commun tall, that i did suit me all points like a man? a galant curtle-axe upon my thigh, a boar spear in my hand; and- in my cœur lie Là what hidden woman's fear Là will- we'll have a swashing and a martial outside, as many autre mannish cowards have that do outface it with leur semblances. celia. what doit i call thee when thou art a man? rosalind. i'll have no pire a name than jove's own page, and par conséquent look you call me ganymede. but what will you be call'd? celia. quelque chose that hath a reference to my state: no plus long celia, but aliena. rosalind. but, cousin, what if we assay'd to voler the clownish fool out of your father's court? aurait he not be a confort to our travel? celia. he'll go le long de o'er the wide monde with me; laisser me seul to woo him. let's away, and get our jewels and our richesse together; concevoir the fittest time and safest way to hide us from pursuit that will be made après my flight. now go we in contenu to liberty, and not to banishment. sortir <<this electronic version of the Achevée travaux of william shakespeare is droits d'auteur 1990-1993 by monde library, inc., and is à condition de by projet gutenberg etext of illinois benedictine Université with permission. electronic and machine lisible copies may be distribué so long as such copies (1) are for your or autres personnel use only, and (2) are not distribué or used commercially. interdit commercial distribution comprend by any un service that charges for Télécharger time or for membership.>> act ii. scène i. the forêt of arden entrer duke senior, amiens, and two or three lords, like foresters duke senior. now, my co-mates and brothers in exile, hath not old Douane made this life more sucré than that of peint pomp? are not celles-ci woods more free from péril than the envious court? here feel we not the penalty of adam, the seasons' difference; as the icy fang and churlish chiding of the winter's wind, lequel when it bites and coups upon my body, even till i shrink with cold, i sourire and say 'this is no flattery; celles-ci are counsellors that feelingly persuade me what i am.' sucré are the uses of adversity, which, like the toad, ugly and venomous, wears yet a précieux bijou in his head; and this our life, exempt from Publique haunt, trouve tongues in trees, books in the running brooks, sermons in stones, and good in everything. i aurait not changement it. amiens. heureux is your grace, that can translate the stubbornness of fortune into so silencieux and so sucré a style. duke senior. come, doit we go and kill us venison? and yet it irks me the poor dappled fools, étant originaire de burghers of this désert city, should, in leur own confines, with forked têtes have leur rond haunches gor'd. première lord. indeed, my lord, the melancholy jaques grieves at that; and, in that kind, swears you do more usurp than doth your frère that hath banish'd you. to-day my lord of amiens and moi même did voler derrière him as he lay le long de sous an oak dont antique root peeps out upon the ruisseau that brawls le long de this wood! to the lequel endroit a poor sequest'red stag, that from the hunter's aim had ta'en a hurt, did come to languish; and, indeed, my lord, the wretched animal heav'd en avant such groans that leur discharge did stretch his leathern coat presque to bursting; and the big rond larmes cours'd one un autre down his innocent nose in piteous chase; and thus the hairy fool, much marked of the melancholy jaques, se tenait on th' extremest verge of the rapide brook, augmenting it with tears. duke senior. but what said jaques? did he not moralize this spectacle? première lord. o, yes, into a thousand similes. first, for his larmes into the needless stream: 'poor deer,' quoth he 'thou mak'st a testament as worldlings do, donnant thy sum of more to that lequel had too much.' then, étant Là alone, left and abandoned of his velvet friends: ''tis right'; quoth he 'thus misère doth part the flux of company.' anon, a careless herd, full of the pasture, jumps le long de by him and jamais stays to saluer him. 'ay,' quoth jaques 'sweep on, you fat and greasy citizens; 'tis just the fashion. wherefore do you look upon that poor and broken bankrupt there?' thus most invectively he pierceth par the body of the country, city, court, yea, and of this our life; swearing that we are mere usurpers, tyrants, and what's worse, to fright the animals, and to kill them up in leur assign'd and originaire de dwelling-place. duke senior. and did you laisser him in this contemplation? seconde lord. we did, my lord, larmes and commenting upon the sobbing deer. duke senior. show me the place; i love to cope him in celles-ci sullen fits, for then he's full of matter. première lord. i'll apporter you to him straight. sortir scène ii. the duke's palais entrer duke frederick, with seigneurs frederick. can it be possible that no man saw them? it ne peux pas be; some villains of my tribunal are of consentement and sufferance in this. première lord. i ne peux pas hear of any that did see her. the ladies, her attendants of her chamber, saw her abed, and in the Matin de bonne heure they a trouvé the bed untreasur'd of leur mistress. seconde lord. my lord, the roynish clown, at whom so oft your la grâce was wont to laugh, is also missing. hisperia, the princess' gentlewoman, confesses that she secretly o'erheard your fille and her cousin much saluer the les pièces and graces of the wrestler that did but lately foil the sinewy charles; and she believes, wherever they are gone, that jeunesse is sûrement in leur company. frederick. send to his brother; chercher that galant hither. if he be absent, apporter his frère to me; i'll make him find him. do this suddenly; and let not chercher and inquisition quail to apporter encore celles-ci insensé runaways. sortir scène iii. avant oliver's maison entrer orlando and adam, réunion orlando. who's there? adam. what, my Jeune master? o my doux master! o my sucré master! o you Mémoire of old sir rowland! why, what make you here? why are you virtuous? why do gens love you? and wherefore are you gentle, strong, and valiant? why aurait you be so fond to overcome the bonny prizer of the humorous duke? your louange is come too swiftly home avant you. know you not, master, to some kind of men leur graces servir them but as enemies? no more do yours. your virtues, doux master, are sanctified and holy traitors to you. o, what a monde is this, when what is comely envenoms him that ours it! orlando. why, what's the matter? adam. o malheureux youth! come not dans celles-ci doors; dans this roof the ennemi of all your graces lives. your brother- no, no brother; yet the son- yet not the son; i will not call him son of him i was sur to call his father- hath entendu your praises; and this nuit he veux dire to burn the lodging où you use to lie, and you dans it. if he fail of that, he will have autre veux dire to cut you off; i overheard him and his practices. this is no place; this maison is but a butchery; abhor it, fear it, do not entrer it. orlando. why, whither, adam, wouldst thou have me go? adam. no matière whither, so you come not here. orlando. what, wouldst thou have me go and beg my food, or with a base and boist'rous épée enforce a thievish vivant on the commun road? this i must do, or know not what to do; yet this i will not do, do how i can. i plutôt will matière me to the malice of a diverted du sang and bloody brother. adam. but do not so. i have five cent crowns, the thrifty hire i sav'd sous your father, lequel i did boutique to be my foster-nurse, when un service devrait in my old membres lie lame, and unregarded age in corners thrown. take that, and he that doth the ravens feed, yea, providently caters for the sparrow, be confort to my age! here is the gold; all this i give you. let me be your servant; bien que i look old, yet i am fort and lusty; for in my jeunesse i jamais did apply hot and rebellious liquors in my blood, nor did not with unbashful forehead woo the veux dire of weakness and debility; par conséquent my age is as a lusty winter, frosty, but kindly. let me go with you; i'll do the un service of a younger man in all your Entreprise and necessities. orlando. o good old man, how well in thee apparaît the constant un service of the antique world, when un service transpiration for duty, not for meed! thou art not for the mode of celles-ci times, où none will transpiration but for promotion, and ayant that do choke leur un service up even with the having; it is not so with thee. but, poor old man, thou prun'st a pourri tree that ne peux pas so much as a blossom rendement in lieu of all thy des douleurs and husbandry. but come thy ways, we'll go le long de together, and ere we have thy youthful wages spent we'll lumière upon some settled low content. adam. master, go on; and i will suivre the to the last gasp, with vérité and loyalty. from seventeen years till now presque four-score here lived i, but now live here no more. at seventeen years many leur fortunes seek, but at fourscore it is too late a week; yet fortune ne peux pas recompense me mieux than to die well and not my master's debtor. sortir scène iv. the forêt of arden entrer rosalind for ganymede, celia for aliena, and pitre alias touchstone rosalind. o jupiter, how se lasser are my spirits! touchstone. i care not for my spirits, if my legs were not weary. rosalind. i pourrait find in my cœur to disgrâce my man's apparel, and to cry like a woman; but i must confort the weaker vessel, as doublet and hose ought to show lui-même courageous to petticoat; therefore, courage, good aliena. celia. i pray you bear with me; i ne peux pas go no further. touchstone. for my part, i had plutôt bear with you than bear you; yet i devrait bear no traverser if i did bear you; for i pense you have no argent in your purse. rosalind. well,. this is the forêt of arden. touchstone. ay, now am i in arden; the more fool i; when i was at home i was in a mieux place; but travellers must be content. entrer corin and silvius rosalind. ay, be so, good touchstone. look you, who vient here, a Jeune man and an old in solennel talk. corin. that is the way to make her mépris you still. silvius. o corin, that thou knew'st how i do love her! corin. i partiellement guess; for i have lov'd ere now. silvius. no, corin, étant old, thou canst not guess, bien que in thy jeunesse thou wast as true a lover as ever sigh'd upon a minuit pillow. but if thy love were ever like to mine, as sure i pense did jamais man love so, how many actions most ridiculous hast thou been tiré to by thy fantasy? corin. into a thousand that i have forgotten. silvius. o, thou didst then jamais love so heartily! if thou rememb'rest not the slightest folie that ever love did make thee run into, thou hast not lov'd; or if thou hast not sat as i do now, wearing thy hearer in thy mistress' praise, thou hast not lov'd; or if thou hast not cassé from entreprise abruptly, as my la passion now fait du me, thou hast not lov'd. o phebe, phebe, phebe! exit silvius rosalind. alas, poor shepherd! searching of thy wound, i have by hard adventure a trouvé mine own. touchstone. and i mine. i remember, when i was in love, i cassé my épée upon a stone, and bid him take that for venir a-night to jane smile; and i rappelles toi the kissing of her batler, and the cow's dugs that her jolie chopt mains had milk'd; and i rappelles toi the wooing of peascod instead of her; from whom i took two cods, and donnant her them again, said with larmes larmes 'wear celles-ci for my sake.' we that are true les amoureux run into étrange capers; but as all is mortel in nature, so is all la nature in love mortel in folly. rosalind. thou speak'st wiser than thou art ware of. touchstone. nay, i doit ne'er be ware of mine own wit till i break my shins against it. rosalind. jove, jove! this shepherd's la passion is much upon my fashion. touchstone. and mine; but it grows quelque chose stale with me. celia. i pray you, one of you question yond man if he for gold will give us any food; i perdre connaissance presque to death. touchstone. holla, you clown! rosalind. peace, fool; he's not thy ensman. corin. who calls? touchstone. your betters, sir. corin. else are they very wretched. rosalind. peace, i say. good even to you, friend. corin. and to you, doux sir, and to you all. rosalind. i prithee, shepherd, if that love or gold can in this désert endroit buy entertainment, apporter us où we may rest nous-mêmes and feed. here's a Jeune maid with travel much oppress'd, and faints for succour. corin. fair sir, i pity her, and wish, for her sake more than for mine own, my fortunes were more able to relieve her; but i am berger to un autre man, and do not shear the fleeces that i graze. my Maître is of churlish disposition, and peu recks to find the way to paradis by Faire actes of hospitality. besides, his cote, his flocks, and bounds of feed, are now on sale; and at our sheepcote now, by raison of his absence, Là is rien that you will feed on; but what is, come see, and in my voix most Bienvenue doit you be. rosalind. what is he that doit buy his flock and pasture? corin. that Jeune swain that you saw here but erewhile, that peu se soucie for buying any thing. rosalind. i pray thee, if it supporter with honesty, buy thou the cottage, pasture, and the flock, and thou shalt have to pay for it of us. celia. and we will mend thy wages. i like this place, and willingly pourrait déchets my time in it. corin. assuredly the chose is to be sold. go with me; if you like upon rapport the soil, the profit, and this kind of life, i will your very faithful feeder be, and buy it with your gold droite suddenly. sortir scène v. un autre part of the forêt entrer amiens, jaques, and autres song amiens. sous the greenwood tree who aime to lie with me, and turn his joyeux note unto the sucré bird's throat, come hither, come hither, come hither. here doit he see no ennemi but hiver and rugueux weather. jaques. more, more, i prithee, more. amiens. it will make you melancholy, monsieur jaques. jaques. i remercier it. more, i prithee, more. i can suck melancholy out of a song, as a weasel sucks eggs. more, i prithee, more. amiens. my voix is ragged; i know i ne peux pas S'il vous plaît you. jaques. i do not le désir you to S'il vous plaît me; i do le désir you to sing. come, more; un autre stanzo. call you 'em stanzos? amiens. what you will, monsieur jaques. jaques. nay, i care not for leur names; they owe me nothing. will you sing? amiens. more at your demande than to S'il vous plaît myself. jaques. well then, if ever i remercier any man, i'll remercier you; but that they call compliment is like th' rencontre of two dog-apes; and when a man thanks me heartily, methinks have donné him a penny, and he renders me the beggarly thanks. come, sing; and you that will not, hold your tongues. amiens. well, i'll end the song. sirs, cover the while; the duke will boisson sous this tree. he hath been all this day to look you. jaques. and i have been all this day to éviter him. he is to disputable for my company. i pense of as many matters as he; but i give paradis thanks, and make no boast of them. come, warble, come. song [all ensemble here] who doth ambition shun, and aime to live i' th' sun, seeking the food he eats, and pleas'd with what he gets, come hither, come hither, come hither. here doit he see no ennemi but hiver and rugueux weather. jaques. i'll give you a verse to this note that i made yesterday in malgré of my invention. amiens. and i'll sing it. jaques. thus it goes: if it do come to pass that any man turn ass, leaving his richesse and ease a stubborn will to please, ducdame, ducdame, ducdame; here doit he see brut imbéciles as he, an if he will come to me. amiens. what's that 'ducdame'? jaques. 'tis a greek invocation, to call imbéciles into a circle. i'll go sleep, if i can; if i cannot, i'll rail against all the first-born of egypt. amiens. and i'll go seek the duke; his banquet is prepar'd. sortir severally scène vi. the forêt entrer orlando and adam adam. dear master, i can go no further. o, i die for food! here lie i down, and mesure out my grave. farewell, kind master. orlando. why, how now, adam! no greater cœur in thee? live a little; confort a little; acclamation thyself a little. if this uncouth forêt rendement n'importe quoi savage, i will Soit be food for it or apporter it for food to thee. thy conceit is nearer décès than thy powers. for my sake be comfortable; hold décès quelque temps at the arm's end. i will here be with the presently; and if i apporter thee not quelque chose to eat, i will give thee laisser to die; but if thou diest avant i come, thou art a mocker of my labour. well said! thou look'st cheerly; and i'll be with thee quickly. yet thou liest in the bleak air. come, i will bear thee to some shelter; and thou shalt not die for lack of a dinner, if Là live n'importe quoi in this desert. cheerly, good adam! sortir scène vii. the forêt a table set out. entrer duke senior, amiens, and lords, like outlaws duke senior. i pense he be transform'd into a beast; for i can nowhere find him like a man. première lord. my lord, he is but even now gone hence; here was he merry, hearing of a song. duke senior. if he, compact of jars, grow musical, we doit have prochainement discord in the spheres. go seek him; tell him i aurait parler with him. entrer jaques première lord. he saves my la main d'oeuvre by his own approach. duke senior. why, how now, monsieur! what a life is this, that your poor friends must woo your company? what, you look merrily! jaques. a fool, a fool! i met a fool i' th' forest, a motley fool. a miserable world! as i do live by food, i met a fool, who laid him down and bask'd him in the sun, and rail'd on lady fortune in good terms, in good set terms- and yet a motley fool. 'good morrow, fool,' quoth i; 'no, sir,' quoth he, 'call me not fool till paradis hath sent me fortune.' and then he drew a dial from his poke, and, looking on it with lack-lustre eye, says very wisely, 'it is ten o'clock; thus we may see,' quoth he, 'how the monde wags; 'tis but an hour ago depuis it was nine; and après one hour more 'twill be eleven; and so, from hour to hour, we ripe and ripe, and then, from hour to hour, we rot and rot; and thereby bloque a tale.' when i did hear the motley fool thus moral on the time, my lungs began to crow like chanticleer that imbéciles devrait be so deep contemplative; and i did rire sans intermission an hour by his dial. o noble fool! a worthy fool! motley's the only wear. duke senior. what fool is this? jaques. o worthy fool! one that hath been a courtier, and says, if Dames be but Jeune and fair, they have the gift to know it; and in his brain, lequel is as dry as the remainder biscuit après a voyage, he hath étrange places cramm'd with observation, the lequel he vents in mangled forms. o that i were a fool! i am ambitious for a motley coat. duke senior. thou shalt have one. jaques. it is my only suit, à condition de that you weed your mieux judgments of all opinion that grows rank in them that i am wise. i must have liberté withal, as grand a charter as the wind, to blow on whom i please, for so imbéciles have; and they that are most galled with my folly, they most must laugh. and why, sir, must they so? the why is plaine as way to parish church: he that a fool doth very wisely hit doth very foolishly, bien que he smart, not to seem senseless of the bob; if not, the wise man's folie is anatomiz'd even by the squand'ring glances of the fool. invest me in my motley; give me laisser to parler my mind, and i will par and par cleanse the foul body of th' infected world, if they will patiently recevoir my medicine. duke senior. fie on thee! i can tell what thou wouldst do. jaques. what, for a counter, aurait i do but good? duke senior. most mischievous foul sin, in chiding sin; for thou thyself hast been a libertine, as sensual as the brutish sting itself; and all th' embossed sores and headed evils that thou with license of free foot hast caught wouldst thou disgorge into the général world. jaques. why, who cries out on fierté that can la bride tax any privé party? doth it not flow as hugely as the sea, till that the wearer's very veux dire do ebb? what femme in the city do i name when that i say the city-woman ours the cost of princes on indigne shoulders? who can come in and say that i mean her, when such a one as she such is her neighbour? or what is he of basest function that says his bravery is not on my cost, thinking that i mean him, but la bride suits his folie to the mettle of my speech? Là then! how then? what then? let me see où my langue hath wrong'd him: if it do him right, then he hath wrong'd himself; if he be free, why then my taxing like a wild-goose flies, unclaim'd of any man. but who vient here? entrer orlando with his épée tiré orlando. forbear, and eat no more. jaques. why, i have eat none yet. orlando. nor shalt not, till necessity be serv'd. jaques. of what kind devrait this cock come of? duke senior. art thou thus bolden'd, man, by thy distress? or else a rude despiser of good manners, that in civility thou seem'st so empty? orlando. you touch'd my vein at first: the thorny point of bare distress hath ta'en from me the show of smooth civility; yet arn i inland bred, and know some nurture. but forbear, i say; he dies that touches any of this fruit till i and my affaires are answered. jaques. an you will not be answer'd with reason, i must die. duke senior. what aurait you have? your gentleness doit Obliger more than your Obliger move us to gentleness. orlando. i presque die for food, and let me have it. duke senior. sit down and feed, and Bienvenue to our table. orlando. parler you so gently? pardon me, i pray you; i pensée that all things had been savage here, and par conséquent put i on the countenance of stern commandment. but whate'er you are that in this désert inaccessible, sous the shade of melancholy boughs, lose and neglect the creeping heures of time; if ever you have look'd on mieux days, if ever been où bells have knoll'd to church, if ever sat at any good man's feast, if ever from your eyelids wip'd a tear, and know what 'tis to pity and be pitied, let gentleness my fort enforcement be; in the lequel hope i blush, and hide my sword. duke senior. true is it that we have seen mieux days, and have with holy bell been knoll'd to church, and sat at good men's feasts, and wip'd our eyes of gouttes that sacré pity hath engend'red; and par conséquent sit you down in gentleness, and take upon commander what help we have that to your wanting may be minist'red. orlando. then but ancêtre your food a peu while, whiles, like a doe, i go to find my fawn, and give it food. Là is an old poor man who après me hath many a se lasser step limp'd in pure love; till he be première suffic'd, oppress'd with two weak evils, age and hunger, i will not toucher a bit. duke senior. go find him out. and we will rien déchets till you return. orlando. i remercier ye; and be heureux for your good comfort! exit duke senior. thou seest we are not all seul unhappy: this wide and universal theatre presents more woeful pageants than the scène où we play in. jaques. all the world's a stage, and all the men and women merely players; they have leur exits and leur entrances; and one man in his time plays many parts, his acts étant Sept ages. at première the infant, mewling and puking in the nurse's arms; then the whining school-boy, with his satchel and shining Matin face, creeping like snail unwillingly to school. and then the lover, sighing like furnace, with a woeful ballad made to his mistress' eyebrow. then a soldier, full of étrange oaths, and bearded like the pard, jaloux in honour, soudain and rapide in quarrel, seeking the bubble réputation even in the cannon's mouth. and then the justice, in fair rond belly with good capon lin'd, with eyes severe and barbe of formal cut, full of wise saws and modern instances; and so he plays his part. the sixth age shifts into the lean and slipper'd pantaloon, with spectacles on nose and pouch on side, his youthful hose, well sav'd, a monde too wide for his shrunk shank; and his big manly voice, turning encore vers childish treble, pipes and whistles in his sound. last scène of all, that ends this étrange eventful history, is seconde childishness and mere oblivion; sans teeth, sans eyes, sans taste, sans chaque thing. re-enter orlando with adam duke senior. welcome. set down your venerable burden. and let him feed. orlando. i remercier you most for him. adam. so had you need; i rare can parler to remercier you for myself. duke senior. welcome; fall to. i will not difficulté you as yet to question you sur your fortunes. give us some music; and, good cousin, sing. song blow, blow, thou hiver wind, thou art not so unkind as man's ingratitude; thy tooth is not so keen, car thou art not seen, bien que thy souffle be rude. heigh-ho! sing heigh-ho! unto the vert holly. most friendship is feigning, most aimant mere folly. then, heigh-ho, the holly! this life is most jolly. freeze, freeze, thou amer sky, that dost not bite so nigh as benefits forgot; bien que thou the waters warp, thy sting is not so tranchant as ami rememb'red not. heigh-ho! sing, &c. duke senior. if that you were the good sir rowland's son, as you have whisper'd faithfully you were, and as mine eye doth his effigies témoin most vraiment limn'd and vivant in your face, be vraiment Bienvenue hither. i am the duke that lov'd your father. the residue of your fortune, go to my cave and tell me. good old man, thou art droite Bienvenue as thy Maître is. support him by the arm. give me your hand, and let me all your fortunes understand. sortir act iii. scène i. the palais entrer duke frederick, oliver, and seigneurs frederick. not see him since! sir, sir, that ne peux pas be. but were i not the mieux part made mercy, i devrait not seek an absent argument of my revenge, thou present. but look to it: find out thy frère wheresoe'er he is; seek him with candle; apporter him dead or vivant dans this twelvemonth, or turn thou no more to seek a vivant in our territory. thy terres and all things that thou dost call thine vaut seizure do we seize into our hands, till thou canst quit thee by thy brother's bouche of what we pense against thee. oliver. o that your altesse knew my cœur in this! i jamais lov'd my frère in my life. frederick. more scélérat thou. well, push him out of doors; and let my officers of such a la nature make an extent upon his maison and lands. do this expediently, and turn him going. sortir scène ii. the forêt entrer orlando, with a papier orlando. hang there, my verse, in témoin of my love; and thou, thrice-crowned reine of night, survey with thy chaste eye, from thy pale sphere above, thy huntress' name that my full life doth sway. o rosalind! celles-ci trees doit be my books, and in leur barks my thoughts i'll character, that chaque eye lequel in this forêt regards doit see thy vertu witness'd chaque where. run, run, orlando; carve on chaque tree, the fair, the chaste, and unexpressive she. exit entrer corin and touchstone corin. and how like you this shepherd's life, Maître touchstone? touchstone. truly, shepherd, in le respect of itself, it is a good life; but in le respect that it is a shepherd's life, it is nought. in le respect that it is solitary, i like it very well; but in le respect that it is private, it is a very vile life. now in le respect it is in the fields, it pleaseth me well; but in le respect it is not in the court, it is tedious. as it is a de rechange life, look you, it fits my humour well; but as Là is no more plenty in it, it goes much against my stomach. hast any philosophy in thee, shepherd? corin. no more but that i know the more one sickens the pire at ease he is; and that he that wants money, means, and content, is sans pour autant three good friends; that the property of rain is to wet, and fire to burn; that good pasture fait du fat sheep; and that a génial cause of the nuit is lack of the sun; that he that hath appris no wit by la nature nor art may complain of good breeding, or vient of a very dull kindred. touchstone. such a one is a Naturel philosopher. wast ever in court, shepherd? corin. no, truly. touchstone. then thou art damn'd. corin. nay, i hope. touchstone. truly, thou art damn'd, like an ill-roasted egg, all on one side. corin. for not étant at court? your reason. touchstone. why, if thou jamais wast at tribunal thou jamais saw'st good manners; if thou jamais saw'st good manners, then thy manners must be wicked; and wickedness is sin, and sin is damnation. thou art in a parlous state, shepherd. corin. not a whit, touchstone. ceux that are good manners at the tribunal are as ridiculous in the pays as the behaviour of the pays is most mockable at the court. you told me you salute not at the court, but you kiss your hands; that courtoisie aurait be uncleanly if courtiers were shepherds. touchstone. instance, briefly; come, instance. corin. why, we are encore handling our ewes; and leur fells, you know, are greasy. touchstone. why, do not your courtier's mains sweat? and is not the grease of a mutton as wholesome as the transpiration of a man? shallow, shallow. a mieux instance, i say; come. corin. besides, our mains are hard. touchstone. your lips will feel them the sooner. shallow again. a more sounder instance; come. corin. and they are souvent tarr'd over with the surgery of our sheep; and aurait you have us kiss tar? the courtier's mains are perfum'd with civet. touchstone. most shallow man! thou worm's meat in le respect of a good pièce of la chair indeed! apprendre of the wise, and perpend: civet is of a baser naissance than tar- the very uncleanly flux of a cat. mend the instance, shepherd. corin. you have too courtly a wit for me; i'll rest. touchstone. wilt thou rest damn'd? god help thee, shallow man! god make incision in thee! thou art raw. corin. sir, i am a true labourer: i earn that i eat, get that i wear; owe no man hate, envy no man's happiness; glad of autre men's good, contenu with my harm; and the greatest of my fierté is to see my ewes graze and my lambs suck. touchstone. that is un autre Facile sin in you: to apporter the ewes and the rams together, and to offre to get your vivant by the copulation of cattle; to be bawd to a bell-wether, and to trahir a she-lamb of a twelvemonth to crooked-pated, old, cuckoldly ram, out of all reasonable match. if thou beest not damn'd for this, the diable himself will have no shepherds; i ne peux pas see else how thou shouldst scape. corin. here vient Jeune Maître ganymede, my new mistress's brother. entrer rosalind, reading a papier rosalind. 'from the east to western inde, no bijou is like rosalinde. her worth, étant mounted on the wind, par all the monde ours rosalinde. all the pictures fairest lin'd are but noir to rosalinde. let no face be kept in mind but the fair of rosalinde.' touchstone. i'll rhyme you so eight years together, dinners, and suppers, and sleeping hours, excepted. it is the droite butter-women's rank to market. rosalind. out, fool! touchstone. for a taste: if a hart do lack a hind, let him seek out rosalinde. if the cat will après kind, so be sure will rosalinde. hiver garments must be lin'd, so must mince rosalinde. they that reap must sheaf and bind, then to cart with rosalinde. sweetest nut hath sourest rind, such a nut is rosalinde. he that sweetest rose will find must find love's prick and rosalinde. this is the very faux gallop of verses; why do you infect toi même with them? rosalind. peace, you dull fool! i a trouvé them on a tree. touchstone. truly, the tree yields bad fruit. rosalind. i'll graff it with you, and then i doit graff it with a medlar. then it will be the earliest fruit i' th' country; for you'll be pourri ere you be half ripe, and that's the droite vertu of the medlar. touchstone. you have said; but qu'il s'agisse wisely or no, let the forêt judge. entrer celia, with a writing rosalind. peace! here vient my sister, reading; supporter aside. celia. 'why devrait this a désert be? for it is unpeopled? no; tongues i'll hang on chaque tree that doit civil sayings show. some, how bref the life of man runs his erring pilgrimage, that the streching of a span buckles in his sum of age; some, of violated vows 'twixt the âmes of ami and friend; but upon the fairest boughs, or at chaque phrase end, will i rosalinda write, teaching all that read to know the quintessence of chaque sprite paradis aurait in peu show. par conséquent paradis la nature charg'd that one body devrait be fill'd with all graces wide-enlarg'd. la nature presently distill'd helen's cheek, but not her heart, cleopatra's majesty, atalanta's mieux part, sad lucretia's modesty. thus rosalinde of many les pièces by heavenly synod was devis'd, of many faces, eyes, and hearts, to have the touches très cher priz'd. paradis aurait that she celles-ci gifts devrait have, and i to live and die her slave.' rosalind. o most doux pulpiter! what fastidieux homily of love have you wearied your parishioners withal, and jamais cried 'have patience, good people.' celia. how now! back, friends; shepherd, go off a little; go with him, sirrah. touchstone. come, shepherd, let us make an honourable retreat; bien que not with bag and baggage, yet with scrip and scrippage. sortir corin and touchstone celia. didst thou hear celles-ci verses? rosalind. o, yes, i entendu them all, and more too; for some of them had in them more feet than the verses aurait bear. celia. that's no matter; the feet pourrait bear the verses. rosalind. ay, but the feet were lame, and pourrait not bear se sans pour autant the verse, and par conséquent se tenait lamely in the verse. celia. but didst thou hear sans pour autant wondering how thy name devrait be hang'd and carved upon celles-ci trees? rosalind. i was Sept of the nine days out of the merveille avant you came; for look here what i a trouvé on a palm-tree. i was jamais so berhym'd depuis pythagoras' time that i was an irish rat, lequel i can hardly remember. celia. trow you who hath done this? rosalind. is it a man? celia. and a chain, that you once wore, sur his neck. changement you colour? rosalind. i prithee, who? celia. o lord, lord! it is a hard matière for friends to meet; but mountains may be remov'd with earthquakes, and so encounter. rosalind. nay, but who is it? celia. is it possible? rosalind. nay, i prithee now, with most petitionary vehemence, tell me who it is. celia. o wonderful, wonderful, most wonderful wonderful, and yet encore wonderful, and après that, out of all whooping! rosalind. good my complexion! dost thou think, bien que i am caparison'd like a man, i have a doublet and hose in my disposition? one inch of delay more is a south sea of discovery. i prithee tell me who is it quickly, and parler apace. i aurait thou could'st stammer, that thou mightst pour this conceal'd man out of thy mouth, as wine vient out of narrow-mouth'd bottle- Soit too much at once or none at all. i prithee take the cork out of thy bouche that i may boisson thy tidings. celia. so you may put a man in your belly. rosalind. is he of god's making? what manière of man? is his head vaut a hat or his chin vaut a beard? celia. nay, he hath but a peu beard. rosalind. why, god will send more if the man will be thankful. let me stay the growth of his beard, if thou delay me not the connaissance of his chin. celia. it is Jeune orlando, that tripp'd up the wrestler's talons and your cœur both in an instant. rosalind. nay, but the diable take mocking! parler sad brow and true maid. celia. i' faith, coz, 'tis he. rosalind. orlando? celia. orlando. rosalind. alas the day! what doit i do with my doublet and hose? what did he when thou saw'st him? what said he? how look'd he? où went he? what fait du he here? did he ask for me? où remains he? how séparé he with thee? and when shalt thou see him again? répondre me in one word. celia. you must borrow me gargantua's bouche first; 'tis a word too génial for any bouche of this age's size. to say ay and no to celles-ci particulars is more than to répondre in a catechism. rosalind. but doth he know that i am in this forest, and in man's apparel? regards he as freshly as he did the day he wrestled? celia. it is as easy to compter atomies as to resolve the propositions of a lover; but take a goût of my finding him, and relish it with good observance. i a trouvé him sous a tree, like a dropp'd acorn. rosalind. it may well be call'd jove's tree, when it gouttes en avant such fruit. celia. give me audience, good madam. rosalind. proceed. celia. Là lay he, stretch'd le long de like a blessés knight. rosalind. bien que it be pity to see such a sight, it well becomes the ground. celia. cry 'holla' to thy tongue, i prithee; it curvets unseasonably. he was furnish'd like a hunter. rosalind. o, ominous! he vient to kill my heart. celia. i aurait sing my song sans pour autant a burden; thou bring'st me out of tune. rosalind. do you not know i am a woman? when i think, i must speak. sweet, say on. celia. you apporter me out. soft! vient he not here? entrer orlando and jaques rosalind. 'tis he; slink by, and note him. jaques. i remercier you for your company; but, good faith, i had as lief have been moi même alone. orlando. and so had i; but yet, for mode sake, i remercier you too for your society. jaques. god buy you; let's meet as peu as we can. orlando. i do le désir we may be mieux strangers. jaques. i pray you mar no more trees with writing love songs in leur barks. orlando. i pray you mar no more of my verses with reading them ill-favouredly. jaques. rosalind is your love's name? orlando. yes, just. jaques. i do not like her name. orlando. Là was no pensée of pleasing you when she was christen'd. jaques. what stature is she of? orlando. just as high as my heart. jaques. you are full of jolie answers. have you not been connaissance with goldsmiths' wives, and conn'd them out of rings? orlando. not so; but i répondre you droite peint cloth, from d'où you have studied your questions. jaques. you have a nimble wit; i pense 'twas made of atalanta's heels. will you sit down with me? and we two will rail against our maîtresse the world, and all our misery. orlando. i will gronder no breather in the monde but myself, against whom i know most faults. jaques. the worst faute you have is to be in love. orlando. 'tis a faute i will not changement for your best virtue. i am se lasser of you. jaques. by my troth, i was seeking for a fool when i a trouvé you. orlando. he is drown'd in the brook; look but in, and you doit see him. jaques. Là i doit see mine own figure. orlando. lequel i take to be Soit a fool or a cipher. jaques. i'll goudronneux no plus long with you; farewell, good signior love. orlando. i am glad of your departure; adieu, good monsieur melancholy. exit jaques rosalind. [aside to celia] i will parler to him like a saucy lackey, and sous that habitude play the fripon with him.- do you hear, forester? orlando. very well; what aurait you? rosalind. i pray you, what is't o'clock? orlando. you devrait ask me what time o' day; there's no clock in the forest. rosalind. then Là is no true lover in the forest, else sighing chaque minute and groaning chaque hour aurait detect the lazy foot of time as well as a clock. orlando. and why not the rapide foot of time? had not that been as proper? rosalind. by no means, sir. time travels in divers paces with divers persons. i'll tell you who time ambles withal, who time trots withal, who time gallops withal, and who he stands encore withal. orlando. i prithee, who doth he trot withal? rosalind. marry, he trots hard with a Jeune maid entre the contract of her mariage and the day it is solemniz'd; if the interim be but a se'nnight, time's pace is so hard that it seems the length of Sept year. orlando. who ambles time withal? rosalind. with a prêtre that lacks latin and a rich man that hath not the gout; for the one sleeps easily car he ne peux pas study, and the autre vies merrily car he feels no pain; the one lacking the fardeau of lean and wasteful learning, the autre connaissance no fardeau of lourd fastidieux penury. celles-ci time ambles withal. orlando. who doth he gallop withal? rosalind. with a voleur to the gallows; for bien que he go as softly as foot can fall, he thinks himself too soon there. orlando. who stays it encore withal? rosalind. with lawyers in the vacation; for they sommeil entre term and term, and then they apercevoir not how time moves. orlando. où habiter you, jolie youth? rosalind. with this shepherdess, my sister; here in the skirts of the forest, like fringe upon a petticoat. orlando. are you originaire de of this place? rosalind. as the coney that you see habiter où she is kindled. orlando. your accent is quelque chose finer than you pourrait purchase in so removed a dwelling. rosalind. i have been told so of many; but En effet an old religious oncle of mine enseigné me to speak, who was in his jeunesse an inland man; one that knew courtship too well, for Là he fell in love. i have entendu him read many lectures against it; and i remercier god i am not a woman, to be touch'd with so many giddy offences as he hath generally tax'd leur entier sex withal. orlando. can you rappelles toi any of the principal evils that he laid to the charge of women? rosalind. Là were none principal; they were all like one un autre as halfpence are; chaque one faute seeming monstrous till his fellow-fault came to rencontre it. orlando. i prithee recount some of them. rosalind. no; i will not cast away my physic but on ceux that are sick. Là is a man haunts the forêt that abuses our Jeune plants with carving 'rosalind' on leur barks; bloque odes upon hawthorns and elegies on brambles; all, forsooth, deifying the name of rosalind. if i pourrait meet that fancy-monger, i aurait give him some good counsel, for he seems to have the quotidian of love upon him. orlando. i am he that is so love-shak'd; i pray you tell me your remedy. rosalind. Là is none of my uncle's marks upon you; he enseigné me how to know a man in love; in lequel cage of rushes i am sure you are not prisoner. orlando. what were his marks? rosalind. a lean cheek, lequel you have not; a blue eye and sunken, lequel you have not; an unquestionable spirit, lequel you have not; a barbe neglected, lequel you have not; but i pardon you for that, for simply your ayant in barbe is a younger brother's revenue. then your hose devrait be ungarter'd, your bonnet unbanded, your sleeve unbutton'd, your shoe untied, and chaque chose sur you demonstrating a careless desolation. but you are no such man; you are plutôt point-device in your accoutrements, as aimant toi même than seeming the lover of any other. orlando. fair youth, i aurait i pourrait make thee croyez i love. rosalind. me croyez it! you may as soon make her that you love croyez it; which, i warrant, she is apter to do than to avouer she does. that is one of the points in the lequel women encore give the lie to leur consciences. but, in good sooth, are you he that bloque the verses on the trees où rosalind is so admired? orlando. i jurer to thee, youth, by the white hand of rosalind, i am that he, that unfortunate he. rosalind. but are you so much in love as your rhymes speak? orlando. ni rhyme nor raison can Express how much. rosalind. love is merely a madness; and, i tell you, deserves as well a dark maison and a whip as madmen do; and the raison why they are not so punish'd and cured is that the lunacy is so ordinary that the whippers are in love too. yet i profess curing it by counsel. orlando. did you ever cure any so? rosalind. yes, one; and in this manner. he was to imagine me his love, his mistress; and i set him chaque day to woo me; at lequel time aurait i, étant but a moonish youth, grieve, be effeminate, changeable, longing and liking, proud, fantastical, apish, shallow, inconstant, full of tears, full of smiles; for chaque la passion quelque chose and for no la passion vraiment anything, as boys and women are for the most part cattle of this colour; aurait now like him, now loathe him; then divertir him, then forswear him; now weep for him, then spit at him; that i drave my suitor from his mad humour of love to a vivant humour of madness; lequel was, to forswear the full stream of the monde and to live in a nook merely monastic. and thus i cur'd him; and this way will i take upon me to wash your liver as clean as a du son sheep's heart, that Là doit not be one spot of love in 't. orlando. i aurait not be cured, youth. rosalind. i aurait cure you, if you aurait but call me rosalind, and come chaque day to my cote and woo me. orlando. now, by the Foi of my love, i will. tell me où it is. rosalind. go with me to it, and i'll show it you; and, by the way, you doit tell me où in the forêt you live. will you go? orlando. with all my heart, good youth. rosalind. nay, you must call me rosalind. come, sister, will you go? sortir scène iii. the forêt entrer touchstone and audrey; jaques derrière touchstone. come apace, good audrey; i will chercher up your goats, audrey. and how, audrey, am i the man yet? doth my Facile feature contenu you? audrey. your features! lord mandat us! what features? touchstone. i am here with thee and thy goats, as the most capricious poet, honnête ovid, was among the goths. jaques. [aside] o connaissance ill-inhabited, pire than jove in a thatch'd house! touchstone. when a man's verses ne peux pas be understood, nor a man's good wit seconded with the vers l'avant enfant understanding, it strikes a man more dead than a génial reckoning in a peu room. truly, i aurait the gods had made thee poetical. audrey. i do not know what 'poetical' is. is it honnête in deed and word? is it a true thing? touchstone. no, truly; for the truest poetry is the most feigning, and les amoureux are donné to poetry; and what they jurer in poetry may be said as les amoureux they do feign. audrey. do you wish, then, that the gods had made me poetical? touchstone. i do, truly, for thou swear'st to me thou art honest; now, if thou wert a poet, i pourrait have some hope thou didst feign. audrey. aurait you not have me honest? touchstone. no, truly, sauf si thou wert hard-favour'd; for honesty coupled to beauté is to have honey a sauce to sugar. jaques. [aside] a material fool! audrey. well, i am not fair; and par conséquent i pray the gods make me honest. touchstone. truly, and to cast away honesty upon a foul slut were to put good meat into an unclean dish. audrey. i am not a slut, bien que i remercier the gods i am foul. touchstone. well, praised be the gods for thy foulness; sluttishness may come hereafter. but be it as it may be, i will marier thee; and to that end i have been with sir oliver martext, the vicar of the next village, who hath promis'd to meet me in this endroit of the forest, and to couple us. jaques. [aside] i aurait fain see this meeting. audrey. well, the gods give us joy! touchstone. amen. a man may, if he were of a craintif heart, stagger in this attempt; for here we have no temple but the wood, no assembly but horn-beasts. but what though? courage! as horns are odious, they are necessary. it is said: 'many a man sait no end of his goods.' right! many a man has good horns and sait no end of them. well, that is the dowry of his wife; 'tis none of his own getting. horns? even so. poor men alone? no, no; the noblest deer hath them as huge as the rascal. is the Célibataire man par conséquent blessed? no; as a wall'd town is more worthier than a village, so is the forehead of a married man more honourable than the bare brow of a bachelor; and by how much defence is mieux than no skill, by so much is horn more précieux than to want. here vient sir oliver. entrer sir oliver martext sir oliver martext, you are well met. will you envoi us here sous this tree, or doit we go with you to your chapel? martext. is Là none here to give the woman? touchstone. i will not take her on gift of any man. martext. truly, she must be given, or the mariage is not lawful. jaques. [discovering himself] proceed, proceed; i'll give her. touchstone. good even, good Maître what-ye-call't; how do you, sir? you are very well met. goddild you for your last company. i am very glad to see you. even a toy in hand here, sir. nay; pray be cover'd. jaques. will you be married, motley? touchstone. as the ox hath his bow, sir, the cheval his curb, and the falcon her bells, so man hath his desires; and as pigeons bill, so wedlock aurait be nibbling. jaques. and will you, étant a man of your breeding, be married sous a bush, like a beggar? get you to église and have a good prêtre that can tell you what mariage is; this compagnon will but join you ensemble as they join wainscot; then one of you will prouver a shrunk panel, and like vert timber warp, warp. touchstone. [aside] i am not in the mind but i were mieux to be married of him than of another; for he is not like to marier me well; and not étant well married, it will be a good excuse for me ci-après, par la suite to laisser my wife. jaques. go thou with me, and let me Conseil thee. touchstone. come, sucré audrey; we must be married or we must live in bawdry. farewell, good Maître oliver. not- o sucré oliver, o courageux oliver, laisser me not derrière thee. but- wind away, begone, i say, i will not to wedding with thee. sortir jaques, touchstone, and audrey martext. 'tis no matter; ne'er a fantastical fripon of them all doit flout me out of my calling. exit scène iv. the forêt entrer rosalind and celia rosalind. jamais talk to me; i will weep. celia. do, i prithee; but yet have the la grâce to considérer that larmes do not devenir a man. rosalind. but have i not cause to weep? celia. as good cause as one aurait desire; par conséquent weep. rosalind. his very hair is of the dissembling colour. celia. quelque chose browner than judas's. marry, his kisses are judas's own children. rosalind. i' faith, his hair is of a good colour. celia. an excellent colour: your chestnut was ever the only colour. rosalind. and his kissing is as full of sanctity as the toucher of holy bread. celia. he hath acheté a pair of cast lips of diana. a nun of winter's sisterhood kisses not more religiously; the very ice of chastity is in them. rosalind. but why did he jurer he aurait come this morning, and vient not? celia. nay, certainly, Là is no vérité in him. rosalind. do you pense so? celia. yes; i pense he is not a pick-purse nor a horse-stealer; but for his verity in love, i do pense him as concave as covered goblet or a worm-eaten nut. rosalind. not true in love? celia. yes, when he is in; but i pense he is not in. rosalind. you have entendu him jurer downright he was. celia. 'was' is not 'is'; besides, the oath of a lover is no stronger than the word of a tapster; they are both the confirmer of faux reckonings. he attends here in the forêt on the duke, your father. rosalind. i met the duke yesterday, and had much question with him. he asked me of what parentage i was; i told him, of as good as he; so he laugh'd and let me go. but what talk we of fathers when Là is such a man as orlando? celia. o, that's a courageux man! he writes courageux verses, parle courageux words, swears courageux oaths, and breaks them bravely, assez traverse, athwart the cœur of his lover; as a puny tilter, that spurs his cheval but on one side, breaks his Personnel like a noble goose. but all's courageux that jeunesse mounts and folie guides. who vient here? entrer corin corin. maîtresse and master, you have oft enquired après the berger that complain'd of love, who you saw sitting by me on the turf, praising the fier disdainful shepherdess that was his mistress. celia. well, and what of him? corin. if you will see a pageant vraiment play'd entre the pale complexion of true love and the red glow of mépris and fier disdain, go Par conséquent a little, and i doit conduite you, if you will mark it. rosalind. o, come, let us remove! the vue of les amoureux feedeth ceux in love. apporter us to this sight, and you doit say i'll prouver a busy actor in leur play. sortir scène v. un autre part of the forêt entrer silvius and phebe silvius. sucré phebe, do not mépris me; do not, phebe. say that you love me not; but say not so in bitterness. the commun executioner, dont cœur th' accustom'd vue of décès fait du hard, des chutes not the axe upon the humbled neck but première begs pardon. will you sterner be than he that dies and vies by bloody drops? entrer rosalind, celia, and corin, at a distance phebe. i aurait not be thy executioner; i fly thee, for i aurait not injure thee. thou tell'st me Là is meurtre in mine eye.
