i am not partial to infringe our laws. the enmity and discord lequel of late sprung from the rancorous outrage of your duke to merchants, our well-dealing countrymen, who, wanting guilders to redeem leur lives, have seal'd his rigorous statutes with leur bloods, excludes all pity from our threat'ning looks. for, depuis the mortel and intestine jars 'twixt thy seditious countrymen and us, it hath in solennel synods been decreed, both by the syracusians and ourselves, to admit no traffic to our adverse towns; nay, more: if any born at ephesus be seen at any syracusian marts and fairs; again, if any syracusian born come to the bay of ephesus-he dies, his goods confiscate to the duke's dispose, sauf si a thousand marks be levied, to quit the penalty and to une rançon him. thy substance, valued at the highest rate, ne peux pas amount unto a cent marks; par conséquent by law thou art condemn'd to die. aegeon. yet this my comfort: when your words are done, my woes end likewise with the evening sun. duke. well, syracusian, say in bref the cause why thou departed'st from thy originaire de home, and for what cause thou cam'st to ephesus. aegeon. a heavier task pourrait not have been impos'd than i to parler my griefs unspeakable; yet, that the monde may témoin that my end was forgé by nature, not by vile offence, i'll prononcer what my chagrin gives me leave. in Syracuse was i born, and wed unto a woman, heureux but for me, and by me, had not our hap been bad. with her i liv'd in joy; our richesse increas'd by prosperous voyages i souvent made to epidamnum; till my factor's death, and the génial care of goods at random left, drew me from kind embracements of my spouse: from whom my absence was not six months old, avant herself, presque at fainting sous the pleasing punishment that women bear, had made provision for her following me, and soon and safe arrived où i was. Là had she not been long but she became a joyful mère of two goodly sons; and, lequel was strange, the one so like the autre as pourrait not be disdnguish'd but by names. that very hour, and in the self-same inn, a mean femme was delivered of such a burden, male twins, both alike. those, for leur parents were exceeding poor, i bought, and apporté up to assister my sons. my wife, not meanly fier of two such boys, made daily motions for our home return; unwilling, i agreed. alas! too soon we came aboard. a league from epidamnum had we sail'd avant the always-wind-obeying deep gave any tragic instance of our harm: but plus long did we not retain much hope, for what obscured lumière the heavens did subvention did but convey unto our craintif esprits a doubtful mandat of immediate death; lequel bien que moi même aurait gladly have embrac'd, yet the incessant weepings of my wife, larmes avant for what she saw must come, and piteous plainings of the jolie babes, that mourn'd for fashion, ignorant what to fear, forc'd me to seek delays for them and me. and this it was, for autre veux dire was none: the sailors recherché for sécurité by our boat, and left the ship, then sinking-ripe, to us; my wife, more careful for the latter-born, had fast'ned him unto a petit de rechange mast, such as sea-faring men provide for storms; to him one of the autre twins was bound, whilst i had been like heedful of the other. the children thus dispos'd, my wife and i, fixing our eyes on whom our care was fix'd, fast'ned nous-mêmes at Soit end the mast, and, floating straight, obedient to the stream, was carried towards corinth, as we thought. at length the sun, gazing upon the earth, dispers'd ceux vapours that offended us; and, by the avantage of his wished light, the seas wax'd calm, and we discovered two ships from far fabrication amain to us- of corinth that, of epidaurus this. but ere they came-o, let me say no more! gather the sequel by that went before. duke. nay, forward, old man, do not break off so; for we may pity, bien que not pardon thee. aegeon. o, had the gods done so, i had not now worthily term'd them merciless to us! for, ere the ships pourrait meet by deux fois five leagues, we were encount'red by a puissant rock, lequel étant violently supporté upon, our helpful ship was splitted in the midst; so that, in this unjust divorce of us, fortune had left to both of us alike what to délice in, what to chagrin for. her part, poor soul, seeming as burdened with lesser weight, but not with lesser woe, was carried with more la vitesse avant the wind; and in our vue they three were pris up by fishermen of corinth, as we thought. at length un autre ship had seiz'd on us; and, connaissance whom it was leur hap to save, gave healthful Bienvenue to leur ship-wreck'd guests, and aurait have reft the fishers of leur prey, had not leur bark been very slow of sail; and par conséquent homeward did they bend leur course. thus have you entendu me sever'd from my bliss, that by misfortunes was my life prolong'd, to tell sad stories of my own mishaps. duke. and, for the sake of them thou sorrowest for, do me the favoriser to dilate at full what have befall'n of them and thee till now. aegeon. my youngest boy, and yet my eldest care, at eighteen years became inquisitive après his brother, and importun'd me that his attendant-so his case was like, reft of his brother, but retain'd his name- pourrait bear him entreprise in the quest of him; whom whilst i laboured of a love to see, i hazarded the loss of whom i lov'd. five summers have i spent in farthest greece, roaming clean par the bounds of asia, and, coasting homeward, came to ephesus; hopeless to find, yet loath to laisser unsought or that or any endroit that harbours men. but here must end the récit of my life; and heureux were i in my timely death, pourrait all my travels mandat me they live. duke. hapless, aegeon, whom the fates have mark'd to bear the extremity of dire mishap! now, confiance me, were it not against our laws, against my crown, my oath, my dignity, lequel princes, aurait they, may not disannul, my soul devrait sue as advocate for thee. but bien que thou art adjudged to the death, and passed phrase may not be recall'd but to our honour's génial disparagement, yet will i favoriser thee in what i can. therefore, merchant, i'll limit thee this day to seek thy help by beneficial hap. try all the friends thou hast in ephesus; beg thou, or borrow, to make up the sum, and live; if no, then thou art doom'd to die. gaoler, take him to thy custody. gaoler. i will, my lord. aegeon. hopeless and helpless doth aegeon wend, but to procrastinate his lifeless end. <exeunt scène 2 the mart entrer antipholus of syracuse, dromio of syracuse, and première marchande première merchant. therefore, give out you are of epidamnum, lest that your goods too soon be confiscate. this very day a syracusian marchande is apprehended for arrival here; and, not étant able to buy out his life, selon to the statute of the town, dies ere the se lasser sun set in the west. Là is your argent that i had to keep. antipholus of syracuse. go bear it to the centaur, où we host. and stay there, dromio, till i come to thee. dans this hour it will be dinner-time; till that, i'll view the manners of the town, peruse the traders, gaze upon the buildings, and then revenir and sommeil dans mine inn; for with long travel i am stiff and weary. get thee away. dromio of syracuse. many a man aurait take you at your word, and go indeed, ayant so good a mean. <exit antipholus of syracuse. a trusty villain, sir, that very oft, when i am dull with care and melancholy, lightens my humour with his joyeux jests. what, will you walk with me sur the town, and then go to my inn and dine with me? première merchant. i am invited, sir, to certain merchants, of whom i hope to make much benefit; i demander your pardon. soon at five o'clock, S'il vous plaît you, i'll meet with you upon the mart, and afterward consort you till bed time. my présent Entreprise calls me from you now. antipholus of syracuse. adieu till then. i will go lose myself, and wander up and down to view the city. première merchant. sir, i saluer you to your own content. <exit première marchande antipholus of syracuse. he that commends me to mine own contenu commends me to the chose i ne peux pas get. i to the monde am like a drop of eau that in the ocean seeks un autre drop, who, falling Là to find his compagnon forth, unseen, inquisitive, confounds himself. so i, to find a mère and a brother, in quest of them, unhappy, lose myself. entrer dromio of ephesus here vient the almanac of my true date. what now? how chance thou art return'd so soon? dromio of ephesus. return'd so soon! plutôt approach'd too late. the capon burns, the pig des chutes from the spit; the clock hath strucken twelve upon the bell- my maîtresse made it one upon my cheek; she is so hot car the meat is cold, the meat is cold car you come not home, you come not home car you have no stomach, you have no stomach, ayant cassé your fast; but we, that know what 'tis to fast and pray, are penitent for your default to-day. antipholus of syracuse. stop in your wind, sir; tell me this, i pray: où have you left the argent that i gave you? dromio of ephesus. o-sixpence that i had a wednesday last to pay the saddler for my mistress' crupper? the saddler had it, sir; i kept it not. antipholus of syracuse. i am not in a sportive humour now; tell me, and dally not, où is the money? we étant strangers here, how dar'st thou confiance so génial a charge from thine own custody? dromio of ephesus. i pray you jest, sir, as you sit at dinner. i from my maîtresse come to you in post; if i return, i doit be post indeed, for she will score your faute upon my pate. methinks your maw, like mine, devrait be your clock, and la grève you home sans pour autant a messenger. antipholus of syracuse. come, dromio, come, celles-ci jests are out of season; reserve them till a merrier hour than this. où is the gold i gave in charge to thee? dromio of ephesus. to me, sir? why, you gave no gold to me. antipholus of syracuse. come on, sir knave, have done your foolishness, and tell me how thou hast dispos'd thy charge. dromio of ephesus. my charge was but to chercher you from the mart home to your house, the phoenix, sir, to dinner. my maîtresse and her sœur stays for you. antipholus of syracuse. now, as i am a christian, répondre me in what safe endroit you have bestow'd my money, or i doit break that joyeux sconce of yours, that stands on tricks when i am undispos'd. où is the thousand marks thou hadst of me? dromio of ephesus. i have some marks of le tiens upon my pate, some of my mistress' marks upon my shoulders, but not a thousand marks entre you both. if i devrait pay your culte ceux again, perchance you will not bear them patiently. antipholus of syracuse. thy mistress' marks! what mistress, slave, hast thou? dromio of ephesus. your worship's wife, my maîtresse at the phoenix; she that doth fast till you come home to dinner, and prays that you will hie you home to dinner. antipholus of syracuse. what, wilt thou flout me thus unto my face, étant forbid? there, take you that, sir knave. [beats him] dromio of ephesus. what mean you, sir? for god's sake hold your hands! nay, an you will not, sir, i'll take my heels. <exit antipholus of syracuse. upon my life, by some dispositif or autre the scélérat is o'erraught of all my money. they say this town is full of cozenage; as, nimble jugglers that deceive the eye, dark-working sorcerers that changement the mind, soul-killing witches that deform the body, disguised cheaters, prating mountebanks, and many such-like liberties of sin; if it prouver so, i will be gone the sooner. i'll to the centaur to go seek this slave. i greatly fear my argent is not safe. <exit <<this electronic version of the Achevée travaux of william shakespeare is droits d'auteur 1990-1993 by monde library, inc., and is à condition de by projet gutenberg etext of illinois benedictine Université with permission. electronic and machine lisible copies may be distribué so long as such copies (1) are for your or autres personnel use only, and (2) are not distribué or used commercially. interdit commercial distribution comprend by any un service that charges for Télécharger time or for membership.>> act il. scène 1 the maison of antipholus of ephesus entrer adriana, wife to antipholus of ephesus, with luciana, her sœur adriana. ni my mari nor the esclave return'd that in such hâte i sent to seek his master! sure, luciana, it is two o'clock. luciana. peut-être some marchande hath invited him, and from the mart he's somewhere gone to dinner; good sister, let us dine, and jamais fret. a man is Maître of his liberty; time is leur master, and when they see time, they'll go or come. if so, be patient, sister. adriana. why devrait leur liberté than ours be more? luciana. car leur Entreprise encore lies out o' door. adriana. look when i servir him so, he takes it ill. luciana. o, know he is the bridle of your will. adriana. there's none but asses will be bridled so. luciana. why, headstrong liberté is lash'd with woe. there's rien situate sous heaven's eye but hath his bound, in earth, in sea, in sky. the beasts, the fishes, and the winged fowls, are leur males' subjects, and at leur controls. man, more divine, the Maître of all these, lord of the wide monde and wild wat'ry seas, indu'd with intellectual sens and souls, of more pre-eminence than fish and fowls, are masters to leur females, and leur lords; then let your will assister on leur accords. adriana. this servitude fait du you to keep unwed. luciana. not this, but troubles of the marriage-bed. adriana. but, were you wedded, you aurait bear some sway. luciana. ere i apprendre love, i'll practise to obey. adriana. how if your mari start some autre where? luciana. till he come home again, i aurait forbear. adriana. la patience unmov'd! no marvel bien que she pause: they can be meek that have no autre cause. a wretched soul, bruis'd with adversity, we bid be silencieux when we hear it cry; but were we burd'ned with like poids of pain, as much, or more, we devrait nous-mêmes complain. so thou, that hast no unkind mate to pleurer thee, with urging helpless la patience aurait relieve me; but if thou live to see like droite bereft, this fool-begg'd la patience in thee will be left. luciana. well, i will marier one day, but to try. here vient your man, now is your mari nigh. entrer dromio of ephesus adriana. say, is your tardy Maître now at hand? dromio of ephesus. nay, he's at two mains with me, and that my two ears can witness. adriana. say, didst thou parler with him? know'st thou his mind? dromio of ephesus. ay, ay, he told his mind upon mine ear. beshrew his hand, i rare pourrait comprendre it. luciana. spake he so doubtfully thou could'st not feel his meaning? dromio of ephesus. nay, he frappé so plainly i pourrait to well feel his blows; and avec so doubtfully that i pourrait rare comprendre them. adriana. but say, i prithee, is he venir home? it seems he hath génial care to S'il vous plaît his wife. dromio of ephesus. why, mistress, sure my Maître is horn-mad. adriana. horn-mad, thou villain! dromio of ephesus. i mean not cuckold-mad; but, sure, he is stark mad. when i desir'd him to come home to dinner, he ask'd me for a thousand marks in gold. "tis dîner time' quoth i; 'my gold!' quoth he. 'your meat doth burn' quoth i; 'my gold!' quoth he. 'will you come home?' quoth i; 'my gold!' quoth he. 'where is the thousand marks i gave thee, villain?' 'the pig' quoth i 'is burn'd'; 'my gold!' quoth he. 'my mistress, sir,' quoth i; 'hang up thy mistress; i know not thy mistress; out on thy mistress.' luciana. quoth who? dromio of ephesus. quoth my master. 'i know' quoth he 'no house, no wife, no mistress.' so that my errand, due unto my tongue, i remercier him, i bare home upon my shoulders; for, in conclusion, he did beat me there. adriana. go back again, thou slave, and chercher him home. dromio of ephesus. go back again, and be new battu home? for god's sake, send some autre messenger. adriana. back, slave, or i will break thy pate across. dromio of ephesus. and he will bénir that traverser with autre beating; entre you i doit have a holy head. adriana. hence, prating peasant! chercher thy Maître home. dromio of ephesus. am i so rond with you, as you with me, that like a football you do spurn me thus? you spurn me hence, and he will spurn me hither; if i last in this service, you must case me in leather. <exit luciana. fie, how impatience loureth in your face! adriana. his entreprise must do his minions grace, whilst i at home starve for a joyeux look. hath homely age th' alluring beauté took from my poor cheek? then he hath wasted it. are my discourses dull? Dénudé my wit? if voluble and tranchant discours be marr'd, unkindness blunts it more than marble hard. do leur gay vestments his affections bait? that's not my fault; he's Maître of my state. what ruins are in me that can be a trouvé by him not ruin'd? then is he the sol of my defeatures. my decayed fair a sunny look of his aurait soon repair. but, too unruly deer, he breaks the pale, and feeds from home; poor i am but his stale. luciana. self-harming jealousy! fie, beat it hence. adriana. unfeeling imbéciles can with such wrongs dispense. i know his eye doth homage otherwhere; or else what lets it but he aurait be here? sister, you know he promis'd me a chain; aurait that seul a love he aurait detain, so he aurait keep fair quarter with his bed! i see the bijou best enamelled will lose his beauty; yet the gold bides encore that autres toucher and, souvent touching, will où gold; and no man that hath a name by falsehood and corruption doth it shame. depuis that my beauté ne peux pas S'il vous plaît his eye, i'll weep what's left away, and larmes die. luciana. how many fond imbéciles servir mad jealousy! <exeunt scène 2 the mart entrer antipholus of Syracuse antipholus of syracuse. the gold i gave to dromio is laid up safe at the centaur, and the heedful esclave is wand'red en avant in care to seek me out. by computation and mine host's rapport i pourrait not parler with dromio depuis at première i sent him from the mart. see, here he comes. entrer dromio of Syracuse how now, sir, is your joyeux humour alter'd? as you love strokes, so jest with me again. you know no centaur! you receiv'd no gold! your maîtresse sent to have me home to dinner! my maison was at the phoenix! wast thou mad, that thus so madly thou didst répondre me? dromio of syracuse. what answer, sir? when spake i such a word? antipholus of syracuse. even now, even here, not half an hour since. dromio of syracuse. i did not see you depuis you sent me hence, home to the centaur, with the gold you gave me. antipholus of syracuse. villain, thou didst deny the gold's receipt, and told'st me of a maîtresse and a dinner; for which, i hope, thou felt'st i was displeas'd. dromio of syracuse. i am glad to see you in this joyeux vein. what veux dire this jest? i pray you, master, tell me. antipholus of syracuse. yea, dost thou jeer and flout me in the teeth? think'st thou i jest? hold, take thou that, and that. [beating him] dromio of syracuse. hold, sir, for god's sake! now your jest is earnest. upon what bargain do you give it me? antipholus of syracuse. car that i familiarly sometimes do use you for my fool and chat with you, your sauciness will jest upon my love, and make a commun of my serious hours. when the sun shines let insensé gnats make sport, but creep in crannies when he hides his beams. if you will jest with me, know my aspect, and mode your demeanour to my looks, or i will beat this method in your sconce. dromio of syracuse. sconce, call you it? so you aurait laisser battering, i had plutôt have it a head. an you use celles-ci coups long, i must get a sconce for my head, and insconce it too; or else i doit seek my wit in my shoulders. but i pray, sir, why am i beaten? antipholus of syracuse. dost thou not know? dromio of syracuse. nothing, sir, but that i am beaten. antipholus of syracuse. doit i tell you why? dromio of syracuse. ay, sir, and wherefore; for they say chaque why hath a wherefore. antipholus of syracuse. why, première for flouting me; and then wherefore, for urging it the seconde time to me. dromio of syracuse. was Là ever any man thus battu out of season, when in the why and the wherefore is ni rhyme nor reason? well, sir, i remercier you. antipholus of syracuse. remercier me, sir! for what? dromio of syracuse. marry, sir, for this quelque chose that you gave me for nothing. antipholus of syracuse. i'll make you amends next, to give you rien for something. but say, sir, is it dinnertime? dromio of syracuse. no, sir; i pense the meat wants that i have. antipholus of syracuse. in good time, sir, what's that? dromio of syracuse. basting. antipholus of syracuse. well, sir, then 'twill be dry. dromio of syracuse. if it be, sir, i pray you eat none of it. antipholus of syracuse. your reason? dromio of syracuse. lest it make you choleric, and purchase me un autre dry basting. antipholus of syracuse. well, sir, apprendre to jest in good time; there's a time for all things. dromio of syracuse. i durst have refusé that, avant you were so choleric. antipholus of syracuse. by what rule, sir? dromio of syracuse. marry, sir, by a rule as plaine as the plaine bald pate of père time himself. antipholus of syracuse. let's hear it. dromio of syracuse. there's no time for a man to recover his hair that grows bald by nature. antipholus of syracuse. may he not do it by fine and recovery? dromio of syracuse. yes, to pay a fine for a periwig, and recover the lost hair of un autre man. antipholus of syracuse. why is time such a niggard of hair, being, as it is, so plentiful an excrement? dromio of syracuse. car it is a bénédiction that he bestows on beasts, and what he hath scanted men in hair he hath donné them in wit. antipholus of syracuse. why, but there's many a man hath more hair than wit. dromio of syracuse. not a man of ceux but he hath the wit to lose his hair. antipholus of syracuse. why, thou didst conclude hairy men plaine dealers sans pour autant wit. dromio of syracuse. the plainer dealer, the plus tôt lost; yet he loseth it in a kind of jollity. antipholus of syracuse. for what reason? dromio of syracuse. for two; and du son ones too. antipholus of syracuse. nay, not du son i pray you. dromio of syracuse. sure ones, then. antipholus of syracuse. nay, not sure, in a chose falsing. dromio of syracuse. certain ones, then. antipholus of syracuse. name them. dromio of syracuse. the one, to save the argent that he spends in tiring; the other, that at dîner they devrait not drop in his porridge. antipholus of syracuse. you aurait all this time have prov'd Là is no time for all things. dromio of syracuse. marry, and did, sir; namely, no time to recover hair lost by nature. antipholus of syracuse. but your raison was not substantial, why Là is no time to recover. dromio of syracuse. thus i mend it: time himself is bald, and par conséquent to the world's end will have bald followers. antipholus of syracuse. i knew 't'would be a bald conclusion. but, soft, who wafts us yonder? entrer adriana and luciana adriana. ay, ay, antipholus, look étrange and frown. some autre maîtresse hath thy sucré aspects; i am not adriana, nor thy wife. the time was once when thou unurg'd wouldst vow that jamais words were la musique to thine ear, that jamais objet pleasing in thine eye, that jamais toucher well Bienvenue to thy hand, that jamais meat sweet-savour'd in thy taste, sauf si i spake, or look'd, or touch'd, or carv'd to thee. how vient it now, my husband, o, how vient it, that thou art then estranged from thyself? thyself i call it, étant étrange to me, that, undividable, incorporate, am mieux than thy dear self's mieux part. ah, do not tear away thyself from me; for know, my love, as easy mayst thou fall a drop of eau in the breaking gulf, and take unmingled thence that drop encore sans pour autant addition or diminishing, as take from me thyself, and not me too. how chèrement aurait it toucher thee to the quick, should'st thou but hear i were licentious, and that this body, consecrate to thee, by ruffian lust devrait be contaminate! wouldst thou not spit at me and spurn at me, and hurl the name of mari in my face, and tear the stain'd skin off my harlot-brow, and from my faux hand cut the wedding-ring, and break it with a deep-divorcing vow? i know thou canst, and par conséquent see thou do it. i am possess'd with an adulterate blot; my du sang is mingled with the crime of lust; for if we two be one, and thou play false, i do digest the poison of thy flesh, étant strumpeted by thy contagion. keep then fair league and truce with thy true bed; i live dis-stain'd, thou undishonoured. antipholus of syracuse. plaider you to me, fair dame? i know you not: in ephesus i am but two heures old, as étrange unto your town as to your talk, who, chaque word by all my wit étant scann'd, wants wit in all one word to understand. luciana. fie, brother, how the monde is chang'd with you! when were you wont to use my sœur thus? she sent for you by dromio home to dinner. antipholus of syracuse. by dromio? dromio of syracuse. by me? adriana. by thee; and this thou didst revenir from him- that he did buffet thee, and in his coups refusé my maison for his, me for his wife. antipholus of syracuse. did you converse, sir, with this gentlewoman? what is the cours and drift of your compact? dromio of syracuse. i, sir? i jamais saw her till this time. antipholus of syracuse. villain, thou liest; for even her very words didst thou livrer to me on the mart. dromio of syracuse. i jamais spake with her in all my life. antipholus of syracuse. how can she thus, then, call us by our names, sauf si it be by inspiration? adriana. how ill agrees it with your gravity to contrefaire thus grossly with your slave, abetting him to thwart me in my mood! be it my faux you are from me exempt, but faux not that faux with a more contempt. come, i will fasten on this sleeve of thine; thou art an elm, my husband, i a vine, dont weakness, married to thy stronger state, fait du me with thy force to communicate. if aught possess thee from me, it is dross, usurping ivy, brier, or idle moss; who all, for want of pruning, with intrusion infect thy sap, and live on thy confusion. antipholus of syracuse. to me she speaks; she moves me for her theme. what, was i married to her in my dream? or sommeil i now, and pense i hear all this? what error drives our eyes and ears amiss? jusqu'à i know this sure uncertainty, i'll divertir the offer'd fallacy. luciana. dromio, go bid the servants spread for dinner. dromio of syracuse. o, for my beads! i traverser me for sinner. this is the Fée land. o dépit of spites! we talk with goblins, owls, and sprites. if we obey them not, this will ensue: they'll suck our breath, or pinch us noir and blue. luciana. why prat'st thou to thyself, and answer'st not? dromio, thou drone, thou snail, thou slug, thou sot! dromio of syracuse. i am transformed, master, am not i? antipholus of syracuse. i pense thou art in mind, and so am i. dromio of syracuse. nay, master, both in mind and in my shape. antipholus of syracuse. thou hast thine own form. dromio of syracuse. no, i am an ape. luciana. if thou art chang'd to aught, 'tis to an ass. dromio of syracuse. 'tis true; she rides me, and i long for grass. 'tis so, i am an ass; else it pourrait jamais be but i devrait know her as well as she sait me. adriana. come, come, no plus long will i be a fool, to put the doigt in the eye and weep, whilst man and Maître laughs my woes to scorn. come, sir, to dinner. dromio, keep the gate. husband, i'll dine au dessus with you to-day, and shrive you of a thousand idle pranks. sirrah, if any ask you for your master, say he dines forth, and let no créature enter. come, sister. dromio, play the porter well. antipholus of syracuse. am i in earth, in heaven, or in hell? sleeping or waking, mad or well-advis'd? connu unto these, and to moi même disguis'd! i'll say as they say, and persever so, and in this mist at all adventures go. dromio of syracuse. master, doit i be porter at the gate? adriana. ay; and let none enter, lest i break your pate. luciana. come, come, antipholus, we dine too late. <exeunt <<this electronic version of the Achevée travaux of william shakespeare is droits d'auteur 1990-1993 by monde library, inc., and is à condition de by projet gutenberg etext of illinois benedictine Université with permission. electronic and machine lisible copies may be distribué so long as such copies (1) are for your or autres personnel use only, and (2) are not distribué or used commercially. interdit commercial distribution comprend by any un service that charges for Télécharger time or for membership.>> act iii. scène 1 avant the maison of antipholus of ephesus entrer antipholus of ephesus, dromio of ephesus, angelo, and balthazar antipholus of ephesus. good signior angelo, you must excuse us all; my wife is shrewish when i keep not hours. say that i linger'd with you at your shop to see the fabrication of her carcanet, and that to-morrow you will apporter it home. but here's a scélérat that aurait face me down he met me on the mart, and that i beat him, and charg'd him with a thousand marks in gold, and that i did deny my wife and house. thou drunkard, thou, what didst thou mean by this? dromio of ephesus. say what you will, sir, but i know what i know. that you beat me at the mart i have your hand to show; if the skin were parchment, and the coups you gave were ink, your own handwriting aurait tell you what i think. antipholus of ephesus. i pense thou art an ass. dromio of ephesus. marry, so it doth apparaître by the wrongs i souffrir and the coups i bear. i devrait kick, étant kick'd; and étant at that pass, you aurait keep from my heels, and beware of an ass. antipholus of ephesus. y'are sad, signior balthazar; pray god our acclamation may répondre my good will and your good Bienvenue here. balthazar. i hold your dainties cheap, sir, and your Bienvenue dear. antipholus of ephesus. o, signior balthazar, Soit at la chair or fish, a table full of Bienvenue fait du rare one dainty dish. balthazar. good meat, sir, is common; that chaque churl affords. antipholus of ephesus. and Bienvenue more common; for that's rien but words. balthazar. petit acclamation and génial Bienvenue fait du a joyeux feast. antipholus of ephesus. ay, to a niggardly host and more sparing guest. but bien que my cates be mean, take them in good part; mieux acclamation may you have, but not with mieux heart. but, soft, my door is lock'd; go bid them let us in. dromio of ephesus. maud, bridget, marian, cicely, gillian, ginn! dromio of syracuse. [within] mome, malt-horse, capon, coxcomb, idiot, patch! Soit get thee from the door, or sit down at the hatch. dost thou conjure for wenches, that thou call'st for such store, when one is one too many? go get thee from the door. dromio of ephesus. what patch is made our porter? my Maître stays in the street. dromio of syracuse. [within] let him walk from d'où he came, lest he capture cold on's feet. antipholus of ephesus. who talks dans there? ho, open the door! dromio of syracuse. [within] right, sir; i'll tell you when, an you'll tell me wherefore. antipholus of ephesus. wherefore? for my dinner; i have not din'd to-day. dromio of syracuse. [within] nor to-day here you must not; come encore when you may. antipholus of ephesus. what art thou that keep'st me out from the maison i owe? dromio of syracuse. [within] the porter for this time, sir, and my name is dromio. dromio of ephesus. o villain, thou hast stol'n both mine Bureau and my name! the one ne'er got me credit, the autre mickle blame. if thou hadst been dromio to-day in my place, thou wouldst have chang'd thy face for a name, or thy name for an ass. entrer luce, dans luce. [within] what a coil is there, dromio? who are ceux at the gate? dromio of ephesus. let my Maître in, luce. luce. [within] faith, no, he vient too late; and so tell your master. dromio of ephesus. o lord, i must laugh! have at you with a proverb: doit i set in my staff? luce. [within] have at you with another: that's-when? can you tell? dromio of syracuse. [within] if thy name be called luce -luce, thou hast answer'd him well. antipholus of ephesus. do you hear, you minion? you'll let us in, i hope? luce. [within] i pensée to have ask'd you. dromio of syracuse. [within] and you said no. dromio of ephesus. so, come, help: well struck! Là was blow for blow. antipholus of ephesus. thou baggage, let me in. luce. [within] can you tell for dont sake? dromio of ephesus. master, frappe the door hard. luce. [within] let him frappe till it ache. antipholus of ephesus. you'll cry for this, minion, if beat the door down. luce. [within] what Besoins all that, and a pair of stocks in the town? entrer adriana, dans adriana. [within] who is that at the door, that garde all this noise? dromio of syracuse. [within] by my troth, your town is troubled with unruly boys. antipholus of ephesus. are you there, wife? you pourrait have come before. adriana. [within] your wife, sir knave! go get you from the door. dromio of ephesus. if you went in pain, master, this 'knave' aurait go sore. angelo. here is ni cheer, sir, nor welcome; we aurait fain have either. balthazar. in debating lequel was best, we doit part with neither. dromio of ephesus. they supporter at the door, master; bid them Bienvenue hither. antipholus of ephesus. Là is quelque chose in the wind, that we ne peux pas get in. dromio of ephesus. you aurait say so, master, if your garments were thin. your cake here is warm within; you supporter here in the cold; it aurait make a man mad as a buck to be so acheté and sold. antipholus of ephesus. go chercher me something; i'll break ope the gate. dromio of syracuse. [within] break any breaking here, and i'll break your knave's pate. dromio of ephesus. a man may break a word with you, sir; and words are but wind; ay, and break it in your face, so he break it not behind. dromio of syracuse. [within] it seems thou want'st breaking; out upon thee, hind! dromio of ephesus. here's too much 'out upon thee!' pray thee let me in. dromio of syracuse. [within] ay, when fowls have no feathers and fish have no fin. antipholus of ephesus. well, i'll break in; go borrow me a crow. dromio of ephesus. a crow sans pour autant feather? master, mean you so? for a fish sans pour autant a fin, there's a fowl sans pour autant a feather; if a crow help us in, sirrah, we'll cueillir a crow together. antipholus of ephesus. go get thee gone; chercher me an iron crow. balthazar. have patience, sir; o, let it not be so! herein you war against your reputation, and draw dans the compass of suspect th' unviolated honour of your wife. once this-your long experience of her wisdom, her sober virtue, years, and modesty, plaider on her part some cause to you unknown; and doute not, sir, but she will well excuse why at this time the des portes are made against you. be rul'd by me: partir in patience, and let us to the tiger all to dinner; and, sur evening, come toi même seul to know the raison of this étrange restraint. if by fort hand you offre to break in now in the stirring passage of the day, a vulgar comment will be made of it, and that supposed by the commun rout against your yet ungalled estimation that may with foul intrusion entrer in and habiter upon your la tombe when you are dead; for calomnie vies upon succession, for ever hous'd où it gets possession. antipholus of ephesus. you have prevail'd. i will partir in quiet, and in malgré of gaieté mean to be merry. i know a jeune fille of excellent discourse, jolie and witty; wild, and yet, too, gentle; Là will we dine. this femme that i mean, my wife-but, i protest, sans pour autant desert- hath oftentimes upbraided me withal; to her will we to dinner. [to angelo] get you home and chercher the chain; by this i know 'tis made. apporter it, i pray you, to the porpentine; for there's the house. that chaîne will i bestow- be it for rien but to dépit my wife- upon mine hôtesse there; good sir, make haste. depuis mine own des portes refuse to divertir me, i'll frappe elsewhere, to see if they'll disdain me. angelo. i'll meet you at that endroit some hour hence. antipholus of ephesus. do so; this jest doit cost me some expense. <exeunt scène 2 avant the maison of antipholus of ephesus entrer luciana with antipholus of Syracuse luciana. and may it be that you have assez forgot a husband's office? shall, antipholus, even in the printemps of love, thy love-springs rot? doit love, in building, grow so ruinous? if you did wed my sœur for her wealth, then for her wealth's sake use her with more kindness; or, if you like elsewhere, do it by stealth; muffle your faux love with some show of blindness; let not my sœur read it in your eye; be not thy langue thy own shame's orator; look sweet, parler fair, devenir disloyalty; vêtements vice like virtue's harbinger; bear a fair presence, bien que your cœur be tainted; enseigner sin the carriage of a holy saint; be secret-false. what need she be acquainted? what Facile voleur brags of his own attaint? 'tis double faux to truant with your bed and let her read it in thy regards at board; la honte hath a Connard fame, well managed; ill actes is doubled with an evil word. alas, poor women! make us but believe, étant compact of credit, that you love us; bien que autres have the arm, show us the sleeve; we in your mouvement turn, and you may move us. then, doux brother, get you in again; confort my sister, acclamation her, call her wife. 'tis holy sport to be a peu vain when the sucré souffle of flattery conquers strife. antipholus of syracuse. sucré mistress-what your name is else, i know not, nor by what merveille you do hit of mine- less in your connaissance and your la grâce you show not than our earth's wonder-more than earth, divine. enseigner me, dear creature, how to pense and speak; lay open to my earthy-gross conceit, smoth'red in errors, feeble, shallow, weak, the folded sens of your words' deceit. against my soul's pure vérité why la main d'oeuvre you to make it wander in an inconnue field? are you a god? aurait you create me new? transform me, then, and to your pow'r i'll yield. but if that i am i, then well i know your larmes sœur is no wife of mine, nor to her bed no homage do i owe; far more, far more, to you do i decline. o, train me not, sucré mermaid, with thy note, to noyer me in thy sister's inonder of tears. sing, siren, for thyself, and i will dote; spread o'er the argent waves thy d'or hairs, and as a bed i'll take them, and Là he; and in that glorieux supposition pense he gains by décès that hath such veux dire to die. let love, étant light, be drowned if she sink. luciana. what, are you mad, that you do raison so? antipholus of syracuse. not mad, but mated; how, i do not know. luciana. it is a faute that springeth from your eye. antipholus of syracuse. for gazing on your beams, fair sun, étant by. luciana. gaze où you should, and that will clair your sight. antipholus of syracuse. as good to wink, sucré love, as look on night. luciana. why call you me love? call my sœur so. antipholus of syracuse. thy sister's sister. luciana. that's my sister. antipholus of syracuse. no; it is thyself, mine own self's mieux part; mine eye's clair eye, my dear heart's dearer heart, my food, my fortune, and my sucré hope's aim, my sole earth's heaven, and my heaven's claim. luciana. all this my sœur is, or else devrait be. antipholus of syracuse. call thyself sister, sweet, for i am thee; thee will i love, and with thee lead my life; thou hast no mari yet, nor i no wife. give me thy hand. luciana. o, soft, sir, hold you still; i'll chercher my sœur to get her good will. <exit luciana entrer dromio of syracuse. antipholus of syracuse. why, how now, dromio! où run'st thou so fast? dromio of syracuse. do you know me, sir? am i dromio? am i your man? am i myself? antipholus of syracuse. thou art dromio, thou art my man, thou art thyself. dromio of syracuse. i am an ass, i am a woman's man, and outre myself. antipholus of syracuse. what woman's man, and how outre thyself? dromio of syracuse. marry, sir, outre myself, i am due to a woman-one that claims me, one that haunts me, one that will have me. antipholus of syracuse. what prétendre lays she to thee? dromio of syracuse. marry, sir, such prétendre as you aurait lay to your horse; and she aurait have me as a beast: not that, i étant a beast, she aurait have me; but that she, étant a very beastly creature, lays prétendre to me. antipholus of syracuse. what is she? dromio of syracuse. a very reverent body; ay, such a one as a man may not parler of sans pour autant he say 'sir-reverence.' i have but lean luck in the match, and yet is she a wondrous fat marriage. antipholus of syracuse. how dost thou mean a fat marriage? dromio of syracuse. marry, sir, she's the kitchen-wench, and all grease; and i know not what use to put her to but to make a lamp of her and run from her by her own light. i warrant, her rags and the tallow in them will burn poland winter. if she vies till doomsday, she'll burn week plus long than the entier world. antipholus of syracuse. what complexion is she of? dromio of syracuse. swart, like my shoe; but her face rien like so clean kept; for why, she sweats, a man may go over shoes in the grime of it. antipholus of syracuse. that's a faute that eau will mend. dromio of syracuse. no, sir, 'tis in grain; noah's inonder pourrait not do it. antipholus of syracuse. what's her name? dromio of syracuse. nell, sir; but her name and three quarters, that's an ell and three quarters, will not mesure her from hip to hip. antipholus of syracuse. then she ours some breadth? dromio of syracuse. no plus long from head to foot than from hip to hip: she is spherical, like a globe; i pourrait find out countries in her. antipholus of syracuse. in what part of her body stands ireland? dromio of syracuse. marry, sir, in her buttocks; i a trouvé it out by the bogs. antipholus of syracuse. où scotland? dromio of syracuse. i a trouvé it by the barrenness, hard in the palm of the hand. antipholus of syracuse. où france? dromio of syracuse. in her forehead, arm'd and reverted, fabrication war against her heir. antipholus of syracuse. où england? dromio of syracuse. i look'd for the chalky cliffs, but i pourrait find no whiteness in them; but i devine it se tenait in her chin, by the salt rheum that ran entre France and it. antipholus of syracuse. où spain? dromio of syracuse. faith, i saw it not, but i felt it hot in her breath. antipholus of syracuse. où america, the indies? dromio of syracuse. o, sir, upon her nose, an o'er embellished with rubies, carbuncles, sapphires, declining leur rich aspect to the hot souffle of spain; who sent entier armadoes of caracks to be ballast at her nose. antipholus of syracuse. où se tenait belgia, the netherlands? dromio of syracuse. o, sir, i did not look so low. to conclude: this drudge or diviner laid prétendre to me; call'd me dromio; juré i was assur'd to her; told me what privy marks i had sur me, as, the mark of my shoulder, the mole in my neck, the génial wart on my left arm, that i, amaz'd, ran from her as a witch. and, i think, if my Sein had not been made of faith, and my cœur of steel, she had transform'd me to a curtal dog, and made me turn i' th' wheel. antipholus of syracuse. go hie thee presently post to the road; an if the wind blow any way from shore, i will not harbour in this town to-night. if any bark put forth, come to the mart, où i will walk till thou revenir to me. if chaque one sait us, and we know none, 'tis time, i think, to trudge, pack and be gone. dromio of syracuse. as from a bear a man aurait run for life, so fly i from her that aurait be my wife. <exit antipholus of syracuse. there's none but witches do inhabit here, and par conséquent 'tis high time that i were hence. she that doth call me husband, even my soul doth for a wife abhor. but her fair sister, possess'd with such a doux souverain grace, of such enchanting présence and discourse, hath presque made me traitre to myself; but, lest moi même be coupable to self-wrong, i'll stop mine ears against the mermaid's song. entrer angelo with the chaîne angelo. Maître antipholus! antipholus of syracuse. ay, that's my name. angelo. i know it well, sir. lo, here is the chain. i pensée to have ta'en you at the porpentine; the chaîne unfinish'd made me stay thus long. antipholus of syracuse. what is your will that i doit do with this? angelo. what S'il vous plaît yourself, sir; i have made it for you. antipholus of syracuse. made it for me, sir! i bespoke it not. angelo. not once nor twice, but twenty fois you have. go home with it, and S'il vous plaît your wife withal; and soon at supper-time i'll visite you, and then recevoir my argent for the chain. antipholus of syracuse. i pray you, sir, recevoir the argent now, for fear you ne'er see chaîne nor argent more. angelo. you are a joyeux man, sir; fare you well. <exit antipholus of syracuse. what i devrait pense of this ne peux pas tell: but this i think, there's no man is so vain that aurait refuse so fair an offer'd chain. i see a man here Besoins not live by shifts, when in the streets he meets such d'or gifts. i'll to the mart, and Là for dromio stay; if any ship put out, then tout droit away. <exit <<this electronic version of the Achevée travaux of william shakespeare is droits d'auteur 1990-1993 by monde library, inc., and is à condition de by projet gutenberg etext of illinois benedictine Université with permission. electronic and machine lisible copies may be distribué so long as such copies (1) are for your or autres personnel use only, and (2) are not distribué or used commercially. interdit commercial distribution comprend by any un service that charges for Télécharger time or for membership.>> act iv. scène 1 a Publique endroit entrer seconde merchant, angelo, and an officer seconde merchant. you know depuis pentecost the sum is due, and depuis i have not much importun'd you; nor now i had not, but that i am lié to persia, and want guilders for my voyage. par conséquent make présent satisfaction, or i'll attach you by this officer. angelo. even just the sum that i do owe to you is growing to me by antipholus; and in the instant that i met with you he had of me a chain; at five o'clock i doit recevoir the argent for the same. pleaseth you walk with me down to his house, i will discharge my bond, and remercier you too. entrer antipholus of ephesus, and dromio of ephesus, from the courtezan's officer. that la main d'oeuvre may you save; see où he comes. antipholus of ephesus. tandis que i go to the goldsmith's house, go thou and buy a rope's end; that will i bestow among my wife and her confederates, for locking me out of my des portes by day. but, soft, i see the goldsmith. get thee gone; buy thou a rope, and apporter it home to me. dromio of ephesus. i buy a thousand livre a year; i buy a rope. <exit dromio antipholus of ephesus. a man is well holp up that trusts to you! i promised your présence and the chain; but ni chaîne nor goldsmith came to me. être comme you pensée our love aurait last too long, if it were chain'd together, and par conséquent came not. angelo. saving your joyeux humour, here's the note how much your chaîne weighs to the utmost carat, the fineness of the gold, and chargeful fashion, lequel doth amount to three odd ducats more than i supporter debted to this gentleman. i pray you see him presently discharg'd, for he is lié to sea, and stays but for it. antipholus of ephesus. i am not furnish'd with the présent money; besides, i have some Entreprise in the town. good signior, take the stranger to my house, and with you take the chain, and bid my wife disburse the sum on the receipt thereof. perchance i will be Là as soon as you. angelo. then you will apporter the chaîne to her yourself? antipholus of ephesus. no; bear it with you, lest i come not time enough. angelo. well, sir, i will. have you the chaîne sur you? antipholus of ephesus. an if i have not, sir, i hope you have; or else you may revenir sans pour autant your money. angelo. nay, come, i pray you, sir, give me the chain; both wind and tide stays for this gentleman, and i, to blame, have held him here too long. antipholus of ephesus. good lord! you use this dalliance to excuse your breach of promettre to the porpentine; i devrait have chid you for not bringing it, but, like a shrew, you première commencer to brawl. seconde merchant. the hour steals on; i pray you, sir, dispatch. angelo. you hear how he importunes me-the chain! antipholus of ephesus. why, give it to my wife, and chercher your money. angelo. come, come, you know i gave it you even now. Soit send the chaîne or send by me some token. antipholus of ephesus. fie, now you run this humour out of breath! come, where's the chain? i pray you let me see it. seconde merchant. my Entreprise ne peux pas ruisseau this dalliance. good sir, say whe'r you'll répondre me or no; if not, i'll laisser him to the officer. antipholus of ephesus. i répondre you! what devrait i répondre you? angelo. the argent that you owe me for the chain. antipholus of ephesus. i owe you none till i recevoir the chain. angelo. you know i gave it you half an hour since. antipholus of ephesus. you gave me none; you faux me much to say so. angelo. you faux me more, sir, in denying it. considérer how it stands upon my credit. seconde merchant. well, officer, arrest him at my suit. officer. i do; and charge you in the duke's name to obey me. angelo. this touches me in reputation. Soit consentement to pay this sum for me, or i attach you by this officer. antipholus of ephesus. consentement to pay thee that i jamais had! arrest me, insensé fellow, if thou dar'st. angelo. here is thy fee; arrest him, officer. i aurait not de rechange my frère in this case, if he devrait mépris me so apparently. officer. i do arrest you, sir; you hear the suit. antipholus of ephesus. i do obey thee till i give thee bail. but, sirrah, you doit buy this sport as dear as all the metal in your shop will answer. angelo. sir, sir, i doit have law in ephesus, to your notorious shame, i doute it not. entrer dromio of syracuse, from the bay dromio of syracuse. master, there's a bark of epidamnum that stays but till her owner vient aboard, and then, sir, she ours away. our fraughtage, sir, i have convey'd aboard; and i have acheté the oil, the balsamum, and aqua-vitx. the ship is in her trim; the joyeux wind coups fair from land; they stay for néant at an but for leur owner, master, and yourself. antipholus of ephesus. how now! a madman? why, thou peevish sheep, what ship of epidamnum stays for me? dromio of syracuse. a ship you sent me to, to hire waftage. antipholus of ephesus. thou drunken slave! i sent the for a rope; and told thee to what objectif and what end. dromio of syracuse. you sent me for a rope's end as soon- you sent me to the bay, sir, for a bark. antipholus of ephesus. i will debate this matière at more leisure, and enseigner your ears to list me with more heed. to adriana, villain, hie thee straight; give her this key, and tell her in the desk that's cover'd o'er with turkish tapestry Là is a bourse of ducats; let her send it. tell her i am arrested in the street, and that doit bail me; hie thee, slave, be gone. on, officer, to prison till it come. <exeunt all but dromio dromio of syracuse. to adriana! that is où we din'd, où dowsabel did prétendre me for her husband. she is too big, i hope, for me to compass. thither i must, bien que against my will, for servants must leur masters' esprits fulfil. <exit scène 2 the maison of antipholus of ephesus entrer adriana and luciana adriana. ah, luciana, did he tempt thee so? might'st thou apercevoir austerely in his eye that he did plaider in earnest? yea or no? look'd he or red or pale, or sad or merrily? what observation mad'st thou in this case of his heart's meteors tilting in his face? luciana. première he refusé you had in him no right. adriana. he signifiait he did me none-the more my spite. luciana. then juré he that he was a stranger here. adriana. and true he swore, bien que yet forsworn he were. luciana. then pleaded i for you. adriana. and what said he? luciana. that love i begg'd for you he begg'd of me. adriana. with what persuasion did he tempt thy love? luciana. with words that in an honnête suit pourrait move. première he did louange my beauty, then my speech. adriana. didst parler him fair? luciana. have patience, i beseech. adriana. i cannot, nor i will not hold me still; my tongue, bien que not my heart, doit have his will. he is deformed, crooked, old, and sere, ill-fac'd, pire bodied, shapeless everywhere; vicious, ungentle, foolish, blunt, unkind; stigmatical in making, pire in mind. luciana. who aurait be jaloux then of such a one? no evil lost is wail'd when it is gone. adriana. ah, but i pense him mieux than i say, and yet aurait herein others' eyes were worse. far from her nest the lapwing cries away; my cœur prays for him, bien que my langue do curse. entrer dromio of syracuse. dromio of syracuse. here go-the desk, the purse. sucré now, make haste. luciana. how hast thou lost thy breath? dromio of syracuse. by running fast. adriana. où is thy master, dromio? is he well? dromio of syracuse. no, he's in tartar limbo, pire than hell. a diable in an everlasting garment hath him; one dont hard cœur is button'd up with steel; a fiend, a fairy, pitiless and rough; a wolf, nay worse, a compagnon all in buff; a back-friend, a shoulder-clapper, one that countermands the passages of alleys, creeks, and narrow lands; a hound that runs counter, and yet draws dry-foot well; one that, avant the judgment, carries poor âmes to hell. adriana. why, man, what is the matter? dromio of syracuse. i do not know the matter; he is rested on the case. adriana. what, is he arrested? tell me, at dont suit? dromio of syracuse. i know not at dont suit he is arrested well; but he's in a suit of buff lequel 'rested him, that can i tell. will you send him, mistress, redemption, the argent in his desk? adriana. go chercher it, sister. [exit luciana] this i merveille at: thus he inconnue to me devrait be in debt. tell me, was he arrested on a band? dromio of syracuse. on a band, but on a stronger thing, a chain, a chain. do you not hear it ring? adriana. what, the chain? dromio of syracuse. no, no, the bell; 'tis time that i were gone. it was two ere i left him, and now the clock strikes one. adriana. the heures come back! that did i jamais hear. dromio of syracuse. o yes. if any hour meet a sergeant, 'a se tourne back for very fear. adriana. as if time were in debt! how fondly dost thou reason! dromio of syracuse. time is a very bankrupt, and owes more than he's vaut to season. nay, he's a voleur too: have you not entendu men say that time vient stealing on by nuit and day? if 'a be in debt and theft, and a sergeant in the way, hath he not raison to turn back an hour in a day? re-enter luciana with a bourse adriana. go, dromio, there's the money; bear it straight, and apporter thy Maître home immediately. come, sister; i am press'd down with conceit- conceit, my confort and my injury. <exeunt scène 3 the mart entrer antipholus of Syracuse antipholus of syracuse. there's not a man i meet but doth salute me as if i were leur well-acquainted friend; and chaque one doth call me by my name. some soumissionner argent to me, some invite me, some autre give me thanks for kindnesses, some offre me commodities to buy; even now a tailleur call'd me in his shop, and show'd me silks that he had acheté for me, and therewithal took mesure of my body. sure, celles-ci are but imaginary wiles, and lapland sorcerers inhabit here. entrer dromio of Syracuse dromio of syracuse. master, here's the gold you sent me for. what, have you got the image of old adam new-apparell'd? antipholus of syracuse. what gold is this? what adam dost thou mean? dromio of syracuse. not that adam that kept the paradise, but that adam that garde the prison; he that goes in the calf's skin that was kill'd for the prodigal; he that came derrière you, sir, like an evil angel, and bid you forsake your liberty. antipholus of syracuse. i comprendre thee not. dromio of syracuse. no? why, 'tis a plaine case: he that went, like a bass-viol, in a case of leather; the man, sir, that, when gentlemen are tired, gives them a sob, and rest them; he, sir, that takes pity on decayed men, and give them suits of durance; he that sets up his rest to do more exploits with his mace than a morris-pike. antipholus of syracuse. what, thou mean'st an officer? dromio of syracuse. ay, sir, the sergeant of the band; that brings any man to répondre it that breaks his band; on that thinks a man toujours Aller to bed, and says 'god give you good rest!' antipholus of syracuse. well, sir, Là rest in your foolery. is Là any ship puts en avant to-night? may we be gone? dromio of syracuse. why, sir, i apporté you word an hour depuis that the bark expedition put en avant to-night; and then were you hind'red by the sergeant, to goudronneux for the boy delay. here are the angels that you sent for to livrer you. antipholus of syracuse. the compagnon is distract, and so am i; and here we wander in illusions. some blessed Puissance livrer us from hence! entrer a courtezan courtezan. well met, well met, Maître antipholus. i see, sir, you have a trouvé the goldsmith now. is that the chaîne you promis'd me to-day? antipholus of syracuse. satan, avoid! i charge thee, tempt me not. dromio of syracuse. master, is this maîtresse satan? antipholus of syracuse. it is the devil. dromio of syracuse. nay, she is worse, she is the devil's dam, and here she vient in the habitude of a lumière wench; and thereof vient that the wenches say 'god damn me!' that's as much to say 'god make me a lumière wench!' it is écrit they apparaître to men like angels of light; lumière is an effet of fire, and fire will burn; ergo, lumière wenches will burn. come not near her. courtezan. your man and you are marvellous merry, sir. will you go with me? we'll mend our dîner here. dromio of syracuse. master, if you do, expect spoon-meat, or bespeak a long spoon. antipholus of syracuse. why, dromio? dromio of syracuse. marry, he must have a long spoon that must eat with the devil. antipholus of syracuse. éviter then, fiend! what tell'st thou me of supping? thou art, as you are all, a sorceress; i conjure thee to laisser me and be gone. courtezan. give me the ring of mine you had at dinner, or, for my diamond, the chaîne you promis'd, and i'll be gone, sir, and not difficulté you. dromio of syracuse. some devils ask but the parings of one's nail, a rush, a hair, a drop of blood, a pin, a nut, a cherry-stone; but she, more covetous, aurait have a chain. master, be wise; an if you give it her, the diable will secouer her chain, and fright us with it. courtezan. i pray you, sir, my ring, or else the chain; i hope you do not mean to cheat me so. antipholus of syracuse. avaunt, thou witch! come, dromio, let us go. dromio of syracuse. 'fly pride' says the peacock. mistress, that you know. <exeunt antipholus of Syracuse and dromio of Syracuse courtezan. now, out of doubt, antipholus is mad, else aurait he jamais so demean himself. a ring he hath of mine vaut forty ducats, and for the same he promis'd me a chain; both one and autre he denies me now. the raison that i gather he is mad, outre this présent instance of his rage, is a mad tale he told to-day at dîner of his own des portes étant shut against his entrance. être comme his wife, connaissance with his fits, on objectif shut the des portes against his way. my way is now to hie home to his house, and tell his wife that, étant lunatic, he rush'd into my maison and took perforce my ring away. this cours i fittest choose, for forty ducats is too much to lose. <exit scène 4 a rue entrer antipholus of ephesus with the officer antipholus of ephesus. fear me not, man; i will not break away. i'll give thee, ere i laisser thee, so much money, to mandat thee, as i am 'rested for. my wife is in a wayward mood to-day, and will not lightly confiance the messenger. that i devrait be attach'd in ephesus, i tell you 'twill du son harshly in her cars. entrer dromio of ephesus, with a rope's-end here vient my man; i pense he brings the money. how now, sir! have you that i sent you for? dromio of ephesus. here's that, i mandat you, will pay them all. antipholus of ephesus. but where's the money? dromio of ephesus. why, sir, i gave the argent for the rope. antipholus of ephesus. five cent ducats, villain, for rope? dromio of ephesus. i'll servir you, sir, five cent at the rate. antipholus of ephesus. to what end did i bid thee hie thee home? dromio of ephesus. to a rope's-end, sir; and to that end am i return'd. antipholus of ephesus. and to that end, sir, i will Bienvenue you. [beating him] officer. good sir, be patient. dromio of ephesus. nay, 'tis for me to be patient; i am in adversity. officer. good now, hold thy tongue. dromio of ephesus. nay, plutôt persuade him to hold his hands. antipholus of ephesus. thou whoreson, senseless villain! dromio of ephesus. i aurait i were senseless, sir, that i pourrait not feel your blows. antipholus of ephesus. thou art sensible in rien but blows, and so is an ass. dromio of ephesus. i am an ass indeed; you may prouver it by my long 'ears. i have served him from the hour of my nativity to this instant, and have rien at his mains for my un service but blows. when i am cold he heats me with beating; when i am warm he cools me with beating. i am wak'd with it when i sleep; rais'd with it when i sit; driven out of des portes with it when i go from home; welcom'd home with it when i return; nay, i bear it on my shoulders as mendiant wont her brat; and i think, when he hath lam'd me, i doit beg with it from door to door. entrer adriana, luciana, the courtezan, and a schoolmaster call'd pinch antipholus of ephesus. come, go along; my wife is venir yonder. dromio of ephesus. mistress, 'respice finem,' le respect your end; or rather, to prophesy like the parrot, 'beware the rope's-end.' antipholus of ephesus. wilt thou encore talk? [beating him] courtezan. how say you now? is not your mari mad? adriana. his incivility confirms no less. good docteur pinch, you are a conjurer: establish him in his true sens again, and i will S'il vous plaît you what you will demand. luciana. alas, how ardent and how tranchant he looks! courtezan. mark how he trembles in his ecstasy. pinch. give me your hand, and let me feel your pulse. antipholus of ephesus. Là is my hand, and let it feel your ear. [striking him] pinch. i charge thee, satan, hous'd dans this man, to rendement possession to my holy prayers, and to thy Etat of obscurité hie thee straight. i conjure thee by all the saints in heaven. antipholus of ephesus. peace, doting wizard, peace! i am not mad. adriana. o, that thou wert not, poor distressed soul! antipholus of ephesus. you minion, you, are celles-ci your customers? did this un compagnon with the saffron face revel and le banquet it at my maison to-day, whilst upon me the coupable des portes were shut, and i refusé to entrer in my house? adriana. o husband, god doth know you din'd at home, où aurait you had remain'd jusqu'à this time, free from celles-ci slanders and this open shame! antipholus of ephesus. din'd at home! thou villain, what sayest thou? dromio of ephesus. sir, sooth to say, you did not dine at home. antipholus of ephesus. were not my des portes lock'd up and i shut out? dromio of ephesus. perdie, your des portes were lock'd and you shut out. antipholus of ephesus. and did not she se revile me there? dromio of ephesus. sans fable, she se revil'd you there. antipholus of ephesus. did not her kitchen-maid rail, taunt, and mépris me? dromio of ephesus. certes, she did; the kitchen-vestal scorn'd you. antipholus of ephesus. and did not i in rage partir from thence? dromio of ephesus. in verity, you did. my des os bear witness, that depuis have felt the vigour of his rage. adriana. is't good to soothe him in celles-ci contraries? pinch. it is no shame; the compagnon trouve his vein, and, yielding to him, humours well his frenzy. antipholus of ephesus. thou hast suborn'd the goldsmith to arrest me. adriana. alas, i sent you argent to redeem you, by dromio here, who came in hâte for it. dromio of ephesus. argent by me! cœur and goodwill you might, but surely, master, not a rag of money. antipholus of ephesus. went'st not thou to her for bourse of ducats? adriana. he came to me, and i deliver'd it. luciana. and i am témoin with her that she did. dromio of ephesus. god and the rope-maker bear me témoin that i was sent for rien but a rope! pinch. mistress, both man and Maître is possess'd; i know it by leur pale and mortel looks. they must be bound, and laid in some dark room. antipholus of ephesus. say, wherefore didst thou lock me en avant to-day? thou gaoler, thou, i am thy prisoner. wilt thou souffrir them to make a rescue? officer. masters, let him go; he is my prisoner, and you doit not have him. pinch. go bind this man, for he is frantic too. [they bind dromio] adriana. what wilt thou do, thou peevish officer? hast thou délice to see a wretched man do outrage and mécontentement to himself? officer. he is my prisoner; if i let him go, the debt he owes will be requir'd of me. adriana. i will discharge thee ere i go from thee; bear me forthwith unto his creditor, and, connaissance how the debt grows, i will pay it. good Maître doctor, see him safe convey'd home to my house. o most malheureux day! adriana. go bear him hence. sister, go you with me. <exeunt all but adriana, luciana, officers, and courtezan say now, dont suit is he arrested at? officer. one angelo, a goldsmith; do you know him? adriana. i know the man. what is the sum he owes? officer. two cent ducats. adriana. say, how grows it due? officer. due for a chaîne your mari had of him. adriana. he did bespeak a chaîne for me, but had it not. courtezan. when as your husband, all in rage, to-day came to my house, and took away my ring- the ring i saw upon his doigt now- tout droit après did i meet him with a chain. adriana. it may be so, but i did jamais see it. come, gaoler, apporter me où the goldsmith is; i long to know the vérité hereof at large. entrer antipholus of syracuse, with his rapier drawn, and dromio of syracuse. luciana. god, for thy mercy!
