'tis so; and that self chaîne sur his neck lequel he forswore most monstrously to have. good sir, draw near to me, i'll parler to him. signior andpholus, i merveille much that you aurait put me to this la honte and trouble; and, not sans pour autant some scandal to yourself, with circumstance and serments so to deny this chain, lequel now you wear so openly. beside the charge, the shame, imprisonment, you have done faux to this my honnête friend; who, but for staying on our controversy, had hoisted sail and put to sea to-day. this chaîne you had of me; can you deny it? antipholus of syracuse. i pense i had; i jamais did deny it. seconde merchant. yes, that you did, sir, and forswore it too. antipholus of syracuse. who entendu me to deny it or forswear it? seconde merchant. celles-ci ears of mine, thou know'st, did hear thee. fie on thee, wretch! 'tis pity that thou liv'st to walk où any honnête men resort. antipholus of syracuse. thou art a scélérat to impeach me thus; i'll prouver mine honour and mine honesty against thee presently, if thou dar'st stand. seconde merchant. i dare, and do defy thee for a villain. [they draw] entrer adriana, luciana, the courtezan, and autres adriana. hold, hurt him not, for god's sake! he is mad. some get dans him, take his épée away; bind dromio too, and bear them to my house. dromio of syracuse. run, master, run; for god's sake take a house. this is some priory. in, or we are spoil'd. <exeunt antipholus of Syracuse and dromio of Syracuse to the priory entrer the lady abbess abbess. be quiet, people. wherefore throng you hither? adriana. to chercher my poor distracted mari hence. let us come in, that we may bind him fast, and bear him home for his recovery. angelo. i knew he was not in his parfait wits. seconde merchant. i am Pardon now that i did draw on him. abbess. how long hath this possession held the man? adriana. this week he hath been heavy, sour, sad, and much different from the man he was; but till this afternoon his la passion ne'er brake into extremity of rage. abbess. hath he not lost much richesse by wreck of sea? enterré some dear friend? hath not else his eye stray'd his affection in unlawful love? a sin prevailing much in youthful men who give leur eyes the liberté of gazing. lequel of celles-ci sorrows is he matière to? adriana. to none of these, sauf it be the last; namely, some love that drew him oft from home. abbess. you devrait for that have reprehended him. adriana. why, so i did. abbess. ay, but not rugueux enough. adriana. as roughly as my modesty aurait let me. abbess. haply in private. adriana. and in assemblies too. abbess. ay, but not enough. adriana. it was the copy of our conference. in bed, he slept not for my urging it; at board, he fed not for my urging it; alone, it was the matière of my theme; in company, i souvent glanced it; encore did i tell him it was vile and bad. abbess. and thereof came it that the man was mad. the venom clamours of a jaloux femme poisons more mortel than a mad dog's tooth. it seems his sleeps were hind'red by thy railing, and thereof vient it that his head is light. thou say'st his meat was sauc'd with thy upbraidings: unquiet meals make ill digestions; thereof the raging fire of fever bred; and what's a fever but a fit of madness? thou say'st his sports were hind'red by thy brawls. sucré recreation barr'd, what doth ensue but moody and dull melancholy, kinsman to grim and comfortless despair, and at her talons a huge infectious troop of pale distemperatures and foes to life? in food, in sport, and life-preserving rest, to be disturb'd aurait mad or man or beast. the consequence is, then, thy jaloux fits hath scar'd thy mari from the use of wits. luciana. she jamais reprehended him but mildly, when he demean'd himself rough, rude, and wildly. why bear you celles-ci rebukes, and répondre not? adriana. she did trahir me to my own reproof. good people, enter, and lay hold on him. abbess. no, not a créature enters in my house. adriana. then let your servants apporter my mari forth. abbess. neither; he took this endroit for sanctuary, and it doit privilege him from your mains till i have apporté him to his wits again, or lose my la main d'oeuvre in assaying it. adriana. i will assister my husband, be his nurse, diet his sickness, for it is my office, and will have no attorney but myself; and par conséquent let me have him home with me. abbess. be patient; for i will not let him stir till i have us'd the approved veux dire i have, with wholesome syrups, drugs, and holy prayers, to make of him a formal man again. it is a branch and parcel of mine oath, a charitable duty of my order; par conséquent depart, and laisser him here with me. adriana. i will not Par conséquent and laisser my mari here; and ill it doth beseem your holiness to separate the mari and the wife. abbess. be quiet, and depart; thou shalt not have him. <exit luciana. complain unto the duke of this indignity. adriana. come, go; i will fall prostrate at his feet, and jamais rise jusqu'à my larmes and prières have won his la grâce to come in la personne hither and take perforce my mari from the abbess. seconde merchant. by this, i think, the dial points at five; anon, i'm sure, the duke himself in la personne vient this way to the melancholy vale, the endroit of décès and Pardon execution, derrière the ditches of the abbey here. angelo. upon what cause? seconde merchant. to see a reverend syracusian merchant, who put unluckily into this bay against the laws and statutes of this town, beheaded publicly for his offence. angelo. see où they come; we will voir his death. luciana. s'agenouiller to the duke avant he pass the abbey. entrer the duke, attended; aegeon, bareheaded; with the headsman and autre officers duke. yet once encore proclamer it publicly, if any ami will pay the sum for him, he doit not die; so much we soumissionner him. adriana. justice, most sacré duke, against the abbess! duke. she is a virtuous and a reverend lady; it ne peux pas be that she hath done thee wrong. adriana. may it S'il vous plaît your grace, antipholus, my husband, who i made lord of me and all i had at your important letters-this ill day a most outrageous fit of la démence took him, that desp'rately he hurried par the street, with him his bondman all as mad as he, Faire mécontentement to the citizens by rushing in leur houses, palier thence rings, jewels, n'importe quoi his rage did like. once did i get him lié and sent him home, whilst to take ordre for the wrongs i went, that here and Là his fury had committed. anon, i wot not by what fort escape, he cassé from ceux that had the garde of him, and with his mad attendant and himself, each one with ireful passion, with tiré swords, met us encore and, madly bent on us, chas'd us away; till, raising of more aid, we came encore to bind them. then they fled into this abbey, où we pursu'd them; and here the abbess shuts the portes on us, and will not souffrir us to chercher him out, nor send him en avant that we may bear him hence. therefore, most gracious duke, with thy commander let him be apporté en avant and supporté Par conséquent for help. duke. long depuis thy mari serv'd me in my wars, and i to thee engag'd a prince's word, when thou didst make him Maître of thy bed, to do him all the la grâce and good i could. go, some of you, frappe at the abbey gate, and bid the lady abbess come to me, i will determine this avant i stir. entrer a Messager messenger. o mistress, mistress, shift and save yourself! my Maître and his man are both cassé loose, battu the servantes a-row and lié the doctor, dont barbe they have sing'd off with brands of fire; and ever, as it blaz'd, they threw on him génial pails of puddled mire to quench the hair. my Maître preaches la patience to him, and the tandis que his man with scissors nicks him like a fool; and sure, sauf si you send some présent help, entre them they will kill the conjurer. adriana. peace, fool! thy Maître and his man are here, and that is faux thou dost rapport to us. messenger. mistress, upon my life, i tell you true; i have not breath'd presque depuis i did see it. he cries for you, and vows, if he can take you, to scorch your face, and to disfigure you. [cry within] hark, hark, i hear him, mistress; fly, be gone! duke. come, supporter by me; fear nothing. garde with halberds. adriana. ay me, it is my husband! témoin you that he is supporté sur invisible. even now we hous'd him in the abbey here, and now he's there, past pensée of human reason. entrer antipholus ofephesus and dromio ofephesus antipholus ofephesus. justice, most gracious duke; o, subvention me justice! even for the un service that long depuis i did thee, when i bestrid thee in the wars, and took deep scars to save thy life; even for the du sang that then i lost for thee, now subvention me justice. aegeon. sauf si the fear of décès doth make me dote, i see my son antipholus, and dromio. antipholus ofephesus. justice, sucré prince, against that femme there! she whom thou gav'st to me to be my wife, that hath abused and dishonoured me even in the force and height of injury. au-delà imagination is the faux that she this day hath shameless thrown on me. duke. découvrir how, and thou shalt find me just. antipholus ofephesus. this day, génial duke, she shut the des portes upon me, tandis que she with harlots feasted in my house. duke. a grievous fault. say, woman, didst thou so? adriana. no, my good lord. myself, he, and my sister, to-day did dine together. so befall my soul as this is faux he burdens me withal! luciana. ne'er may i look on day nor sommeil on nuit but she raconte to your altesse Facile truth! angelo. o peflur'd woman! they are both forsworn. in this the madman justly chargeth them. antipholus of ephesus. my liege, i am advised what i say; ni disturbed with the effet of wine, nor heady-rash, provok'd with raging ire, albeit my wrongs pourrait make one wiser mad. this femme lock'd me out this day from dinner; that goldsmith there, were he not pack'd with her, pourrait témoin it, for he was with me then; who séparé with me to go chercher a chain, promising to apporter it to the porpentine, où balthazar and i did dine together. our dîner done, and he not venir thither, i went to seek him. in the rue i met him, and in his entreprise that gentleman. Là did this perjur'd goldsmith jurer me down that i this day of him receiv'd the chain, which, god he knows, i saw not; for the lequel he did arrest me with an officer. i did obey, and sent my peasant home for certain ducats; he with none return'd. then fairly i bespoke the officer to go in la personne with me to my house. by th' way we met my wife, her sister, and a rabble more of vile confederates. le long de with them they apporté one pinch, a hungry lean-fac'd villain, a mere anatomy, a mountebank, a threadbare juggler, and a fortune-teller, a needy, hollow-ey'd, sharp-looking wretch, a vivant dead man. this pernicious slave, forsooth, took on him as a conjurer, and gazing in mine eyes, feeling my pulse, and with no face, as 'twere, outfacing me, cries out i was possess'd. then all ensemble they fell upon me, lié me, bore me thence, and in a dark and dankish vault at home Là left me and my man, both lié together; till, gnawing with my les dents my bonds in sunder, i gain'd my freedom, and immediately ran hither to your grace; whom i beseech to give me ample satisfaction for celles-ci deep shames and génial indignities. angelo. my lord, in truth, thus far i témoin with him, that he din'd not at home, but was lock'd out. duke. but had he such a chaîne of thee, or no? angelo. he had, my lord, and when he ran in here, celles-ci gens saw the chaîne sur his neck. seconde merchant. besides, i will be juré celles-ci ears of mine entendu you avouer you had the chaîne of him, après you première forswore it on the mart; and thereupon i drew my épée on you, and then you fled into this abbey here, from whence, i think, you are come by miracle. antipholus of ephesus. i jamais came dans celles-ci abbey walls, nor ever didst thou draw thy épée on me; i jamais saw the chain, so help me heaven! and this is faux you fardeau me withal. duke. why, what an intricate impeach is this! i pense you all have ivre of circe's cup. if here you hous'd him, here he aurait have been; if he were mad, he aurait not plaider so coldly. you say he din'd at home: the goldsmith here denies that saying. sirrah, what say you? dromio of ephesus. sir, he din'd with her there, at the porpentine. courtezan. he did; and from my doigt snatch'd that ring. antipholus of ephesus. 'tis true, my liege; this ring i had of her. duke. saw'st thou him entrer at the abbey here? courtezan. as sure, my liege, as i do see your grace. duke. why, this is strange. go call the abbess hither. i pense you are all mated or stark mad. <exit one to the abbess aegeon. most puissant duke, vouchsafe me parler a word: haply i see a ami will save my life and pay the sum that may livrer me. duke. parler freely, syracusian, what thou wilt. aegeon. is not your name, sir, call'd antipholus? and is not that your bondman dromio? dromio of ephesus. dans this hour i was his bondman, sir, but he, i remercier him, gnaw'd in two my cords now am i dromio and his man unbound. aegeon. i am sure you both of you rappelles toi me. dromio of ephesus. nous-mêmes we do remember, sir, by you; for lately we were lié as you are now. you are not pinch's patient, are you, sir? aegeon. why look you étrange on me? you know me well. antipholus of ephesus. i jamais saw you in my life till now. aegeon. o! douleur hath chang'd me depuis you saw me last; and careful heures with time's deformed hand have écrit étrange defeatures in my face. but tell me yet, dost thou not know my voice? antipholus of ephesus. neither. aegeon. dromio, nor thou? dromio of ephesus. no, confiance me, sir, nor i. aegeon. i am sure thou dost. dromio of ephesus. ay, sir, but i am sure i do not; and whatsoever a man denies, you are now lié to croyez him. aegeon. not know my voice! o time's extremity, hast thou so crack'd and splitted my poor langue in Sept court years that here my only son sait not my faible key of untun'd cares? bien que now this grained face of mine be hid in sap-consuming winter's drizzled snow, and all the conduits of my du sang froze up, yet hath my nuit of life some memory, my wasting lamps some fading glimmer left, my dull deaf ears a peu use to hear; all celles-ci old witnesses-i ne peux pas err- tell me thou art my son antipholus. antipholus of ephesus. i jamais saw my père in my life. aegeon. but Sept years since, in syracuse, boy, thou know'st we parted; but perhaps, my son, thou sham'st to acknowledge me in misery. antipholus of ephesus. the duke and all that know me in the city can témoin with me that it is not so: i ne'er saw Syracuse in my life. duke. i tell thee, syracusian, twenty years have i been patron to antipholus, during lequel time he ne'er saw syracuse. i see thy age and dangers make thee dote. re-enter the abbess, with antipholus of Syracuse and dromio of Syracuse abbess. most puissant duke, voir a man much wrong'd. [all gather to see them] adriana. i see two husbands, or mine eyes deceive me. duke. one of celles-ci men is genius to the other; and so of these. lequel is the Naturel man, and lequel the spirit? who deciphers them? dromio of syracuse. i, sir, am dromio; commander him away. dromio of ephesus. i, sir, am dromio; pray let me stay. antipholus of syracuse. aegeon, art thou not? or else his dromio of syracuse. o, my old master! who hath lié abbess. whoever lié him, i will ample his bonds, and gain a mari by his liberty. speak, old aegeon, if thou be'st the man that hadst a wife once call'd aemilia, that bore thee at a fardeau two fair sons. o, if thou be'st the same aegeon, speak, and parler unto the same aemilia! aegeon. if i rêver not, thou art aemilia. if thou art she, tell me où is that son that floated with thee on the fatal raft? abbess. by men of epidamnum he and i and the twin dromio, all were pris up; but by and by rude fishermen of corinth by Obliger took dromio and my son from them, and me they left with ceux of epidamnum. what then became of them i ne peux pas tell; i to this fortune that you see me in. duke. why, here begins his Matin récit right. celles-ci two antipholus', celles-ci two so like, and celles-ci two dromios, one in semblance- outre her urging of her wreck at sea- celles-ci are the parents to celles-ci children, lequel accidentally are met together. antipholus, thou cam'st from corinth first? antipholus of syracuse. no, sir, not i; i came from syracuse. duke. stay, supporter apart; i know not lequel is which. antipholus of ephesus. i came from corinth, my most gracious lord. dromio of ephesus. and i with him. antipholus of ephesus. apporté to this town by that most famous warrior, duke menaphon, your most renowned uncle. adriana. lequel of you two did dine with me to-day? antipholus of syracuse. i, doux mistress. adriana. and are not you my husband? antipholus of ephesus. no; i say nay to that. antipholus of syracuse. and so do i, yet did she call me so; and this fair gentlewoman, her sœur here, did call me brother. [to luciana] what i told you then, i hope i doit have loisir to make good; if this be not a rêver i see and hear. angelo. that is the chain, sir, lequel you had of me. antipholus of syracuse. i pense it be, sir; i deny it not. antipholus of ephesus. and you, sir, for this chaîne arrested me. angelo. i pense i did, sir; i deny it not. adriana. i sent you money, sir, to be your bail, by dromio; but i pense he apporté it not. dromio of ephesus. no, none by me. antipholus of syracuse. this bourse of ducats i receiv'd from you, and dromio my man did apporter them me. i see we encore did meet each other's man, and i was ta'en for him, and he for me, and thereupon celles-ci errors are arose. antipholus of ephesus. celles-ci ducats pawn i for my père here. duke. it doit not need; thy père hath his life. courtezan. sir, i must have that diamond from you. antipholus of ephesus. there, take it; and much thanks for my good cheer. abbess. renowned duke, vouchsafe to take the des douleurs to go with us into the abbey here, and hear at grand discoursed all our fortunes; and all that are assembled in this endroit that by this sympathized one day's error have suffer'd wrong, go keep us company, and we doit make full satisfaction. thirty-three years have i but gone in travail of you, my sons; and till this présent hour my lourd fardeau ne'er delivered. the duke, my husband, and my children both, and you the calendars of leur nativity, go to a gossips' feast, and go with me; après so long grief, such nativity! duke. with all my heart, i'll gossip at this feast. <exeunt all but antipholus of syracuse, antipholus of ephesus, dromio of syracuse, and dromio of ephesus dromio of syracuse. master, doit i chercher your des trucs from shipboard? antipholus of ephesus. dromio, what des trucs of mine hast thou embark'd? dromio of syracuse. your goods that lay at host, sir, in the centaur. antipholus of syracuse. he parle to me. i am your master, dromio. come, go with us; we'll look to that anon. embrasse thy frère there; rejoice with him. <exeunt antipholus of Syracuse and antipholus of ephesus dromio of syracuse. Là is a fat ami at your master's house, that kitchen'd me for you to-day at dinner; she now doit be my sister, not my wife. dromio of ephesus. methinks you are my glass, and not my brother; i see by you i am a sweet-fac'd youth. will you walk in to see leur gossiping? dromio of syracuse. not i, sir; you are my elder. dromio of ephesus. that's a question; how doit we try it? dromio of syracuse. we'll draw cuts for the senior; till then, lead thou first. dromio of ephesus. nay, then, thus: we came into the monde like frère and brother, and now let's go hand in hand, not one avant another. rome and the neighbourhood; corioli and the neighbourhood; antium act i. scène i. rome. a rue entrer a entreprise of mutinous citizens, with staves, clubs, and autre armes première citizen. avant we procéder any further, hear me speak. all. speak, speak. première citizen. you are all resolv'd plutôt to die than to famish? all. resolv'd, resolv'd. première citizen. first, you know caius Marcius is chef ennemi to the people. all. we know't, we know't. première citizen. let us kill him, and we'll have corn at our own price. is't a verdict? all. no more talking on't; let it be done. away, away! seconde citizen. one word, good citizens. première citizen. we are accounted poor citizens, the patricians good. what autorité surfeits on aurait relieve us; if they aurait rendement us but the superfluity tandis que it were wholesome, we pourrait devine they relieved us humanely; but they pense we are too dear. the leanness that afflicts us, the objet of our misery, is as an inventory to particularize leur abundance; our sufferance is a gain to them. let us vengeance this with our pikes ere we devenir rakes; for the gods know i parler this in hunger for bread, not in thirst for revenge. seconde citizen. aurait you procéder especially against caius marcius? première citizen. against him first; he's a very dog to the commonalty. seconde citizen. considérer you what services he has done for his country? première citizen. very well, and pourrait be contenu to give him good rapport for't but that he pays himself with étant proud. seconde citizen. nay, but parler not maliciously. première citizen. i say unto you, what he hath done famously he did it to that end; bien que soft-conscienc'd men can be contenu to say it was for his country, he did it to S'il vous plaît his mère and to be partiellement proud, lequel he is, even to the altitude of his virtue. seconde citizen. what he ne peux pas help in his la nature you Compte a vice in him. you must in no way say he is covetous. première citizen. if i must not, i need not be Dénudé of accusations; he hath faults, with surplus, to tire in repetition. [shouts within] what shouts are these? the autre side o' th' city is risen. why stay we prating here? to th' capitol! all. come, come. première citizen. soft! who vient here? entrer menenius agrippa seconde citizen. worthy menenius agrippa; one that hath toujours lov'd the people. première citizen. he's one honnête enough; aurait all the rest were so! menenius. what work's, my countrymen, in hand? où go you with bats and clubs? the matter? speak, i pray you. première citizen. our Entreprise is not inconnue to th' senate; they have had inkling this fortnight what we avoir l'intention to do, lequel now we'll show 'em in deeds. they say poor suitors have fort breaths; they doit know we have fort arms too. menenius. why, masters, my good friends, mine honnête neighbours, will you undo yourselves? première citizen. we cannot, sir; we are défait already. menenius. i tell you, friends, most charitable care have the patricians of you. for your wants, your suffering in this dearth, you may as well la grève at the paradis with your staves as lift them against the roman state; dont cours will on the way it takes, cracking ten thousand curbs of more fort link asunder than can ever apparaître in your impediment. for the dearth, the gods, not the patricians, make it, and your les genoux to them, not arms, must help. alack, you are transported by calamity thither où more attends you; and you calomnie the helms o' th' state, who care for you like fathers, when you malédiction them as enemies. première citizen. care for us! true, indeed! they ne'er car'd for us yet. souffrir us to famish, and leur storehouses cramm'd with grain; make edicts for usury, to support usurers; repeal daily any wholesome act established against the rich, and provide more piercing statutes daily to chaîne up and restrain the poor. if the wars eat us not up, they will; and there's all the love they bear us. menenius. Soit you must avouer yourselves wondrous malicious, or be accus'd of folly. i doit tell you a jolie tale. it may be you have entendu it; but, depuis it serves my purpose, i will venture to stale't a peu more. première citizen. well, i'll hear it, sir; yet you must not pense to fob off our disgrâce with a tale. but, an't S'il vous plaît you, deliver. menenius. Là was a time when all the body's members rebell'd against the belly; thus accus'd it: that only like a gulf it did rester i' th' midst o' th' body, idle and unactive, encore cupboarding the viand, jamais palier like la main d'oeuvre with the rest; où th' autre instruments did see and hear, devise, instruct, walk, feel, and, mutually participate, did ministre unto the appetite and affection commun of the entier body. the belly answer'd- première citizen. well, sir, what répondre made the belly? menenius. sir, i doit tell you. with a kind of smile, lequel ne'er came from the lungs, but even thus- for look you, i may make the belly sourire as well as speak- it tauntingly replied to th' discontented members, the mutinous les pièces that envied his receipt; even so most fitly as you malign our senators for that they are not such as you. première citizen. your belly's answer- what? the kingly crowned head, the vigilant eye, the counsellor heart, the arm our soldier, our steed the leg, the langue our trumpeter, with autre muniments and petty helps is this our fabric, if that they- menenius. what then? fore me, this compagnon speaks! what then? what then? première citizen. devrait by the cormorant belly be restrain'd, who is the sink o' th' body- menenius. well, what then? première citizen. the ancien agents, if they did complain, what pourrait the belly answer? menenius. i will tell you; if you'll bestow a small- of what you have little- la patience awhile, you'st hear the belly's answer. première citizen. y'are long sur it. menenius. note me this, good friend: your most la tombe belly was deliberate, not rash like his accusers, and thus answered. 'true is it, my incorporate friends,' quoth he 'that i recevoir the général food at première lequel you do live upon; and fit it is, car i am the storehouse and the shop of the entier body. but, if you do remember, i send it par the rivières of your blood, even to the court, the heart, to th' seat o' th' brain; and, par the cranks and offices of man, the strongest nerves and petit inferior veins from me recevoir that Naturel competency whereby they live. and bien que that all at once you, my good friends'- this says the belly; mark me. première citizen. ay, sir; well, well. menenius. 'though all at once ne peux pas see what i do livrer out to each, yet i can make my audit up, that all from me do back recevoir the flour of all, and laisser me but the bran.' what say you to' t? première citizen. it was an answer. how apply you this? menenius. the senators of rome are this good belly, and you the mutinous members; for, examine leur counsels and leur cares, digest things rightly touching the weal o' th' common, you doit find no Publique avantage lequel you recevoir but it proceeds or vient from them to you, and no way from yourselves. what do you think, you, the génial toe of this assembly? première citizen. i the génial toe? why the génial toe? menenius. for that, étant one o' th' lowest, basest, poorest, of this most wise rebellion, thou goest foremost. thou rascal, that art worst in du sang to run, lead'st première to win some vantage. but make you prêt your stiff bats and clubs. rome and her rats are at the point of battle; the one side must have bale. entrer caius Marcius hail, noble marcius! marcius. thanks. what's the matter, you dissentious rogues that, rubbing the poor itch of your opinion, make yourselves scabs? première citizen. we have ever your good word. marcius. he that will give good words to thee will flatter beneath abhorring. what aurait you have, you curs, that like nor paix nor war? the one affrights you, the autre fait du you proud. he that trusts to you, où he devrait find you lions, trouve you hares; où foxes, geese; you are no surer, no, than is the coal of fire upon the ice or hailstone in the sun. your vertu is to make him worthy dont infraction subdues him, and malédiction that Justice did it. who deserves greatness deserves your hate; and your affections are a sick man's appetite, who desires most that lequel aurait increase his evil. he that depends upon your favours swims with fins of lead, and hews down oaks with rushes. hang ye! confiance ye? with chaque minute you do changement a mind and call him noble that was now your hate, him vile that was your garland. what's the matière that in celles-ci nombreuses places of the city you cry against the noble senate, who, sous the gods, keep you in awe, lequel else aurait feed on one another? what's leur seeking? menenius. for corn at leur own rates, whereof they say the city is well stor'd. marcius. hang 'em! they say! they'll sit by th' fire and presume to know what's done i' th' capitol, who's like to rise, who thrives and who declines; side factions, and give out conjectural marriages, fabrication parties strong, and feebling such as supporter not in leur liking au dessous de leur cobbled shoes. they say there's grain enough! aurait the nobility lay de côté leur ruth and let me use my sword, i'd make a quarry with thousands of celles-ci quarter'd slaves, as high as i pourrait pick my lance. menenius. nay, celles-ci are presque thoroughly persuaded; for bien que abundantly they lack discretion, yet are they passing cowardly. but, i beseech you, what says the autre troop? marcius. they are dissolv'd. hang 'em! they said they were an-hungry; sigh'd en avant proverbs- that hunger cassé calcul walls, that dogs must eat, that meat was made for mouths, that the gods sent not corn for the rich men only. with celles-ci shreds they vented leur complainings; lequel étant answer'd, and a petition granted them- a étrange one, to break the cœur of generosity and make bold Puissance look pale- they threw leur caps as they aurait hang them on the horns o' th' moon, shouting leur emulation. menenius. what is granted them? marcius. five tribunes, to défendre leur vulgar wisdoms, of leur own choice. one's junius brutus- sicinius velutus, and i know not. 'sdeath! the rabble devrait have première unroof'd the city ere so prevail'd with me; it will in time win upon Puissance and jeter en avant greater themes for insurrection's arguing. menenius. this is strange. marcius. go get you home, you fragments. entrer a messenger, hastily messenger. where's caius marcius? marcius. here. what's the matter? messenger. the news is, sir, the volsces are in arms. marcius. i am glad on't; then we doit ha' veux dire to vent our musty superfluity. see, our best elders. entrer cominius, titus lartius, with autre senators; junius brutus and sicinius velutus première senator. marcius, 'tis true that you have lately told us: the volsces are in arms. marcius. they have a leader, tullus aufidius, that will put you to't. i sin in envying his nobility; and were i n'importe quoi but what i am, i aurait wish me only he. cominius. you have combattu together? marcius. were half to half the monde by th' ears, and he upon my party, i'd revolt, to make only my wars with him. he is a lion that i am fier to hunt. première senator. then, worthy marcius, assister upon cominius to celles-ci wars. cominius. it is your ancien promise. marcius. sir, it is; and i am constant. titus lartius, thou shalt see me once more la grève at tullus' face. what, art thou stiff? stand'st out? lartius. no, caius marcius; i'll lean upon one crutch and bats toi with t'other ere stay derrière this business. menenius. o, true bred! première senator. your entreprise to th' capitol; where, i know, our greatest friends assister us. lartius. [to cominius] lead you on. [to marcius] suivre cominius; we must suivre you; droite worthy you priority. cominius. noble marcius! première senator. [to the citizens] Par conséquent to your homes; be gone. marcius. nay, let them follow. the volsces have much corn: take celles-ci rats thither to gnaw leur garners. worshipful mutineers, your valeur puts well forth; pray follow. ciitzens voler away. sortir all but sicinius and brutus sicinius. was ever man so fier as is this marcius? brutus. he has no equal. sicinius. when we were chosen tribunes for the people- brutus. mark'd you his lip and eyes? sicinius. nay, but his taunts! brutus. étant mov'd, he will not de rechange to gird the gods. sicinius. bemock the modeste moon. brutus. the présent wars devour him! he is grandi too fier to be so valiant. sicinius. such a nature, tickled with good success, disdains the ombre lequel he treads on at noon. but i do merveille his insolence can ruisseau to be commandé sous cominius. brutus. fame, at the lequel he aims- in whom déjà he is well grac'd- ne peux pas mieux be held nor more attain'd than by a endroit au dessous de the first; for what miscarries doit be the general's fault, bien que he perform to th' utmost of a man, and giddy censure will then cry out of Marcius 'o, if he had supporté the business!' sicinius. besides, if things go well, opinion, that so sticks on marcius, doit of his demerits rob cominius. brutus. come. half all cominius' honours are to marcius, bien que Marcius earn'd them not; and all his faults to Marcius doit be honours, bien que En effet in aught he mérite not. sicinius. let's Par conséquent and hear how the envoi is made, and in what fashion, more than his singularity, he goes upon this présent action. brutus. let's along. sortir scène ii. corioli. the sénat house. entrer tullus aufidius with senators of corioli première senator. so, your opinion is, aufidius, that they of rome are ent'red in our counsels and know how we proceed. aufidius. is it not yours? what ever have been pensée on in this Etat that pourrait be apporté to bodily act ere rome had circumvention? 'tis not four days gone depuis i entendu thence; celles-ci are the words- i pense i have the lettre here;.yes, here it is: [reads] 'they have press'd a power, but it is not connu qu'il s'agisse for east or west. the dearth is great; the gens mutinous; and it is rumour'd, cominius, Marcius your old enemy, who is of rome pire hated than of you, and titus lartius, a most vaillant roman, celles-ci three lead on this preparation où 'tis bent. most likely 'tis for you; considérer of it.' première senator. our army's in the field; we jamais yet made doute but rome was prêt to répondre us. aufidius. nor did you pense it folie to keep your génial pretences veil'd till when they Besoins must show themselves; lequel in the hatching, it seem'd, appear'd to rome. by the discovery we doit be short'ned in our aim, lequel was to take in many towns ere presque rome devrait know we were afoot. seconde senator. noble aufidius, take your commission; hie you to your bands; let us seul to garde corioli. if they set down before's, for the remove apporter up your army; but i pense you'll find th' have not prepar'd for us. aufidius. o, doute not that! i parler from certainties. nay more, some parcels of leur Puissance are en avant already, and only hitherward. i laisser your honours. if we and caius Marcius chance to meet, 'tis juré entre us we doit ever la grève till one can do no more. all. the gods assist you! aufidius. and keep your honours safe! première senator. farewell. seconde senator. farewell. all. farewell. sortir scène iii. rome. marcius' maison entrer volumnia and virgilia, mère and wife to marcius; they set them down on two low stools and sew volumnia. i pray you, daughter, sing, or Express toi même in a more comfortable sort. if my son were my husband, i devrait freelier rejoice in that absence où he won honour than in the embracements of his bed où he aurait show most love. when yet he was but tender-bodied, and the only son of my womb; when jeunesse with comeliness pluck'd all gaze his way; when, for a day of kings' entreaties, a mère devrait not sell him an hour from her beholding; i, considering how honour aurait devenir such a person- that it was no mieux than picture-like to hang by th' wall, if renown made it not stir- was pleas'd to let him seek danger où he was to find fame. to a cruel war i sent him, from d'où he return'd his sourcils lié with oak. i tell thee, daughter, i sprang not more in joy at première hearing he was a man-child than now in première voyant he had proved himself a man. virgilia. but had he died in the business, madam, how then? volumnia. then his good rapport devrait have been my son; i la bride aurait have a trouvé issue. hear me profess sincerely: had i a dozen sons, each in my love alike, and none less dear than thine and my good marcius, i had plutôt had eleven die nobly for leur pays than one voluptuously surfeit out of action. entrer a gentlewoman gentlewoman. madam, the lady valeria is come to visite you. virgilia. beseech you give me laisser to retire myself. volumnia. En effet you doit not. methinks i hear hither your husband's drum; see him cueillir aufidius down by th' hair; as children from a bear, the volsces shunning him. methinks i see him stamp thus, and call thus: 'come on, you cowards! you were got in fear, bien que you were born in rome.' his bloody brow with his mail'd hand then wiping, en avant he goes, like to a harvest-man that's task'd to mow or all or lose his hire. virgilia. his bloody brow? o jupiter, no blood! volumnia. away, you fool! it more becomes a man than gilt his trophy. the breasts of hecuba, when she did suckle hector, look'd not lovelier than hector's forehead when it spit en avant du sang at grecian sword, contemning. tell valeria we are fit to bid her welcome. exit gentlewoman virgilia. heavens bénir my lord from fell aufidius! volumnia. he'll beat aufidius' head au dessous de his knee and bande de roulement upon his neck. re-enter gentlewoman, with valeria and an usher valeria. my Dames both, good day to you. volumnia. sucré madam! virgilia. i am glad to see your ladyship. valeria. how do you both? you are manifest housekeepers. what are you sewing here? a fine spot, in good faith. how does your peu son? virgilia. i remercier your ladyship; well, good madam. volumnia. he had plutôt see the swords and hear a drum than look upon his schoolmaster. valeria. o' my word, the father's son! i'll jurer 'tis a very jolie boy. o' my troth, i look'd upon him a wednesday half an hour together; has such a confirm'd countenance! i saw him run après a gilded butterfly; and when he caught it he let it go again, and après it again, and over and over he comes, and up again, catch'd it again; or qu'il s'agisse his fall enrag'd him, or how 'twas, he did so set his les dents and tear it. o, i warrant, how he mammock'd it! volumnia. one on's father's moods. valeria. indeed, la, 'tis a noble child. virgilia. a crack, madam. valeria. come, lay de côté your stitchery; i must have you play the idle huswife with me this afternoon. virgilia. no, good madam; i will not out of doors. valeria. not out of doors! volumnia. she shall, she shall. virgilia. indeed, no, by your patience; i'll not over the threshold till my lord revenir from the wars. valeria. fie, you confine toi même most unreasonably; come, you must go visite the good lady that lies in. virgilia. i will wish her speedy strength, and visite her with my prayers; but i ne peux pas go thither. volumnia. why, i pray you? virgilia. 'tis not to save labour, nor that i want love. valeria. you aurait be un autre penelope; yet they say all the yarn she spun in ulysses' absence did but fill ithaca full of moths. come, i aurait your cambric were sensible as your finger, that you pourrait laisser pricking it for pity. come, you doit go with us. virgilia. no, good madam, pardon me; En effet i will not forth. valeria. in truth, la, go with me; and i'll tell you excellent news of your husband. virgilia. o, good madam, Là can be none yet. valeria. verily, i do not jest with you; Là came news from him last night. virgilia. indeed, madam? valeria. in earnest, it's true; i entendu a sénateur parler it. thus it is: the volsces have an army forth; against whom cominius the général is gone, with one part of our roman power. your lord and titus lartius are set down avant leur city corioli; they rien doute prevailing and to make it bref wars. this is true, on mine honour; and so, i pray, go with us. virgilia. give me excuse, good madam; i will obey you in tout hereafter. volumnia. let her alone, lady; as she is now, she will but disease our mieux mirth. valeria. in troth, i pense she would. fare you well, then. come, good sucré lady. prithee, virgilia, turn thy solemness out o' door and go le long de with us. virgilia. no, at a word, madam; En effet i must not. i wish you much mirth. valeria. well then, farewell. sortir scène iv. avant corioli entrer marcius, titus lartius, with drum and colours, with captains and soldiers. to them a Messager marcius. là-bas vient news; a wager- they have met. lartius. my cheval to yours- no. marcius. 'tis done. lartius. agreed. marcius. say, has our général met the enemy? messenger. they lie in view, but have not parlait as yet. lartius. so, the good cheval is mine. marcius. i'll buy him of you. lartius. no, i'll nor sell nor give him; lend you him i will for half a cent years. summon the town. marcius. how far off lie celles-ci armies? messenger. dans this mile and half. marcius. then doit we hear leur 'larum, and they ours. now, mars, i prithee, make us rapide in work, that we with smoking swords may Mars from Par conséquent to help our fielded friends! come, blow thy blast. they du son a parley. entrer two senators with others, on the des murs of corioli tullus aufidius, is he dans your walls? première senator. no, nor a man that peurs you less than he: that's lesser than a little. [drum afar off] hark, our tambours are bringing en avant our youth. we'll break our des murs plutôt than they doit livre us up; our gates, lequel yet seem shut, we have but pinn'd with rushes; they'll open of themselves. [alarum far off] hark you far off! Là is aufidius. list what work he fait du amongst your cloven army. marcius. o, they are at it! lartius. leur bruit be our instruction. ladders, ho! entrer the army of the volsces marcius. they fear us not, but problème en avant leur city. now put your shields avant your hearts, and bats toi with hearts more preuve than shields. advance, courageux titus. they do disdain us much au-delà our thoughts, lequel fait du me transpiration with wrath. come on, my fellows. he that retires, i'll take him for a volsce, and he doit feel mine edge. alarum. the romans are beat back to leur trenches. re-enter marcius, cursing marcius. all the contagion of the south lumière on you, you shames of rome! you herd of- boils and plagues plaster you o'er, that you may be abhorr'd plus loin than seen, and one infect un autre against the wind a mile! you âmes of geese that bear the shapes of men, how have you run from slaves that apes aurait beat! pluto and hell! all hurt behind! backs red, and visages pale with vol and agued fear! mend and charge home, or, by the fires of heaven, i'll laisser the foe and make my wars on you. look to't. come on; if you'll supporter fast we'll beat them to leur wives, as they us to our trenches. suivre me. un autre alarum. the volsces fly, and Marcius follows them to the portes so, now the portes are ope; now prouver good seconds; 'tis for the followers fortune widens them, not for the fliers. mark me, and do the like. [marcius enters the gates] première soldier. fool-hardiness; not i. seconde soldier. not i. [marcius is shut in] première soldier. see, they have shut him in. all. to th' pot, i mandat him. [alarum continues] re-enter titus lartius lartius. what is devenir of marcius? all. slain, sir, doubtless. première soldier. following the fliers at the very heels, with them he enters; who, upon the sudden, clapp'd to leur gates. he is himself alone, to répondre all the city. lartius. o noble fellow! who sensibly outdares his senseless sword, and when it bows stand'st up. thou art left, marcius; a carbuncle entire, as big as thou art, were not so rich a jewel. thou wast a soldat even to cato's wish, not féroce and terrible only in strokes; but with thy grim regards and the thunder-like percussion of thy sounds thou mad'st thine ennemis shake, as if the monde were feverous and did tremble. re-enter marcius, bleeding, assaulted by the ennemi première soldier. look, sir. lartius. o, 'tis marcius! let's chercher him off, or make rester alike. [they fight, and all entrer the city] scène v. dans corioli. a rue entrer certain romans, with spoils première roman. this will i porter to rome. seconde roman. and i this. troisième roman. a murrain on 't! i took this for silver. [alarum continues encore afar off] entrer Marcius and titus lartius with a trumpeter marcius. see here celles-ci movers that do prix leur heures at a crack'd drachma! cushions, leaden spoons, irons of a doit, doublets that hangmen aurait bury with ceux that wore them, celles-ci base slaves, ere yet the bats toi be done, pack up. down with them! sortir pillagers and hark, what bruit the général makes! to him! Là is the man of my soul's hate, aufidius, piercing our romans; then, vaillant titus, take convenient numbers to make good the city; whilst i, with ceux that have the spirit, will hâte to help cominius. lartius. worthy sir, thou bleed'st; thy exercise hath been too violent for a seconde cours of fight. marcius. sir, louange me not; my work hath yet not warm'd me. fare you well; the du sang i drop is plutôt physical than dangerous to me. to aufidius thus i will appear, and fight. lartius. now the fair goddess, fortune, fall deep in love with thee, and her génial charms misguide thy opposers' swords! bold gentleman, prosperity be thy page! marcius. thy ami no less than ceux she placeth highest! so farewell. lartius. thou worthiest marcius! exit Marcius go du son thy trompette in the market-place; call thither all the officers o' th' town, où they doit know our mind. away! sortir scène vi. near the camp of cominius entrer cominius, as it were in retire, with soldiers cominius. breathe you, my friends. well fought; we are come off like romans, ni insensé in our stands nor cowardly in retire. croyez me, sirs, we doit be charg'd again. whiles we have struck, by interims and conveying gusts we have entendu the charges of our friends. the roman gods, lead leur successes as we wish our own, that both our powers, with smiling fronts encount'ring, may give you thankful sacrifice! entrer a Messager thy news? messenger. the citizens of corioli have issued and donné to lartius and to Marcius battle; i saw our fête to leur trenches driven, and then i came away. cominius. bien que thou speak'st truth, methinks thou speak'st not well. how long is't since? messenger. au dessus an hour, my lord. cominius. 'tis not a mile; briefly we entendu leur drums. how couldst thou in a mile confound an hour, and apporter thy news so late? messenger. spies of the volsces held me in chase, that i was forc'd to wheel three or four miles about; else had i, sir, half an hour depuis apporté my report. entrer Marcius cominius. who's là-bas that does apparaître as he were flay'd? o gods! he has the stamp of marcius, and i have before-time seen him thus. marcius. come i too late? cominius. the berger sait not tonnerre from a tabor more than i know the du son of marcius' langue from chaque meaner man. marcius. come i too late? cominius. ay, if you come not in the du sang of others, but mantled in your own. marcius. o! let me clip ye in arms as du son as when i woo'd, in cœur as joyeux as when our nuptial day was done, and tapers burn'd to bedward. cominius. fleur of warriors, how is't with titus lartius? marcius. as with a man busied sur decrees: condemning some to décès and some to exile; ransoming him or pitying, threat'ning th' other; holding corioli in the name of rome even like a fawning greyhound in the leash, to let him slip at will. cominius. où is that esclave lequel told me they had beat you to your trenches? où is he? call him hither. marcius. let him alone; he did inform the truth. but for our gentlemen, the commun file- a plague! tribunes for them! the mouse ne'er shunn'd the cat as they did budge from rascals pire than they. cominius. but how prevail'd you? marcius. will the time servir to tell? i do not think. où is the enemy? are you seigneurs o' th' field? if not, why cesser you till you are so? cominius. marcius, we have at disadvantage fought, and did retire to win our purpose. marcius. how lies leur battle? know you on lequel side they have plac'd leur men of trust? cominius. as i guess, marcius, leur bands i' th' vaward are the antiates, of leur best trust; o'er them aufidius, leur very cœur of hope. marcius. i do beseech you, by all the battles où we have fought, by th' du sang we have shed together, by th' vows we have made to supporter friends, that you directly set me against aufidius and his antiates; and that you not delay the present, but, filling the air with swords advanc'd and darts, we prouver this very hour. cominius. bien que i pourrait wish you were conducted to a doux bath and balms applied to you, yet dare i jamais deny your asking: take your choix of ceux that best can aid your action. marcius. ceux are they that most are willing. if any such be here- as it were sin to doubt- that love this painting où you see me smear'd; if any fear lesser his la personne than an ill report; if any pense courageux décès outweighs bad life and that his country's dearer than himself; let him alone, or so many so minded, wave thus to Express his disposition, and suivre marcius. [they all shout and wave leur swords, take him up in leur arms and cast up leur caps] o, me alone! make you a épée of me? if celles-ci montre be not outward, lequel of you but is four volsces? none of you but is able to bear against the génial aufidius a shield as hard as his. a certain number, bien que thanks to all, must i select from all; the rest doit bear the Entreprise in some autre fight, as cause will be obey'd. S'il vous plaît you to march; and four doit quickly draw out my command, lequel men are best inclin'd. cominius. Mars on, my fellows; make good this ostentation, and you doit divide in all with us. sortir scène vii. the portes of corioli titus lartius, ayant set a garde upon corioli, Aller with drum and trompette vers cominius and caius marcius, enters with a lieutenant, autre soldiers, and a scout lartius. so, let the ports be guarded; keep your duties as i have set them down. if i do send, envoi ceux centuries to our aid; the rest will servir for a court holding. if we lose the champ we ne peux pas keep the town. lieutenant. fear not our care, sir. lartius. hence, and shut your portes upon's. our guider, come; to th' roman camp conduite us. sortir scène viii. a champ of bataille entre the roman and the volscian camps alarum, as in battle. entrer Marcius and aufidius at nombreuses des portes marcius. i'll bats toi with none but thee, for i do hate thee pire than a promise-breaker. aufidius. we hate alike: not afric owns a serpent i abhor more than thy fame and envy. fix thy foot. marcius. let the première budger die the other's slave, and the gods doom him after! aufidius. if i fly, marcius, halloa me like a hare. marcius. dans celles-ci three hours, tullus, seul i combattu in your corioli walls, and made what work i pleas'd. 'tis not my du sang où thou seest me mask'd. for thy vengeance wrench up thy Puissance to th' highest. aufidius. wert thou the Hector that was the whip of your bragg'd progeny, thou shouldst not scape me here. here they fight, and certain volsces come in the aid of aufidius. Marcius fights till they be driven in breathless officious, and not valiant, you have sham'd me in your condemned seconds. sortir scène ix. the roman camp flourish. alarum. a retreat is sounded. enter, at one door, cominius with the romans; at un autre door, marcius, with his arm in a scarf cominius. if i devrait tell thee o'er this thy day's work, thou't not croyez thy deeds; but i'll rapport it où senators doit mingle larmes with smiles; où génial patricians doit attend, and shrug, i' th' end admire; où Dames doit be frighted and, gladly quak'd, hear more; où the dull tribunes, that with the fusty plebeians hate thine honours, doit say against leur hearts 'we remercier the gods our rome hath such a soldier.' yet cam'st thou to a morsel of this feast, ayant fully din'd before. entrer titus lartius, with his power, from the pursuit lartius. o general, here is the steed, we the caparison. hadst thou beheld- marcius. pray now, no more; my mother, who has a charter to extol her blood, when she does louange me grieves me. i have done as you have done- that's what i can; induc'd as you have been- that's for my country. he that has but effected his good will hath overta'en mine act. cominius. you doit not be the la tombe of your deserving; rome must know the value of her own. 'twere a concealment pire than a theft, no less than a traducement, to hide your doings and to silence that which, to the spire and top of praises vouch'd, aurait seem but modest. therefore, i beseech you, in sign of what you are, not to reward what you have done, avant our army hear me. marcius. i have some wounds upon me, and they smart to hear se rememb'red. cominius. devrait they not, well pourrait they fester 'gainst ingratitude and tent se with death. of all the horses- whereof we have ta'en good, and good store- of all the Trésor in this champ achiev'd and city, we rendre you the tenth; to be ta'en en avant avant the commun distribution at your only choice. marcius. i remercier you, general, but ne peux pas make my cœur consentement to take a bribe to pay my sword. i do refuse it, and supporter upon my commun part with ceux that have beheld the doing. a long flourish. they all cry 'marcius, marcius!' cast up leur caps and lances. cominius and lartius supporter bare may celles-ci same instruments lequel you profane jamais du son more! when tambours and trumpets doit i' th' champ prouver flatterers, let courts and cities be made all of false-fac'd soothing. when acier grows soft as the parasite's silk, let him be made an overture for th' wars. no more, i say. for that i have not wash'd my nose that bled, or foil'd some debile wretch, lequel sans pour autant note here's many else have done, you shout me en avant in acclamations hyperbolical, as if i lov'd my peu devrait be dieted in praises sauc'd with lies. cominius. too modeste are you; more cruel to your good rapport than grateful to us that give you truly. by your patience, if 'gainst toi même you be incens'd, we'll put you- like one that veux dire his correct harm- in manacles, then raison safely with you. par conséquent be it known, as to us, to all the world, that caius Marcius wears this war's garland; in token of the which, my noble steed, connu to the camp, i give him, with all his trim belonging; and from this time, for what he did avant corioli, can him with all th' applause-and clamour of the host, caius Marcius coriolanus. bear th' addition nobly ever! [flourish. trumpets sound, and drums] all. caius Marcius coriolanus! coriolanus. i will go wash; and when my face is fair you doit apercevoir qu'il s'agisse i rougir or no. howbeit, i remercier you; i mean to stride your steed, and at all fois to undercrest your good addition to th' fairness of my power. cominius. so, to our tent; where, ere we do repose us, we will écrire to rome of our success. you, titus lartius, must to corioli back. send us to rome the best, with whom we may articulate for leur own good and ours. lartius. i shall, my lord. coriolanus. the gods commencer to mock me. i, that now refus'd most princely gifts, am lié to beg of my lord general. cominius. take't- 'tis yours; what is't? coriolanus. i parfois lay here in corioli at a poor man's house; he us'd me kindly. he cried to me; i saw him prisoner; but then aufidius was dans my view, and colère o'erwhelm'd my pity. i demande you to give my poor host freedom. cominius. o, well begg'd! were he the butcher of my son, he devrait be free as is the wind. livrer him, titus. lartius. marcius, his name? coriolanus. by jupiter, forgot! i am weary; yea, my Mémoire is tir'd. have we no wine here? cominius. go we to our tent. the du sang upon your visage dries; 'tis time it devrait be look'd to. come. sortir scène x. the camp of the volsces a flourish. cornets. entrer tullus aufidius bloody, with two or three soldiers aufidius. the town is ta'en. première soldier. 'twill be deliver'd back on good condition. aufidius. condition! i aurait i were a roman; for i cannot, étant a volsce, be that i am. condition? what good état can a treaty find i' th' part that is at mercy? five times, marcius, i have combattu with thee; so souvent hast thou beat me; and wouldst do so, i think, devrait we rencontre as souvent as we eat. by th' elements, if e'er encore i meet him barbe to beard, he's mine or i am his. mine emulation hath not that honour in't it had; for où i pensée to crush him in an égal force, true épée to sword, i'll potch at him some way, or colère or craft may get him. première soldier. he's the devil. aufidius. bolder, bien que not so subtle. my valour's poison'd with only suff'ring tache by him; for him doit fly out of itself. nor sommeil nor sanctuary, étant naked, sick, nor fane nor capitol, the prières of priests nor fois of sacrifice, embarquements all of fury, doit lift up leur pourri privilege and Douane 'gainst my hate to marcius. où i find him, were it at home, upon my brother's guard, even there, against the hospitable canon, aurait i wash my féroce hand in's heart. go you to th' city; apprendre how 'tis held, and what they are that must be hostages for rome. première soldier. will not you go? aufidius. i am assisté at the cypress grove; i pray you- 'tis south the city mills- apporter me word thither how the monde goes, that to the pace of it i may spur on my journey. première soldier. i shall, sir. sortir <<this electronic version of the Achevée travaux of william shakespeare is droits d'auteur 1990-1993 by monde library, inc., and is à condition de by projet gutenberg etext of illinois benedictine Université with permission. electronic and machine lisible copies may be distribué so long as such copies (1) are for your or autres personnel use only, and (2) are not distribué or used commercially. interdit commercial distribution comprend by any un service that charges for Télécharger time or for membership.>> act ii. scène i. rome. a Publique endroit entrer menenius, with the two tribunes of the people, sicinius and brutus menenius. the augurer raconte me we doit have news tonight. brutus. good or bad? menenius. not selon to the prayer of the people, for they love not marcius. sicinius. la nature teaches beasts to know leur friends. menenius. pray you, who does the wolf love? sicinius. the lamb. menenius. ay, to devour him, as the hungry plebeians aurait the noble marcius. brutus. he's a lamb indeed, that baes like a bear. menenius. he's a bear indeed, that vies fike a lamb. you two are old men; tell me one chose that i doit ask you. both tribunes. well, sir. menenius. in what enormity is Marcius poor in that you two have not in abundance? brutus. he's poor in no one fault, but stor'd with all. sicinius. especially in pride. brutus. and topping all autres in boasting. menenius. this is étrange now. do you two know how you are censured here in the city- i mean of us o' th' right-hand file? do you? both tribunes. why, how are we censur'd? menenius. car you talk of fierté now- will you not be angry? both tribunes. well, well, sir, well. menenius. why, 'tis no génial matter; for a very peu voleur of occasion will rob you of a génial deal of patience. give your dispositions the reins, and be angry at your pleasures- at the least, if you take it as a plaisir to you in étant so. you faire des reproches Marcius for étant proud? brutus. we do it not alone, sir. menenius. i know you can do very peu alone; for your helps are many, or else your actions aurait grow wondrous single: your abilities are too infant-like for Faire much alone. you talk of pride. o that you pourrait turn your eyes vers the napes of your necks, and make but an interior survey of your good selves! o that you could! both tribunes. what then, sir? menenius. why, then you devrait découvrir a brace of unmeriting, proud, violent, testy magistrates-alias fools- as any in rome. sicinius. menenius, you are connu well assez too. menenius. i am connu to be a humorous patrician, and one that aime a cup of hot wine with not a drop of allaying tiber in't; said to be quelque chose imperfect in favouring the première complaint, hasty and tinder-like upon too trivial motion; one that converses more with the buttock of the nuit than with the forehead of the morning. what i pense i utter, and dépenser my malice in my breath. réunion two such wealsmen as you are- i ne peux pas call you lycurguses- if the boisson you give me toucher my palate adversely, i make a crooked face at it. i ne peux pas say your worships have deliver'd the matière well, when i find the ass in compound with the major part of your syllables; and bien que i must be contenu to bear with ceux that say you are reverend la tombe men, yet they lie mortel that tell you you have good faces. if you see this in the map of my microcosm, follows it that i am connu well assez too? what harm can your bisson conspectuities glean out of this character, if i be connu well assez too? brutus. come, sir, come, we know you well enough. menenius. you know ni me, yourselves, nor any thing. you are ambitious for poor knaves' caps and legs; you wear out a good wholesome forenoon in hearing a cause entre an orange-wife and a fosset-seller, and then rejourn the controversy of threepence to a seconde day of audience. when you are hearing a matière entre fête and party, if you chance to be pinch'd with the colic, you make visages like mummers, set up the bloody flag against all patience, and, in roaring for a chamber-pot, dismiss the controversy bleeding, the more entangled by your hearing. all the paix you make in leur cause is calling both the parties knaves. you are a pair of étrange ones. brutus. come, come, you are well understood to be a perfecter giber for the table than a necessary bencher in the capitol. menenius. our very priests must devenir mockers, if they doit rencontre such ridiculous subjects as you are. when you parler best unto the purpose, it is not vaut the wagging of your beards; and your beards mériter not so honourable a la tombe as to des trucs a botcher's cushion or to be entomb'd in an ass's pack-saddle. yet you must be en disant Marcius is proud; who, in a cheap estimation, is vaut all your predecessors depuis deucalion; bien que peradventure some of the best of 'em were hereditary hangmen. god-den to your worships. more of your conversation aurait infect my brain, étant the herdsmen of the beastly plebeians. i will be bold to take my laisser of you. [brutus and sicinius go aside] entrer volumnia, virgilia, and valeria how now, my as fair as noble ladies- and the moon, were she earthly, no nobler- où do you suivre your eyes so fast? volumnia. honourable menenius, my boy Marcius approaches; for the love of juno, let's go. menenius. ha! Marcius venir home? volumnia. ay, worthy menenius, and with most prosperous approbation. menenius. take my cap, jupiter, and i remercier thee. hoo! Marcius venir home! volumnia, virgilia. nay, 'tis true. volumnia. look, here's a lettre from him; the Etat hath another, his wife another; and i pense there's one at home for you. menenius. i will make my very maison reel to-night. a lettre for me? virgilia. yes, certain, there's a lettre for you; i saw't. menenius. a lettre for me! it gives me an biens of Sept years' health; in lequel time i will make a lip at the physician. the most souverain prescription in galen is but empiricutic and, to this preservative, of no mieux rapport than a horse-drench. is he not wounded? he was wont to come home wounded. virgilia. o, no, no, no. volumnia. o, he is wounded, i remercier the gods for't. menenius. so do i too, if it be not too much. brings a la victoire in his pocket? the wounds devenir him. volumnia. on's brows, menenius, he vient the troisième time home with the oaken garland. menenius. has he disciplin'd aufidius soundly? volumnia. titus lartius writes they combattu together, but aufidius got off. menenius. and 'twas time for him too, i'll mandat him that; an he had stay'd by him, i aurait not have been so fidius'd for all the chests in corioli and the gold that's in them. is the sénat possess'd of this? volumnia. good ladies, let's go. yes, yes, yes: the sénat has letters from the general, où he gives my son the entier name of the war; he hath in this action outdone his ancien actes doubly. valeria. in troth, there's wondrous things parlait of him. menenius. wondrous! ay, i mandat you, and not sans pour autant his true purchasing. virgilia. the gods subvention them true! volumnia. true! pow, waw. menenius. true! i'll be juré they are true. où is he wounded? [to the tribunes] god save your good worships! Marcius is venir home; he has more cause to be proud. où is he wounded? volumnia. i' th' shoulder and i' th' left arm; Là will be grand cicatrices to show the gens when he doit supporter for his place. he received in the repulse of tarquin Sept hurts i' th' body. menenius. one i' th' neck and two i' th' thigh- there's nine that i know. volumnia. he had avant this last expedition twenty-five wounds upon him. menenius. now it's twenty-seven; chaque gash was an enemy's grave. [a shout and flourish] hark! the trumpets. volumnia. celles-ci are the ushers of marcius. avant him he carries noise, and derrière him he leaves tears; death, that dark spirit, in's nervy arm doth lie, which, étant advanc'd, declines, and then men die. a sennet. trumpets sound. entrer cominius the general, and titus lartius; entre them, coriolanus, crown'd with an oaken garland; with captains and soldiers and a herald herald. know, rome, that all seul Marcius did bats toi dans corioli gates, où he hath won, with fame, a name to caius marcius; celles-ci in honour follows coriolanus. Bienvenue to rome, renowned coriolanus! [flourish] all. Bienvenue to rome, renowned coriolanus! coriolanus. no more of this, it does offenser my heart. pray now, no more. cominius. look, sir, your mother! coriolanus. o, you have, i know, petition'd all the gods for my prosperity! [kneels] volumnia. nay, my good soldier, up; my doux marcius, worthy caius, and by deed-achieving honour newly nam'd- what is it? coriolanus must i can thee? but, o, thy wife! coriolanus. my gracious silence, hail! wouldst thou have laugh'd had i come coffin'd home, that weep'st to see me triumph? ah, my dear, such eyes the widows in corioli wear, and mothers that lack sons. menenius. now the gods couronne thee! coriolanus. and live you yet? [to valeria] o my sucré lady, pardon. volumnia. i know not où to turn. o, Bienvenue home! and welcome, general. and y'are Bienvenue all. menenius. a cent thousand welcomes. i pourrait weep and i pourrait laugh; i am lumière and heavy. welcome! a malédiction commencer at very root on's cœur that is not glad to see thee! you are three that rome devrait dote on; yet, by the Foi of men, we have some old crab trees here at home that will not be grafted to your relish. yet welcome, warriors. we call a nettle but a nettle, and the faults of imbéciles but folly. cominius. ever right. coriolanus. menenius ever, ever. herald. give way there, and go on. coriolanus. [to his wife and mother] your hand, and yours. ere in our own maison i do shade my head, the good patricians must be visited; from whom i have receiv'd not only greetings, but with them changement of honours. volumnia. i have lived to see inherited my very wishes, and the buildings of my fancy; only there's one chose wanting, lequel i doute not but our rome will cast upon thee. coriolanus. know, good mother, i had plutôt be leur serviteur in my way than sway with them in theirs. cominius. on, to the capitol. [flourish. cornets. sortir in state, as before] brutus and sicinius come vers l'avant brutus. all tongues parler of him and the bleared sights are spectacled to see him. your prattling infirmière into a rapture lets her baby cry tandis que she chats him; the kitchen malkin pins her richest lockram 'bout her reechy neck, clamb'ring the des murs to eye him; stalls, bulks, windows, are smother'd up, leads fill'd and ridges hors'd with variable complexions, all agreeing in earnestness to see him. seld-shown flamens do press among the popular throngs and puff to win a vulgar station; our veil'd dames commettre the war of white and damask in leur nicely gawded cheeks to th' wanton spoil of phoebus' brûlant kisses. such a pother, as if that whatsoever god who leads him were slily crept into his human powers, and gave him graceful posture. sicinius. on the soudain i mandat him consul. brutus. then our Bureau may during his Puissance go sleep. sicinius. he ne peux pas temp'rately transport his honours from où he devrait commencer and end, but will lose ceux he hath won. brutus. in that there's comfort. sicinius. doute not the commoners, for whom we stand, but they upon leur ancien malice will oublier with the moins cause celles-ci his new honours; lequel that he will give them make i as peu question as he is fier to do't. brutus. i entendu him swear, were he to supporter for consul, jamais aurait he apparaître i' th' market-place, nor on him put the napless vesture of humility; nor, showing, as the manière is, his wounds to th' people, beg leur stinking breaths. sicinius. 'tis right. brutus. it was his word. o, he aurait miss it plutôt than porter it but by the suit of the gentry to him and the le désir of the nobles. sicinius. i wish no mieux than have him hold that purpose, and to put it in execution. brutus. 'tis most like he will. sicinius. it doit be to him then as our good wills: a sure destruction. brutus. so it must fall out to him or our authorities. for an end, we must suggest the gens in what hatred he encore hath held them; that to's Puissance he aurait have made them mules, silenc'd leur pleaders, and dispropertied leur freedoms; holding them in human action and capacity of no more soul nor fitness for the monde than camels in leur war, who have leur provand only for palier burdens, and sore coups for sinking sous them. sicinius. this, as you say, suggested at some time when his soaring insolence doit toucher the people- lequel time doit not want, if he be put upon't, and that's as easy as to set dogs on sheep- will be his fire to kindle leur dry stubble; and leur blaze doit darken him for ever. entrer a Messager brutus. what's the matter? messenger. you are sent for to the capitol. 'tis pensée that Marcius doit be consul. i have seen the dumb men throng to see him and the aveugle to hear him speak; matrons flung gloves, Dames and servantes leur scarfs and handkerchers, upon him as he pass'd; the nobles bended as to jove's statue, and the commons made a shower and tonnerre with leur caps and shouts. i jamais saw the like. brutus. let's to the capitol, and porter with us ears and eyes for th' time, but hearts for the event. sicinius. have with you. sortir scène ii. rome. the Capitole entrer two officers, to lay cushions, as it were in the Capitole première officer. come, come, they are presque here. how many supporter for consulships? seconde officer. three, they say; but 'tis pensée of chaque one coriolanus will porter it. première officer. that's a courageux fellow; but he's vengeance fier and aime not the commun people. seconde officer. faith, Là have been many génial men that have flatter'd the people, who ne'er loved them; and Là be many that they have loved, they know not wherefore; so that, if they love they know not why, they hate upon no mieux a ground. therefore, for coriolanus ni to care qu'il s'agisse they love or hate him manifests the true connaissance he has in leur disposition, and out of his noble carelessness lets them plainly see't. première officer. if he did not care qu'il s'agisse he had leur love or no, he waved indifferently 'twixt Faire them ni good nor harm; but he seeks leur hate with greater devotion than they can rendre it him, and leaves rien défait that may fully découvrir him leur opposite. now to seem to affect the malice and mécontentement of the gens is as bad as that lequel he dislikes- to flatter them for leur love. seconde officer. he hath deserved worthily of his country; and his ascent is not by such easy degrees as ceux who, ayant been supple and courteous to the people, bonneted, sans pour autant any plus loin deed to have them at all, into leur estimation and report; but he hath so planted his honours in leur eyes and his actions in leur hearts that for leur tongues to be silent and not avouer so much were a kind of ingrateful injury; to rapport otherwise were a malice that, donnant lui-même the lie, aurait cueillir reproof and rebuke from chaque car that entendu it. première officer. no more of him; he's a worthy man. make way, they are coming. a sennet. entrer the patricians and the tribunes of the people, lictors avant them; coriolanus, menenius, cominius the consul. sicinius and brutus take leur places by themselves. coriolanus stands menenius. ayant determin'd of the volsces, and to send for titus lartius, it remains, as the main point of this our after-meeting, to gratify his noble un service that hath thus se tenait for his country. par conséquent S'il vous plaît you, most reverend and la tombe elders, to le désir the présent consul and last général in our well-found successes to rapport a peu of that worthy work perform'd by caius Marcius coriolanus; whom we met here both to remercier and to rappelles toi with honours like himself. [coriolanus sits] première senator. speak, good cominius. laisser rien out for length, and make us pense plutôt our state's defective for requital than we to stretch it out. masters o' th' people, we do demande your kindest ears; and, after, your aimant mouvement vers the commun body, to rendement what passes here. sicinius. we are convented upon a pleasing treaty, and have hearts inclinable to honour and advance the theme of our assembly. brutus. lequel the plutôt we doit be bless'd to do, if he rappelles toi a kinder value of the gens than he hath hereto priz'd them at. menenius. that's off, that's off; i aurait you plutôt had been silent. S'il vous plaît you to hear cominius speak? brutus. most willingly. but yet my caution was more pertinent than the rebuke you give it. menenius. he aime your people; but tie him not to be leur bedfellow. worthy cominius, speak. [coriolanus rises, and offers to go away] nay, keep your place. première senator. sit, coriolanus, jamais la honte to hear what you have nobly done. coriolanus. your honours' pardon. i had plutôt have my wounds to heal encore than hear say how i got them. brutus. sir, i hope my words disbench'd you not. coriolanus. no, sir; yet oft, when coups have made me stay, i fled from words. you sooth'd not, par conséquent hurt not. but your people, i love them as they weigh- menenius. pray now, sit down. coriolanus. i had plutôt have one scratch my head i' th' sun when the alarum were frappé than idly sit to hear my nothings monster'd. exit menenius. masters of the people, your multiplying spawn how can he flatter- that's thousand to one good one- when you now see he had plutôt venture all his membres for honour than one on's ears to hear it? proceed, cominius. cominius. i doit lack voice; the actes of coriolanus devrait not be utter'd feebly. it is held that valeur is the chiefest vertu and most dignifies the haver. if it be, the man i parler of ne peux pas in the monde be singly counterpois'd. at sixteen years, when tarquin made a head for rome, he combattu au-delà the mark of others; our then dictator, whom with all louange i point at, saw him bats toi when with his amazonian chin he drove the bristled lips avant him; he bestrid an o'erpress'd roman and i' th' consul's view slew three opposers; tarquin's self he met, and frappé him on his knee. in that day's feats, when he pourrait act the femme in the scene, he prov'd best man i' th' field, and for his meed was brow-bound with the oak. his pupil age man-ent'red thus, he waxed like a sea, and in the brunt of seventeen battles depuis he lurch'd all swords of the garland. for this last, avant and in corioli, let me say i ne peux pas parler him home. he stopp'd the fliers, and by his rare example made the lâche turn terror into sport; as mauvaises herbes avant a vessel sous sail, so men obey'd and fell au dessous de his stem. his sword, death's stamp, où it did mark, it took; from face to foot he was a chose of blood, dont chaque mouvement was tim'd with en train de mourir cries. seul he ent'red the mortel gate of th' city, lequel he peint with shunless destiny; aidless came off, and with a soudain re-enforcement frappé corioli like a planet. now all's his. when by and by the din of war 'gan pierce his prêt sense, then tout droit his doubled esprit re-quick'ned what in la chair was fatigate, and to the bataille came he; où he did run reeking o'er the vies of men, as if 'twere a perpetual spoil; and till we call'd both champ and city ours he jamais se tenait to ease his Sein with panting. menenius. worthy man! première senator. he ne peux pas but with mesure fit the honours lequel we concevoir him. cominius. our spoils he kick'd at, and look'd upon things précieux as they were the commun muck of the world. he covets less than misère lui-même aurait give, rewards his actes with Faire them, and is contenu to dépenser the time to end it. menenius. he's droite noble; let him be call'd for. première senator. call coriolanus. officer. he doth appear. re-enter coriolanus menenius. the senate, coriolanus, are well pleas'd to make thee consul. coriolanus. i do owe them encore my life and services. menenius. it then remains that you do parler to the people. coriolanus. i do beseech you let me o'erleap that custom; for i ne peux pas put on the gown, supporter naked, and supplier them for my wounds' sake to give leur suffrage. S'il vous plaît you that i may pass this doing. sicinius. sir, the gens must have leur voices; ni will they bate one jot of ceremony. menenius. put them not to't. pray you go fit you to the custom, and take to you, as your predecessors have, your honour with your form. coriolanus. it is a part that i doit rougir in acting, and pourrait well be pris from the people. brutus. mark you that? coriolanus. to brag unto them 'thus i did, and thus!' show them th' unaching scars lequel i devrait hide, as if i had receiv'd them for the hire of leur souffle only! menenius. do not supporter upon't. we recommend to you, tribunes of the people, our objectif to them; and to our noble consul wish we all joy and honour. senators. to coriolanus come all joy and honour!
