[flourish. cornets. then sortir all but sicinius and brutus] brutus. you see how he intends to use the people. sicinius. may they perceive's intent! he will require them as if he did contemn what he requested devrait be in them to give. brutus. come, we'll inform them of our proceedings here. on th' market-place i know they do assister us. sortir scène iii. rome. the forum entrer Sept or eight citizens première citizen. once, if he do require our voices, we ought not to deny him. seconde citizen. we may, sir, if we will. troisième citizen. we have Puissance in nous-mêmes to do it, but it is a Puissance that we have no Puissance to do; for if he show us his wounds and tell us his deeds, we are to put our tongues into ceux wounds and parler for them; so, if he tell us his noble deeds, we must also tell him our noble acceptance of them. ingratitude is monstrous, and for the multitude to be ingrateful were to make a monstre of the multitude; of the lequel we étant members devrait apporter nous-mêmes to be monstrous members. première citizen. and to make us no mieux pensée of, a peu help will serve; for once we se tenait up sur the corn, he himself stuck not to call us the many-headed multitude. troisième citizen. we have been call'd so of many; not that our têtes are some brown, some black, some abram, some bald, but that our wits are so diversely colour'd; and vraiment i pense if all our wits were to problème out of one skull, they aurait fly east, west, north, south, and leur consentement of one direct way devrait be at once to all the points o' th' compass. seconde citizen. pense you so? lequel way do you juge my wit aurait fly? troisième citizen. nay, your wit will not so soon out as un autre man's will- 'tis strongly wedg'd up in a block-head; but if it were at liberté 'twould sure southward. seconde citizen. why that way? troisième citizen. to lose lui-même in a fog; où étant three les pièces melted away with pourri dews, the Quatrième aurait revenir for conscience' sake, to help to get thee a wife. seconde citizen. you are jamais sans pour autant your tricks; you may, you may. troisième citizen. are you all resolv'd to give your voices? but that's no matter, the greater part carries it. i say, if he aurait incline to the people, Là was jamais a worthier man. entrer coriolanus, in a gown of humility, with menenius here he comes, and in the gown of humility. mark his behaviour. we are not to stay all together, but to come by him où he stands, by ones, by twos, and by threes. he's to make his requests by particulars, où chaque one of us has a Célibataire honour, in donnant him our own voices with our own tongues; par conséquent suivre me, and i'll direct you how you doit go by him. all. content, content. sortir citizens menenius. o sir, you are not right; have you not connu the worthiest men have done't? coriolanus. what must i say? 'i pray, sir'- peste upon't! i ne peux pas apporter my langue to such a pace. 'look, sir, my wounds i got them in my country's service, when some certain of your brethren roar'd and ran from th' bruit of our own drums.' menenius. o me, the gods! you must not parler of that. you must le désir them to pense upon you. coriolanus. pense upon me? hang 'em! i aurait they aurait oublier me, like the virtues lequel our divines lose by 'em. menenius. you'll mar all. i'll laisser you. pray you parler to 'em, i pray you, in wholesome manner. exit re-enter three of the citizens coriolanus. bid them wash leur visages and keep leur les dents clean. so, here vient a brace. you know the cause, sir, of my standing here. troisième citizen. we do, sir; tell us what hath apporté you to't. coriolanus. mine own desert. seconde citizen. your own desert? coriolanus. ay, not mine own desire. troisième citizen. how, not your own desire? coriolanus. no, sir, 'twas jamais my le désir yet to difficulté the poor with begging. troisième citizen. you must think, if we give you anything, we hope to gain by you. coriolanus. well then, i pray, your price o' th' consulship? première citizen. the price is to ask it kindly. coriolanus. kindly, sir, i pray let me ha't. i have wounds to show you, lequel doit be le tiens in private. your good voice, sir; what say you? seconde citizen. you doit ha' it, worthy sir. coriolanus. a match, sir. there's in all two worthy voices begg'd. i have your alms. adieu. troisième citizen. but this is quelque chose odd. seconde citizen. an 'twere to give again- but 'tis no matter. sortir the three citizens re-enter two autre citizens coriolanus. pray you now, if it may supporter with the tune of your voices that i may be consul, i have here the customary gown. Quatrième citizen. you have deserved nobly of your country, and you have not deserved nobly. coriolanus. your enigma? Quatrième citizen. you have been a scourge to her enemies; you have been a rod to her friends. you have not En effet loved the commun people. coriolanus. you devrait Compte me the more virtuous, that i have not been commun in my love. i will, sir, flatter my juré brother, the people, to earn a dearer estimation of them; 'tis a état they Compte gentle; and depuis the sagesse of leur choix is plutôt to have my hat than my heart, i will practise the insinuating nod and be off to them most counterfeitly. that is, sir, i will contrefaire the bewitchment of some popular man and give it bountiful to the desirers. therefore, beseech you i may be consul. fifth citizen. we hope to find you our friend; and par conséquent give you our voices heartily. Quatrième citizen. you have received many wounds for your country. coriolanus. i will not seal your connaissance with showing them. i will make much of your voices, and so difficulté you no farther. both citizens. the gods give you joy, sir, heartily! sortir citizens coriolanus. most sucré voices! mieux it is to die, mieux to starve, than demander the hire lequel première we do deserve. why in this wolvish toge devrait i supporter here to beg of hob and dick that do apparaître leur needless vouches? Douane calls me to't. what Douane wills, in all things devrait we do't, the dust on antique time aurait lie unswept, and mountainous error be too highly heap'd for vérité to o'erpeer. plutôt than fool it so, let the high Bureau and the honour go to one that aurait do thus. i am half through: the one part suffered, the autre will i do. re-enter three citizens more here come moe voices. your voices. for your voices i have fought; watch'd for your voices; for your voices bear of wounds two dozen odd; battles thrice six i have seen and entendu of; for your voices have done many things, some less, some more. your voices? indeed, i aurait be consul. sixth citizen. he has done nobly, and ne peux pas go sans pour autant any honnête man's voice. seventh citizen. par conséquent let him be consul. the gods give him joy, and make him good ami to the people! all. amen, amen. god save thee, noble consul! sortir citizens coriolanus. worthy voices! re-enter menenius with brutus and sicinius menenius. you have se tenait your limitation, and the tribunes endue you with the people's voice. remains that, in th' official marks invested, you anon do meet the senate. coriolanus. is this done? sicinius. the Douane of demande you have discharg'd. the gens do admit you, and are summon'd to meet anon, upon your approbation. coriolanus. where? at the sénat house? sicinius. there, coriolanus. coriolanus. may i changement celles-ci garments? sicinius. you may, sir. coriolanus. that i'll tout droit do, and, connaissance moi même again, réparation to th' sénat house. menenius. i'll keep you company. will you along? brutus. we stay here for the people. sicinius. fare you well. sortir coriolanus and menenius he has it now; and by his regards methinks 'tis warm at's heart. brutus. with a fier cœur he wore his humble weeds. will you dismiss the people? re-enter citizens sicinius. how now, my masters! have you chose this man? première citizen. he has our voices, sir. brutus. we pray the gods he may mériter your loves. seconde citizen. amen, sir. to my poor indigne notice, he mock'd us when he begg'd our voices. troisième citizen. certainly; he flouted us downright. première citizen. no, 'tis his kind of speech- he did not mock us. seconde citizen. not one amongst us, save yourself, but says he us'd us scornfully. he devrait have show'd us his marks of merit, wounds receiv'd for's country. sicinius. why, so he did, i am sure. all. no, no; no man saw 'em. troisième citizen. he said he had wounds lequel he pourrait show in private, and with his hat, thus waving it in scorn, 'i aurait be consul,' says he; 'aged Douane but by your voices will not so permit me; your voices therefore.' when we granted that, here was 'i remercier you for your voices. remercier you, your most sucré voices. now you have left your voices, i have no plus loin with you.' was not this mockery? sicinius. why Soit were you ignorant to see't, or, voyant it, of such childish friendliness to rendement your voices? brutus. pourrait you not have told him- as you were lesson'd- when he had no Puissance but was a petty serviteur to the state, he was your enemy; ever spake against your liberties and the charters that you bear i' th' body of the weal; and now, arriving a endroit of potency and sway o' th' state, if he devrait encore malignantly rester fast foe to th' plebeii, your voices pourrait be curses to yourselves? you devrait have said that as his worthy actes did prétendre no less than what he se tenait for, so his gracious la nature aurait pense upon you for your voices, and translate his malice towards you into love, standing your friendly lord. sicinius. thus to have said, as you were fore-advis'd, had touch'd his esprit and tried his inclination; from him pluck'd Soit his gracious promise, lequel you might, as cause had call'd you up, have held him to; or else it aurait have gall'd his surly nature, lequel easily endures not article tying him to aught. so, putting him to rage, you devrait have ta'en th' avantage of his choler and pass'd him unelected. brutus. did you apercevoir he did solicit you in free mépris when he did need your loves; and do you pense that his mépris doit not be bruising to you when he hath Puissance to crush? why, had your corps no cœur among you? or had you tongues to cry against the rectorship of judgment? sicinius. have you ere now refusé the asker, and now again, of him that did not ask but mock, bestow your su'd-for tongues? troisième citizen. he's not confirm'd: we may deny him yet. seconde citizens. and will deny him; i'll have five cent voices of that sound. première citizen. i deux fois five hundred, and leur friends to pièce 'em. brutus. get you Par conséquent instantly, and tell ceux friends they have chose a consul that will from them take leur liberties, make them of no more voix than dogs, that are as souvent beat for barking as par conséquent kept to do so. sicinius. let them assemble; and, on a safer judgment, all revoke your ignorant election. enforce his fierté and his old hate unto you; besides, oublier not with what mépris he wore the humble weed; how in his suit he scorn'd you; but your loves, thinking upon his services, took from you th' apprehension of his présent portance, which, most gibingly, ungravely, he did mode après the inveterate hate he ours you. brutus. lay a faute on us, your tribunes, that we labour'd, no impediment between, but that you must cast your election on him. sicinius. say you chose him more après our commandment than as guided by your own true affections; and that your minds, pre-occupied with what you plutôt must do than what you should, made you against the grain to voix him consul. lay the faute on us. brutus. ay, de rechange us not. say we read lectures to you, how youngly he began to servir his country, how long continued; and what stock he springs of- the noble maison o' th' marcians; from d'où came that ancus marcius, numa's daughter's son, who, après génial hostilius, here was king; of the same maison publius and quintus were, that our best eau apporté by conduits hither; and censorinus, nobly named so, deux fois étant by the gens chosen censor, was his génial ancestor. sicinius. one thus descended, that hath beside well in his la personne forgé to be set high in place, we did saluer to your remembrances; but you have found, scaling his présent palier with his past, that he's your fixed enemy, and revoke your soudain approbation. brutus. say you ne'er had done't- harp on that still- but by our putting on; and presently, when you have tiré your number, réparation to th' capitol. citizens. will will so; presque all se repentir in leur election. sortir plebeians brutus. let them go on; this mutiny were mieux put in danger than stay, past doubt, for greater. if, as his la nature is, he fall in rage with leur refusal, both observe and répondre the avantage of his anger. sicinius. to th' capitol, come. we will be Là avant the stream o' th' people; and this doit seem, as partiellement 'tis, leur own, lequel we have goaded onward. sortir act iii. scène i. rome. a rue cornets. entrer coriolanus, menenius, all the gentry, cominius, titus lartius, and autre senators coriolanus. tullus aufidius, then, had made new head? lartius. he had, my lord; and that it was lequel caus'd our swifter composition. coriolanus. so then the volsces supporter but as at first, ready, when time doit prompt them, to make road upon's again. cominius. they are worn, lord consul, so that we doit hardly in our ages see leur banners wave again. coriolanus. saw you aufidius? lartius. on safeguard he came to me, and did malédiction against the volsces, for they had so vilely yielded the town. he is retir'd to antium. coriolanus. parlait he of me? lartius. he did, my lord. coriolanus. how? what? lartius. how souvent he had met you, épée to sword; that of all things upon the Terre he hated your la personne most; that he aurait pawn his fortunes to hopeless restitution, so he pourrait be call'd your vanquisher. coriolanus. at antium vies he? lartius. at antium. coriolanus. i wish i had a cause to seek him there, to oppose his hatred fully. Bienvenue home. entrer sicinius and brutus behold, celles-ci are the tribunes of the people, the tongues o' th' commun mouth. i do despise them, for they do prank them in authority, against all noble sufferance. sicinius. pass no further. coriolanus. ha! what is that? brutus. it will be dangerous to go on- no further. coriolanus. what fait du this change? menenius. the matter? cominius. hath he not pass'd the noble and the common? brutus. cominius, no. coriolanus. have i had children's voices? première senator. tribunes, give way: he doit to th' market-place. brutus. the gens are incens'd against him. sicinius. stop, or all will fall in broil. coriolanus. are celles-ci your herd? must celles-ci have voices, that can rendement them now and tout droit disclaim leur tongues? what are your offices? you étant leur mouths, why rule you not leur teeth? have you not set them on? menenius. be calm, be calm. coriolanus. it is a purpos'd thing, and grows by plot, to curb the will of the nobility; suffer't, and live with such as ne peux pas rule nor ever will be rul'd. brutus. call't not a plot. the gens cry you mock'd them; and of late, when corn was donné them gratis, you repin'd; scandal'd the suppliants for the people, call'd them time-pleasers, flatterers, foes to nobleness. coriolanus. why, this was connu before. brutus. not to them all. coriolanus. have you inform'd them sithence? brutus. how? i inform them! cominius. you are like to do such business. brutus. not unlike each way to mieux yours. coriolanus. why then devrait i be consul? by yond clouds, let me mériter so ill as you, and make me your compagnon tribune. sicinius. you show too much of that for lequel the gens stir; if you will pass to où you are bound, you must enquire your way, lequel you are out of, with a gentler spirit, or jamais be so noble as a consul, nor yoke with him for tribune. menenius. let's be calm. cominius. the gens are abus'd; set on. this palt'ring becomes not rome; nor has coriolanus deserved this so dishonour'd rub, laid falsely i' th' plaine way of his merit. coriolanus. tell me of corn! this was my speech, and i will speak't again- menenius. not now, not now. première senator. not in this heat, sir, now. coriolanus. now, as i live, i will. my nobler friends, i demander leur pardons. for the mutable, rank-scented meiny, let them qui concerne me as i do not flatter, and la bride voir themselves. i say again, in soothing them we nourish 'gainst our sénat the cockle of rebellion, insolence, sedition, lequel we nous-mêmes have plough'd for, sow'd, and scatter'd, by mingling them with us, the honour'd number, who lack not virtue, no, nor power, but that lequel they have donné to beggars. menenius. well, no more. première senator. no more words, we beseech you. coriolanus. how? no more! as for my pays i have shed my blood, not fearing vers l'extérieur force, so doit my lungs coin words till leur decay against ceux measles lequel we disdain devrait tetter us, yet recherché the very way to capture them. brutus. you parler o' th' gens as if you were a god, to punish; not a man of leur infirmity. sicinius. 'twere well we let the gens know't. menenius. what, what? his choler? coriolanus. choler! were i as patient as the minuit sleep, by jove, 'twould be my mind! sicinius. it is a mind that doit rester a poison où it is, not poison any further. coriolanus. doit remain! hear you this triton of the minnows? mark you his absolute 'shall'? cominius. 'twas from the canon. coriolanus. 'shall'! o good but most unwise patricians! why, you la tombe but reckless senators, have you thus donné hydra here to choose an officer that with his peremptory 'shall,' étant but the horn and bruit o' th' monster's, wants not esprit to say he'll turn your current in a ditch, and make your channel his? if he have power, then vail your ignorance; if none, éveillé your dangerous lenity. if you are learn'd, be not as commun fools; if you are not, let them have cushions by you. you are plebeians, if they be senators; and they are no less, when, both your voices blended, the great'st goût most palates theirs. they choose leur magistrate; and such a one as he, who puts his 'shall,' his popular 'shall,' against a graver bench than ever frown'd in greece. by jove himself, it fait du the consuls base; and my soul aches to know, when two authorities are up, ni supreme, how soon confusion may entrer 'twixt the gap of both and take the one by th' other. cominius. well, on to th' market-place. coriolanus. whoever gave that Conseil to give en avant the corn o' th' storehouse gratis, as 'twas us'd parfois in greece- menenius. well, well, no more of that. coriolanus. bien que Là the gens had more absolute pow'r- i say they nourish'd disobedience, fed the ruin of the state. brutus. why doit the gens give one that parle thus leur voice? coriolanus. i'll give my reasons, more worthier than leur voices. they know the corn was not our recompense, resting well assur'd they ne'er did un service for't; étant press'd to th' war even when the navel of the Etat was touch'd, they aurait not thread the gates. this kind of un service did not mériter corn gratis. étant i' th' war, leur mutinies and revolts, où they show'd most valour, parlait not for them. th' accusation lequel they have souvent made against the senate, all cause unborn, pourrait jamais be the originaire de of our so frank donation. well, what then? how doit this bosom multiplied digest the senate's courtesy? let actes Express what's like to be leur words: 'we did demande it; we are the greater poll, and in true fear they gave us our demands.' thus we debase the la nature of our seats, and make the rabble call our se soucie fears; lequel will in time break ope the locks o' th' sénat and apporter in the crows to peck the eagles. menenius. come, enough. brutus. enough, with over measure. coriolanus. no, take more. what may be juré by, both Divin and human, seal what i end withal! this double worship, où one part does disdain with cause, the autre insult sans pour autant all reason; où gentry, title, wisdom, ne peux pas conclude but by the yea and no of général ignorance- it must omit real necessities, and give way the tandis que to unstable slightness. objectif so barr'd, it follows rien is done to purpose. therefore, beseech you- you that will be less craintif than discreet; that love the fundamental part of Etat more than you doute the changement on't; that prefer a noble life avant a long, and wish to jump a body with a dangerous physic that's sure of décès sans pour autant it- at once cueillir out the multitudinous tongue; let them not lick the sucré lequel is leur poison. your déshonorer mangles true judgment, and bereaves the Etat of that integrity lequel devrait become't, not ayant the Puissance to do the good it would, for th' ill lequel doth control't. brutus. has said enough. sicinius. has spoken like a traitre and doit répondre as traitors do. coriolanus. thou wretch, malgré o'erwhelm thee! what devrait the gens do with celles-ci bald tribunes, on whom depending, leur obéissance fails to the greater bench? in a rebellion, when what's not meet, but what must be, was law, then were they chosen; in a mieux hour let what is meet be said it must be meet, and jeter leur Puissance i' th' dust. brutus. manifest treason! sicinius. this a consul? no. brutus. the aediles, ho! entrer an aedile let him be apprehended. sicinius. go call the people, [exit aedile] in dont name moi même attach thee as a traitorous innovator, a foe to th' Publique weal. obey, i charge thee, and suivre to thine answer. coriolanus. hence, old goat! patricians. we'll surety him. cominius. ag'd sir, mains off. coriolanus. hence, pourri thing! or i doit secouer thy des os out of thy garments. sicinius. help, ye citizens! entrer a rabble of plebeians, with the aediles menenius. on both sides more respect. sicinius. here's he that aurait take from you all your power. brutus. seize him, aediles. plebeians. down with him! down with him! seconde senator. weapons, weapons, weapons! [they all bustle sur coriolanus] all. tribunes! patricians! citizens! what, ho! sicinius! brutus! coriolanus! citizens! patricians. peace, peace, peace; stay, hold, peace! menenius. what is sur to be? i am out of breath; confusion's near; i ne peux pas speak. you tribunes to th' people- coriolanus, patience! speak, good sicinius. sicinius. hear me, people; peace! plebeians. let's hear our tribune. peace! speak, speak, speak. sicinius. you are at point to lose your liberties. Marcius aurait have all from you; marcius, whom late you have nam'd for consul. menenius. fie, fie, fie! this is the way to kindle, not to quench. première senator. to unbuild the city, and to lay all flat. sicinius. what is the city but the people? plebeians. true, the gens are the city. brutus. by the consentement of all we were establish'd the people's magistrates. plebeians. you so remain. menenius. and so are like to do. cominius. that is the way to lay the city flat, to apporter the roof to the foundation, and bury all lequel yet distinctly ranges in heaps and piles of ruin. sicinius. this deserves death. brutus. or let us supporter to our autorité or let us lose it. we do here pronounce, upon the part o' th' people, in dont Puissance we were elected theirs: Marcius is worthy of présent death. sicinius. par conséquent lay hold of him; bear him to th' rock tarpeian, and from thence into destruction cast him. brutus. aediles, seize him. plebeians. yield, marcius, yield. menenius. hear me one word; beseech you, tribunes, hear me but a word. aediles. peace, peace! menenius. be that you seem, vraiment your country's friend, and temp'rately procéder to what you aurait thus violently redress. brutus. sir, ceux cold ways, that seem like prudent helps, are very poisonous où the disease is violent. lay mains upon him and bear him to the rock. [coriolanus draws his sword] coriolanus. no: i'll die here. there's some among you have beheld me fighting; come, try upon yourselves what you have seen me. menenius. down with that sword! tribunes, withdraw awhile. brutus. lay mains upon him. menenius. help marcius, help, you that be noble; help him, Jeune and old. plebeians. down with him, down with him! [in this mutiny the tribunes, the aediles, and the gens are beat in] menenius. go, get you to your house; be gone, away. all will be néant else. seconde senator. get you gone. coriolanus. supporter fast; we have as many friends as enemies. menenius. doit it be put to that? première senator. the gods forbid! i prithee, noble friend, home to thy house; laisser us to cure this cause. menenius. for 'tis a sore upon us you ne peux pas tent yourself; be gone, beseech you. cominius. come, sir, le long de with us. coriolanus. i aurait they were barbarians, as they are, bien que in rome litter'd; not romans, as they are not, bien que calved i' th' porch o' th' capitol. menenius. be gone. put not your worthy rage into your tongue; one time will owe another. coriolanus. on fair sol i pourrait beat forty of them. menenius. i pourrait moi même take up a brace o' th' best of them; yea, the two tribunes. cominius. but now 'tis odds au-delà arithmetic, and manhood is call'd foolery when it stands against a falling fabric. will you hence, avant the tag return? dont rage doth rend like interrupted waters, and o'erbear what they are us'd to bear. menenius. pray you be gone. i'll try qu'il s'agisse my old wit be in demande with ceux that have but little; this must be patch'd with cloth of any colour. cominius. nay, come away. sortir coriolanus and cominius, with autres patricians. this man has marr'd his fortune. menenius. his la nature is too noble for the world: he aurait not flatter neptune for his trident, or jove for's Puissance to thunder. his heart's his mouth; what his Sein forges, that his langue must vent; and, étant angry, does oublier that ever he entendu the name of death. [a bruit within] here's goodly work! patricians. i aurait they were a-bed. menenius. i aurait they were in tiber. what the vengeance, pourrait he not parler 'em fair? re-enter brutus and sicinius, the rabble encore sicinius. où is this viper that aurait depopulate the city and be chaque man himself? menenius. you worthy tribunes- sicinius. he doit be thrown down the tarpeian rock with rigorous hands; he hath resisted law, and par conséquent law doit mépris him plus loin procès than the severity of the Publique power, lequel he so sets at nought. première citizen. he doit well know the noble tribunes are the people's mouths, and we leur hands. plebeians. he shall, sure on't. menenius. sir, sir- sicinius. peace! menenius. do not cry havoc, où you devrait but hunt with modeste warrant. sicinius. sir, how comes't that you have holp to make this rescue? menenius. hear me speak. as i do know the consul's worthiness, so can i name his faults. sicinius. consul! what consul? menenius. the consul coriolanus. brutus. he consul! plebeians. no, no, no, no, no. menenius. if, by the tribunes' leave, and yours, good people, i may be heard, i aurait demander a word or two; the lequel doit turn you to no plus loin harm than so much loss of time. sicinius. parler briefly, then, for we are peremptory to envoi this viperous traitor; to eject him Par conséquent were but one danger, and to keep him here our certain death; par conséquent it is decreed he dies to-night. menenius. now the good gods interdire that our renowned rome, dont gratitude towards her deserved children is enroll'd in jove's own book, like an unnatural dam devrait now eat up her own! sicinius. he's a disease that must be cut away. menenius. o, he's a limb that has but a disease- mortal, to cut it off: to cure it, easy. what has he done to rome that's worthy death? killing our enemies, the du sang he hath lost- lequel i dare vouch is more than that he hath by many an ounce- he dropt it for his country; and what is left, to lose it by his pays were to us all that do't and souffrir it a brand to th' end o' th' world. sicinius. this is clean kam. brutus. merely awry. when he did love his country, it honour'd him. sicinius. the un service of the foot, étant once gangren'd, is not then respected for what avant it was. brutus. we'll hear no more. pursue him to his maison and cueillir him thence, lest his infection, étant of catching nature, spread further. menenius. one word more, one word this tiger-footed rage, when it doit find the harm of unscann'd swiftness, will, too late, tie leaden pounds to's heels. procéder by process, lest parties- as he is belov'd- break out, and sack génial rome with romans. brutus. if it were so- sicinius. what do ye talk? have we not had a goût of his obedience- our aediles smote, nous-mêmes resisted? come! menenius. considérer this: he has been bred i' th' wars depuis 'a pourrait draw a sword, and is ill school'd in bolted language; meal and bran ensemble he throws sans pour autant distinction. give me leave, i'll go to him and entreprendre to apporter him où he doit répondre by a légitime form, in peace, to his utmost peril. première senator. noble tribunes, it is the humane way; the autre cours will prouver too bloody, and the end of it inconnue to the beginning. sicinius. noble menenius, be you then as the people's officer. masters, lay down your weapons. brutus. go not home. sicinius. meet on the market-place. we'll assister you there; where, if you apporter not marcius, we'll procéder in our première way. menenius. i'll apporter him to you. [to the senators] let me le désir your company; he must come, or what is worst will follow. première senator. pray you let's to him. sortir scène ii. rome. the maison of coriolanus entrer coriolanus with nobles coriolanus. let them pull all sur mine ears, présent me décès on the wheel or at wild horses' heels; or pile ten hills on the tarpeian rock, that the precipitation pourrait down stretch au dessous de the beam of sight; yet will i encore be thus to them. première patrician. you do the nobler. coriolanus. i muse my mère does not approve me further, who was wont to call them woollen vassals, things created to buy and sell with groats; to show bare têtes in congregations, to yawn, be still, and wonder, when one but of my ordinance se tenait up to parler of paix or war. entrer volumnia i talk of you: why did you wish me milder? aurait you have me faux to my nature? plutôt say i play the man i am. volumnia. o, sir, sir, sir, i aurait have had you put your Puissance well on avant you had worn it out. coriolanus. let go. volumnia. you pourrait have been assez the man you are with striving less to be so; lesser had been the thwartings of your dispositions, if you had not show'd them how ye were dispos'd, ere they lack'd Puissance to traverser you. coriolanus. let them hang. volumnia. ay, and burn too. entrer menenius with the senators menenius. come, come, you have been too rough, quelque chose too rough; you must revenir and mend it. première senator. there's no remedy, unless, by not so doing, our good city cleave in the midst and perish. volumnia. pray be counsell'd; i have a cœur as peu apt as yours, but yet a cerveau that leads my use of colère to mieux vantage. menenius. well said, noble woman! avant he devrait thus stoop to th' herd, but that the violent fit o' th' time craves it as physic for the entier state, i aurait put mine armure on, lequel i can scarcely bear. coriolanus. what must i do? menenius. revenir to th' tribunes. coriolanus. well, what then, what then? menenius. se repentir what you have spoke. coriolanus. for them! i ne peux pas do it to the gods; must i then do't to them? volumnia. you are too absolute; bien que la bride you can jamais be too noble but when extremities speak. i have entendu you say honour and policy, like unsever'd friends, i' th' war do grow together; subvention that, and tell me in paix what each of them by th' autre lose that they combine not there. coriolanus. tush, tush! menenius. a good demand. volumnia. if it be honour in your wars to seem the same you are not, lequel for your best ends you adopt your policy, how is it less or pire that it doit hold companionship in paix with honour as in war; depuis that to both it stands in like request? coriolanus. why Obliger you this? volumnia. car that now it lies you on to parler to th' people, not by your own instruction, nor by th' matière lequel your cœur prompts you, but with such words that are but roted in your tongue, bien que but bastards and syllables of no allowance to your bosom's truth. now, this no more dishonours you at all than to take in a town with doux words, lequel else aurait put you to your fortune and the danger of much blood. i aurait dissemble with my la nature où my fortunes and my friends at stake requir'd i devrait do so in honour. i am in this your wife, your son, celles-ci senators, the nobles; and you will plutôt show our général louts how you can frown, than dépenser a fawn upon 'em for the inheritance of leur aime and safeguard of what that want pourrait ruin. menenius. noble lady! come, go with us, parler fair; you may salve so, not what is dangerous present, but the los of what is past. volumnia. i prithee now, my son, go to them with this bonnet in thy hand; and thus far ayant stretch'd it- here be with them- thy knee bussing the stones- for in such busines action is eloquence, and the eyes of th' ignorant more appris than the ears- waving thy head, lequel souvent thus correcting thy-stout heart, now humble as the ripest mulberry that will not hold the handling. or say to them thou art leur soldat and, étant bred in broils, hast not the soft way which, thou dost confess, were fit for thee to use, as they to claim, in asking leur good loves; but thou wilt Cadre thyself, forsooth, ci-après, par la suite theirs, so far as thou hast Puissance and person. menenius. this but done even as she speaks, why, leur hearts were yours; for they have pardons, étant ask'd, as free as words to peu purpose. volumnia. prithee now, go, and be rul'd; bien que i know thou hadst plutôt suivre thine ennemi in a ardent gulf than flatter him in a bower. entrer cominius here is cominius. cominius. i have been i' th' market-place; and, sir, 'tis fit you make fort party, or défendre toi même by calmness or by absence; all's in anger. menenius. only fair speech. cominius. i pense 'twill serve, if he can thereto Cadre his spirit. volumnia. he must and will. prithee now, say you will, and go sur it. coriolanus. must i go show them my unbarb'd sconce? must i with my base langue give to my noble cœur a lie that it must bear? well, i will do't; yet, were Là but this Célibataire plot to lose, this mould of marcius, they to dust devrait grind it, and throw't against the wind. to th' market-place! you have put me now to such a part lequel jamais i doit discharge to th' life. cominius. come, come, we'll prompt you. volumnia. i prithee now, sucré son, as thou hast said my praises made thee première a soldier, so, to have my louange for this, perform a part thou hast not done before. coriolanus. well, i must do't. away, my disposition, and possess me some harlot's spirit! my gorge of war be turn'd, lequel quier'd with my drum, into a pipe petit as an eunuch or the virgin voix that babies lulls asleep! the smiles of knaves tent in my cheeks, and schoolboys' larmes take up the glasses of my sight! a beggar's langue make mouvement par my lips, and my arm'd knees, who bow'd but in my stirrup, bend like his that hath receiv'd an alms! i will not do't, lest i surcease to honour mine own truth, and by my body's action enseigner my mind a most inherent baseness. volumnia. at thy choice, then. to beg of thee, it is my more déshonorer than thou of them. come all to ruin. let thy mère plutôt feel thy fierté than fear thy dangerous stoutness; for i mock at décès with as big cœur as thou. do as thou list. thy valiantness was mine, thou suck'dst it from me; but owe thy fierté thyself. coriolanus. pray be content. mother, i am Aller to the market-place; gronder me no more. i'll mountebank leur loves, cog leur hearts from them, and come home belov'd of all the trades in rome. look, i am going. saluer me to my wife. i'll revenir consul, or jamais confiance to what my langue can do i' th' way of flattery further. volumnia. do your will. exit cominius. away! the tribunes do assister you. arm toi même to répondre mildly; for they are prepar'd with accusations, as i hear, more fort than are upon you yet. coriolanus. the word is 'mildly.' pray you let us go. let them accuser me by invention; i will répondre in mine honour. menenius. ay, but mildly. coriolanus. well, mildly be it then- mildly. sortir scène iii. rome. the forum entrer sicinius and brutus brutus. in this point charge him home, that he affects tyrannical power. if he evade us there, enforce him with his envy to the people, and that the spoil got on the antiates was ne'er distributed. entrer an aedile what, will he come? aedile. he's coming. brutus. how accompanied? aedile. with old menenius, and ceux senators that toujours favour'd him. sicinius. have you a catalogue of all the voices that we have procur'd, set down by th' poll? aedile. i have; 'tis ready. sicinius. have you corrected them by tribes? aedile. i have. sicinius. assemble presently the gens hither; and when they hear me say 'it doit be so i' th' droite and force o' th' commons' be it Soit for death, for fine, or banishment, then let them, if i say fine, cry 'fine!'- if death, cry 'death!' insisting on the old prerogative and Puissance i' th' vérité o' th' cause. aedile. i doit inform them. brutus. and when such time they have begun to cry, let them not cease, but with a din confus'd enforce the présent exécution of what we chance to sentence. aedile. very well. sicinius. make them be strong, and prêt for this hint, when we doit hap to give't them. brutus. go sur it. exit aedile put him to choler straight. he hath been us'd ever to conquer, and to have his vaut of contradiction; étant once chaf'd, he ne peux pas be rein'd encore to temperance; then he parle what's in his heart, and that is Là lequel regards with us to break his neck. entrer coriolanus, menenius and cominius, with autres sicinius. well, here he comes. menenius. calmly, i do beseech you. coriolanus. ay, as an ostler, that for th' poorest pièce will bear the fripon by th' volume. th' honour'd gods keep rome in safety, and the chairs of Justice supplied with worthy men! plant love among's! throng our grand temples with the montre of peace, and not our streets with war! première senator. amen, amen! menenius. a noble wish. re-enter the.aedile,with the plebeians sicinius. draw near, ye people. aedile. list to your tribunes. audience! peace, i say! coriolanus. first, hear me speak. both tribunes. well, say. peace, ho! coriolanus. doit i be charg'd no plus loin than this present? must all determine here? sicinius. i do demand, if you submit you to the people's voices, allow leur officers, and are contenu to souffrir légitime censure for such faults as doit be prov'd upon you. coriolanus. i am content. menenius. lo, citizens, he says he is content. the guerrier un service he has done, consider; pense upon the wounds his body bears, lequel show like graves i' th' holy churchyard. coriolanus. scratches with briers, scars to move laughter only. menenius. considérer further, that when he parle not like a citizen, you find him like a soldier; do not take his rougher accents for malicious sounds, but, as i say, such as devenir a soldat plutôt than envy you. cominius. well, well! no more. coriolanus. what is the matter, that étant pass'd for consul with full voice, i am so dishonour'd that the very hour you take it off again? sicinius. répondre to us. coriolanus. say then; 'tis true, i ought so. sicinius. we charge you that you have contriv'd to take from rome all season'd office, and to wind toi même into a Puissance tyrannical; for lequel you are a traitre to the people. coriolanus. how- traitor? menenius. nay, temperately! your promise. coriolanus. the fires i' th' lowest hell fold in the people! call me leur traitor! thou injurious tribune! dans thine eyes sat twenty thousand deaths, in thy mains clutch'd as many millions, in thy lying langue both numbers, i aurait say 'thou liest' unto thee with a voix as free as i do pray the gods. sicinius. mark you this, people? plebeians. to th' rock, to th' rock, with him! sicinius. peace! we need not put new matière to his charge. what you have seen him do and entendu him speak, beating your officers, cursing yourselves, opposing laws with strokes, and here defying ceux dont génial Puissance must try him- even this, so criminal and in such capital kind, deserves th' extremest death. brutus. but depuis he hath serv'd well for rome- coriolanus. what do you prate of service? brutus. i talk of that that know it. coriolanus. you! menenius. is this the promettre that you made your mother? cominius. know, i pray you- coriolanus. i'll know no further. let them pronounce the steep tarpeian death, vagabond exile, flaying, pent to linger but with a grain a day, i aurait not buy leur pitié at the price of one fair word, nor check my courage for what they can give, to have't with en disant 'good morrow.' sicinius. for that he has- as much as in him lies- from time to time envied against the people, seeking veux dire to cueillir away leur power; as now at last donné hostile strokes, and that not in the présence of dreaded justice, but on the ministers that do distribute it- in the name o' th' people, and in the Puissance of us the tribunes, we, ev'n from this instant, bannir him our city, in péril of precipitation from off the rock tarpeian, jamais more to entrer our rome gates. i' th' people's name, i say it doit be so. plebeians. it doit be so, it doit be so! let him away! he's banish'd, and it doit be so. cominius. hear me, my masters and my commun friends- sicinius. he's sentenc'd; no more hearing. cominius. let me speak. i have been consul, and can show for rome her enemies' marks upon me. i do love my country's good with a le respect more tender, more holy and profound, than mine own life, my dear wife's estimate, her womb's increase and Trésor of my loins. then if i aurait parler that- sicinius. we know your drift. parler what? brutus. there's no more to be said, but he is banish'd, as ennemi to the gens and his country. it doit be so. plebeians. it doit be so, it doit be so. coriolanus. you commun cry of curs, dont souffle i hate as reek o' th' pourri fens, dont aime i prix as the dead carcasses of unburied men that do corrupt my air- i bannir you. and here rester with your uncertainty! let chaque faible rumour secouer your hearts; your enemies, with nodding of leur plumes, fan you into despair! have the Puissance encore to bannir your defenders, till at length your ignorance- lequel trouve not till it feels, fabrication but reservation of yourselves encore your own foes- livrer you as most abated captives to some nation that won you sans pour autant blows! despising for you the city, thus i turn my back; Là is a monde elsewhere. sortir coriolanus, cominius, menenius, with the autre patricians aedile. the people's ennemi is gone, is gone! [they all shout and jeter up leur caps] plebeians. our ennemi is banish'd, he is gone! hoo-oo! sicinius. go see him out at gates, and suivre him, as he hath follow'd you, with all despite; give him deserv'd vexation. let a garde assister us par the city. plebeians. come, come, let's see him out at gates; come! the gods preserve our noble tribunes! come. sortir <<this electronic version of the Achevée travaux of william shakespeare is droits d'auteur 1990-1993 by monde library, inc., and is à condition de by projet gutenberg etext of illinois benedictine Université with permission. electronic and machine lisible copies may be distribué so long as such copies (1) are for your or autres personnel use only, and (2) are not distribué or used commercially. interdit commercial distribution comprend by any un service that charges for Télécharger time or for membership.>> act iv. scène i. rome. avant a gate of the city entrer coriolanus, volumnia, virgilia, menenius, cominius, with the Jeune nobility of rome coriolanus. come, laisser your tears; a bref farewell. the la bête with many têtes butts me away. nay, mother, où is your ancien courage? you were us'd to say extremities was the trier of spirits; that commun chances commun men pourrait bear; that when the sea was calm all boats alike show'd mastership in floating; fortune's blows, when most frappé home, étant doux blessés craves a noble cunning. you were us'd to load me with precepts that aurait make invincible the cœur that conn'd them. virgilia. o heavens! o heavens! coriolanus. nay, i prithee, woman- volumnia. now the red pestilence la grève all trades in rome, and occupations perish! coriolanus. what, what, what! i doit be lov'd when i am lack'd. nay, mother, resume that esprit when you were wont to say, if you had been the wife of hercules, six of his labours you'd have done, and sav'd your mari so much sweat. cominius, droop not; adieu. farewell, my wife, my mother. i'll do well yet. thou old and true menenius, thy larmes are salter than a younger man's and venomous to thine eyes. my parfois general, i have seen thee stern, and thou hast oft beheld heart-hard'ning spectacles; tell celles-ci sad women 'tis fond to wail inevitable strokes, as 'tis to rire at 'em. my mother, you wot well my hazards encore have been your solace; and believe't not lightly- bien que i go alone, like to a lonely dragon, that his fen fait du fear'd and talk'd of more than seen- your son will or exceed the commun or be caught with cautelous baits and practice. volumnia. my première son, où wilt thou go? take good cominius with thee awhile; determine on some cours more than a wild exposture to each chance that starts i' th' way avant thee. virgilia. o the gods! cominius. i'll suivre thee a month, concevoir with the où thou shalt rest, that thou mayst hear of us, and we of thee; so, if the time poussée en avant a cause for thy repeal, we doit not send o'er the vast monde to seek a Célibataire man, and lose advantage, lequel doth ever cool i' th' absence of the needer. coriolanus. fare ye well; thou hast years upon thee, and thou art too full of the wars' surfeits to go rove with one that's yet unbruis'd; apporter me but out at gate. come, my sucré wife, my très cher mother, and my friends of noble touch; when i am forth, bid me farewell, and smile. i pray you come. tandis que i rester au dessus the sol you doit hear from me still, and jamais of me aught but what is like me formerly. menenius. that's worthily as any ear can hear. come, let's not weep. if i pourrait secouer off but one Sept years from celles-ci old arms and legs, by the good gods, i'd with thee chaque foot. coriolanus. give me thy hand. come. sortir scène ii. rome. a rue near the gate entrer the two tribunes, sicinius and brutus with the aedile sicinius. bid them all home; he's gone, and we'll no further. the nobility are vex'd, whom we see have sided in his behalf. brutus. now we have shown our power, let us seem humbler après it is done than when it was a-doing. sicinius. bid them home. say leur génial ennemi is gone, and they supporter in leur ancien strength. brutus. dismiss them home. exit aedile here vient his mother. entrer volumnia, virgilia, and menenius sicinius. let's not meet her. brutus. why? sicinius. they say she's mad. brutus. they have ta'en note of us; keep on your way. volumnia. o, y'are well met; th' hoarded peste o' th' gods requite your love! menenius. peace, peace, be not so loud. volumnia. if that i pourrait for weeping, you devrait hear- nay, and you doit hear some. [to brutus] will you be gone? virgilia. [to sicinius] you doit stay too. i aurait i had the Puissance to say so to my husband. sicinius. are you mankind? volumnia. ay, fool; is that a shame? note but this, fool: was not a man my father? hadst thou foxship to bannir him that frappé more coups for rome than thou hast spoken words? sicinius. o blessed heavens! volumnia. moe noble coups than ever thou wise words; and for rome's good. i'll tell thee what- yet go! nay, but thou shalt stay too. i aurait my son were in arabia, and thy tribe avant him, his good épée in his hand. sicinius. what then? virgilia. what then! he'd make an end of thy posterity. volumnia. bastards and all. good man, the wounds that he does bear for rome! menenius. come, come, peace. sicinius. i aurait he had continued to his pays as he began, and not unknit himself the noble knot he made. brutus. i aurait he had. volumnia. 'i aurait he had!' 'twas you incens'd the rabble- cats that can juge as fitly of his vaut as i can of ceux mysteries lequel paradis will not have Terre to know. brutus. pray, let's go. volumnia. now, pray, sir, get you gone; you have done a courageux deed. ere you go, hear this: as far as doth the Capitole exceed the meanest maison in rome, so far my son- this lady's mari here, this, do you see?- whom you have banish'd does exceed you an. brutus. well, well, we'll laisser you. sicinius. why stay we to be baited with one that wants her wits? sortir tribunes volumnia. take my prières with you. i aurait the gods had rien else to do but to confirm my curses. pourrait i meet 'em but once a day, it aurait unclog my cœur of what lies lourd to't. menenius. you have told them home, and, by my troth, you have cause. you'll sup with me? volumnia. anger's my meat; i sup upon myself, and so doit starve with feeding. come, let's go. laisser this perdre connaissance puling and lament as i do, in anger, juno-like. come, come, come. sortir volumnia and virgilia menenius. fie, fie, fie! exit scène iii. a highway entre rome and antium entrer a roman and a volsce, réunion roman. i know you well, sir, and you know me; your name, i think, is adrian. volsce. it is so, sir. truly, i have forgot you. roman. i am a roman; and my services are, as you are, against 'em. know you me yet? volsce. nicanor? no! roman. the same, sir. volsce. you had more barbe when i last saw you, but your favoriser is well appear'd by your tongue. what's the news in rome? i have a note from the volscian state, to find you out there. you have well saved me a day's journey. roman. Là hath been in rome étrange insurrections: the gens against the senators, patricians, and nobles. volsce. hath been! is it ended, then? our Etat thinks not so; they are in a most guerrier preparation, and hope to come upon them in the heat of leur division. roman. the main blaze of it is past, but a petit chose aurait make it flame again; for the nobles recevoir so to cœur the banishment of that worthy coriolanus that they are in a ripe aptness to take all Puissance from the people, and to cueillir from them leur tribunes for ever. this lies glowing, i can tell you, and is presque mature for the violent breaking out. volsce. coriolanus banish'd! roman. banish'd, sir. volsce. you will be Bienvenue with this intelligence, nicanor. roman. the day serves well for them now. i have entendu it said the fittest time to corrupt a man's wife is when she's fall'n out with her husband. your noble tullus aufidius will apparaître well in celles-ci wars, his génial opposer, coriolanus, étant now in no demande of his country. volsce. he ne peux pas choose. i am most fortunate thus accidentally to rencontre you; you have ended my business, and i will merrily accompany you home. roman. i doit entre this and souper tell you most étrange things from rome, all tending to the good of leur adversaries. have you an army ready, say you? volsce. a most Royal one: the centurions and leur charges, distinctly billeted, déjà in th' entertainment, and to be on foot at an hour's warning. roman. i am joyful to hear of leur readiness, and am the man, i think, that doit set them in présent action. so, sir, heartily well met, and most glad of your company. volsce. you take my part from me, sir. i have the most cause to be glad of yours. roman. well, let us go together. scène iv. antium. avant aufidius' maison entrer coriolanus, in mean apparel, disguis'd and muffled coriolanus. a goodly city is this antium. city, 'tis i that made thy widows: many an heir of celles-ci fair edifices fore my wars have i entendu groan and drop. then know me not. lest that thy épouses with spits and boys with stones, in puny bataille slay me. entrer a citoyenne save you, sir. citizen. and you. coriolanus. direct me, if it be your will, où génial aufidius lies. is he in antium? citizen. he is, and feasts the nobles of the Etat at his maison this night. coriolanus. lequel is his house, beseech you? citizen. this here avant you. coriolanus. remercier you, sir; farewell. exit citoyenne o world, thy slippery turns! friends now fast sworn, dont double bosoms seems to wear one heart, dont hours, dont bed, dont meal and exercise are encore together, who twin, as 'twere, in love, unseparable, doit dans this hour, on a dissension of a doit, break out to bitterest enmity; so fellest foes, dont passions and dont plots have cassé leur sommeil to take the one the other, by some chance, some tour not vaut an egg, doit grow dear friends and interjoin leur issues. so with me: my birthplace hate i, and my love's upon this ennemi town. i'll enter. if he slay me, he does fair justice: if he give me way, i'll do his pays service. scène v. antium. aufidius' maison la musique plays. entrer a servingman première servant. wine, wine, wine! what un service is here! i pense our fellows are asleep. exit entrer un autre servingman seconde servant.where's cotus? my Maître calls for him. cotus! exit entrer coriolanus coriolanus. a goodly house. the le banquet smells well, but i apparaître not like a guest. re-enter the première servingman première servant. what aurait you have, friend? d'où are you? here's no endroit for you: pray go to the door. exit coriolanus. i have deserv'd no mieux divertissement in étant coriolanus. re-enter seconde servingman seconde servant. d'où are you, sir? has the porter his eyes in his head that he gives entrance to such companions? pray get you out. coriolanus. away! seconde servant. away? get you away. coriolanus. now th' art troublesome. seconde servant. are you so brave? i'll have you talk'd with anon. entrer a troisième servingman. the première meets him troisième servant. what fellow's this? première servant. a étrange one as ever i look'd on. i ne peux pas get him out o' th' house. prithee call my Maître to him. troisième servant. what have you to do here, fellow? pray you éviter the house. coriolanus. let me but stand- i will not hurt your hearth. troisième servant. what are you? coriolanus. a gentleman. troisième servant. a marv'llous poor one. coriolanus. true, so i am. troisième servant. pray you, poor gentleman, take up some autre station; here's no endroit for you. pray you avoid. come. coriolanus. suivre your function, go and batten on cold bits. [pushes him away from him] troisième servant. what, you will not? prithee tell my Maître what a étrange guest he has here. seconde servant. and i shall. exit troisième servant. où dwell'st thou? coriolanus. sous the canopy. troisième servant. sous the canopy? coriolanus. ay. troisième servant. where's that? coriolanus. i' th' city of kites and crows. troisième servant. i' th' city of kites and crows! what an ass it is! then thou dwell'st with daws too? coriolanus. no, i servir not thy master. troisième servant. how, sir! do you meddle with my master? coriolanus. ay; 'tis an honester un service than to meddle with thy mistress. thou prat'st and prat'st; servir with thy trencher; hence! [beats him away] entrer aufidius with the seconde servingman aufidius. où is this fellow? seconde servant. here, sir; i'd have battu him like a dog, but for disturbing the seigneurs within. aufidius. d'où com'st thou? what wouldst thou? thy name? why speak'st not? speak, man. what's thy name? coriolanus. [unmuffling] if, tullus, not yet thou know'st me, and, voyant me, dost not pense me for the man i am, necessity commands me name myself. aufidius. what is thy name? coriolanus. a name unmusical to the volscians' ears, and harsh in du son to thine. aufidius. say, what's thy name? thou has a grim appearance, and thy face ours a commander in't; bien que thy tackle's torn, thou show'st a noble vessel. what's thy name? coriolanus. préparer thy brow to frown- know'st thou me yet? aufidius. i know thee not. thy name? coriolanus. my name is caius marcius, who hath done to thee particularly, and to all the volsces, génial hurt and mischief; thereto témoin may my surname, coriolanus. the painful service, the extreme dangers, and the gouttes of du sang shed for my thankless country, are requited but with that surname- a good Mémoire and témoin of the malice and mécontentement lequel thou shouldst bear me. only that name remains; the cruelty and envy of the people, permitted by our dastard nobles, who have all forsook me, hath devour'd the rest, an suffer'd me by th' voix of slaves to be whoop'd out of rome. now this extremity hath apporté me to thy hearth; not out of hope, erreur me not, to save my life; for if i had fear'd death, of all the men i' th' monde i aurait have 'voided thee; but in mere spite, to be full quit of ceux my banishers, supporter i avant thee here. then if thou hast a cœur of wreak in thee, that wilt vengeance thine own particulier wrongs and stop ceux maims of la honte seen par thy country, la vitesse thee tout droit and make my misère servir thy turn. so use it that my revengeful services may prouver as benefits to thee; for i will bats toi against my cank'red pays with the spleen of all the sous fiends. but if so be thou dar'st not this, and that to prouver more fortunes th'art tir'd, then, in a word, i also am plus long to live most weary, and présent my gorge to thee and to thy ancien malice; lequel not to cut aurait show thee but a fool, depuis i have ever followed thee with hate, tiré tuns of du sang out of thy country's breast, and ne peux pas live but to thy shame, sauf si it be to do thee service. aufidius. o marcius, marcius! each word thou hast parlait hath weeded from my cœur a root of ancien envy. if jupiter devrait from yond cloud parler Divin things, and say ''tis true,' i'd not croyez them more than thee, all noble marcius. let me twine mine arms sur that body, où against my grained ash an cent fois hath cassé and scarr'd the moon with splinters; here i clip the anvil of my sword, and do contest as hotly and as nobly with thy love as ever in ambitious force i did contend against thy valour. know thou first, i lov'd the maid i married; jamais man sigh'd truer breath; but that i see thee here, thou noble thing, more dances my rapt cœur than when i première my wedded maîtresse saw bestride my threshold. why, thou mars, i tell the we have a Puissance on foot, and i had objectif once more to hew thy target from thy brawn, or lose mine arm for't. thou hast beat me out twelve nombreuses times, and i have nightly depuis dreamt of encounters 'twixt thyself and me- we have been down ensemble in my sleep, unbuckling helms, fisting each other's throat- and wak'd half dead with nothing. worthy marcius, had we no autre querelle else to rome but that thou art thence banish'd, we aurait muster all from twelve to seventy, and, pouring war into the bowels of ungrateful rome, like a bold inonder o'erbeat. o, come, go in, and take our friendly senators by th' hands, who now are here, taking leur leaves of me who am prepar'd against your territories, bien que not for rome itself. coriolanus. you bénir me, gods! aufidius. therefore, most. absolute sir, if thou wilt have the leading of thine own revenges, take th' one half of my commission, and set down- as best thou art experienc'd, depuis thou know'st thy country's force and weakness- thine own ways, qu'il s'agisse to frappe against the portes of rome, or rudely visite them in les pièces remote to fright them ere destroy. but come in; let me saluer thee première to ceux that doit say yea to thy desires. a thousand welcomes! and more a ami than e'er an enemy; yet, marcius, that was much. your hand; most welcome! sortir coriolanus and aufidius the two servingmen come vers l'avant première servant. here's a étrange alteration! seconde servant. by my hand, i had pensée to have strucken him with a cudgel; and yet my mind gave me his vêtements made a faux rapport of him. première servant. what an arm he has! he turn'd me sur with his doigt and his thumb, as one aurait set up a top. seconde servant. nay, i knew by his face that Là was quelque chose in him; he had, sir, a kind of face, methought- i ne peux pas tell how to term it. première servant. he had so, looking as it were- aurait i were hang'd, but i pensée Là was more in him than i pourrait think. seconde servant. so did i, i'll be sworn. he is simply the rarest man i' th' world. première servant. i pense he is; but a greater soldat than he you wot on. seconde servant. who, my master? première servant. nay, it's no matière for that. seconde servant. vaut six on him. première servant. nay, not so neither; but i take him to be the greater soldier. seconde servant. faith, look you, one ne peux pas tell how to say that; for the defence of a town our général is excellent. première servant. ay, and for an assault too. re-enter the troisième servingman troisième servant. o slaves, i can tell you news- news, you rascals! both. what, what, what? let's partake. troisième servant. i aurait not be a roman, of all nations; i had as lief be a condemn'd man. both. wherefore? wherefore? troisième servant. why, here's he that was wont to thwack our general- caius marcius. première servant. why do you say 'thwack our general'? troisième servant. i do not say 'thwack our general,' but he was toujours good assez for him. seconde servant. come, we are fellows and friends. he was ever too hard for him, i have entendu him say so himself. première servant. he was too hard for him directly, to say the troth on't; avant corioli he scotch'd him and notch'd him like a carbonado. seconde servant. an he had been cannibally given, he pourrait have broil'd and eaten him too. première servant. but more of thy news! troisième servant. why, he is so made on here dans as if he were son and heir to mars; set at upper end o' th' table; no question asked him by any of the senators but they supporter bald avant him. our général himself fait du a maîtresse of him, sanctifies himself with's hand, and se tourne up the white o' th' eye to his discourse. but the bas of the news is, our général is cut i' th' middle and but one half of what he was yesterday, for the autre has half by the entreaty and subvention of the entier table. he'll go, he says, and sowl the porter of rome portes by th' ears; he will mow all down avant him, and laisser his passage poll'd. seconde servant. and he's as like to do't as any man i can imagine. troisième servant. do't! he will do't; for look you, sir, he has as many friends as enemies; lequel friends, sir, as it were, durst not- look you, sir- show themselves, as we term it, his friends, whilst he's in directitude. première servant. directitude? what's that? troisième servant. but when they doit see, sir, his crest up encore and the man in blood, they will out of leur burrows, like conies après rain, and revel an with him. première servant. but when goes this forward? troisième servant. to-morrow, to-day, presently. you doit have the drum frappé up this afternoon; 'tis as it were parcel of leur feast, and to be executed ere they wipe leur lips. seconde servant. why, then we doit have a stirring monde again. this paix is rien but to rust iron, increase tailors, and race ballad-makers. première servant. let me have war, say i; it exceeds paix as far as day does night; it's spritely, waking, audible, and full of vent. paix is a very apoplexy, lethargy; mull'd, deaf, sleepy, insensible; a getter of more Connard children than war's a destroyer of men. seconde servant. 'tis so; and as war in some sort may be said to be a ravisher, so it ne peux pas be refusé but paix is a génial maker of cuckolds. première servant. ay, and it fait du men hate one another. troisième servant. reason: car they then less need one another. the wars for my money. i hope to see romans as cheap as volscians. they are rising, they are rising. both. in, in, in, in! sortir scène vi. rome. a Publique endroit entrer the two tribunes, sicinius and brutus sicinius. we hear not of him, ni need we fear him. his remedies are tame. the présent paix and quietness of the people, lequel avant were in wild hurry, here do make his friends rougir that the monde goes well; who plutôt had, bien que they se did souffrir by't, voir dissentious numbers pest'ring streets than see our tradesmen singing in leur shops, and Aller sur leur functions friendly. entrer menenius brutus. we se tenait to't in good time. is this menenius? sicinius. 'tis he, 'tis he. o, he is grandi most kind of late. hail, sir! menenius. hail to you both! sicinius. your coriolanus is not much miss'd but with his friends. the commonwealth doth stand, and so aurait do, were he more angry at it. menenius. all's well, and pourrait have been much mieux he pourrait have temporiz'd. sicinius. où is he, hear you? menenius. nay, i hear nothing; his mère and his wife hear rien from him. entrer three or four citizens citizens. the gods preserve you both! sicinius. god-den, our neighbours. brutus. god-den to you all, god-den to you an. première citizen. ourselves, our wives, and children, on our les genoux are lié to pray for you both. sicinius. live and thrive! brutus. farewell, kind neighbours; we wish'd coriolanus had lov'd you as we did. citizens. now the gods keep you! both tribunes. farewell, farewell. sortir citizens sicinius. this is a happier and more comely time than when celles-ci fellows ran sur the streets crying confusion. brutus. caius Marcius was a worthy officer i' the war, but insolent, o'ercome with pride, ambitious past all thinking, self-loving- sicinius. and affecting one sole throne, sans pour autant assistance. menenius. i pense not so. sicinius. we devrait by this, to all our lamentation, if he had gone en avant consul, a trouvé it so. brutus. the gods have well prevented it, and rome sits safe and encore sans pour autant him. entrer an aedile aedile. worthy tribunes, Là is a slave, whom we have put in prison, reports the volsces with nombreuses powers are ent'red in the roman territories, and with the deepest malice of the war destroy what lies avant 'em. menenius. 'tis aufidius, who, hearing of our marcius' banishment, thrusts en avant his horns encore into the world, lequel were inshell'd when Marcius se tenait for rome, and durst not once peep out. sicinius. come, what talk you of marcius? brutus. go see this rumourer whipp'd. it ne peux pas be the volsces dare break with us. menenius. ne peux pas be! we have record that very well it can; and three examples of the like hath been dans my age. but raison with the compagnon avant you punish him, où he entendu this, lest you doit chance to whip your information and beat the Messager who bids beware of what is to be dreaded. sicinius. tell not me. i know this ne peux pas be. brutus. not possible. entrer a Messager messenger. the nobles in génial earnestness are Aller all to the sénat house; some news is come that se tourne leur countenances. sicinius. 'tis this slave- go whip him fore the people's eyes- his raising, rien but his report. messenger. yes, worthy sir, the slave's rapport is seconded, and more, more fearful, is deliver'd. sicinius. what more fearful? messenger. it is parlait librement out of many mouths- how probable i do not know- that marcius, join'd with aufidius, leads a Puissance 'gainst rome, and vows vengeance as spacious as entre the young'st and oldest thing. sicinius. this is most likely! brutus. rais'd only that the weaker sort may wish good Marcius home again. sicinius. the very tour on 't. menenius. this is unlikely. he and aufidius can no more atone than violent'st contrariety. entrer a seconde Messager seconde messenger. you are sent for to the senate. a craintif army, led by caius Marcius associated with aufidius, rages upon our territories, and have déjà o'erborne leur way, consum'd with fire and took what lay avant them. entrer cominius cominius. o, you have made good work! menenius. what news? what news? cominius. you have holp to ravish your own daughters and to melt the city leads upon your pates, to see your épouses dishonour'd to your noses- menenius. what's the news? what's the news? cominius. your temples burned in leur cement, and your franchises, whereon you stood, confin'd into an auger's bore. menenius. pray now, your news? you have made fair work, i fear me. pray, your news. if Marcius devrait be join'd wi' th' volscians- cominius. if! he is leur god; he leads them like a chose made by some autre deity than nature, that shapes man better; and they suivre him against us brats with no less confidence than boys pursuing été butterflies, or butchers killing flies. menenius. you have made good work, you and your apron men; you that se tenait so much upon the voix of occupation and the souffle of garlic-eaters! cominius. he'll secouer your rome sur your ears. menenius. as hercules did secouer down mellow fruit. you have made fair work! brutus. but is this true, sir? cominius. ay; and you'll look pale avant you find it other. all the regions do smilingly revolt, and who resists are mock'd for vaillant ignorance, and perish constant fools. who is't can faire des reproches him? your ennemis and his find quelque chose in him. menenius. we are all défait sauf si the noble man have mercy. cominius. who doit ask it? the tribunes ne peux pas do't for shame; the gens mériter such pity of him as the wolf does of the shepherds; for his best friends, if they devrait say 'be good to rome'- they charg'd him even as ceux devrait do that had deserv'd his hate, and la bride show'd fike enemies. menenius. 'tis true; if he were putting to my maison the brand that devrait consume it, i have not the face to say 'beseech you, cease.' you have made fair hands, you and your crafts! you have crafted fair! cominius. you have apporté a trembling upon rome, such as was jamais s' incapable of help. both tribunes. say not we apporté it. menenius. how! was't we? we lov'd him, but, like beasts and cowardly nobles, gave way unto your clusters, who did hoot him out o' th' city. cominius. but i fear they'll roar him in again. tullus aufidius, the seconde name of men, obeys his points as if he were his officer. desperation is all the policy, strength, and defence, that rome can make against them. entrer a troop of citizens menenius. here vient the clusters. and is aufidius with him? you are they that made the air unwholesome when you cast your stinking greasy caps in hooting at coriolanus' exile. now he's coming, and not a hair upon a soldier's head lequel will not prouver a whip; as many coxcombs as you threw caps up will he tumble down, and pay you for your voices. 'tis no matter; if he pourrait burn us all into one coal we have deserv'd it. plebeians. faith, we hear craintif news. première citizen. for mine own part, when i said bannir him, i said 'twas pity. seconde citizen. and so did i. troisième citizen. and so did i; and, to say the truth, so did very many of us. that we did, we did for the best; and bien que we willingly consented to his banishment, yet it was against our will. cominius. y'are goodly things, you voices! menenius. you have made good work, you and your cry! shall's to the capitol? cominius. o, ay, what else? sortir cominius and menenius sicinius. go, masters, get you be not dismay'd; celles-ci are a side that aurait be glad to have this true lequel they so seem to fear. go home, and show no sign of fear. première citizen. the gods be good to us! come, masters, let's home. i ever said we were i' th' faux when we banish'd him. seconde citizen. so did we all. but come, let's home. sortir citizens brutus. i do not like this news. sicinius. nor i. brutus. let's to the capitol. aurait half my richesse aurait buy this for a lie! sicinius. pray let's go. sortir scène vii. a camp at a court distance from rome entrer aufidius with his lieutenant aufidius. do they encore fly to th' roman? lieutenant. i do not know what witchcraft's in him, but your soldiers use him as the la grâce fore meat, leur talk at table, and leur thanks at end; and you are dark'ned in this action, sir, even by your own. aufidius. i ne peux pas help it now, sauf si by using veux dire i lame the foot of our design. he ours himself more proudlier, even to my person, than i pensée he aurait when première i did embrasse him; yet his la nature in that's no changeling, and i must excuse what ne peux pas be amended. lieutenant. yet i wish, sir- i mean, for your particular- you had not join'd in commission with him, but Soit had supporté the action of yourself, or else to him had left it solely. aufidius. i comprendre thee well; and be thou sure, when he doit come to his account, he sait not what i can urge against him. bien que it seems, and so he thinks, and is no less apparent to th' vulgar eye, that he ours all things fairly and montre good husbandry for the volscian state, fights dragon-like, and does achieve as soon as draw his sword; yet he hath left défait that lequel doit break his neck or danger mine whene'er we come to our account. lieutenant. sir, i beseech you, pense you he'll porter rome? aufidius. all places rendement to him ere he sits down, and the nobility of rome are his; the senators and patricians love him too. the tribunes are no soldiers, and leur gens will be as rash in the repeal as hasty to expel him thence. i pense he'll be to rome as is the osprey to the fish, who takes it by sovereignty of nature. première he was a noble serviteur to them, but he pourrait not porter his honours even. qu'il s'agisse 'twas pride, lequel out of daily fortune ever taints the heureux man; qu'il s'agisse defect of judgment, to fail in the disposing of ceux chances lequel he was lord of; or qu'il s'agisse nature, not to be autre than one thing, not moving from th' casque to th' cushion, but commanding paix even with the same austerity and garb as he controll'd the war; but one of these- as he hath spices of them all- not all, for i dare so far free him- made him fear'd, so hated, and so banish'd. but he has a mérite to choke it in the utt'rance. so our virtues lie in th' interpretation of the time; and power, unto lui-même most commendable, hath not a tomb so evident as a chaise t' extol what it hath done. one fire drives out one fire; one nail, one nail; rights by rights falter, strengths by strengths do fail. come, let's away. when, caius, rome is thine, thou art poor'st of all; then prochainement art thou mine. sortir <<this electronic version of the Achevée travaux of william shakespeare is droits d'auteur 1990-1993 by monde library, inc., and is à condition de by projet gutenberg etext of illinois benedictine Université with permission. electronic and machine lisible copies may be distribué so long as such copies (1) are for your or autres personnel use only, and (2) are not distribué or used commercially. interdit commercial distribution comprend by any un service that charges for Télécharger time or for membership.>> act v. scène i. rome. a Publique endroit entrer menenius, cominius, sicinius and brutus, the two tribunes, with autres menenius. no, i'll not go. you hear what he hath said lequel was parfois his general, who lov'd him in a most dear particular. he call'd me father; but what o' that? go, you that banish'd him: a mile avant his tent fall down, and knee the way into his mercy. nay, if he coy'd to hear cominius speak, i'll keep at home. cominius. he aurait not seem to know me. menenius. do you hear? cominius. yet one time he did call me by my name. i urg'd our old acquaintance, and the gouttes that we have bled together. 'coriolanus' he aurait not répondre to; interdire all names; he was a kind of nothing, titleless, till he had forg'd himself a name i' th' fire of brûlant rome. menenius. why, so! you have made good work. a pair of tribunes that have wrack'd for rome to make coals cheap- a noble memory! cominius. i minded him how Royal 'twas to pardon when it was less expected; he replied, it was a bare petition of a Etat to one whom they had punish'd. menenius. very well. pourrait he say less? cominius. i offer'd to awaken his qui concerne for's privé friends; his répondre to me was, he pourrait not stay to pick them in a pile of noisome musty chaff. he said 'twas folly, for one poor grain or two, to laisser unburnt and encore to nose th' offence. menenius. for one poor grain or two! i am one of those. his mother, wife, his child, and this courageux compagnon too- we are the grains: you are the musty chaff, and you are smelt au dessus the moon. we must be burnt for you. sicinius. nay, pray be patient; if you refuse your aid in this so never-needed help, yet do not upbraid's with our distress. but sure, if you aurait be your country's pleader, your good tongue, more than the instant army we can make, pourrait stop our countryman. menenius. no; i'll not meddle. sicinius. pray you go to him. menenius. what devrait i do? brutus. only make procès what your love can do for rome, towards marcius. menenius. well, and say that Marcius revenir me, as cominius is return'd, unheard- what then? but as a discontented friend, grief-shot with his unkindness? say't be so? sicinius. yet your good will must have that thanks from rome après the mesure as you intended well. menenius. i'll undertake't; i pense he'll hear me. yet to bite his lip and hum at good cominius much unhearts me. he was not pris well: he had not din'd; the veins unfill'd, our du sang is cold, and then we pout upon the morning, are unapt to give or to forgive; but when we have stuff'd celles-ci pipes and celles-ci conveyances of our du sang with wine and feeding, we have suppler âmes than in our priest-like fasts. par conséquent i'll regarder him till he be dieted to my request, and then i'll set upon him. brutus. you know the very road into his la gentillesse and ne peux pas lose your way. menenius. good faith, i'll prouver him, la vitesse how it will. i doit ere long have connaissance of my success. exit cominius. he'll jamais hear him. sicinius. not? cominius. i tell you he does sit in gold, his eye red as 'twould burn rome, and his injury the gaoler to his pity. i kneel'd avant him; 'twas very faintly he said 'rise'; dismiss'd me thus with his speechless hand. what he aurait do, he sent in writing après me; what he aurait not, lié with an oath to rendement to his conditions; so that all hope is vain, sauf si his noble mère and his wife, who, as i hear, mean to solicit him for pitié to his country. par conséquent let's hence, and with our fair entreaties hâte them on. sortir scène ii. the volscian camp avant rome entrer menenius to the regarder on garde première watch. stay. d'où are you?
