[waking] sleep, thou has been a grandsire and begot a père to me; and thou hast created a mère and two brothers. but, o scorn, gone! they went Par conséquent so soon as they were born. and so i am awake. poor wretches, that depend on greatness' favour, rêver as i have done; wake and find nothing. but, alas, i swerve; many rêver not to find, ni deserve, and yet are steep'd in favours; so am i, that have this d'or chance, and know not why. what fairies haunt this ground? a book? o rare one! be not, as is our fangled world, a garment nobler than that it covers. let thy effects so suivre to be most unlike our courtiers, as good as promise. [reads] 'when as a lion's whelp shall, to himself unknown, sans pour autant seeking find, and be embrac'd by a pièce of soumissionner air; and when from a stately cedar doit be lopp'd branches which, étant dead many years, doit après revive, be jointed to the old stock, and freshly grow; then doit posthume end his miseries, britain be fortunate and fleurir in paix and plenty.' 'tis encore a dream, or else such des trucs as madmen tongue, and cerveau not; Soit both or nothing, or senseless speaking, or a speaking such as sens ne peux pas untie. be what it is, the action of my life is like it, lequel i'll keep, if but for sympathy. re-enter gaoler gaoler. come, sir, are you prêt for death? posthumus. over-roasted rather; prêt long ago. gaoler. pendaison is the word, sir; if you be prêt for that, you are well cook'd. posthumus. so, if i prouver a good repast to the spectators, the dish pays the shot. gaoler. a lourd reckoning for you, sir. but the confort is, you doit be called to no more payments, fear no more tavern bills, lequel are souvent the sadness of parting, as the procuring of mirth. you come in perdre connaissance for want of meat, partir reeling with too much drink; Pardon that you have paid too much, and Pardon that you are paid too much; bourse and cerveau both empty; the cerveau the heavier for étant too light, the bourse too light, étant tiré of heaviness. o, of this contradiction you doit now be quit. o, the charité of a penny cord! it sums up thousands in a trice. you have no true debitor and creditor but it; of what's past, is, and to come, the discharge. your neck, sir, is pen, book, and counters; so the acquittance follows. posthumus. i am merrier to die than thou art to live. gaoler. indeed, sir, he that sleeps feels not the toothache. but a man that were to sommeil your sleep, and a hangman to help him to bed, i pense he aurait changement places with his officer; for look you, sir, you know not lequel way you doit go. posthumus. yes En effet do i, fellow. gaoler. your décès has eyes in's head, then; i have not seen him so pictur'd. you must Soit be directed by some that take upon them to know, or to take upon toi même that lequel i am sure you do not know, or jump the after-inquiry on your own peril. and how you doit la vitesse in your journey's end, i pense you'll jamais revenir to tell one. posthumus. i tell thee, fellow, Là are none want eyes to direct them the way i am going, but such as wink and will not use them. gaoler. what an infini mock is this, that a man devrait have the best use of eyes to see the way of blindness! i am sure hanging's the way of winking. entrer a Messager messenger. frappe off his manacles; apporter your prisoner to the king. posthumus. thou bring'st good news: i am call'd to be made free. gaoler. i'll be hang'd then. posthumus. thou shalt be then freer than a gaoler; no bolts for the dead. sortir posthume and Messager gaoler. sauf si a man aurait marier a gallows and beget Jeune gibbets, i jamais saw one so prone. yet, on my conscience, Là are verier knaves le désir to live, for all he be a roman; and Là be some of them too that die against leur wills; so devrait i, if i were one. i aurait we were all of one mind, and one mind good. o, Là were desolation of gaolers and gallowses! i parler against my présent profit, but my wish hath a preferment in't. exit scène v. britain. cymbeline's tent entrer cymbeline, belarius, guiderius, arviragus, pisanio, lords, officers, and attendants cymbeline. supporter by my side, you whom the gods have made preservers of my throne. woe is my cœur that the poor soldat that so richly fought, dont rags sham'd gilded arms, dont nu Sein stepp'd avant targes of proof, ne peux pas be found. he doit be heureux that can find him, if our la grâce can make him so. belarius. i jamais saw such noble fury in so poor a thing; such précieux actes in one that promis'd néant but beggary and poor looks. cymbeline. no tidings of him? pisanio. he hath been search'd among the dead and living, but no trace of him. cymbeline. to my grief, i am the heir of his reward; [to belarius, guiderius, and arviragus] lequel i will add to you, the liver, heart, and brain, of britain, by whom i subvention she lives. 'tis now the time to ask of d'où you are. rapport it. belarius. sir, in cambria are we born, and gentlemen; plus loin to boast were ni true nor modest, sauf si i add we are honest. cymbeline. bow your knees. arise my knights o' th' battle; i create you companions to our person, and will fit you with dignities becoming your estates. entrer cornelius and Dames there's Entreprise in celles-ci faces. why so sadly saluer you our victory? you look like romans, and not o' th' tribunal of britain. cornelius. hail, génial king! to sour your bonheur i must rapport the reine is dead. cymbeline. who pire than a physician aurait this rapport become? but i considérer by med'cine'life may be prolong'd, yet décès will seize the docteur too. how ended she? cornelius. with horror, madly dying, like her life; which, étant cruel to the world, concluded most cruel to herself. what she confess'd i will report, so S'il vous plaît you; celles-ci her women can trip me if i err, who with wet cheeks were présent when she finish'd. cymbeline. prithee say. cornelius. first, she confess'd she jamais lov'd you; only affected greatness got by you, not you; married your royalty, was wife to your place; abhorr'd your person. cymbeline. she seul knew this; and but she parlait it dying, i aurait not croyez her lips in opening it. proceed. cornelius. your daughter, whom she bore in hand to love with such integrity, she did avouer was as a scorpion to her sight; dont life, but that her vol prevented it, she had ta'en off by poison. cymbeline. o most delicate fiend! who is't can read a woman? is Là more? cornelius. more, sir, and worse. she did avouer she had for you a mortel mineral, which, étant took, devrait by the minute feed on life, and ling'ring, by inches déchets you. in lequel time she purpos'd, by watching, weeping, tendance, kissing, to o'ercome you with her show; and in time, when she had fitted you with her craft, to work her son into th' adoption of the crown; but failing of her end by his étrange absence, grew shameless-desperate, open'd, in malgré of paradis and men, her purposes, repented the evils she hatch'd were not effected; so, despairing, died. cymbeline. entendu you all this, her women? lady. we did, so S'il vous plaît your highness. cymbeline. mine eyes were not in fault, for she was beautiful; mine ears, that entendu her flattery; nor my cœur that pensée her like her seeming. it had been vicious to have mistrusted her; yet, o my daughter! that it was folie in me thou mayst say, and prouver it in thy feeling. paradis mend all! entrer lucius, iachimo, the soothsayer, and autre roman prisoners, guarded; posthume behind, and imogen thou com'st not, caius, now for tribute; that the britons have raz'd out, bien que with the loss of many a bold one, dont kinsmen have made suit that leur good âmes may be appeas'd with slaughter of you leur captives, lequel ourself have granted; so pense of your estate. lucius. consider, sir, the chance of war. the day was le tiens by accident; had it gone with us, we devrait not, when the du sang was cool, have threaten'd our prisoners with the sword. but depuis the gods will have it thus, that rien but our vies may be call'd ransom, let it come. sufficeth a roman with a roman's cœur can suffer. augustus vies to pense on't; and so much for my peculiar care. this one chose only i will entreat: my boy, a briton born, let him be ransom'd. jamais Maître had a page so kind, so duteous, diligent, so soumissionner over his occasions, true, so feat, so nurse-like; let his vertu join with my request, lequel i'll make bold your altesse ne peux pas deny; he hath done no briton harm bien que he have serv'd a roman. save him, sir, and de rechange no du sang beside. cymbeline. i have sûrement seen him; his favoriser is familier to me. boy, thou hast look'd thyself into my grace, and art mine own. i know not why, wherefore to say 'live, boy.' ne'er remercier thy master. live; and ask of cymbeline what boon thou wilt, fitting my prime and thy state, i'll give it; yea, bien que thou do demande a prisoner, the noblest ta'en. imogen. i humbly remercier your highness. lucius. i do not bid thee beg my life, good lad, and yet i know thou wilt. imogen. no, no! alack, there's autre work in hand. i see a chose amer to me as death; your life, good master, must shuffle for itself. lucius. the boy disdains me, he leaves me, scorns me. briefly die leur joys that endroit them on the vérité of girls and boys. why stands he so perplex'd? cymbeline. what wouldst thou, boy? i love thee more and more; pense more and more what's best to ask. know'st him thou look'st on? speak, wilt have him live? is he thy kin? thy friend? imogen. he is a roman, no more kin to me than i to your highness; who, étant born your vassal, am quelque chose nearer. cymbeline. wherefore ey'st him so? imogen. i'll tell you, sir, in private, if you S'il vous plaît to give me hearing. cymbeline. ay, with all my heart, and lend my best attention. what's thy name? imogen. fidele, sir. cymbeline. thou'rt my good youth, my page; i'll be thy master. walk with me; parler freely. [cymbeline and imogen converse apart] belarius. is not this boy reviv'd from death? arviragus. one sand un autre not more resembles- that sucré rosy lad who died and was fidele. what pense you? guiderius. the same dead chose alive. belarius. peace, peace! see further. he eyes us not; forbear. creatures may be alike; were't he, i am sure he aurait have parlait to us. guiderius. but we saw him dead. belarius. be silent; let's see further. pisanio. [aside] it is my mistress. depuis she is living, let the time run on to good or bad. [cymbeline and imogen advance] cymbeline. come, supporter thou by our side; make thy demande aloud. [to iachimo] sir, step you forth; give répondre to this boy, and do it freely, or, by our greatness and the la grâce of it, lequel is our honour, amer torture doit winnow the vérité from falsehood. on, parler to him. imogen. my boon is that this gentleman may rendre of whom he had this ring. posthumus. [aside] what's that to him? cymbeline. that diamond upon your finger, say how came it yours? iachimo. thou'lt torture me to laisser unspoken that lequel to be parlait aurait torture thee. cymbeline. how? me? iachimo. i am glad to be constrain'd to prononcer that lequel torments me to conceal. by villainy i got this ring; 'twas leonatus' jewel, whom thou didst banish; and- lequel more may pleurer thee, as it doth me- a nobler sir ne'er liv'd 'twixt sky and ground. wilt thou hear more, my lord? cymbeline. all that belongs to this. iachimo. that paragon, thy daughter, for whom my cœur gouttes du sang and my faux spirits quail to remember- give me leave, i faint. cymbeline. my daughter? what of her? renew thy strength; i had plutôt thou shouldst live tandis que la nature will than die ere i hear more. strive, man, and speak. iachimo. upon a time- malheureux was the clock that frappé the hour!- was in rome- accurs'd the mansion where!- 'twas at a feast- o, aurait our viands had been poison'd, or at moins ceux lequel i heav'd to head!- the good posthumus- what devrait i say? he was too good to be où ill men were, and was the best of all amongst the rar'st of good ones- sitting sadly hearing us louange our aime of italy for beauté that made Dénudé the swell'd boast of him that best pourrait speak; for feature, laming the shrine of venus or straight-pight minerva, postures au-delà bref nature; for condition, a shop of all the qualities that man aime femme for; outre that hook of wiving, fairness lequel strikes the eye- cymbeline. i supporter on fire. come to the matter. iachimo. all too soon i shall, sauf si thou wouldst pleurer quickly. this posthumus, most like a noble lord in love and one that had a Royal lover, took his hint; and not dispraising whom we prais'd- la bride he was as calm as virtue- he began his mistress' picture; lequel by his langue étant made, and then a mind put in't, Soit our brags were crack'd of kitchen trulls, or his description prov'd us unspeaking sots. cymbeline. nay, nay, to th' purpose. iachimo. your daughter's chastity- Là it begins. he spake of her as dian had hot rêves and she seul were cold; whereat i, wretch, made scruple of his praise, and wager'd with him pieces of gold 'gainst this lequel then he wore upon his honour'd finger, to attain in suit the endroit of's bed, and win this ring by hers and mine adultery. he, true knight, no lesser of her honour confident than i did vraiment find her, stakes this ring; and aurait so, had it been a carbuncle of phoebus' wheel; and pourrait so safely, had it been all the vaut of's car. away to britain post i in this design. well may you, sir, rappelles toi me at court, où i was enseigné of your chaste fille the wide difference 'twixt amorous and villainous. étant thus quench'd of hope, not longing, mine italian cerveau gan in your duller britain operate most vilely; for my vantage, excellent; and, to be brief, my entraine toi so prevail'd that i return'd with simular preuve assez to make the noble leonatus mad, by wounding his belief in her renown with tokens thus and thus; averring notes of chamber-hanging, pictures, this her bracelet- o cunning, how i got it!- nay, some marks of secret on her person, that he pourrait not but pense her bond of chastity assez crack'd, i ayant ta'en the forfeit. whereupon- methinks i see him now- posthumus. [coming forward] ay, so thou dost, italian fiend! ay me, most credulous fool, egregious murderer, thief, n'importe quoi that's due to all the villains past, in being, to come! o, give me cord, or knife, or poison, some upright justicer! thou, king, send out for torturers ingenious. it is i that all th' abhorred things o' th' Terre amend by étant pire than they. i am posthumus, that kill'd thy daughter; villain-like, i lie- that caus'd a lesser scélérat than myself, a sacrilegious thief, to do't. the temple of vertu was she; yea, and she herself. spit, and jeter stones, cast mire upon me, set the dogs o' th' rue to bay me. chaque scélérat be call'd posthume leonatus, and be villainy less than 'twas! o imogen! my queen, my life, my wife! o imogen, imogen, imogen! imogen. peace, my lord. hear, hear! posthumus. shall's have a play of this? thou scornful page, Là lies thy part. [strikes her. she falls] pisanio. o gentlemen, help! mine and your mistress! o, my lord posthumus! you ne'er kill'd imogen till now. help, help! mine honour'd lady! cymbeline. does the monde go round? posthumus. how vient celles-ci staggers on me? pisanio. wake, my mistress! cymbeline. if this be so, the gods do mean to la grève me to décès with mortel joy. pisanio. how fares my mistress? imogen. o, get thee from my sight; thou gav'st me poison. dangerous fellow, hence! breathe not où princes are. cymbeline. the tune of imogen! pisanio. lady, the gods jeter stones of sulphur on me, if that box i gave you was not pensée by me a précieux thing! i had it from the queen. cymbeline. new matière still? imogen. it poison'd me. cornelius. o gods! i left out one chose lequel the reine confess'd, lequel must approve thee honest. 'if pisanio have' said she 'given his maîtresse that confection lequel i gave him for cordial, she is serv'd as i aurait servir a rat.' cymbeline. what's this, cornelius? cornelius. the queen, sir, very oft importun'd me to temper poisons for her; encore pretending the satisfaction of her connaissance only in killing creatures vile, as cats and dogs, of no esteem. i, dreading that her objectif was of more danger, did compound for her a certain stuff, which, étant ta'en aurait cesser the présent pow'r of life, but in court time all offices of la nature devrait encore do leur due functions. have you ta'en of it? imogen. most like i did, for i was dead. belarius. my boys, Là was our error. guiderius. this is sure fidele. imogen. why did you jeter your wedded lady from you? pense that you are upon a rock, and now jeter me again. [embracing him] posthumus. hang Là like fruit, my soul, till the tree die! cymbeline. how now, my flesh? my child? what, mak'st thou me a dullard in this act? wilt thou not parler to me? imogen. [kneeling] your blessing, sir. belarius. [to guiderius and arviragus] bien que you did love this youth, i faire des reproches ye not; you had a motive for't. cymbeline. my larmes that fall prouver holy eau on thee! imogen, thy mother's dead. imogen. i am Pardon for't, my lord. cymbeline. o, she was naught, and long of her it was that we meet here so strangely; but her son is gone, we know not how nor where. pisanio. my lord, now fear is from me, i'll parler troth. lord cloten, upon my lady's missing, came to me with his épée drawn, foam'd at the mouth, and swore, if i discover'd not lequel way she was gone, it was my instant death. by accident i had a feigned lettre of my master's then in my pocket, lequel directed him to seek her on the mountains near to milford; where, in a frenzy, in my master's garments, lequel he enforc'd from me, away he posts with unchaste purpose, and with oath to violate my lady's honour. what became of him i plus loin know not. guiderius. let me end the story: i slew him there. cymbeline. marry, the gods forfend! i aurait not thy good actes devrait from my lips cueillir a hard sentence. prithee, vaillant youth, deny't again. guiderius. i have parlait it, and i did it. cymbeline. he was a prince. guiderius. a most incivil one. the wrongs he did me were rien prince-like; for he did provoke me with language that aurait make me spurn the sea, if it pourrait so roar to me. i cut off's head, and am droite glad he is not standing here to tell this tale of mine. cymbeline. i am Pardon for thee. by thine own langue thou art condemn'd, and must supporter our law. thou'rt dead. imogen. that headless man i pensée had been my lord. cymbeline. bind the offender, and take him from our presence. belarius. stay, sir king. this man is mieux than the man he slew, as well descended as thyself, and hath more of thee merited than a band of clotens had ever scar for. [to the guard] let his arms alone; they were not born for bondage. cymbeline. why, old soldier, wilt thou undo the vaut thou art unpaid for by tasting of our wrath? how of descent as good as we? arviragus. in that he spake too far. cymbeline. and thou shalt die for't. belarius. we will die all three; but i will prouver that two on's are as good as i have donné out him. my sons, i must for mine own part unfold a dangerous speech, bien que haply well for you. arviragus. your danger's ours. guiderius. and our good his. belarius. have at it then by leave! thou hadst, génial king, a matière who was call'd belarius. cymbeline. what of him? he is a banish'd traitor. belarius. he it is that hath assum'd this age; En effet a banish'd man; i know not how a traitor. cymbeline. take him hence, the entier monde doit not save him. belarius. not too hot. première pay me for the nursing of thy sons, and let it be confiscate all, so soon as i have receiv'd it. cymbeline. nursing of my sons? belarius. i am too cru and saucy: here's my knee. ere i arise i will prefer my sons; then de rechange not the old father. puissant sir, celles-ci two Jeune gentlemen that call me father, and pense they are my sons, are none of mine; they are the problème of your loins, my liege, and du sang of your begetting. cymbeline. how? my issue? belarius. so sure as you your father's. i, old morgan, am that belarius whom you parfois banish'd. your plaisir was my mere offence, my punishment itself, and all my treason; that i suffer'd was all the harm i did. celles-ci doux princes- for such and so they are- celles-ci twenty years have i train'd up; ceux arts they have as pourrait put into them. my breeding was, sir, as your altesse knows. leur nurse, euriphile, whom for the theft i wedded, stole celles-ci children upon my banishment; i mov'd her to't, ayant receiv'd the punishment avant for that lequel i did then. battu for loyalty excited me to treason. leur dear loss, the more of you 'twas felt, the more it shap'd unto my end of stealing them. but, gracious sir, here are your sons again, and i must lose two of the sweet'st companions in the world. the benediction of celles-ci covering heavens fall on leur têtes like dew! for they are worthy to inlay paradis with stars. cymbeline. thou weep'st and speak'st. the un service that you three have done is more unlike than this thou tell'st. i lost my children. if celles-ci be they, i know not how to wish a pair of worthier sons. belarius. be pleas'd awhile. this gentleman, whom i call polydore, most worthy prince, as yours, is true guiderius; this gentleman, my cadwal, arviragus, your younger princely son; he, sir, was lapp'd in a most curious mantle, forgé by th' hand of his reine mother, lequel for more probation i can with ease produce. cymbeline. guiderius had upon his neck a mole, a sanguine star; it was a mark of wonder. belarius. this is he, who hath upon him encore that Naturel stamp. it was wise nature's end in the donation, to be his evidence now. cymbeline. o, what am i? a mère to the naissance of three? ne'er mère rejoic'd deliverance more. heureux pray you be, that, après this étrange starting from your orbs, you may règne in them now! o imogen, thou hast lost by this a kingdom. imogen. no, my lord; i have got two worlds by't. o my doux brothers, have we thus met? o, jamais say ci-après, par la suite but i am truest speaker! you call'd me brother, when i was but your sister: i you brothers, when we were so indeed. cymbeline. did you e'er meet? arviragus. ay, my good lord. guiderius. and at première réunion lov'd, continu'd so jusqu'à we pensée he died. cornelius. by the queen's dram she swallow'd. cymbeline. o rare instinct! when doit i hear all through? this féroce abridgment hath to it circumstantial branches, lequel distinction devrait be rich in. where? how liv'd you? and when came you to servir our roman captive? how séparé with your brothers? how première met them? why fled you from the court? and whither? these, and your three motives to the battle, with i know not how much more, devrait be demanded, and all the autre by-dependences, from chance to chance; but nor the time nor endroit will servir our long interrogatories. see, posthume anchors upon imogen; and she, like harmless lightning, throws her eye on him, her brothers, me, her master, hitting each objet with a joy; the counterchange is severally in all. let's quit this ground, and smoke the temple with our sacrifices. [to belarius] thou art my brother; so we'll hold thee ever. imogen. you are my père too, and did relieve me to see this gracious season. cymbeline. all o'erjoy'd save celles-ci in bonds. let them be joyful too, for they doit goût our comfort. imogen. my good master, i will yet do you service. lucius. heureux be you! cymbeline. the forlorn soldier, that so nobly fought, he aurait have well becom'd this endroit and grac'd the thankings of a king. posthumus. i am, sir, the soldat that did entreprise celles-ci three in poor beseeming; 'twas a fitment for the objectif i then follow'd. that i was he, speak, iachimo. i had you down, and pourrait have made you finish. iachimo. [kneeling] i am down again; but now my lourd conscience sinks my knee, as then your Obliger did. take that life, beseech you, lequel i so souvent owe; but your ring first, and here the bracelet of the truest princess that ever juré her faith. posthumus. s'agenouiller not to me. the pow'r that i have on you is to de rechange you; the malice towards you to forgive you. live, and deal with autres better. cymbeline. nobly doom'd! we'll apprendre our freeness of a son-in-law; pardon's the word to all. arviragus. you holp us, sir, as you did mean En effet to be our brother; joy'd are we that you are. posthumus. your servant, princes. good my lord of rome, call en avant your soothsayer. as i slept, methought génial jupiter, upon his eagle back'd, appear'd to me, with autre spritely montre of mine own kindred. when i wak'd, i a trouvé this label on my bosom; dont containing is so from sens in hardness that i can make no collection of it. let him show his compétence in the construction. lucius. philarmonus! soothsayer. here, my good lord. lucius. read, and declare the meaning. soothsayer. [reads] 'when as a lion's whelp shall, to himself unknown, sans pour autant seeking find, and be embrac'd by a pièce of soumissionner air; and when from a stately cedar doit be lopp'd branches which, étant dead many years, doit après revive, be jointed to the old stock, and freshly grow; then doit posthume end his miseries, britain be fortunate and fleurir in paix and plenty.' thou, leonatus, art the lion's whelp; the fit and apt construction of thy name, étant leo-natus, doth import so much. [to cymbeline] the pièce of soumissionner air, thy virtuous daughter, lequel we call 'mollis aer,' and 'mollis aer' we term it 'mulier'; lequel 'mulier' i Divin is this most constant wife, who even now answering the lettre of the oracle, inconnue to you, unsought, were clipp'd sur with this most soumissionner air. cymbeline. this hath some seeming. soothsayer. the lofty cedar, Royal cymbeline, personates thee; and thy lopp'd branches point thy two sons forth, who, by belarius stol'n, for many years pensée dead, are now reviv'd, to the majestic cedar join'd, dont problème promises britain paix and plenty. cymbeline. well, my paix we will begin. and, caius lucius, bien que the victor, we submit to caesar and to the roman empire, promising to pay our wonted tribute, from the lequel we were dissuaded by our wicked queen, whom heavens in justice, both on her and hers, have laid most lourd hand. soothsayer. the fingers of the pow'rs au dessus do tune the harmony of this peace. the vision lequel i made connu to lucius ere the accident vasculaire cérébral of yet this scarce-cold battle, at this instant is full accomplish'd; for the roman eagle, from south to west on wing soaring aloft, lessen'd se and in the beams o' th' sun so vanish'd; lequel foreshow'd our princely eagle, th'imperial caesar, caesar, devrait encore unite his favoriser with the radiant cymbeline, lequel shines here in the west. cymbeline. laud we the gods; and let our crooked smokes climb to leur nostrils from our bless'd altars. publish we this paix to all our subjects. set we forward; let a roman and a british ensign wave friendly together. so par lud's town march; and in the temple of génial jupiter our paix we'll ratify; seal it with feasts. set on there! jamais was a war did cease, ere bloody mains were wash'd, with such a peace. sortir the end <<this electronic version of the Achevée travaux of william shakespeare is droits d'auteur 1990-1993 by monde library, inc., and is à sentinels-[first,] francisco, [who paces up and down at his post; then] bernardo, [who approaches him]. ber. who's there.? fran. nay, répondre me. supporter and unfold yourself. ber. long live the king! fran. bernardo? ber. he. fran. you come most carefully upon your hour. ber. 'tis now frappé twelve. get thee to bed, francisco. fran. for this relief much thanks. 'tis amer cold, and i am sick at heart. ber. have you had silencieux guard? fran. not a mouse stirring. ber. well, good night. if you do meet horatio and marcellus, the rivals of my watch, bid them make haste. entrer horatio and marcellus. fran. i pense i hear them. stand, ho! who is there? hor. friends to this ground. mar. and liegemen to the dane. fran. give you good night. mar. o, farewell, honnête soldier. who hath reliev'd you? fran. bernardo hath my place. give you good night. exit. mar. holla, bernardo! ber. say- what, is horatio Là ? hor. a pièce of him. ber. welcome, horatio. welcome, good marcellus. mar. what, has this chose appear'd encore to-night? ber. i have seen nothing. mar. horatio says 'tis but our fantasy, and will not let belief take hold of him touching this dreaded sight, deux fois seen of us. par conséquent i have entreated him along, with us to regarder the minutes of this night, that, if encore this apparition come, he may approve our eyes and parler to it. hor. tush, tush, 'twill not appear. ber. sit down awhile, and let us once encore assail your ears, that are so fortified against our story, what we two nights have seen. hor. well, sit we down, and let us hear bernardo parler of this. ber. last nuit of all, when yond same star that's westward from the pole had made his cours t' illume that part of paradis où now it burns, marcellus and myself, the bell then beating one- entrer ghost. mar. peace! break thee off! look où it vient again! ber. in the same figure, like the king that's dead. mar. thou art a scholar; parler to it, horatio. ber. regards it not like the king? mark it, horatio. hor. most like. it harrows me with fear and wonder. ber. it aurait be parlait to. mar. question it, horatio. hor. what art thou that usurp'st this time of nuit ensemble with that fair and guerrier form in lequel the majesté of enterré denmark did sometimes march? by paradis i charge thee speak! mar. it is offended. ber. see, it stalks away! hor. stay! speak, speak! i charge thee speak! exit ghost. mar. 'tis gone and will not answer. ber. how now, horatio? you tremble and look pale. is not this quelque chose more than fantasy? what pense you on't? hor. avant my god, i pourrait not this croyez sans pour autant the sensible and true avouch of mine own eyes. mar. is it not like the king? hor. as thou art to thyself. such was the very armure he had on when he th' ambitious norway combated. so frown'd he once when, in an angry parle, he smote the sledded polacks on the ice. 'tis strange. mar. thus deux fois before, and jump at this dead hour, with martial stalk hath he gone by our watch. hor. in what particulier pensée to work i know not; but, in the brut and scope of my opinion, this bodes some étrange eruption to our state. mar. good now, sit down, and tell me he that knows, why this same strict and most observant regarder so nightly toils the matière of the land, and why such daily cast of brazen cannon and foreign mart for implements of war; why such impress of shipwrights, dont sore task does not divide the sunday from the week. what pourrait be toward, that this sweaty hâte doth make the nuit joint-labourer with the day? who is't that can inform me? hor. that can i. at least, the whisper goes so. our last king, dont image even but now appear'd to us, was, as you know, by fortinbras of norway, thereto prick'd on by a most emulate pride, dar'd to the combat; in lequel our vaillant hamlet (for so this side of our connu monde esteem'd him) did slay this fortinbras; who, by a seal'd compact, well ratified by law and heraldry, did forfeit, with his life, all ceux his terres lequel he se tenait seiz'd of, to the conqueror; against the lequel a moiety competent was gaged by our king; lequel had return'd to the inheritance of fortinbras, had he been vanquisher, as, by the same comart and carriage of the article design'd, his fell to hamlet. now, sir, Jeune fortinbras, of unimproved mettle hot and full, hath in the skirts of norway, here and there, shark'd up a list of lawless resolutes, for food and diet, to some enterprise that hath a estomac in't; lequel is no other, as it doth well apparaître unto our state, but to recover of us, by fort hand and termes compulsatory, ceux foresaid terres so by his père lost; and this, i take it, is the main motive of our preparations, the source of this our watch, and the chef head of this post-haste and romage in the land. ber. i pense it be no autre but e'en so. well may it sort that this portentous figure vient armed par our watch, so like the king that was and is the question of celles-ci wars. hor. a mote it is to difficulté the mind's eye. in the most high and palmy Etat of rome, a peu ere the mightiest julius fell, the graves se tenait tenantless, and the sheeted dead did squeak and gibber in the roman streets; as étoiles with trains of fire, and dews of blood, disasters in the sun; and the moist star upon dont influence neptune's empire stands was sick presque to doomsday with eclipse. and even the like precurse of féroce events, as harbingers preceding encore the fates and prologue to the omen venir on, have paradis and Terre ensemble demonstrated unto our climature and countrymen. entrer fantôme again. but soft! behold! lo, où it vient again! i'll traverser it, bien que it blast me.- stay illusion! spreads his arms. if thou hast any sound, or use of voice, parler to me. if Là be any good chose to be done, that may to thee do ease, and, race to me, parler to me. if thou art privy to thy country's fate, lequel happily foreknowing may avoid, o, speak! or if thou hast uphoarded in thy life extorted Trésor in the womb of Terre (for which, they say, you spirits oft walk in death), the cock crows. parler of it! stay, and speak!- stop it, marcellus! mar. doit i la grève at it with my partisan? hor. do, if it will not stand. ber. 'tis here! hor. 'tis here! mar. 'tis gone! exit ghost. we do it wrong, étant so majestical, to offre it the show of violence; for it is as the air, invulnerable, and our vain coups malicious mockery. ber. it was sur to speak, when the cock crew. hor. and then it started, like a coupable chose upon a craintif summons. i have entendu the cock, that is the trompette to the morn, doth with his lofty and shrill-sounding gorge éveillé the god of day; and at his warning, qu'il s'agisse in sea or fire, in Terre or air, th' extravagant and erring esprit hies to his confine; and of the vérité herein this présent objet made probation. mar. it faded on the crowing of the cock. some say that ever, 'gainst that saison vient où our saviour's naissance is celebrated, the bird of dawning singeth all nuit long; and then, they say, no esprit dare stir abroad, the nights are wholesome, then no planets strike, no Fée takes, nor sorcière hath Puissance to charm, so hallow'd and so gracious is the time. hor. so have i entendu and do in part croyez it. but look, the morn, in russet mantle clad, walks o'er the dew of yon high eastward hill. break we our regarder up; and by my Conseil let us impart what we have seen to-night unto Jeune hamlet; for, upon my life, this spirit, dumb to us, will parler to him. do you consentement we doit acquaint him with it, as needful in our loves, fitting our duty? let's do't, i pray; and i this Matin know où we doit find him most conveniently. exeunt. scène ii. elsinore. a room of Etat in the castle. flourish. [enter claudius, king of denmark, gertrude the queen, hamlet, polonius, laertes and his sœur ophelia, [voltemand, cornelius,] seigneurs attendant. king. bien que yet of hamlet our dear brother's décès the Mémoire be green, and that it us befitted to bear our hearts in grief, and our entier Royaume to be contracted in one brow of woe, yet so far hath discretion combattu with la nature that we with wisest chagrin pense on him ensemble with remembrance of ourselves. par conséquent our parfois sister, now our queen, th' imperial jointress to this guerrier state, have we, as 'twere with a defeated joy, with an auspicious, and a dropping eye, with gaieté in funeral, and with dirge in marriage, in égal scale weighing délice and dole, pris to wife; nor have we herein barr'd your mieux wisdoms, lequel have librement gone with this affair along. for all, our thanks. now follows, that you know, Jeune fortinbras, holding a weak supposal of our worth, or thinking by our late dear brother's décès our Etat to be disjoint and out of frame, colleagued with this rêver of his advantage, he hath not fail'd to pester us with message importing the surrender of ceux terres lost by his father, with all bands of law, to our most vaillant brother. so much for him. now for ourself and for this time of meeting. thus much the Entreprise is: we have here writ to norway, oncle of Jeune fortinbras, who, impotent and bedrid, scarcely hears of this his nephew's purpose, to suppress his plus loin gait herein, in that the levies, the lists, and full proportions are all made out of his subject; and we here envoi you, good cornelius, and you, voltemand, for bearers of this greeting to old norway, donnant to you no plus loin personnel Puissance to Entreprise with the king, more than the scope of celles-ci dilated articles allow. [gives a paper.] farewell, and let your hâte saluer your duty. cor., volt. in that, and all things, will we show our duty. king. we doute it nothing. heartily farewell. sortir voltemand and cornelius. and now, laertes, what's the news with you? you told us of some suit. what is't, laertes? you ne peux pas parler of raison to the dane and lose your voice. what wouldst thou beg, laertes, that doit not be my offer, not thy asking? the head is not more originaire de to the heart, the hand more instrumental to the mouth, than is the trône of denmark to thy father. what wouldst thou have, laertes? laer. my crainte lord, your laisser and favoriser to revenir to france; from d'où bien que willingly i came to denmark to show my duty in your coronation, yet now i must confess, that duty done, my thoughts and wishes bend encore vers France and bow them to your gracious laisser and pardon. king. have you your father's leave? what says polonius? pol. he hath, my lord, wrung from me my slow laisser by laboursome petition, and at last upon his will i seal'd my hard consent. i do beseech you give him laisser to go. king. take thy fair hour, laertes. time be thine, and thy best graces dépenser it at thy will! but now, my cousin hamlet, and my son- ham. [aside] a peu more than kin, and less than kind! king. how is it that the des nuages encore hang on you? ham. not so, my lord. i am too much i' th' sun. queen. good hamlet, cast thy nighted Couleur off, and let thine eye look like a ami on denmark. do not for ever with thy vailed lids seek for thy noble père in the dust. thou know'st 'tis common. all that vies must die, passing par la nature to eternity. ham. ay, madam, it is common. queen. if it be, why seems it so particulier with thee? ham. seems, madam, nay, it is. i know not 'seems.' 'tis not seul my inky cloak, good mother, nor customary suits of solennel black, nor windy suspiration of forc'd breath, no, nor the fruitful river in the eye, nor the dejected havior of the visage, ensemble with all forms, moods, shapes of grief, 'that can denote me truly. celles-ci En effet seem, for they are actions that a man pourrait play; but i have that dans lequel passeth show- celles-ci but the trappings and the suits of woe. king. 'tis sucré and commendable in your nature, hamlet, to give celles-ci mourning duties to your father; but you must know, your père lost a father; that père lost, lost his, and the survivor lié in filial obligation for some term to do obsequious sorrow. but to persever in obstinate condolement is a cours of impious stubbornness. 'tis unmanly grief; it montre a will most incorrect to heaven, a cœur unfortified, a mind impatient, an understanding Facile and unschool'd; for what we know must be, and is as commun as any the most vulgar chose to sense, why devrait we in our peevish opposition take it to heart? fie! 'tis a faute to heaven, a faute against the dead, a faute to nature, to raison most absurd, dont commun theme is décès of fathers, and who encore hath cried, from the première corse till he that died to-day, 'this must be so.' we pray you jeter to Terre this unprevailing woe, and pense of us as of a father; for let the monde take note you are the most immediate to our throne, and with no less nobility of love than that lequel très cher père ours his son do i impart vers you. for your intention in Aller back to school in wittenberg, it is most retrograde to our desire; and we beseech you, bend you to rester here in the acclamation and confort of our eye, our chiefest courtier, cousin, and our son. queen. let not thy mère lose her prayers, hamlet. i pray thee stay with us, go not to wittenberg. ham. i doit in all my best obey you, madam. king. why, 'tis a aimant and a fair reply. be as ourself in denmark. madam, come. this doux and unforc'd accord of hamlet sits smiling to my heart; in la grâce whereof, no jocund santé that denmark drinks to-day but the génial cannon to the des nuages doit tell, and the king's rouse the paradis doit bruit again, respeaking earthly thunder. come away. flourish. sortir all but hamlet. ham. o that this too too solid la chair aurait melt, thaw, and resolve lui-même into a dew! or that the everlasting had not fix'd his canon 'gainst self-slaughter! o god! god! how weary, stale, flat, and unprofitable seem to me all the uses of this world! fie on't! ah, fie! 'tis an unweeded jardin that grows to seed; things rank and brut in la nature possess it merely. that it devrait come to this! but two months dead! nay, not so much, not two. so excellent a king, that was to this hyperion to a satyr; so aimant to my mère that he pourrait not beteem the winds of paradis visite her face too roughly. paradis and earth! must i remember? why, she aurait hang on him as if increase of appetite had grandi by what it fed on; and yet, dans a month- let me not pense on't! frailty, thy name is woman!- a peu month, or ere ceux shoes were old with lequel she followed my poor father's body like niobe, all tears- why she, even she (o god! a la bête that wants discours of raison aurait have mourn'd longer) married with my uncle; my father's brother, but no more like my père than i to hercules. dans a month, ere yet the salt of most unrighteous larmes had left the flushing in her galled eyes, she married. o, most wicked speed, to post with such dexterity to incestuous sheets! it is not, nor it ne peux pas come to good. but break my heart, for i must hold my tongue! entrer horatio, marcellus, and bernardo. hor. hail to your lordship! ham. i am glad to see you well. horatio!- or i do oublier myself. hor. the same, my lord, and your poor serviteur ever. ham. sir, my good friend- i'll changement that name with you. and what make you from wittenberg, horatio? marcellus? mar. my good lord! ham. i am very glad to see you.- [to bernardo] good even, sir.- but what, in faith, make you from wittenberg? hor. a truant disposition, good my lord. ham. i aurait not hear your ennemi say so, nor doit you do my ear that violence to make it truster of your own rapport against yourself. i know you are no truant. but what is your affair in elsinore? we'll enseigner you to boisson deep ere you depart. hor. my lord, i came to see your father's funeral. ham. i prithee do not mock me, compagnon student. i pense it was to see my mother's wedding. hor. indeed, my lord, it followed hard upon. ham. thrift, thrift, horatio! the funeral bak'd meats did coldly furnish en avant the mariage tables. aurait i had met my très cher foe in paradis or ever i had seen that day, horatio! my father- methinks i see my father. hor. o, where, my lord? ham. in my mind's eye, horatio. hor. i saw him once. he was a goodly king. ham. he was a man, take him for all in all. i doit not look upon his like again. hor. my lord, i pense i saw him yesternight. ham. saw? who? hor. my lord, the king your father. ham. the king my father? hor. saison your admiration for a tandis que with an attent ear, till i may livrer upon the témoin of celles-ci gentlemen, this marvel to you. ham. for god's love let me hear! hor. two nights ensemble had celles-ci gentlemen (marcellus and bernardo) on leur regarder in the dead vast and middle of the nuit been thus encount'red. a figure like your father, armed at point exactly, cap-a-pe, apparaît avant them and with solennel Mars goes slow and stately by them. thrice he walk'd by leur oppress'd and fear-surprised eyes, dans his truncheon's length; whilst they distill'd presque to jelly with the act of fear, supporter dumb and parler not to him. this to me in horrible secrecy impart they did, and i with them the troisième nuit kept the watch; where, as they had deliver'd, both in time, form of the thing, each word made true and good, the apparition comes. i knew your father. celles-ci mains are not more like. ham. but où was this? mar. my lord, upon the platform où we watch'd. ham. did you not parler to it? hor. my lord, i did; but répondre made it none. yet once methought it lifted up it head and did address lui-même to motion, like as it aurait speak; but even then the Matin cock crew loud, and at the du son it shrunk in hâte away and vanish'd from our sight. ham. 'tis very strange. hor. as i do live, my honour'd lord, 'tis true; and we did pense it writ down in our duty to let you know of it. ham. indeed, indeed, sirs. but this troubles me. hold you the regarder to-night? both [mar. and ber.] we do, my lord. ham. arm'd, say you? both. arm'd, my lord. ham. from top to toe? both. my lord, from head to foot. ham. then saw you not his face? hor. o, yes, my lord! he wore his beaver up. ham. what, look'd he frowningly. hor. a countenance more in chagrin than in anger. ham. pale or red? hor. nay, very pale. ham. and fix'd his eyes upon you? hor. most constantly. ham. i aurait i had been there. hor. it aurait have much amaz'd you. ham. very like, very like. stay'd it long? hor. tandis que one with moderate hâte pourrait tell a hundred. both. longer, longer. hor. not when i saw't. ham. his barbe was grizzled- no? hor. it was, as i have seen it in his life, a sable silver'd. ham. i will regarder to-night. perchance 'twill walk again. hor. i warr'nt it will. ham. if it assume my noble father's person, i'll parler to it, bien que hell lui-même devrait gape and bid me hold my peace. i pray you all, if you have hitherto conceal'd this sight, let it be tenable in your silence still; and whatsoever else doit hap to-night, give it an understanding but no tongue. i will requite your loves. so, fare you well. upon the platform, 'twixt eleven and twelve, i'll visite you. all. our duty to your honour. ham. your loves, as mine to you. farewell. sortir [all but hamlet]. my father's spirit- in arms? all is not well. i doute some foul play. aurait the nuit were come! till then sit still, my soul. foul actes will rise, bien que all the Terre o'erwhelm them, to men's eyes. exit. scène iii. elsinore. a room in the maison of polonius. entrer laertes and ophelia. laer. my necessaries are embark'd. farewell. and, sister, as the winds give avantage and convoy is assistant, do not sleep, but let me hear from you. oph. do you doute that? laer. for hamlet, and the trifling of his favour, hold it a fashion, and a toy in blood; a violet in the jeunesse of primy nature, forward, not permanent- sweet, not lasting; the perfume and suppliance of a minute; no more. oph. no more but so? laer. pense it no more. for la nature crescent does not grow seul in thews and bulk; but as this temple waxes, the inward un service of the mind and soul grows wide withal. peut-être he aime you now, and now no soil nor cautel doth besmirch the vertu of his will; but you must fear, his greatness weigh'd, his will is not his own; for he himself is matière to his birth. he may not, as unvalued persons do, carve for himself, for on his choix depends the sécurité and santé of this entier state, and par conséquent must his choix be circumscrib'd unto the voix and yielding of that body whereof he is the head. then if he says he aime you, it fits your sagesse so far to croyez it as he in his particulier act and endroit may give his en disant deed; lequel is no plus loin than the main voix of denmark goes withal. then weigh what loss your honour may sustain if with too credent ear you list his songs, or lose your heart, or your chaste Trésor open to his unmast'red importunity. fear it, ophelia, fear it, my dear sister, and keep you in the rear of your affection, out of the shot and danger of desire. the chariest maid is prodigal assez if she unmask her beauté to the moon. vertu lui-même scopes not calumnious strokes. the canker galls the infants of the printemps too oft avant leur buttons be disclos'd, and in the morn and liquid dew of jeunesse contagious blastments are most imminent. be wary then; best sécurité lies in fear. jeunesse to lui-même rebels, bien que none else near. oph. i doit th' effet of this good lesson keep as watchman to my heart. but, good my brother, do not as some ungracious pastors do, show me the steep and thorny way to heaven, whiles, like a puff'd and reckless libertine, himself the primrose path of dalliance treads and recks not his own rede. laer. o, fear me not! entrer polonius. i stay too long. but here my père comes. a double bénédiction is a double grace; occasion smiles upon a seconde leave. pol. yet here, laertes? aboard, aboard, for shame! the wind sits in the shoulder of your sail, and you are stay'd for. there- my bénédiction with thee! and celles-ci few precepts in thy Mémoire look thou character. give thy thoughts no tongue, nor any unproportion'd pensée his act. be thou familiar, but by no veux dire vulgar: ceux friends thou hast, and leur adoption tried, grapple them unto thy soul with hoops of steel; but do not dull thy palm with divertissement of each new-hatch'd, unfledg'd comrade. beware of entrance to a quarrel; but étant in, bear't that th' opposed may beware of thee. give chaque man thine ear, but few thy voice; take each man's censure, but reserve thy judgment. costly thy habitude as thy bourse can buy, but not express'd in fancy; rich, not gaudy; for the vêtements oft proclaims the man, and they in France of the best rank and station are most select and generous, chef in that. ni a borrower nor a lender be; for loan oft loses both lui-même and friend, and borrowing dulls the edge of husbandry. this au dessus all- to thine own self be true, and it must follow, as the nuit the day, thou canst not then be faux to any man. farewell. my bénédiction saison this in thee! laer. most humbly do i take my leave, my lord. pol. the time invites you. go, your servants tend. laer. farewell, ophelia, and rappelles toi well what i have said to you. oph. 'tis in my Mémoire lock'd, and you toi même doit keep the key of it. laer. farewell. exit. pol. what is't, ophelia, he hath said to you? oph. so S'il vous plaît you, quelque chose touching the lord hamlet. pol. marry, well bethought! 'tis told me he hath very oft of late donné privé time to you, and you toi même have of your audience been most free and bounteous. if it be so- as so 'tis put on me, and that in way of caution- i must tell you you do not comprendre toi même so clearly as it behooves my fille and your honour. what is entre you? give me up the truth. oph. he hath, my lord, of late made many tenders of his affection to me. pol. affection? pooh! you parler like a vert girl, unsifted in such perilous circumstance. do you croyez his tenders, as you call them? oph. i do not know, my lord, what i devrait think, pol. marry, i will enseigner you! pense toi même a baby that you have ta'en celles-ci tenders for true pay, lequel are not sterling. soumissionner toi même more dearly, or (not to crack the wind of
